Divine Attributes
beginner

Three Additional Proofs: Consensus, Perfection, and Order
Sanusi presents three additional arguments for divine power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three supplementary arguments for God's power and knowledge: consensus and scripture, the nature of perfection, and the order of the cosmos.

Core Idea
Three quick proofs: scripture says so, perfection is good, order implies a wise maker.

After the sixth proof, Sanusi offers three more arguments that are often used to establish that the Creator is powerful and knowing. These are drawn from consensus and revelatory texts, the inherent perfections of attributes like power and life, and the observed order and craftsmanship in the universe. Each argument has its own strength and weaknesses, which the author will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify each proof: the proof from consensus and scripture (al-ijma' wa al-nusus), the proof from perfection (al-kamal), and the proof from cosmic order (al-nizam).
2
Understand the first proof: consensus and scripture
The first proof appeals to the consensus of Muslims and the definitive texts of Quran and Sunnah, which affirm God's power and knowledge. Consider the role of revelation in theological reasoning.
3
Understand the second proof: perfections and their opposites
The second proof argues that power, knowledge, and life are perfections, and their opposites (ignorance, inability, death) are deficiencies. Since God must be free from deficiency, He must possess these perfections.
4
Understand the third proof: order implies a wise maker
The third proof is a classic design argument: the universe displays subtle craftsmanship, perfect order, and solidity, so its maker must be knowing and powerful by necessity.
5
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how these three proofs together reinforce the conclusion, but note that each relies on certain premises that may be challenged, as the author will later do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بموجب لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع أجزاء البسيط على السواء. السادس: فاعل الحيوانات وأعضائها على صورها وأشكالها، يجب أن يكون قادراً مختاراً. إذ لو كان طبيعة النطفة أو أمراً خارجياً موجباً لزم أن يكون الحيوان على شكل الكرة، إن كانت النطفة بسيطة، لأن ذلك مقتضى الطبيعية، ونسبة الموجب إلى أجزاء البسيط على السوية، وعلى شكل كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى البعض إن كانت النطفة مركبة من البسائط بمثل ما ذكر. وقد يتمسك في إثبات كون الباري قادراً عالماً بالإجماع، والنصوص القطعية من الكتاب والسنة، وبأن القدرة والعلم والحياة ونحو ذلك صفات كمال. وأضدادها من العجز والجهل والممات سمات نقص يجب تنزيه الله عنها. وبأن صانع العالم على ما فيه من لطائف الصنع وكمال الانتظام والإحكام عالم قادر بحكم الضرورة. وهذه الوجوه لا تخلو عن محال مناقشة: أما الستة الأول: قلما لا يخفى على المتأمل فيها الواقف على قواعد الفلسفة. وأما السابع: فلان مرجع الأدلة السمعية إلى الكتاب، ودلالة المعجزات، وهل يتم الإقرار بها، والإذعان لها قبل التصديق بكون الباري قادراً عالماً فيه تردد وتأمل. وأما الثامن: فلأنه فرع جواز اتصافه بها وكونها كمالات في حقه ووجوب اتصافه بكل كمال ونحو ذلك من المقدمات التي ربما يناقش فيها. وأما التاسع: فلابتنائه على أن ما يشاهد من أمر السماء والأرض مستند إلى الواجب بلا واسطة إلى بعض معلولاته على ما يزعم الفلسفي، لكن من كان طالباً للحق غير هائم في أودية الضلال ربما يستفيد من هذه الوجوه القطع واليقين بلا احتمال.
By necessity, preponderance would require a preponderater, because the relation of the necessary cause to all parts of the simple is equal. The sixth: The maker of animals and their limbs in their forms and shapes must be powerful and willful. For if it were the nature of the sperm or an external necessary cause, then the animal would necessarily be spherical, if the sperm were simple, because that is the requirement of nature, and the relation of the necessary cause to the parts of the simple is equal; and in the form of spheres attached to one another if the sperm were composed of simples in the manner mentioned. And one may also adduce in proving that the Creator is powerful and knowing: consensus, and the definitive texts from the Book and the Sunna; and that power, knowledge, life and the like are attributes of perfection, while their opposites—inability, ignorance, death—are marks of deficiency from which God must be declared free; and that the Maker of the world, given what is in it of subtle craftsmanship and perfection of order and solidity, is necessarily knowing and powerful by necessity. And these arguments are not free from grounds for discussion: As for the first six, they are hardly hidden from the one who reflects on them and is versed in the rules of philosophy. As for the seventh: because the reference of scriptural proofs is to the Book and the evidence of miracles, and whether one acknowledges and submits to them before affirming that the Creator is powerful and knowing involves hesitation and reflection. As for the eighth: because it depends on the permissibility of His being described with them, and their being perfections in His regard, and the necessity of His being described with every perfection, and the like, which are premises that may be questioned. As for the ninth: because it is based on the claim that what is observed in the matter of heaven and earth is referred to the Necessary Being without mediation to some of His effects, as the philosopher asserts. But whoever is a seeker of truth, not straying in the valleys of error, may perhaps gain from these arguments certainty and conviction without doubt.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of arguments for God's power and knowledge, presenting the sixth proof and three additional proofs, followed by a critique. The sixth proof focuses on the creation of animals: the maker of animals and their limbs must be powerful and willful. If the cause were merely natural necessity (e.g., the nature of the sperm), then the resulting animal would have a simple spherical shape (if the sperm is simple) or a composite of spheres (if the sperm is composite), but we observe diverse forms, so the cause must be an agent with will. Then the author presents three further proofs: (1) consensus and definitive texts from Quran and Sunnah; (2) the argument that attributes like power and knowledge are perfections, and their opposites are deficiencies from which God must be free; (3) the argument from the observed order and perfection of the world, which implies a knowing and powerful maker by necessity. The author then states that these arguments are not free from discussion, and briefly critiques each. The first six proofs (likely those previously mentioned) are not hidden from one versed in philosophy (i.e., they are understood by philosophers but may not be decisive). The seventh proof (scripture) depends on prior acceptance of God's power and knowledge, creating a potential circularity. The eighth proof (perfection) relies on premises about what constitutes perfection and its attribution to God, which can be disputed. The ninth proof (causality) assumes that observed phenomena are directly caused by God without intermediate causes, a claim made by philosophers but not universally accepted. The passage ends on a hopeful note: the sincere seeker of truth may still attain certainty from these arguments.

Key Terms
الإجماع
النصوص القطعية
صفات كمال
العجز
الجهل
الممات
لطائف الصنع
كمال الانتظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these proofs are irrefutable logical demonstrations.
Correction: These proofs are plausible but rely on premises that can be questioned, as Sanusi will show in his critique.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three additional proofs mentioned in the passage?
A) Consensus and definitive texts
B) The argument from perfection and deficiency
C) The argument from efficient causation
D) The argument from cosmic order

Key Takeaway
Three common arguments for God's power and knowledge are based on scripture, the nature of perfection, and the order of the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the order of the world proves that God is all-knowing and all-powerful, what area of your life where you are living in disorder or rebellion against His wisdom will you surrender to Him today?
?
The attribute of perfection includes that God has life, knowledge, and power. Do you truly live as if God knows every thought and deed of yours? What specific habit would change if you deeply believed that?
?
Consensus of the righteous is a proof of God's attributes. Are you seeking the company of those whose faith strengthens yours, or are you isolating yourself? Commit to one action to join a community of believers this week.

Critique of the Proofs: A Scholarly Assessment
Sanusi critiques the six proofs and three additional proofs, noting their weaknesses but affirming their potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the potential weaknesses of the proofs for God's power and knowledge as critiqued by the author, and why they may still yield certainty for the sincere seeker.

Core Idea
The proofs are not flawless, but they can still convince the truth-seeker.

After presenting several proofs, Sanusi does not present them as unassailable. He offers a critique, pointing out that each proof has grounds for discussion. The first six proofs (presumably those from earlier sections) are only fully understood by those versed in philosophy. The scriptural proof may involve circularity, the perfection argument rests on disputed premises, and the cosmic argument assumes a particular view of causality. Yet he ends on a hopeful note, suggesting a sincere seeker may still attain certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author systematically addresses each type of proof: the first six, the seventh (scripture), the eighth (perfection), and the ninth (cosmic order).
2
Understand the critique of the first six proofs
The author says these proofs are 'hardly hidden from the one who reflects and is versed in philosophy.' This implies they are not easily accessible to everyone and may rely on philosophical concepts.
3
Understand the critique of the seventh proof (scripture)
The scriptural proof depends on the authority of revelation, but accepting that authority itself requires prior belief in God's power and knowledge, creating a potential circularity.
4
Understand the critique of the eighth proof (perfection)
This proof relies on premises such as what constitutes a perfection in God and that God must possess all perfections. These premises can be debated.
5
Understand the critique of the ninth proof (cosmic order)
This proof assumes that the observed phenomena are directly caused by God without intermediate causes, a claim made by philosophers but not universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
Do you demand flawless proofs before you submit to God, or are you willing to accept that human reasoning has limitations? Which attitude has defined your spiritual journey so far, and what will you do to change it starting today?

First Objection: Need for a Preponderating Factor and Its Refutation
Objection against divine power and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine power regarding the need for a preponderating factor, its implication of infinite regress, and the refutation that denies both implications by appealing to the will itself and distinguishing between types of preponderating.

Core Idea
God's will is inherently preponderating — no external factor needed.

Opponents of divine power often raise a dilemma: either God's power needs a preponderating factor to choose an action, leading to infinite regress, or it does not, making it impossible to prove God's existence through His actions. Sanusi records this objection and then offers a concise refutation that denies both horns. Let's examine how he defends divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its dilemma
First, identify the opponent's argument: if the attachment of power requires a preponderating factor (مرجح), then infinite regress follows; if not, then we cannot prove the Maker. Understand the two horns.
The opponent tries to force a contradiction in the concept of divine power.
2
Study the refutation's two points
The refutation denies both implications. It first says the preponderating factor can be the will itself (الإرادة) by its essence, stopping the regress. Second, it distinguishes between a capable agent's choice and a possible thing's existence: the former does not need an external cause, the latter does.
The key distinction: agent's self-determination vs. thing's coming into existence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is important: it preserves God's free will as self-sufficient, without requiring an external cause for His choices, and avoids the absurdity of infinite regress.
This is a classic kalam argument for divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة المخالفين في قدرة الله تعالى قال: تمسك المخالف بوجوه : الأول : تعلق القدرة إن افتقر إلى مرجح تسلسل، وإن لم يفتقر انسد باب إثبات الصانع، ورد بمنع الملازمتين لجواز أن يكون المرجح تعلق الإرادة لذاتها ولأن ترجيح القادر أحد مقدوريه بلا مرجح بمعنى تخصيصه بلا داعية غير ترجيح الممكن بلا مرجح، يعني تحققه بلا مؤثر .
Arguments of the opponents regarding Allah's power. He said: The opponent held on to [several] points: The first: The attachment of power: if it requires a preponderating factor, an infinite regress follows; and if it does not require it, the door of proving the Maker is closed. It was refuted by denying both implications because it is permissible that the preponderating factor be the attachment of will by its very essence, and because the preponderating of the capable [agent] one of his two possible actions without a preponderating factor, in the sense of specifying it without an inclination, is different from the preponderating of a possible [thing] without a preponderating factor, meaning its existence without an effect.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage records the first of several objections typically raised by opponents (likely philosophers or theologians who deny that God acts with free choice) regarding the attribute of power (القدرة). The objection poses a dilemma: If God's power requires a preponderating factor (مرجح) to select one action over another, this leads to an infinite regress (تسلسل) because that factor itself would require another, and so on. If instead no factor is needed, then the door to proving the existence of the Maker (إثبات الصانع) is closed, because we would have no way to distinguish God's act from mere chance. The refutation (ورد) denies both implications (منع الملازمتين) by offering two points. First, the preponderating factor could simply be the attachment of will (الإرادة) by its very essence (لذاتها)—that is, God's will is inherently decisive and does not need an external cause. Second, the opponent's argument confuses two senses of 'preponderating without a factor'. When a capable agent (القادر) chooses between two possible actions, his choice without an external inclination (داعية) is simply self-determination. This is different from a possible thing (الممكن) existing without an effective cause (مؤثر), which would be absurd. So the dilemma fails.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة (attachment of power)
مرجح (preponderating factor)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
إثبات الصانع (proving the Maker)
الملازمتين (the two implications)
الإرادة (will)
القادر (the capable agent)
المقدور (possible action)
الداعية (inclination)
الممكن (the possible)
المؤثر (effective cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every act, including God's, requires an external preponderating factor to determine which possible action occurs.
Correction: The refutation shows that God's will itself can serve as the preponderating factor by its very essence, without needing an external cause. Additionally, the agent's self-determination is different from a possible thing's need for a cause.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation respond to the opponent's claim that if power needs a preponderating factor, an infinite regress follows?
A) It accepts the infinite regress as possible.
B) It denies that a preponderating factor is needed at all.
C) It states that the preponderating factor can be the will itself by its essence.
D) It says the preponderating factor is the possible itself.

Key Takeaway
God's will is inherently decisive and does not require an external cause to specify His actions, thereby avoiding both infinite regress and the impossibility of proving the Maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems senseless, do you instinctively demand to know the 'preponderating factor' — the reason why God allowed it? How will you consciously submit to His will as inherently decisive and wise, without waiting for an explanation?
?
If God's will is self-sufficient and needs no external cause, does your dua reflect that trust, or do you sometimes feel your prayer is the cause that 'moves' God? What specific change can you make in your dua tonight to align with this truth?
?
Think of a past decision where you procrastinated because you waited for a clear sign or reason (a 'preponderating factor'). In light of this lesson—that God's will is decisive without external factors—what step can you take today to act decisively in obedience to Him, trusting His guidance already given?

The Attachment of Divine Power and Will: Eternal or Temporal?
Second objection and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God's power and will must be either eternal or temporal is refuted by distinguishing the direction of attachment from the creation event.

Core Idea
God's eternal will can be directed to a future creation without the world being eternal.

This passage tackles a subtle objection about the relationship between God's eternal attributes and temporal creation. Critics argue that if God's power and will are attached to creation, that attachment must itself be either eternal or temporal — both posing problems. The refutation shows two ways the attachment can avoid these pitfalls, preserving both divine transcendence and creatio ex nihilo.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the concise style: the objection is stated in a few lines, then refuted.
2
Identify the two horns of the objection
The objection presents a dichotomy: the attachment is either eternal (قديم) or temporal (حادث). List the consequences of each: eternal → world eternal; temporal → infinite regress.
3
Understand the refutation's two responses
The refutation denies the forced choice (المنع) and offers two alternatives. First: the attachment is eternal but directed to creation at a future time (في ما لا يزال). Second: the attachment itself is temporal by its own essence (لذاتها). Trace how each avoids the unwanted consequences.
This shows that the attachment's mode need not imply the world's eternity.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this defense maintains Allah's transcendent eternity while affirming that the world began in time. The eternal attribute attaches to a temporal act without contradiction.
This is a key move in kalam to reconcile divine attributes with creation ex nihilo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة إما قديم فيلزم قدم العالم، وإما حادث فتتسلسل الحوادث ورد بالمنع الجواز أن يتعلقا في الأزل بإيجاده في ما لا يزال، أو يكون حدوث تعلقهما لذاتها.
The second: That the attachment of power and will: either it is eternal, then the eternity of the world follows; or it is temporal, then temporal events regress infinitely. It was refuted by denying [the dichotomy] and permitting that they attach in pre-eternity to its bringing into existence in what is yet to come, or that the temporality of their attachment is by their essence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: the attachment (تعلق) of God's power and will to creation must be either eternal (قديم) or temporal (حادث). If eternal, then the world must be eternal (since the attachment would have always been effective), which contradicts Islamic creation ex nihilo. If temporal, then the attachment itself would be a temporal event requiring another attachment, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events (تسلسل الحوادث). The refutation (ورد) denies the necessity of this dichotomy (المنع) and offers two possibilities: either the attachment is eternal in the sense that it is directed in pre-eternity (الأزل) to bringing the world into existence at the future time (في ما لا يزال) — that is, God's eternal will decided to create at a specific later time — or the attachment itself becomes temporal by its own essence (لذاتها), meaning it is inherently a temporal event without needing a prior attachment. Thus, the world need not be eternal, and the regress is avoided.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة والإرادة
قديم
حادث
قدم العالم
تسلسل الحوادث
الأزل
في ما لا يزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the attachment is eternal, the world must be eternal.
Correction: The refutation shows that an eternal attachment can be directed to a future creation, so the world need not be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, how can the attachment of power be eternal without entailing the eternity of the world?
A) It cannot; the opponent's argument stands.
B) The attachment is eternal but directed to creation at a future time.
C) The attachment is not eternal but temporal without regress.
D) Both B and C are given as possibilities.

Key Takeaway
The attachment of God's power and will can be eternal without entailing the eternity of the world, by being directed to a future creation or being temporal by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal will was directed to your creation at a specific time before you existed, how does that affect your sense of purpose and your trust in His timing in your life right now?
?
The refutation shows that God's attachment to creation is not constrained by our categories of 'eternal' or 'temporal'. Are there areas in your life where you try to limit God by your own understanding of time and causation? What would it mean to let go of that limitation?
?
Reflect on the idea that God's power and will are attached to you without needing a chain of prior causes. How does this certainty about God's direct involvement challenge any excuse you might make for delaying obedience or repentance?

The Third Objection: Necessity and Free Will
Third objection and refutation on necessity and free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity that arises from a capable agent does not negate free will, and how it differs from the necessity of a mere cause.

Core Idea
Necessity from a free agent confirms choice; necessity from a cause eliminates it.

The third objection claims that if an agent possesses all conditions for an act, the act necessarily occurs, seemingly negating free will. This objection challenges the Islamic understanding of divine and human agency. In this passage, the author presents the objection and its refutation, clarifying a crucial distinction between the necessity that arises from a free agent and that which arises from a non-voluntary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage presenting the objection: if the agent has all requirements, the act becomes necessary because failure is impossible for a capable agent.
Notice the term 'الفاعل' (agent) and 'القادر' (capable).
2
Understand the refutation
Read the refutation starting from 'ورد بأن'. The distinction is drawn between necessity from a free agent (القادر) and necessity from a necessitating cause (الموجب).
The refutation uses the phrase 'إن شاء ترك' (if he wills, he leaves) to illustrate choice.
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine attributes: Allah is the perfect free agent, and His actions are necessarily good but freely chosen.
This shows that necessity and free will are compatible in the divine context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الفاعل إن استجمع جميع ما لا بد منه وجب رده الامتناع التخلف في القادر، ورد بأن الوجوب من القادر لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه، بخلاف الوجوب من الموجب فإنه لا يصح فيه إنه إن شاء ترك .
The third: That the agent, if he collects all that is necessary, his act becomes necessary [literally: its occurrence is necessary] due to the impossibility of failure in the capable. It was refuted by [saying] that the necessity from the capable agent does not negate choice but rather actualizes it, unlike the necessity from the necessitating cause, for in that case it is not valid that 'if he wills he leaves'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection: if a capable agent (الفاعل) possesses all the conditions required for an act (استجمع جميع ما لا بد منه), the act necessarily occurs (وجب رده) because it is impossible for a capable agent to fail when all conditions are met. The opponent claims that this necessity would negate free choice (اختيار). The refutation (ورد) distinguishes between two kinds of necessity: the necessity that arises from a free agent (القادر) and the necessity that arises from a necessitating cause (الموجب). In a free agent, the necessity is compatible with choice; indeed, it actualizes choice (يحققه) because the agent's will is the source. In contrast, a necessitating cause (like a natural cause) produces its effect necessarily without any possibility of refraining: if the cause is present, the effect follows, and it is not valid to say 'if it wills, it leaves it'. So the necessity of the free agent does not negate freedom.

Key Terms
الفاعل
القادر
الاختيار
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any necessity negates free will.
Correction: Necessity arising from a free agent's own choice does not negate free will; it actualizes it. Only necessity from an external cause negates choice.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation accept that the act necessarily occurs when all conditions are met?
A) It denies that the act necessarily occurs.
B) It says this necessity does not negate choice in a free agent.
C) It says the agent is not truly capable.
D) It says the act is contingent anyway.

Key Takeaway
Necessity from a free agent is compatible with free will and even actualizes it, unlike necessity from an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel compelled to act in a way that aligns with your deepest convictions, do you experience that as a loss of freedom or as its fullest expression? How does this lesson's distinction between two kinds of necessity reshape your answer? Be honest about a specific decision you face.
?
The lesson says necessity from a free agent 'actualizes choice.' Think of a habit or commitment you have cultivated intentionally—prayer, honesty, patience. In what sense has it become 'necessary' for you? Does that make it less free or more free? What does that say about your relationship with Allah?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's will is all-encompassing, does that ever make your own choices feel empty or predetermined? Read the refutation again: how does it directly address that fear? What would change in your life if you fully accepted that your choices are real and meaningful even within Allah's necessity?

Fourth Objection: Power's Equal Relation to Existence and Non-existence
The fourth objection that power relates equally to existence and non-existence, so creation would be impossible, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that divine power relates equally to existence and non-existence, and how the refutation clarifies that power over non-existence means the ability to refrain from creating, not the ability to create non-existence as an act.

Core Idea
Power over non-existence means refraining from creating, not willing non-existence as a positive act.

This passage presents the fourth objection against the doctrine that God's power is specific to creating existence. The objection argues that if power relates equally to existence and non-existence, then creating existence is impossible because non-existence is eternal and cannot be an object of power. The refutation clarifies what it means to have power over non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated first, then the refutation.
The objection is concise; pay attention to the claim that non-existence is eternal and a pure negation.
2
Understand the objection's reasoning
The objector says that power relates equally to existence and non-existence. Since non-existence cannot be an object of power (being eternal and pure negation), existence also cannot be an object of power. Thus creation is impossible.
The key premise is that equal relation implies equal impossibility.
3
Study the refutation's distinction
The refutation reinterprets 'power over non-existence' not as creating non-existence, but as the ability to withhold creation: if He wills He does not create, and if He does not will He does not create.
This refutation preserves the possibility of creation while affirming God's power over non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن نسبة القدرة إلى الوجود نسبتها إلى العدم، وهو لا يصلح مقدوراً لكونه أزلياً ونفياً محضاً فكذلك الوجود. ورد بأن معنى القدرة على العدم أنه إن شاء لم يفعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل لا إن شاء فعل العدم.
The fourth: That the relation of power to existence is the same as its relation to non-existence, but non-existence is not suitable as an object of power because it is eternal and a pure negation, so likewise existence. It was refuted by [saying] that the meaning of power over non-existence is that if He wills, He does not do it, and if He does not will, He does not do it; not that He wills non-existence as an act.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth objection: power (القدرة) relates equally (نسبة واحدة) to both existence (الوجود) and non-existence (العدم). Non-existence, however, cannot be an object of power (مقدور) because it is eternal (أزلي) and a pure negation (نفي محض) — it does not have a real existence to be created. Therefore, if power cannot affect non-existence, it cannot be said to 'create' existence either, if the relation is the same. The refutation clarifies that 'power over non-existence' does not mean that God wills non-existence as something positive to be brought about. Rather, it means that if He wills not to create, He does not create (إن شاء لم يفعل), and if He does not will to create, He also does not create (وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل). In other words, power over non-existence is simply the ability to refrain from creating. So existence is the actual object of power when He wills it.

Key Terms
نسبة (relation)
القدرة (power)
الوجود (existence)
العدم (non-existence)
مقدور (object of power)
أزلي (eternal)
نفي محض (pure negation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power over non-existence means God can actively bring about non-existence as a positive act.
Correction: The refutation explains that power over non-existence is the ability to refrain from creating, not to create non-existence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the correct meaning of God's power over non-existence?
A) God actively brings about non-existence.
B) God has no power over non-existence.
C) It means God can choose not to create.
D) It means God creates non-existence as a substance.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that power over non-existence is simply the ability to refrain from creating, not a power to create non-existence itself, thus preserving the possibility of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your circumstances are so impossible that even God cannot create a way out? How does this lesson's insistence that God's power is over existence alone—and that His 'power over non-existence' is His freedom to refrain—reshape your trust in His ability to bring good from nothing?
?
When you face a prolonged difficulty, do you secretly doubt that God can create a positive change because you see no potential for existence? Reflect on the refutation: power over non-existence is simply the choice to refrain. What if the difficulty itself is a refraining on God's part—not a limitation—and He could create at any moment? Will you wait with patience or demand immediate action?
?
Think of a time you blamed God or yourself for something that did not happen—a missed opportunity, an unanswered prayer. Did you treat non-existence as if it were a 'thing' God could have willed? How does realizing that God never wills non-existence as an act but merely refrains from creating, set you free from guilt and restore your trust in His perfect wisdom?

The Fifth Objection: Need or Futility?
The fifth objection and its refutation via intrinsic or other-related preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection against divine freedom is refuted by distinguishing between intrinsic and other-related appropriateness.

Core Idea
An act's appropriateness can be intrinsic or for others, avoiding both need and futility.

This lesson presents the fifth objection to divine freedom of action: if an act is more appropriate than refraining, it implies God seeks perfection; if not, it's futile. The refutation offers a nuanced answer: the act can be more appropriate in itself or for the benefit of creation. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the dilemma: either the act is more appropriate, implying need, or it is not, implying futility.
The objection is based on the idea that any preference must have a purpose.
2
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if the act is more appropriate, then God would be acquiring perfection from another (الاستكمال بالغير). The second horn: if not, then the act is futile (عبث). Understand why both seem problematic for divine perfection.
3
Learn the refutation's key insight
The refutation says that it suffices to negate futility if the act is more appropriate either in itself (في نفسه) or in relation to another (بالنسبة إلى الغير). This dissolves the dilemma.
Intrinsic appropriateness means the act is inherently fitting; other-related means it benefits creation.
4
Apply the refutation to the objection
Consider how the refutation avoids both charges: it does not require God to be perfected by the act, nor is it futile, because the act has value either intrinsically or for others.
5
Review the key terms
Review the key terms: المختار, أولى, الاستكمال بالغير, العبث, نفي العبث. Make sure you can explain each in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المختار إن كان الفعل أولى به من الترك يلزم الاستكمال بالغير وإلا فالعبث ورد بأنه يكفي في نفي العبث كونه أولى في نفسه أو بالنسبة إلى الغير.
The fifth: That the free agent: if the act is more appropriate to him than refraining, then acquiring perfection from another follows; otherwise, [it is] futility. It was refuted by [saying] that it suffices to negate futility that it is more appropriate in itself or in relation to another.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage deals with the fifth objection concerning an agent who acts by choice (المختار). The opponent argues: if performing the act is more appropriate (أولى) for the agent than refraining (الترك), then the agent would be seeking perfection from something outside himself (الاستكمال بالغير), which is impossible for a perfect God. If, on the other hand, the act is not more appropriate, then it would be futile (عبث). The refutation (ورد) states that it is sufficient to negate futility that the act is more appropriate either in itself (في نفسه) — i.e., it is inherently fitting for the agent — or in relation to another (بالنسبة إلى الغير) — i.e., it benefits creation. Thus, there is no need to posit that God acquires perfection from outside; the act's appropriateness is intrinsic or for the benefit of others, avoiding both the charge of need and the charge of futility.

Key Terms
المختار (the free agent)
الفعل (act)
الترك (refraining)
أولى (more appropriate/more excellent)
الاستكمال بالغير (acquiring perfection from another)
العبث (futility)
نفي العبث (negating futility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every act must either perfect the agent or be futile.
Correction: An act can be more appropriate in itself (intrinsically fitting) or for the benefit of others, without perfecting the agent or being futile.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation avoid the conclusion that God's act is either for His perfection or futile?
A) It accepts that it is for His perfection.
B) It accepts that it is futile.
C) It says the act can be more appropriate in itself or for others.
D) It denies that God acts by choice.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows divine acts are not futile nor do they perfect God, because they are inherently fitting or beneficial for creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every divine act is either intrinsically good or beneficial for others, can you still complain about a trial in your life right now? Be honest: what does your heart say about God's wisdom in that situation? Will you commit to trusting that it is never futile?
?
The objection assumes God must either gain something or be arbitrary. Where in your own life do you judge God's actions based on whether they seem to 'improve' your situation or make sense to you? Are you willing to accept that God's acts are infinitely fitting even when you see no benefit? What would that look like practically?
?
Think of a recent disappointment. Did you, even silently, think that God's decree was pointless or that He did not care? If not, then what? If you did, how does the refutation in this lesson challenge that thought? Will you repent and realign your heart with the truth that every divine act is inherently appropriate and meaningful?

Sixth Objection: An Eternal Effect from a Free Agent
The sixth objection and its refutation using the distinction between intrinsic and conditional possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against the possibility of an eternal effect from a free agent and how the refutation resolves it by distinguishing between intrinsic possibility and impossibility due to dependency.

Core Idea
An effect can be possible in itself yet not actual in eternity because it depends on a free choice.

The sixth objection presents a dilemma: if an eternal act of a free agent were impossible from eternity, then later becoming possible would imply a change. If it were possible, then an eternal effect could depend on a free agent. The refutation cuts through by distinguishing between intrinsic possibility and impossibility due to being an effect of a free cause. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the entire argument is in one sentence.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection presents two conditional statements: if the effect is impossible in pre-eternity, then a change would occur; if it is possible, then an eternal thing could depend on a free agent. Find these two parts in the text.
The Arabic uses 'إن امتنع' and 'وإن أمكن' to mark the two conditions.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that in pre-eternity the thing is possible in itself but impossible because it is an effect of the free agent. This distinction avoids both absurdities.
Key terms: 'ممكن لذاته' (possible in itself) and 'ممتنع لكونه أثر المختار' (impossible because it is an effect of the free agent).
4
Reflect on why the distinction works
The impossibility in pre-eternity is not intrinsic but due to its dependency on a free cause that has not yet caused it. Therefore, no change in the essence occurs, and the eternal dependence is not problematic because the effect's possibility in itself remains unchanging.
This preserves both the freedom of the agent and the logical consistency of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن أثر المختار إن امتنع في الأزل لزم الانقلاب وإن أمكن لزم جواز استناد الأزلي إلى المختار ورد بأنه في الأزل ممكن لذاته ممتنع لكونه أثر المختار.
The sixth: That the effect of the free agent: if it is impossible in pre-eternity, then [the impossibility] changes [into possibility], and if it is possible, then the possibility of an eternal thing depending on a free agent follows. It was refuted by [saying] that it is in pre-eternity possible in itself, impossible because it is an effect of the free agent.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final objection regarding the possibility of an eternal effect (أثر) from a free agent (المختار). The opponent argues: if such an effect is impossible in pre-eternity (الأزل) — that is, cannot exist from eternity — then later it becomes possible, which would be a change in the nature of the effect (انقلاب), which is impossible. If, on the other hand, it is possible in pre-eternity, then it would be permissible for an eternal thing to depend on the free agent, which the opponent might find objectionable. The refutation (ورد) clarifies that the effect in pre-eternity is possible in itself (ممكن لذاته) but impossible specifically because it is an effect of the free agent (ممتنع لكونه أثر المختار). That is, its impossibility is not intrinsic but due to its dependency on a free cause that has not yet caused it. This does not involve a change in the essence of the thing; rather, the thing remains possible in itself, but it does not actually exist because the agent has not willed it in eternity. Thus, the objection does not force an absurdity.

Key Terms
أثر المختار (effect of the free agent)
الأزل (pre-eternity)
امتنع (impossible)
انقلاب (change/transformation)
أمكن (possible)
استناد الأزلي (dependence of the eternal)
ممكن لذاته (possible in itself)
ممتنع لكونه أثر المختار (impossible because it is an effect of the free agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible in itself, it must be actual in eternity.
Correction: The refutation shows that something can be possible in itself yet not actual because its actuality depends on the free agent's choice, which may not have occurred in pre-eternity. Thus, possibility in itself does not entail actuality.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation explain the status of an effect of the free agent in pre-eternity?
A) It is impossible both in itself and as an effect.
B) It is possible in itself but impossible because it is an effect of the free agent.
C) It is necessary in itself.
D) It is impossible in itself but possible as an effect.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between intrinsic possibility and conditional impossibility due to dependency on a free agent resolves the dilemma without implying change or eternal dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that nothing can become actual without God's free choice, how would that change the way you ask Him for something you deeply desire? Are you treating that desire as if it must happen because it is possible in itself, or are you submitting to His timing?
?
Think of an area in your life where you feel stuck — a situation that seems impossible to change. Is that impossibility intrinsic, or is it conditional? Does this lesson give you hope that the same God who chose not to create in eternity can choose to act in your life without any change in His nature? How does that press you to pray with renewed earnestness?
?
The objection assumed that if a thing is possible, it must be actual at some point. Do you ever live as if God owes you a certain outcome because it is good and possible? How does the refutation — that possibility alone does not necessitate actuality — expose a lack of trust in God's wisdom and will?

The First Objection on Divine Power and Its Refutation
First objection on divine power and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the Ash'ari view of divine power and how it is refuted by denying both implications and distinguishing between specification and causation.

Core Idea
The refutation distinguishes an agent's choice from a cause's preponderance.

In this passage, Sanusi presents an objection that challenges the Ash'ari doctrine of divine power and will. The objection claims that if God's power needs a preponderating factor, it leads to infinite regress; if not, the proof for God's existence collapses. The refutation denies both implications and clarifies the role of the divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic objection and its translation. Notice that the objection presents a dilemma with two horns: either divine power requires a preponderating factor (leading to infinite regress) or it does not (closing the door to proving God).
The objection is structured as a conditional argument.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if the attachment of power depends on a preponderating factor, that factor itself would need another, causing an infinite chain (tasalsul). The second horn: if it does not, then the principle that every possible requires a preponderating factor (used to prove God) is violated.
This dilemma targets the consistency of the Ash'ari position.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation (radd) denies both implications (man' al-mulazamatayn). It offers two reasons: first, the preponderating factor can be God's will itself, which attaches by its essence; second, the powerful agent's specification of one of two possible acts without a motive is not the same as a possible thing existing without an effect.
Pay attention to the distinction between two senses of 'without preponderating factor'.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the refutation defends the Ash'ari view: God's will is a self-sufficient preponderator that does not require another factor, and choosing between possibilities is different from causing existence.
This lesson shows how kalam objections are refuted through precise conceptual distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : تعلق القدرة إن افتقر إلى مرجح تسلسل، وإن لم يفتقر انسد باب إثبات الصانع، ورد بمنع الملازمتين لجواز أن يكون المرجح تعلق الإرادة لذاتها ولأن ترجيح القادر أحد مقدوريه بلا مرجح بمعنى تخصيصه بلا داعية غير ترجيح الممكن بلا مرجح، يعني تحققه بلا مؤثر .
The first: The attachment of power if it depends on a preponderating factor leads to infinite regress, and if it does not depend, the door to proving the Maker is closed. It is refuted by denying both implications because it is possible that the preponderating factor is the attachment of the will in itself, and because the preponderance of the powerful agent of one of his two possible acts without a preponderating factor in the sense of specifying it without a motive is not the same as the preponderance of the possible without a preponderating factor, meaning its existence without an effect.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a classical kalam objection against the Ash'ari view that God acts by power and will. The objection states that if the attachment of divine power to a specific act requires a preponderating factor (marjiḥ), that factor itself would need another, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). If it does not require a factor, then the principle that every possible requires a preponderating factor (which is used to prove God's existence) collapses, closing the door to establishing the Maker (insidad bab ithbat al-sani'). The refutation (radd) denies both implications: first, the preponderating factor can be God's will itself, which attaches to one of two equal possibilities by its essence (li-dhatiha), similar to a hungry person choosing one of two identical loaves. Second, the powerful agent's specification of one possible act without an external motive is not equivalent to a contingent thing's existence without a cause (mu'aththir); the former is about choice, the latter about creation, so the objection conflates two different senses of 'without preponderating factor'.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
مرجح
تسلسل
إثبات الصانع
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the preponderance of an agent's choice without a motive is the same as a possible's existence without a cause.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that the agent's specification of one of two possible acts without an external motive is not equivalent to a contingent thing's existence without an effect; the former is about choice, the latter about causation.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, what can serve as the preponderating factor without leading to infinite regress?
A) The essence of the agent
B) The will itself
C) Another preponderating factor
D) The intrinsic disposition of the possible act

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the first objection shows that God's will serves as a self-sufficient preponderating factor and distinguishes an agent's choice from causal determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice between two equally good options, do you search for an external 'preponderating factor' to decide, or do you trust that God's will guides your heart even without a clear motive? In what areas of your life are you waiting for a sign instead of acting on faith?
?
If God's will is self-sufficient and does not depend on any external factor, why do you often delay obedience to clear commands until you feel a 'spiritual motive'? What does this reveal about your trust in the sufficiency of God's will?
?
The refutation distinguishes between an agent's free choice and a contingent thing's need for a cause. In your own life, are you living as a responsible agent who makes choices before God, or are you passively waiting for circumstances to cause you to act? Which mindset aligns more with the truth that God's will is the ultimate decider?

The Second Objection: Eternity vs. Origination of Divine Attachment
Presents the second objection on the eternity or temporality of the attachment of power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against divine power and will concerning the attachment's eternity or temporality, and how it is refuted by distinguishing eternal will to a temporal effect.

Core Idea
Eternal will can target a temporal creation without making the world eternal.

This lesson presents a classic kalam objection: if God's power and will are attached to creation, that attachment must be either eternal or originated. Each option seems problematic. The Ash'ari response clarifies that an eternal attachment can be directed to a future creation, avoiding both the eternity of the world and an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the key terms: تعلق القدرة, قديم, حادث, تسلسل الحوادث.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection presents a dichotomy: attachment is either eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). Understand why each leads to a problem.
Eternal attachment implies eternal effect; temporal attachment implies infinite regress.
3
Examine the refutation's two proposals
The refutation offers two ways out: (a) the attachment is eternal but to a future creation, or (b) the attachment's temporality is intrinsic. Focus on (a) as the main Ash'ari answer.
Proposal (b) is more subtle and often debated.
4
Ask: how does this defend Ash'ari theology?
Consider how this response preserves both God's eternal attributes and the world's temporality. This is a key move in the atomistic occasionalist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة إما قديم فيلزم قدم العالم، وإما حادث فتتسلسل الحوادث ورد بالمنع الجواز أن يتعلقا في الأزل بإيجاده في ما لا يزال، أو يكون حدوث تعلقهما لذاتها.
The second: That the attachment of power and will either is eternal, then the eternity of the world follows, or is temporally originated, then temporal events would regress infinitely. It is refuted by denying [this] with the possibility that they attach in pre-eternity to bringing it into existence in what will not cease (future), or that the temporality of their attachment is due to their own essence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This second objection argues that the attachment of divine power and will to the creation of the world must be either eternal (qadim) or temporally originated (hadith). If eternal, then the effect (the world) must be eternal, which is false. If temporally originated, then that attachment itself would be a new event (hadith) requiring another attachment, leading to infinite regress of attachments (tasalsul al-hawadith). The response refutes this by stating two possible alternatives: either (a) the attachment is eternal but relates to the world's existence in the future (fi ma la yazal), meaning God eternally willed the world to exist at a specific later time, or (b) the temporality of the attachment (i.e., its being a new state) is due to its own essence (li-dhatiha), not requiring another cause. Both options avoid the regress.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
تعلق الإرادة
قديم
حادث
تسلسل الحوادث
إيجاد في ما لا يزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's will is eternal, then its object must also be eternal.
Correction: God's eternal will can be directed to a temporal object (creation at a specific time) without making the object eternal.

Checkpoint
What does the response to the second objection propose to avoid the eternity of the world?
A) That the attachment is temporally originated without cause
B) That the attachment is eternal but relates to a future creation
C) That power and will are not real attributes
D) That the world is eternal after all

Key Takeaway
The attachment of divine power and will can be eternal in God yet relate to a temporal creation, preserving both divine eternity and world's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God willed the universe into existence at a specific moment. Does this truth settle your heart that His plan is timeless, even when your personal circumstances feel chaotic? How can you actively trust His eternal will today instead of demanding immediate answers?
?
If God's attachment to creation is eternal yet creation is temporal, do you live as if God's decree for your life was fixed before your birth? What would it look like to surrender to a decree that was set in eternity, especially in an area where you are impatient?
?
The objection assumes an eternal will forces an eternal world, but we know it does not. When you doubt God's timing in your life, do you unconsciously fall into this logical error? Name one specific disappointment where you felt God was 'too late' and correct that feeling with this teaching's truth.

Third Objection: Agent's Necessity and Free Choice
Presents the third objection on the compatibility of necessity and choice in the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection regarding the compatibility of necessity and free choice in the agent, and how the response distinguishes between necessity from a powerful agent and necessity from a mere necessitator.

Core Idea
Necessity from a powerful agent does not negate choice; it confirms it.

This lesson presents the third objection raised against the view that God acts by free choice. The objection claims that if an agent possesses all prerequisites, the act becomes necessary, eliminating freedom. The response offers a crucial distinction: necessity arising from the agent's own power and will is not compulsion but rather the realization of choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated first, then the refutation.
2
Identify the objection
The objection argues that when an agent has all necessary conditions, the act becomes necessary because non-occurrence is impossible. Understand why this seems to negate free will.
3
Understand the response
The response distinguishes between two types of necessity: from a powerful agent (al-qadir) and from a necessitator (al-mujib). Note the key phrase: 'الوجوب من القادر لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه'.
In the first type, the agent could have refrained if He willed; in the second, He cannot.
4
Apply the distinction
The objection confuses voluntary necessity with involuntary compulsion. Reflect on why this distinction is important for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الفاعل إن استجمع جميع ما لا بد منه وجب رده ) الامتناع التخلف في القادر، ورد بأن الوجوب من القادر لا ينافي الاختيار بل يحققه، بخلاف الوجوب من الموجب فإنه لا يصح فيه إنه إن شاء ترك .
The third: That if the agent collects all that is necessary, the act becomes necessary due to the impossibility of non-occurrence in the powerful agent. It is refuted by [saying] that necessity from the powerful agent does not negate choice but rather realizes it, unlike necessity from the necessitator, for in that case it is not valid that 'if He willed He would leave'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This objection argues that if a powerful agent (qadir) possesses all necessary conditions for an act (such as power, will, knowledge, etc.), the act becomes necessary and its non-occurrence impossible, which would negate free choice (ikhtiyar). The response distinguishes between two types of necessity: necessity from a powerful agent (wujub min al-qadir) and necessity from a mere necessitator (wujub min al-mujib). In the former, the agent could have refrained if He willed, and the necessity arises from His own choice, which actually confirms free will. In the latter, like natural causation, the agent cannot refrain, so it does not involve choice. Thus, the objection conflates two different kinds of necessity.

Key Terms
فاعل
واجب
مختار
موجب
تخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any necessity in the act of an agent negates free will.
Correction: Necessity from a powerful agent (voluntary necessity) actually confirms free choice, unlike compulsion from a necessitator.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how does necessity from a powerful agent relate to free choice?
A) It negates free choice
B) It is irrelevant to free choice
C) It confirms and realizes free choice
D) It applies only to natural agents

Key Takeaway
Necessity that arises from the agent's own power and will does not compromise free choice; rather, it fulfills it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own acts of worship, do you ever feel they are obligatory tasks you must complete to avoid punishment, or are they choices you make out of love? How does the distinction between necessity from a powerful agent and necessity from a necessitator change how you view your obedience? Will you commit to performing one act of worship today as a conscious choice, affirming your love, rather than a routine compulsion?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Do you feel 'compelled' to commit it, as if you have no choice? How does the lesson's teaching that true power and will confirm choice—not negate it—challenge that feeling? What concrete step can you take to reclaim your agency and choose obedience over compulsion?
?
The objection assumes that if God's act is necessary, then He is not free. Do you ever assume that because God will inevitably do something (e.g., judge justly), He is somehow forced? How does this passage reveal that God's necessity is a result of His perfect will, making Him the freest of all? Are you willing to trust that His decrees are not arbitrary but arise from His perfect wisdom and will, and surrender your own limited understanding to that?

The Fourth Objection: Power's Relation to Existence and Non-Existence
The fourth objection and its refutation regarding power's equal relation to existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the Ash'ari view of divine power and its refutation through the distinction between positive and privative power.

Core Idea
Power over non-existence means ability to refrain, not ability to create nothingness.

This lesson presents a subtle objection: if divine power relates equally to existence and non-existence, and non-existence is eternal and cannot be an object of power, then existence too cannot be an object of power. The response clarifies that 'power over non-existence' is simply the ability to abstain from creating, not the power to produce non-existence as a positive act. Understanding this distinction is key to the Ash'ari theory of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice the parallel drawn between existence and non-existence.
2
Understand the flaw pointed out
The objection says non-existence is pure negation (nafy mahd) and eternal, so not a valid object of power, then concludes existence is likewise invalid.
This would undermine God's creative power.
3
Examine the response
The refutation reinterprets what 'power over non-existence' means. It is not the ability to create non-existence but the ability to refrain from creating existence.
The response uses a conditional: if He wills, He does not do; if He does not will, He does not do.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Contrast positive power (to bring about existence) with privative power (to abstain). This saves the doctrine.
This distinction is central to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن نسبة القدرة إلى الوجود نسبتها إلى العدم، وهو لا يصلح مقدوراً لكونه أزلياً ونفياً محضاً فكذلك الوجود. ورد بأن معنى القدرة على العدم أنه إن شاء لم يفعل وإن لم يشأ لم يفعل لا إن شاء فعل العدم.
The fourth: That the relation of power to existence is the same as its relation to non-existence, but non-existence is not suitable as an object of power because it is eternal and pure negation, so likewise existence. It is refuted by [saying] that the meaning of power over non-existence is that if He wills He does not do, and if He does not will He does not do, not that if He wills He does non-existence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This objection argues that since power relates equally to existence and non-existence (i.e., God can bring about either), and non-existence is eternal and a pure negation (nafy mahd) and thus cannot be an object of power (maqdur), the same must apply to existence, so existence too cannot be willed. This would undermine God's ability to create. The response clarifies that 'power over non-existence' does not mean the ability to produce non-existence as a positive act (which is impossible), but rather the ability to refrain from causing existence: 'if He wills, He does not do; if He does not will, He does not do.' This is a different sense, so the analogy fails. Power over existence is positive, power over non-existence is privative.

Key Terms
نسبة القدرة
وجود
عدم
مقدور
نفي محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power over non-existence is the ability to create non-existence as a positive entity.
Correction: The response clarifies it means ability to refrain from creating existence.

Checkpoint
How does the response reinterpret 'power over non-existence'?
A) As the ability to create non-existence
B) As the ability to refrain from acting
C) As the inability to act
D) As the equal relation to both existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection is refuted by distinguishing between positive power (to bring about existence) and privative power (to refrain from acting), preserving God's ability to create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed that power must relate to existence and non-existence in the same way. Have you ever subconsciously judged God's actions by human standards, placing limits on His power? What step will you take to ensure your understanding of God's attributes remains grounded in revelation, not analogy?
?
The refutation shows that God's ability to refrain is an act of wisdom and will, not weakness. Think of a specific situation where you struggled with God's seeming inaction. How does this lesson reshape your trust in His wisdom when He chooses not to act?
?
If you truly internalize that God's power over non-existence is His freedom to withhold existence, how should that affect your patience and submission to His decree? Commit to one practical change today based on this understanding.

The Fifth Objection: Preference, Futility, and the Refutation
Objection and refutation on divine preference and futility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine free choice leads to either God's need for completion or futility, and how the Ash'ari response resolves it.

Core Idea
An act can be better for others without God needing anything.

In this lesson, we encounter a classic objection against the Ash'ari view that God acts by free choice (ikhtiyar). The objection presents a dilemma: if an act is more appropriate for God than refraining, it implies God needs that act for completion; if not, creation is futile. The refutation cleverly shifts the basis of appropriateness away from God's own perfection. By exploring this exchange, you will see how careful distinctions in kalam defuse apparent theological dilemmas.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two conditional statements that form the dilemma.
The objection assumes that 'more appropriate' must relate to the agent's own perfection.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if the act is more appropriate for the agent, it implies completion by another (استكمال بالغير). The second: if not, the act is futile (عبث). Understand each implication.
3
Analyze the refutation
The response rejects the dilemma by stating that 'more appropriate' can be in itself (في نفسه) or in relation to another (بالنسبة إلى الغير). The latter suffices to negate futility without implying God's need.
The refutation does not deny that God's acts are appropriate; it denies that this appropriateness must be for God's own completion.
4
Consider the underlying principle
Reflect on how this response upholds God's absolute self-sufficiency while affirming the wisdom and purpose of creation. The act's benefit for creatures is sufficient reason.
This is a foundational move in defending divine free will and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المختار إن كان الفعل أولى به من الترك يلزم الاستكمال بالغير وإلا فالعبث ورد بأنه يكفي في نفي العبث كونه أولى في نفسه أو بالنسبة إلى الغير.
The fifth: That if the act is more appropriate for the free agent than refraining, it entails completion by another; otherwise [if not more appropriate] it is futile. It is refuted by [saying] that it suffices for negating futility that it is more appropriate in itself or in relation to another.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This objection targets the Ash'ari view that God acts by free choice (ikhtiyar). It states: if performing the act is more appropriate (awla) for the agent than refraining, then the agent needs that act to complement Himself, implying imperfection. If the act is not more appropriate, then creation is futile (abath), which is unworthy of God. The response counters that the act may be more appropriate in itself (fi nafsihi) or in relation to another (bil-nisba ila al-ghayr), such as benefiting creatures. This suffices to negate futility without implying that God is completed by something external. Thus, the objection wrongly assumes that preference for an act must be for the agent's own perfection.

Key Terms
مختار
أولى
استكمال بالغير
عبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act is more appropriate for God to do, then God must need that act to complete Himself or else the act is pointless.
Correction: The act can be more appropriate because it brings benefit to creatures, not because God needs it. This still gives the act purpose without implying any deficiency in God.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how can an act be 'more appropriate' without implying God's need for completion?
A) By being necessary for God's essence
B) By being appropriate in itself or for others
C) By being obligatory
D) By being arbitrary

Key Takeaway
God's free choice is defended by distinguishing that an act's appropriateness may be for the benefit of others, not for God's own completion, thus avoiding both futility and need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something good for someone else, do you ever expect something in return, or do you do it solely for their benefit? How does God's example of acting purely for others challenge your motives?
?
If you truly believed that God's actions are never futile, how would that change the way you interpret difficult events in your life? Can you name one specific event you need to reframe?
?
Have you ever felt that God needs your worship or obedience? How does the lesson's distinction that God's acts are for the benefit of others, not for His own completion, reshape your understanding of your relationship with Him?

Sixth Objection on Temporal Priority and the Effect of a Free Agent
Presents the sixth objection on the possibility of the effect's occurrence in pre-eternity from a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against the temporal creation of the world by a free agent and its refutation based on the distinction between possibility in itself and impossibility due to being an effect.

Core Idea
Something can be possible in itself yet impossible due to the absence of the agent's will.

This lesson examines the sixth objection concerning how the world could be created in time if it was possible in pre-eternity. The objector presents a dilemma: either the effect was impossible in pre-eternity, implying an alteration in God's attributes, or it was possible, leading to an eternal effect. The refutation offers a nuanced distinction that avoids both horns.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the objection carefully. Notice the two horns: if the effect is impossible in pre-eternity, then alteration (inqilab) follows; if it is possible, then an eternal thing depends on a free agent.
The objector assumes that possibility in itself suffices for actual existence in pre-eternity.
2
Grasp the refutation's distinction
The response distinguishes between the effect's intrinsic possibility (mumkin li-dhatihi) and its impossibility due to being the effect of a free agent (athar al-mukhtar). This means the effect is possible in itself but cannot exist until the agent wills it.
This avoids both alteration and an eternal effect.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this preserves divine freedom and avoids logical problems. The world's non-existence in pre-eternity is not due to any intrinsic impossibility but because the agent has not yet chosen to create it.
This is a key argument for the Ash'ari view of temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن أثر المختار إن امتنع في الأزل لزم الانقلاب وإن أمكن لزم جواز استناد الأزلي إلى المختار ورد بأنه في الأزل ممكن لذاته ممتنع لكونه أثر المختار.
The sixth: That if the effect of the free agent is impossible in pre-eternity, then alteration (inqilab) follows; and if it is possible, then the possibility of an eternal thing depending on the free agent follows. It is refuted by [saying] that it is possible in itself in pre-eternity but impossible due to its being the effect of the free agent.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This sixth objection argues about the temporal status of the world as the effect (athar) of a free agent. If the world's existence was impossible in pre-eternity (al-azal), then it would have changed from impossibility to possibility, which is an alteration (inqilab) in the divine attribute? The second horn: if it was possible, then an eternal thing (the world) could depend on the free agent, which seems problematic. The response distinguishes two aspects: the world is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi) even in pre-eternity, but its existence at that time is impossible because it is the effect of a free agent (athar al-mukhtar), meaning the agent has not yet willed it. So there is no change in the essence of the world; rather, the impossibility is due to the absence of the agent's will. This avoids both alteration and the need for an eternal effect.

Key Terms
أثر المختار
الأزل
انقلاب
ممكن لذاته
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible in itself, it must always be possible to exist at any time, regardless of external conditions.
Correction: The refutation shows that possibility in itself is necessary but not sufficient for actual existence; additional conditions, such as the free agent's will, can prevent actuality without altering intrinsic possibility.

Checkpoint
Why is the world impossible in pre-eternity according to the response?
A) Because it is impossible in itself
B) Because it is the effect of a free agent who has not yet willed it
C) Because pre-eternity excludes temporality
D) Because God cannot create eternal things

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection is resolved by distinguishing between intrinsic possibility and actual existence contingent upon the free agent's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a significant decision you are postponing. Are you treating it as 'impossible' when really it is only 'possible in itself' but pending your will? What would it take for you to write 'will it' today?
?
If Allah's will is the sole condition that brings possibility into actuality, how does that reshape your sense of urgency in turning to Him for what seems impossible in your life?
?
When you look at your own habits, where have you assumed a change in your character is impossible when in fact you have simply not willed it? How can the distinction in this passage help you break that assumption?
