Seventh Objection: Knowledge and Possibility
Presents the seventh objection on knowledge determining possibility and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection that divine knowledge determines the necessity or impossibility of an act, and the Ash'ari refutation that knowledge follows divine power.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge does not necessitate; it follows divine power.

This lesson examines a classical objection against Ash'ari theology: if God knows eternally that an act will occur, doesn't that make its existence necessary? The objection claims this negates divine power over the act. The brief yet profound refutation clarifies the relationship between knowledge and power in Ash'ari thought.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: it argues that God's eternal knowledge fixes the outcome, making the act either necessary or impossible, and therefore not an object of power.
Notice the logical structure: knowledge of existence → necessity; knowledge of non-existence → impossibility.
2
Analyze the refutation
The refutation states that He knows its existence 'by His power.' Understand how this shifts the relationship: knowledge does not independently determine; it is contingent on the free exercise of power.
This is a key Ash'ari principle: knowledge follows the known, not vice versa.
3
Grasp the theological implication
Consider why this response is important: it preserves divine freedom and the genuine possibility (maqdur) of actions, avoiding fatalism.
The objection would imply determinism; the refutation reaffirms voluntary agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أنه يعلم في الأزل وجود الأثر فيجب أو عدمه فيمتنع فلا يكون مقدوراً ، ورد بأنه يعلم وجوده بقدرته).
The seventh: That He knows in pre-eternity the existence of the effect, so it is necessary; or its non-existence, so it is impossible; thus it is not an object of power. It is refuted by [saying] that He knows its existence by His power.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This seventh objection argues that God's eternal knowledge (ilm) determines the outcome: if He knows that an effect will exist, then its existence is necessary (yajib); if He knows it will not exist, its existence is impossible (yamtanu). In either case, the thing is not a genuine object of power (maqdur) because its existence is fixed by knowledge. The response is brief: 'He knows its existence by His power' (ya'lamu wujudahu bi-qudratihi). This means that God's knowledge does not precede His power independently; rather, He knows that He will bring it into existence through His power. Thus, the possibility remains because the knowledge is dependent on the free volition. The objection assumes knowledge as a separate determining factor, but in Ash'ari theology, knowledge follows the object known (al-'ilm tabi' li-l-ma'lum).

Key Terms
يعلم في الأزل
يجب
يمتنع
مقدور
بقدرته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge creates necessity independently of divine will and power.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, knowledge is dependent on the object known and on the exercise of divine power; it does not necessitate outcomes by itself.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the claim that divine knowledge makes the effect necessary or impossible?
A) By saying knowledge does not exist in pre-eternity
B) By saying He knows its existence by His power
C) By saying the effect is always possible
D) By saying knowledge is not about future contingents

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge does not determine outcomes independently; it follows the divine will and power, preserving genuine contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own future, do you ever feel resigned because you assume God's knowledge already fixed everything, leaving no room for your efforts or prayers? How does the truth that God's knowledge is coupled with His power—that He 'knows by His power'—change the way you approach your responsibilities and your reliance on Him right now?
?
Think of a situation where you felt trapped, believing that God's knowledge of your hardship meant it was inevitable and unchangeable. How does the refutation in this lesson—that God knows the effect by His power, not independently—give you concrete hope and motivate you to turn to His power in dua instead of despair?
?
Are there areas of your life where you secretly doubt the efficacy of your worship or supplication, thinking that 'whatever will be will be' and that your actions don't matter? What would it look like for you to truly live as if God's knowledge of your outcome is inseparable from His power to respond, and how would that transform your daily devotion?

The First Objection on Power, Choice, and Preponderance
Presents the objection and two-fold response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed version of the objection that if God acts by power and choice, it either leads to infinite regress or undercuts the proof for God's existence, and how the response denies both implications by distinguishing different meanings of 'without a preponderating factor'.

Core Idea
The objection conflates two senses of 'without a preponderating factor'—the response untangles them.

Sanusi now presents a more thorough version of an objection already encountered. The philosopher argues that divine power acting by choice faces a dilemma: either the specification of one of two equal acts needs a further factor (leading to regress), or it doesn't (undermining the causal principle used to prove God). The answer carefully denies both horns, distinguishing between a cause for existence and a motive for choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two horns of the dilemma presented in the opening sentences.
The objection uses the phrase 'افتقر إلى مرجح' (depends on a preponderating factor).
2
Identify the two horns
Identify the first horn: if it depends on a preponderating factor, leads to infinite regress. Identify the second horn: if it does not, it closes the door to proving the Maker.
The principle at stake is 'امتناع الترجح بلا مرجح' (impossibility of preponderance without a preponderating factor).
3
Examine the first denial
Read how the response denies the first implication by proposing the will (al-irada) as the preponderating factor that specifies by its essence. Note the human examples (hungry person, fleeing person).
This is presented as more appropriate than the view in al-Mawaqif.
4
Examine the second denial
Read how the response denies the second implication by distinguishing between a possible thing existing without a cause and an agent specifying one act without an external motive.
The key is that the two cases are different—'لا يلزم من جواز هذا جواز ذلك'.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the proof of God and the understanding of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو كان الباري تعالى فاعلاً بالقدرة والاختيار دون الإيجاب، فتعلق قدرته بأحد مقدوريه بالمتساويين، بالنظر إلى نفس القدرة دون الآخرين، افتقر إلى مرجح بنقل الكلام إلى تأثيره في ذلك المرجح ، ولزم التسلسل في المرجحات، وإن لم يفتقر لزم انسداد باب ثبات (۱) الصانع، لأن مبناه على امتناع الترجح بلا مرجح، وافتقار وقوع الممكن إلى مؤثر. والجواب : منع الملازمتين. أي لا نسلم أنه لو افتقر تعلق القدرة إلى مرجح لزم التسلسل الجواز أن يكون الترجيح هو الإرادة (۲) التي تتعلق بأحد المتساويين لذاتها، كما في اختيار الجائع أحد الرغيفين، والهارب أحد الطريقين، ولا يخفى أن هذا أولى مما قال في المواقف اقتداء بالإمام أن القدرة تتعلق لذاتها . ولا نسلّم أنه لو لم يفتقر إلى مرجح لزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع ، فإن المفضي إلى ذلك جواز ترجيح الممكن بلا ،مرجح بمعنى تحققه بلا مؤثر ، لا ترجيح القادر أحد ، مقدوريه بلا ،مرجح بمعنی تخصیصه بالإيقاع من غير داعية .، ولا يلزم من جواز هذا جواز ذلك.
The first: If the Most High God is an agent by power and choice without necessitation, then the attachment of His power to one of His two equally possible objects, considering the power itself without others, either depends on a preponderating factor, transferring the discussion to its effect on that factor, leading to infinite regress of preponderating factors; or it does not depend, then the door to establishing the Maker is closed because it is based on the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderating factor and the need of a possible thing for an effect. The answer: Denial of both implications. That is, we do not grant that if the attachment of power depends on a preponderating factor it leads to regress, because it is possible that the preponderance is the will that attaches to one of two equals by its essence, as in the choice of the hungry person for one of two loaves, and the fleeing person for one of two paths. It is obvious that this is more appropriate than what was said in al-Mawaqif following the Imam that power attaches by its essence. And we do not grant that if it does not depend on a preponderating factor it closes the door to proving the Maker, for what leads to that is the permissibility of preponderance of a possible thing without a preponderating factor in the sense of its existence without an effect, not the preponderance of the powerful agent of one of his possible acts without a preponderating factor in the sense of specifying it for performance without a motive. And the permissibility of this does not entail the permissibility of that.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a more elaborate version of the first objection and response already seen on page 96. It presents the same dilemma: if the attachment of divine power to one of two equal possible acts requires a preponderating factor (marjiḥ), then that factor itself needs a factor ad infinitum; if not, the principle that every possible needs a preponderating factor (used to prove God) is violated. The answer (jawab) denies both implications. First, the preponderating factor can be the will itself (al-irada), which specifies one of two equals by its essence (li-dhatiha), as in human examples. This is considered superior (awla) to the view in al-Mawaqif that power itself specifies. Second, the closure of the proof would only follow if one allows a possible thing to exist without any cause (mu'aththir), which is different from allowing a powerful agent to specify one of his acts without an external motive (da'iya). The two cases are distinct. Thus, the objection conflates two different meanings of 'without preponderating factor'.

Key Terms
الباري تعالى
فاعل بالقدرة والاختيار
إيجاب
متساويان
مرجح
تسلسل المرجحات
إثبات الصانع
الإرادة
داعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an agent chooses one of two equal options without an external motive, it contradicts the principle that every possible needs a cause.
Correction: The response clarifies that the principle applies to existence of contingents, not to specification of actions by an agent. The agent's will itself provides the specification without needing an external preponderator.

Checkpoint
What example does the response give to illustrate the will's specification without external motive?
A) A hungry person choosing one of two loaves
B) A person choosing between two equally good actions
C) God's power attaching by essence
D) The impossibility of preponderance without a factor

Key Takeaway
The response defends divine power and choice by showing that the will can specify without infinite regress and that agent specification does not violate the causal principle used to prove God.

Second Objection: The Dilemma of Eternal vs. Temporal Attachment
Second objection: dilemma on eternal vs. temporal attachment of divine power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection from the second set, which presents a dilemma about whether God's power and will attach to creation eternally or temporally, and the consequent infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's eternal will cannot attach to a temporal creation without dilemma.

In this lesson, we encounter the second objection from the second set of arguments against the temporal creation of the world. The objection presents a stark dilemma: either God's power and will are eternally attached to creating the world, or they become attached at some moment in time. Each option leads to a difficulty. Let us examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: it presents two possibilities and their consequences.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The first horn: if the attachment is eternal (azali), then the world must be eternal because the effect cannot lag behind a complete cause. The second horn: if the attachment is temporally originated (hadith), then that attachment itself is an event requiring another attachment, leading to infinite regress.
This parallels the second objection from the first set.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'complete cause' (tamm al-illa)
Understand that in kalam, a complete cause necessitates its effect immediately. This is key to the first horn's argument.
If the cause is complete, any delay would be arbitrary.
4
Recognize the incompleteness of this lesson
Note that the response to this objection is not provided here; it is cut off. This lesson only presents the objection itself.
The full objection-answer pair is not available in this window.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة بإيجاد العالم إن كان أزلياً لزم كون العالم أزلياً لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن كان حادثاً ينقل الكلام إلى تعلقهما بأحداث ذلك التعلق ، وتتسلسل التعلقات الحادثة
The second: That the attachment of power and will to bringing the world into existence: if it is eternal, then the world must be eternal due to the impossibility of lagging behind the complete cause; and if it is temporally originated, then the discussion transfers to their attachment to the occurrences of that attachment, and there would be infinite regress of temporally originated attachments.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage begins the second objection from the second set, which closely parallels the second objection from the first set. It presents a dilemma: if God's power and will attached to creating the world eternally (azaliyyan), then the world would be eternal because the effect cannot lag behind the complete cause (tamm al-illa). If the attachment is temporally originated (hadith), then that attachment itself is a new event requiring another attachment, leading to infinite regress of attachments (tasalsul al-ta'alliqat al-haditha). Unfortunately, the response (radd) is not included in this window as the text ends. This incomplete lesson is flagged for review.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's will is eternal, He can still create a temporal world without any logical problem.
Correction: Kalam argues that if the will's attachment to creation is eternal, the creation itself must be eternal (co-eternal with God) because the effect cannot be delayed from a complete cause. Thus, an eternal will with a temporal creation is inconsistent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second objection presents a dilemma: if God's will is eternally attached to creation, the world must be eternal; if temporarily attached, infinite regress ensues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The dilemma shows that reason alone hits a wall when contemplating God's relationship to creation. When you face such logical tensions, do you flee to blind faith or dismiss reason? Or do you sit in the tension and seek deeper understanding? Which path do you actually take?
?
This lesson cuts off before the response—you are left with an objection, not a resolution. Are you comfortable with incomplete knowledge about the divine? What does your discomfort (or comfort) reveal about your trust in God's wisdom versus your need to have all answers?

Divine Foreknowledge and Power
Seventh objection and its response on divine knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection against divine omnipotence based on foreknowledge and the response that underscores the compatibility of God's knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge does not eliminate contingency; it knows through power.

This lesson tackles a classical theological dilemma: if God knows everything in eternity, do His creatures have any real power? The objection claims that foreknowledge necessitates outcomes, making them impossible to be objects of power. The response, however, clarifies that God's knowledge of events is precisely through His power to bring them about.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the objection
Read the first part of the passage describing the objection. Identify the logical premises: God's eternal knowledge of existence or non-existence leads to necessity or impossibility. Conclude that the effect is not within power.
Notice how the objection builds on the assumption that God's knowledge is independent and causal.
2
Understand the response
Read the response: 'رد بأنه يعلم وجوده بقدرته' (it was replied that He knows its existence by His power). Consider how this reframes the relationship: knowledge is tied to the exercise of power, not prior determination.
The response preserves contingency by making the effect dependent on power, not on a brute fact of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع: أنه يعلم في الأزل وجود الأثر فيجب أو عدمه فيمتنع فلا يكون مقدوراً، ورد بأنه يعلم وجوده بقدرته.
The seventh: that He knows in eternity the existence of the effect, so it is necessary, or its non-existence, so it is impossible; thus it is not within power. And it was replied that He knows its existence by His power.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents a brief objection and its response. The opponent argues that if God knows in eternity that an effect will exist, then existence is necessary for it; if He knows it will not exist, non-existence is necessary; therefore, the effect is not within divine power (since power requires contingency). The response counters that God's knowledge of existence is through His power, meaning the effect is known as existing because He will create it by His power, not because it is pre-determined independently.

Key Terms
الأزل
الأثر
مقدور
قدرته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal foreknowledge removes contingency from actions, making them determined.
Correction: God knows contingent events as dependent on His power, so they remain contingent (possible to be or not be) until He creates them.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how does God know the existence of an effect?
A) Through the necessity of the effect itself.
B) Through His power.
C) Through the impossibility of non-existence.
D) Through the effect's own essence.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge encompasses events as determined by His power, preserving contingency and the scope of divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life—including your own choices—is known by God specifically as coming from His power? How does that change the way you view your successes and failures?
?
If God's knowledge is not passive but tied to His will, then nothing in your life is accidental. Are you at peace with that, or do you secretly wish some things were outside His control?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you remember that God knows it as an object of His power? What would it mean for you to live today with that conviction?

Second Argument: The Dilemma of Eternal Attachment and Infinite Regress
Second objection: dilemma of eternal or temporal attachment, with Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari position on the world's temporality, and the Ash'ari response denying both horns of the dilemma.

Core Idea
Eternal attachment does not necessitate an eternal world; temporal attachment need not lead to infinite regress.

This passage presents a second objection against the Ash'ari view that the world is temporal. The opponent argues that whether the attachment (ta'alluq) of divine power and will to the world's creation is eternal or temporal, problems arise: either the world must be eternal, or an infinite regress of attachments occurs. The reply shows both inferences are invalid. Let's examine the logic and the counter-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dilemma
Find the two possibilities presented: eternal attachment and temporal attachment. Note the consequence claimed for each.
The opponent is trying to force a contradiction.
2
Understand the First Horn
If the attachment is eternal, then the world must be eternal because the complete cause cannot be delayed. Understand why this seems problematic.
This relies on the principle that a complete cause necessitates its effect without delay.
3
Understand the Second Horn
If the attachment is temporal (created), then that attachment itself would require another attachment to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress of temporal attachments.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered impossible in contingent matters.
4
Examine the Answer
Read the two-part rebuttal: first, the first implication is denied because eternal attachment can be for a future event; second, the second implication is denied because the temporality of the attachment can be due to the essences themselves, not requiring another attachment.
The final point about attachments being mental considerations is key to ending the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة بإيجاد العالم إن كان أزلياً لزم كون العالم أزلياً لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن كان حادثاً ينقل الكلام إلى تعلقهما بأحداث ذلك التعلق، وتتسلسل التعلقات الحادثة. والجواب: منع الملازمتين. أما الأولى: فلجواز أن تتعلق القدرة والإرادة في الأزل بإيجاد العالم فيما لا يزال. وأما الثانية: فلجواز أن يكون حدوث تعلق القدرة والإرادة لذاتهما من غير افتقار إلى حدوث تعلق آخر. على أن التعلقات اعتبارات عقلية ينقطع التسلسل فيها بانقطاع الاعتبار.
The second: That the attachment of power and will to the creation of the world – if it were eternal, it would necessitate the world's being eternal, because of the impossibility of delay from the complete cause; and if it were temporal, it would transfer the discussion to their attachment to the occurrences of that attachment, and there would be an infinite regress of temporal attachments. The answer: Denial of the two implications. As for the first: because it is possible that the power and will attach in eternity to the creation of the world in what will not cease. As for the second: because it is possible that the temporality of the attachment of power and will is by their very essences, without needing another temporal attachment. Moreover, the attachments are mental considerations; infinite regress ends with the cessation of consideration.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The opponent argues from a dilemma: if the attachment (تعلق) of divine power and will to creating the world is eternal, then the world must be eternal (since the complete cause cannot be delayed). If the attachment is temporal (created), then that attachment itself would require another attachment to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress of temporal attachments. The answer denies both implications. For the first, it is possible that the power and will are eternally attached to the creation of the world 'in what will not cease' (فيما لا يزال), i.e., the world is created at a time but the attachment was eternal with respect to that future event. For the second, it is possible that the temporal occurrence of the attachment is due to the very essences (لذاتهما) of power and will, not requiring a further attachment. Moreover, attachments are mental considerations (اعتبارات عقلية) – they exist in the mind, not externally – so the infinite regress stops when one stops considering them.

Key Terms
تعلق
أزلي
حادث
تسلسل
اعتبارات عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's power and will are eternally attached to creating the world, then the world must be eternal.
Correction: The Ash'ari response argues that eternal attachment can be for a future temporal event; the effect need not be concurrent with the attachment.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what stops the infinite regress of temporal attachments?
A) The impossibility of the world being eternal.
B) The attachments are mental considerations.
C) The power and will are self-subsistent.
D) The world is created in time.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because eternal attachment can be directed to a future event, and temporal attachment can be due to the essence without infinite regress; moreover, attachments are mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded from God immediate fulfillment of His promises, treating His eternal will as if it must produce an instant effect? How can the truth that God's eternal attachment can be directed to a future event change your patience and trust in His timing?
?
The opponent's dilemma assumes God's power must conform to human logic of cause and effect. In which area of your life are you most tempted to judge God's actions by your own standards of necessity or timing? What would it mean to truly submit to the fact that His attachments are not bound by your reasoning?
?
If the attachments of divine power and will are mere mental considerations, does that make them less real or more profound? How does this teaching affect your sense of accountability before a God whose will is not constrained by external chains of causality, yet remains intimately tied to your existence?

The Complete Cause and Divine Choice
Third objection: if all conditions are met, the effect is necessary; otherwise impossible; thus no choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection of necessitation preserves divine choice through the concept of 'necessity by choice'.

Core Idea
Necessity by choice is the key to reconciling divine power and freedom.

This passage presents a powerful objection: if God has all conditions for an act, He cannot refrain, so He is not free. The answer reframes the issue by introducing the subtle distinction between necessity by choice and pure necessitation. Prepare to see how Ash'ari theologians defended divine freedom without compromising causal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'فلا فرق بين الموجب والمختار') and its translation. Pay attention to the two conditional scenarios.
The objection uses a logical 'if...then' structure.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection argues: if all conditions are met, the effect is necessary; if not, it is impossible. Therefore, there is no difference between a necessitating cause and a free chooser.
The conclusion is that God would be a necessitating cause, not a free agent.
3
Study the answer
Read the answer starting from 'والجواب'. Notice it concedes the necessity but adds the crucial qualifier 'by choice'.
The answer does not deny necessity; it redefines it.
4
Grasp the concept of الوجوب بالاختيار
This term is central. It means the necessity arises from the agent's own choice, so the agent could have done otherwise if He willed.
This preserves freedom because the alternative is possible in principle.
5
Apply the distinction
Contrast a necessary cause (موجب) which has no alternative, with a free agent (مختار) whose act is necessary by choice.
Try to explain the difference in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن الواجب إذا استجمع جميع ما لا بد منه في صدور الأثر عنه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً، وجب صدور الأثر عنه، بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك لامتناع عدم الأثر عند تمام المؤثر، فلا يكون مختاراً بل موجباً، وإن لم يستجمع جميع ما لابدّ منه امتنع صدور الأثر ضرورة امتناع وجود الأثر بدون المؤثر، وحاصل هذا يؤول إلى أنه لا فرق بين الموجب والمختار. والجواب: أنه لو سلم امتناع عدم الأثر عند تمام المؤثر المختار. فلا نسلم أن هذا يستلزم كون الفاعل موجباً لا مختاراً، فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف له لأنه بحيث لو شاء لترك بخلاف الموجب فظهر الفرق.
The third: That the Necessary, if it gathers all that is necessary for the effect to emanate from it, whether existent or nonexistent, the effect would necessarily emanate from it, so that it cannot desist, due to the impossibility of the effect's non-existence when the complete cause is realized; thus it is not choice but necessitating. And if it did not gather all that is necessary, the effect's emanation would be impossible, due to the impossibility of the existence of the effect without the cause. The conclusion of this is that there is no difference between the necessitating and the choice. The answer: Even if it is granted that non-existence of the effect is impossible when the complete cause of the choice agent is realized, we do not grant that this necessitates the agent being necessitating not choice. For the necessity by choice (الوجوب بالاختيار) establishes choice, not opposing it, because it is such that if it willed it could leave (the act), contrary to the necessitating. Thus the difference appears.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The opponent argues: if the Necessary Being (الواجب) i.e., God, possesses all conditions necessary for the effect (both existential and non-existential), then the effect must necessarily occur; He cannot refrain because non-existence would be impossible with the complete cause. Thus He is not a free chooser (مختار) but a necessitating cause (موجب). If He lacks any condition, the effect cannot occur. Hence, there is no difference between a necessary agent and a free agent. The answer concedes that non-existence of the effect is impossible when the complete cause of a free agent is realized, but denies that this negates choice. The key distinction is 'necessity by choice' (الوجوب بالاختيار). This means the necessity arises from the agent's own choice; it is compatible with freedom because the agent could have refrained if He had willed otherwise. A necessary agent (موجب) has no such alternative. So the difference is clear.

Key Terms
الواجب
موجب
مختار
الوجوب بالاختيار
تمام المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's action is necessary, He cannot be free.
Correction: Ash'aris teach that necessity by choice (الوجوب بالاختيار) is compatible with freedom because the agent could have refrained if He willed.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the free chooser from the necessitating cause according to the answer?
A) The free chooser can also be necessitated.
B) The free chooser's act is necessary by choice, and he could have left it.
C) The necessitating cause can refrain.
D) There is no real distinction.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari concept of 'necessity by choice' reconciles divine causal power with genuine freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God’s actions are necessary by His own choice, does that make you feel that your fate is predetermined in a way that eliminates your responsibility? Reflect: if God could have willed otherwise but chose to create you, what does that oblige you to do with your life?
?
The objection argues that if a cause is complete, the effect must follow, and thus there is no choice. Where in your life have you used the excuse 'I had no choice' to avoid accountability? After this lesson, can you truly say that? If God’s necessity by choice preserves His freedom, what does that imply about your own excuses?
?
Read the phrase 'إذا شاء لترك' (if He willed, He could leave). God could have refrained from creating you, guiding you, or forgiving you. How does this truth shake your complacency? What are you doing today that requires His choice to continue? Are you living as if His choice is certain, or are you taking it for granted?

The Fourth Objection: Power over Non-Existence and Its Answer
Fourth objection and Ash'ari answer on power over non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response clarifies that power over non-existence means the ability to refrain from creating, not to create non-existence as a positive entity.

Core Idea
Power over non-existence is the ability to not create, not to create a non-existent thing.

This lesson examines a key objection against the Ash'ari position on divine power: if God has power to create, does He also have power to annihilate? The objection seems logical, but the Ash'ari answer distinguishes between affecting non-existence and refraining from action. Let's follow the reasoning through the source passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage silently
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Notice the structure: an objection (الرابع) followed by a response (والجواب).
2
Identify the opponent's premise
The opponent argues that since power relates equally to opposites, power over existence implies power over non-existence. Find this premise in the text.
They say: 'نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء'.
3
Trace the refutation
How does the answer avoid the conclusion that non-existence itself is an effect? Look for the key phrase 'معنى كون العدم مقدوراً' (the meaning of non-existence being within power).
The answer redefines what 'power over non-existence' means.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Reflect on the difference between making something happen and allowing something not to happen. Why does this matter for divine attributes?
This preserves that God's power is not passive but active will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن الفاعل لو كان قادراً على وجود الشيء، لكان قادراً على عدمه، لأن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء، لكن اللازم باطل، لأن العدم الأصلي أزلي ولا شيء من الأزلي بأثر للقادر، وأيضاً العدم نفي محض لا يصلح متعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة. لأن معناه التأثر، وحيث لا تأثير فلا أثر. والجواب: أن معنى كون العدم مقدوراً: أن الفاعل إن شاء لم يفعل، أي إن شاء أن لا يوجد الشيء لم يوجده، أو إن لم يشأ لم يفعل. أي إن لم يشأ أن يوجده لم يوجده.
The fourth: That if the agent were able over the existence of a thing, he would be able over its non-existence, because the relation of power to both sides is equal. But the consequent is false, because the original non-existence is eternal, and nothing eternal is an effect of the powerful. Also, non-existence is pure negation, not suitable as an object of power and will, because their meaning is being affected, and where there is no affecting, there is no effect. The answer: The meaning of non-existence being within power is that the agent, if He wills, does not act, i.e., if He wills that the thing not exist, He does not bring it into existence; or if He does not will, He does not act, i.e., if He does not will to bring it into existence, He does not bring it into existence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its answer. The opponent argues that if God has power over the existence of something, He must also have power over its non-existence, since power relates equally to opposites. However, the consequent is false because original non-existence (العدم الأصلي) is eternal, and nothing eternal can be an effect of an agent. Moreover, non-existence is a pure negation and cannot be an object of power and will, which imply affecting something. The answer clarifies the meaning of 'non-existence being within power': it means the agent, if He wills, does not act (i.e., refrains from creating). So 'having power over non-existence' does not mean producing non-existence as an effect, but rather the ability to not produce existence. This preserves the asymmetry: power over non-existence is simply the capacity to refrain, not to create a negative entity.

Key Terms
العدم الأصلي
قدرة
أزلي
أثر
نفي محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence is an entity that can be created by God's power.
Correction: Non-existence is a pure negation; 'power over non-existence' means the ability to refrain from creating, not to create non-existence as an effect.

Checkpoint
How does the answer interpret 'non-existence being within power'?
A) As the ability to bring non-existence into existence.
B) As the agent's ability to refrain from creating if He wills.
C) As a separate power over both existence and non-existence equally.
D) As the impossibility of non-existence being an effect.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence being within power means the agent can choose not to bring about existence, not that non-existence itself is an effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is entirely dependent on God's will to create and sustain it, do you live with a constant awareness that at any moment He could simply withhold existence? How would that change your priorities today?
?
The opponent assumed God's power must be symmetrical—if He can create, He must be able to annihilate. But the Ash'ari answer shows that power over non-existence is just refraining from creating. In your own life, where have you assumed that your abilities must be symmetrical (e.g., if you can build something, you must be able to destroy it)? How does that assumption lead to pride or overconfidence?
?
Non-existence is pure negation—it has no reality. Yet humans often fear non-existence (death) as if it were a powerful entity. How does this lesson's teaching that non-existence is not an object of power reshape your fear of death or your sense of security in God's hand?

Refutation of the Third Philosophers' Argument: God's Choice Not Compromised
Taftazani refutes the claim that a complete agent necessitates its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the claim that a complete agent necessitates its effect, and why 'necessitation by choice' is consistent with divine freedom.

Core Idea
Necessitation arising from choice affirms freedom, not negates it.

The philosophers argue that if God has all prerequisites for action, then action must necessarily occur, implying God is not truly free. Taftazani's answer introduces a subtle but crucial distinction: the necessity that arises from free will is itself an expression of choice. This passage unpacks that argument and its refutation, clarifying the nature of divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow: first the philosophers' objection, then Taftazani's answer.
Notice the key term 'تمام المؤثر' (complete cause).
2
Identify the two claims
Distinguish the philosophers' claim (if complete cause, effect must occur; otherwise impossible → no difference between necessitating and free) from Taftazani's response (grants impossibility of non‑occurrence but denies it negates choice).
Look for the phrase 'فلا نسلم' — Taftazani refuses the conclusion.
3
Understand 'necessitation by choice'
Taftazani's key move: the free agent could have refrained if he willed; this 'if he willed' clause preserves freedom even when the act is necessarily done given that will. Contrast with a purely necessitating agent who could not do otherwise under any condition.
The difference lies in the source of necessity: from will vs. from nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن الواجب إذا استجمع جميع ما لا بد منه في صدور الأثر عنه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً، وجب صدور الأثر عنه، بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك لامتناع عدم الأثر عند تمام المؤثر، فلا يكون مختاراً بل موجباً، وإن لم يستجمع جميع ما لابدّ منه امتنع صدور الأثر ضرورة امتناع وجود الأثر بدون المؤثر، وحاصل هذا يؤول إلى أنه لا فرق بين الموجب والمختار . والجواب : أنه لو سلم امتناع عدم الأثر عند تمام المؤثر المختار. فلا نسلم أن هذا يستلزم كون الفاعل موجباً لا مختاراً، فإن الوجوب بالاختيار محقق للاختيار لا مناف له لأنه بحيث لو شاء لترك بخلاف الموجب فظهر الفرق .
The third: that the necessary [being], if it gathers all that is indispensable for the emanation of the effect from it, whether existent or non-existent, the effect necessarily emanates from it, such that it cannot refrain, because the non-existence of the effect is impossible when the cause is complete, so it is not free but rather necessitating; and if it does not gather all that is indispensable, the effect's emanation is impossible necessarily because the effect cannot exist without the cause. The upshot of this is that there is no difference between the necessitating and the free agent. The answer: Even if we grant the impossibility of the effect's non-existence when the free cause is complete, we do not grant that this entails that the agent is necessitating and not free, for necessitation by choice realizes choice and does not contradict it, because [the free agent] is such that if he willed, he would leave [the act], unlike the necessitating agent. So the difference becomes clear.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument of the philosophers against divine freedom and Taftazani's rebuttal. The philosophers argue that if God (the Wājib) possesses all prerequisites for action, the action must necessarily occur, meaning God cannot refrain, thus He is not free but necessitating. Conversely, if He lacks prerequisites, action is impossible. Hence no distinction between free and necessitating agents. Taftazani answers by granting the impossibility of the act's non‑existence when the free cause is complete, but denies this entails coercion. He introduces the key concept of 'necessitation by choice' (al-wujūb bi-l-ikhtiyār): the determination to act that arises from free will is itself an expression of choice, not its negation. The free agent could have done otherwise if he had willed; the necessitating agent cannot. Thus the distinction stands.

Key Terms
الوجوب بالاختيار
الموجب
المختار
تمام المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's action is necessary (must occur), then God is not free.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that necessity arising from free choice (al-wujūb bi-l-ikhtiyār) is compatible with freedom. The free agent could have refrained if he had willed, preserving choice.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's answer, why does the free agent differ from the necessitating agent?
A) Because the free agent can never refrain from acting
B) Because the free agent could have refrained if he willed
C) Because the necessitating agent can also will
D) Because the free agent's act is impossible

Key Takeaway
The free agent's ability to refrain 'if he willed' distinguishes 'necessitation by choice' from mere coercion, preserving divine freedom even when the act is necessarily performed given that will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel God's will is absolute—like a trial you cannot escape—do you ever doubt His wisdom or attribute compulsion to Him? How does Taftazani's distinction between 'necessitation by choice' and coercion reshape your trust in God's actions in your life? What specific circumstance right now do you need to surrender to that truth?
?
The philosophers argued that if God's act is necessary, He is not free. Have you ever felt that God's decrees leave no room for your own choices? Reflect on the phrase 'if he willed, he would leave it.' In what area of your life are you acting as though God's sovereignty negates your responsibility? What would change today if you believed that God's commands are 'necessitation by choice'—both fully divine and fully free?
?
Consider a recurring sin or bad habit you feel powerless to change. If God's freedom is preserved even when His action is necessary, what does that imply about your capacity to choose differently in that area? Are you using 'it's God's will' as an excuse? What does Taftazani's counterfactual 'if he willed, he would leave it' challenge you to actually will to change starting now?

Power Over Non-Existence: Objection and Answer
Taftazani explains how non-existence can be an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine power relates to non-existence and how Taftazani refutes the philosophers' objection.

Core Idea
Power over non-existence means the freedom to refrain from creating, not making non-existence an object.

The philosophers argue that if God can create, He must also be able to annihilate, but they claim non-existence cannot be an object of power. Taftazani clarifies that 'power over non-existence' simply means God can choose not to bring something about. This lesson unpacks the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the philosophers' argument: power relates equally to both sides, so if God can create, He can also destroy. But they claim this leads to absurdities.
Notice the two reasons they give: non-existence is eternal and cannot be an effect, and non-existence is pure negation.
2
Identify Taftazani's answer
Find where Taftazani explains what 'power over non-existence' really means. He says it means the agent can will not to act.
He distinguishes between willing non-existence as an act and refraining from creating.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
Contrast the two meanings: making non-existence a positive object vs. the agent's choice to not bring something into existence.
Taftazani denies that the latter is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن الفاعل لو كان قادراً على وجود الشيء، لكان قادراً على عدمه، لأن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء، لكن اللازم باطل، لأن العدم الأصلي أزلي ولا شيء من الأزلي بأثر للقادر، وأيضاً العدم نفي محض لا يصلح متعلقاً للقدرة والإرادة . لأن معناه التأثر، وحيث لا تأثير فلا أثر. والجواب : أن معنى كون العدم مقدوراً. أن الفاعل إن شاء لم يفعل، أي إن شاء أن لا يوجد الشيء لم يوجده ، أو إن لم يشأ لم يفعل . أي إن لم يشأ أن يوجده لم يوجده . ولا نسلم استحالة ذلك، وإنما المستحيل هو أنه شاء فعل العدم،
The fourth: that if the agent had power over the existence of a thing, he would have power over its non-existence, because power relates equally to both sides. But the consequent is false, because original non-existence is eternal, and nothing eternal is an effect of the powerful. Also, non-existence is pure negation and is not a valid object for power and will, because its meaning is being affected, and where there is no effect there is no effect. The answer: The meaning of non-existence being within power is that the agent, if he wills, does not act, i.e., if he wills that the thing not exist, he does not bring it into existence, or if he does not will, he does not act. i.e., if he does not will to bring it into existence, he does not bring it into existence. And we do not grant the impossibility of that; the impossible is only that he willed the act of non-existence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection of the philosophers: if God has power to create, He must also have power over non-existence, since power is equal towards both existence and non-existence. However, they claim this is false because non-existence is eternal and cannot be an effect of a powerful agent, and also because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be the object of power or will, which require some effect. Taftazani answers by clarifying what 'power over non-existence' means: it means the agent can choose not to bring something into existence, i.e., his will to refrain from acting. This does not require making non-existence an object of power in a positive sense. He denies that this is impossible; the only impossibility would be if God willed non-existence as an act, which is not what is meant.

Key Terms
العدم
القدرة
النسبة إلى الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence must be a positive entity to be an object of power.
Correction: Non-existence is not a positive entity; power over it means the ability to refrain from creating.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'power over non-existence' according to Taftazani?
A) The ability to make non-existence into a positive entity
B) The ability to will that something not exist, i.e., to refrain from creating
C) The ability to create non-existence as an effect
D) The ability to change non-existence into existence

Key Takeaway
Power over non-existence is not about making non-existence an object but about the agent's free choice to refrain from creating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where God has not given you something you desire, do you attribute that to His inability or to His wise choice to refrain? How does this lesson correct your heart's assumption?
?
How does understanding that non-existence is not an object of power but a refraining from action reshape your trust in God's perfect will? Where in your life do you need to accept God's 'refraining' as a deliberate act of wisdom?
?
Do you ever think that God's power is constrained by what He has not done? This lesson shows that refraining from creating is an exercise of power. What does that mean for your own sense of control? Are you willing to let God refrain from acting in your life without doubting His love?

Refutation of the Fifth Argument: Preference and Futility
Taftazani denies that lack of personal benefit implies futile action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the claim that God's acts must be either personally beneficial or futile.

Core Idea
An act can be meaningful without benefiting the doer.

Philosophers argued that God's acts are either preferential for Him (implying He is completed by another) or not preferential (implying futility). Both seem impossible for God. Taftazani responds by denying that lack of personal benefit automatically makes an act futile. Let's examine his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the philosophical objection presented in the first two lines. Notice the dilemma: either the act is better for God (implying completion by another) or it is not (implying futility).
This is a standard kalām objection based on the concept of 'preference without a preferrer'.
2
Identify Taftazani's denial
In the answer, Taftazani begins by denying the premise that a non‑preferable act is automatically futile. Underline his phrase 'we do not grant'.
3
Analyze the distinction
Taftazani distinguishes between an act being 'more appropriate in itself' or 'in relation to another' versus being personally beneficial to the agent. This is the core of his response.
He argues that the act can be non‑futile if it has intrinsic appropriateness or benefits others.
4
Consider the concession
Taftazani even concedes that if one insists on calling such an act 'futile' (because no benefit accrues to the agent), he does not grant that futility is impossible for God. Why? Because God is not subject to need or benefit.
This move blocks the objection by rejecting the underlying assumption that futility is always impossible for the Necessary Being.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Think about why this argument matters for Islamic theology: it safeguards God's absolute independence while affirming that His actions have purpose and wisdom, even if not for His own benefit.
This is a classic example of how kalām defends divine attributes against philosophical objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: أن الفاعل للشيء بطريق القدرة والاختيار إن كان الفعل أولى به من الترك لزم استكماله بالغير، وإن لم يكن أولى لزم كون فعله عبثاً وكلا الأمرين محال على الواجب. الجواب : أنا لا نسلم أن الفعل إذا لم يكن أولى به كان عبثا لما لا يكفي في نفي العبث كونه أولى في نفس الأمر - أو بالنسبة إلى الغير من غير أن تكون تلك الأولوية أولى بالفاعل، وإن سمي مثله عبثاً بناء على خلوه من نفع للفاعل. فلا نسلم استحالته على الواجب.
The fifth: that the agent of a thing by way of power and choice, if the act is more appropriate for him than refraining, then he would be completed by another; and if it is not more appropriate, then his act would be futile. Both are impossible for the Necessary [Being]. The answer: We do not grant that if the act is not more appropriate for him it would be futile, because what suffices to negate futility is its being more appropriate in itself - or in relation to another - without that appropriateness being a benefit for the agent. And even if something similar is called 'futility' based on its being devoid of benefit for the agent, we do not grant its impossibility for the Necessary [Being].
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses the fifth philosophical objection: if God's act is preferential for Him, it implies He is completed by another; if not, it is futile. Both are impossible for God. Taftazani answers by denying that lack of personal preference entails futility. He argues that an act can be 'more appropriate' in itself or beneficial for others, not necessarily for the agent. Even if one calls such an act futile because it offers no benefit to the agent, Taftazani does not grant that such futility is impossible for God, because God is not subject to need or benefit in the same way creatures are.

Key Terms
الأولوية
العبث
استكمال بالغير
النفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any action that does not benefit the agent is futile.
Correction: Taftazani argues that an act can be meaningful if it is appropriate in itself or beneficial to others, even if the agent receives no benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's answer, what can make an act non‑futile even if it is not personally beneficial to God?
A) It must be beneficial to the agent
B) The act is more appropriate in itself or beneficial to others
C) The agent's will alone makes it non‑futile
D) Futility is impossible for God regardless

Key Takeaway
Taftazani defends God's actions from the charge of futility by showing that an act can be meaningful without personally benefiting the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you check whether it benefits you? Can you recall a time you did something purely because it was right, with no personal gain? That act may be closer to the spirit of this lesson.
?
If God’s actions are not constrained by personal benefit, how does that challenge your view of your own actions? Are you willing to do what is right even when it costs you and brings no return?
?
Imagine you are praised for a good deed you did secretly. Does the praise feel gratifying? This lesson shows that God does not act for praise or benefit. Can you let go of needing recognition for your good deeds?

The Sixth Argument and Taftazani's Distinction
Taftazani denies that possibility in eternity entails contradictory outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's key distinction between possibility in essence and possibility relative to dependence on a cause, and how it refutes the sixth argument against God's free will.

Core Idea
Possibility is relative: something can be possible in itself yet impossible in a given time-frame.

Philosophers argued that if God were free, He could change the impossible into possible or make an eternal thing an effect — both absurd. Taftazani responds by denying the entailment and introducing a nuanced distinction about possibility. In this lesson, we unpack the argument and his rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' argument
Read the passage to grasp the two problematic outcomes the philosophers predict if God is truly free: either the impossible becomes possible, or an eternal thing becomes an effect.
Note the logical structure: if A then (B or C), both B and C are impossible.
2
Identify Taftazani's denial and distinction
Taftazani denies the second entailment and introduces a distinction: something can be possible in its essence in eternity, yet impossible to occur in eternity due to its dependence on a cause that will act later.
This is the core of the refutation — possibility is not absolute but conditional.
3
Relate the analogy of a temporal event
Taftazani gives the example of a temporally originated thing: it is possible in its essence from eternity, but it cannot occur before its time because its existence depends on a cause that will bring it about at a specific time.
Think of an eclipse predicted for next year: it's possible now but cannot happen now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: أن الباري تعالى لو كان قادراً مختاراً لزم انقلاب الممتنع ممكناً أو جواز كون الأزلي أثراً للقادر وكلاهما محال. وجه اللزوم أن أثره إن كان ممتنعاً في الأزل ، وقد صار ممكناً فيما لا يزال فهو الأمر الأول، وإن كان ممكناً وقد أوجده القادر فهو الثاني لأن إمكانه في الأزل). مع أن الاستناد إلى القادر في قوة إمكان استناده إلى القادر مع كونه في الأزل. والجواب : منع الملازمة الثانية لجواز أن يكون ممكناً في الأزل نظراً إلى ذاته وممتنع وقوعه في الأزل، نظراً إلى وصف استناده إلى القادر كالحادث ممكن في الأزل لذاته،
The sixth: that if the Most High Creator were powerful and free, it would entail the conversion of the impossible into possible, or the possibility of the eternal being an effect of the powerful, both of which are impossible. The manner of entailment is that His effect, if it was impossible in eternity and became possible in the future, that is the first [case]; and if it was possible and the powerful brought it into existence, that is the second, because its possibility in eternity, despite its dependence on the powerful, is in the strength of the possibility of its dependence on the powerful while being in eternity. The answer: we deny the second entailment, because it is possible that [the effect] was possible in eternity with respect to its essence and impossible to occur in eternity with respect to the description of its dependence on the powerful, like a temporally originated thing being possible in eternity with respect to its essence...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth philosophical argument against God's free will. The philosophers argue that if God were free, He could change the impossible into possible or make an eternal thing an effect, both impossible. The reasoning: either the effect (the world) was impossible in eternity and becomes possible later, which is change of impossibility, or it was possible and God creates it, implying something eternal (the possibility) is an effect. Taftazani answers by denying the second entailment. He introduces a distinction: something can be possible in itself (in its essence) in eternity, but impossible with respect to its occurrence in eternity because of its dependence on a cause that will act later. He gives the analogy of a temporal event that is possible in essence from eternity but cannot occur until its time. The answer appears to continue, but the page ends here. The lesson captures the core rebuttal.

Key Terms
الممتنع
الممكن
الأزلي
الأثر
الاستناد إلى القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility is an absolute property: if something is possible, it must be possible under all conditions and times.
Correction: Taftazani shows that possibility can be relative: something may be possible in its essence from eternity, yet impossible to occur in eternity because its occurrence depends on a temporal cause.

Checkpoint
What distinction does Taftazani use to answer the sixth objection?
A) The effect is possible in itself but impossible in relation to a temporal condition
B) The effect is impossible in itself and possible by another
C) The effect is always eternal
D) The powerful can only create necessary beings

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the argument by showing that possibility can be relative: something may be possible in itself yet impossible to occur before its cause acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If something is possible in its essence but impossible to occur in my life until Allah wills it, how does that change my anxious striving for outcomes I cannot control? What specific worry will I surrender to His timing today?
?
Taftazani shows that possibility is relative to cause and time. Where in my life am I treating a possibility as absolute—demanding it happen now—without trusting the wisdom of the Causer? Will I wait on His appointed time?
?
The philosophers thought eternal possibility necessitated contradiction. What comfort do I take in knowing that Allah's creative power is not bound by human logical puzzles? How will I praise Him specifically for His unconstrained will right now?

The Seventh Objection and Its Answer
Taftazani presents the seventh objection that divine knowledge precludes power and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine foreknowledge does not negate power: God knows that the act will occur by His own power, so the necessity is conditional and confirms power.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge of what you will do by your own power does not remove that power.

This objection seems powerful: if God knows everything in advance, then everything is either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for power. But the answer turns the objection on its head—it shows that God's knowledge that something will be done by His own power is precisely what makes it within His power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection argues that any effect is either 'necessarily occurring' or 'necessarily not occurring' based on God's pre-eternal knowledge. If God knows it will happen, it is necessary; if He knows it will not, it is impossible.
This is a deterministic argument that seems to leave no room for power.
2
Understand the conclusion against power
The objection concludes that nothing that is necessary or impossible can be within power (maqdūr), because power requires both ability to act and ability to refrain. In the necessary case, refraining is impossible; in the impossible case, acting is impossible.
The objection claims power is undermined in both cases.
3
Read the answer carefully
The answer does not deny the premise. Instead, it says that God knows the act will occur by His power. The necessity here is based on God's knowledge of His own free action. Thus, the act is possible in itself but necessary due to divine will and knowledge.
This kind of necessity does not negate power; it confirms it.
4
Reflect on why the answer works
Ask yourself: If God knows that He will do something by His own power, does that mean He cannot do otherwise? No, because His knowledge is based on His free decree. The necessity is conditional on His will, not external compulsion.
This resolves the objection: power is not removed but actualized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن أثر الباري تعالى إما واجب الوقوع أو ممتنع الوقوع، لأنه إما يعلم في الأزل وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع ، وإلا لزم الجهل ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع بمقدور لزوال مكنة الترك في الأولى والفعل في الثانية. بل كليهما في كليهما . والجواب : أنه يعلم ممتنع وقوعه بقدرته ومثل هذا الوجوب لا ينافي المقدورية بل يحققها .
The seventh: That the effect of the Exalted Creator is either necessarily occurring or necessarily not occurring, because He either knows in pre-eternity its occurrence, so it is necessary, or He knows its non-occurrence, so it is impossible, otherwise ignorance would follow, and nothing that is necessary or impossible is within power due to the removal of the ability to refrain in the first and the ability to act in the second. Rather, both are in both. The answer: He knows that its occurrence by His power is impossible, and this kind of necessity does not contradict being within power but rather actualizes it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is the seventh objection and its answer. The objection argues that divine knowledge in pre-eternity determines every event as either necessarily occurring or necessarily not occurring; thus, nothing is within God's power because power requires the ability to both do and refrain. The answer clarifies that the necessity here is based on God's knowledge that the event will occur by His own power. This kind of conditional necessity (the act is necessitated by foreknowledge) does not negate that it is within His power; rather, it affirms that His power is effective and that He will freely bring it about. The event is possible in itself but necessary due to divine will and knowledge—this necessity confirms power rather than removing it.

Key Terms
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
علم في الأزل
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows everything in advance, then He cannot truly have power to choose otherwise; foreknowledge compels the act.
Correction: Foreknowledge of what one will do by one's own power does not remove that power. God's knowledge is based on His free decree, so the necessity is conditional and confirms power.

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the seventh objection argue that divine foreknowledge does not negate power?
A) By claiming that God's knowledge is not deterministic.
B) By stating that God knows the act as occurring by His power, so the necessity is based on that power.
C) By denying that God has foreknowledge of future contingents.
D) By asserting that power is prior to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge does not preclude power; it confirms it because God knows that He will freely bring about the act by His power.

?
If you truly believe God's knowledge does not remove your power, why do you sometimes feel powerless against your habits? What would change if you fully internalized that your actions are truly your own while God knows them?
?
The answer says divine knowledge actualizes power. How does this transform your understanding of reliance on God? Are you using His foreknowledge as a crutch or as a motivator to act?

Divine Foreknowledge and Power
The author refutes the claim that necessary or impossible events are not in God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine foreknowledge does not negate but rather confirms divine power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of impossibility affirms His power.

This passage presents a subtle objection against divine power: if God knows everything from eternity, then everything is either necessary or impossible and thus beyond His power. The author responds by clarifying that this kind of necessity actually demonstrates the perfection of divine power. Let's read the objection and its precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objection presents a dilemma based on God's eternal knowledge.
The objection uses a logical form 'either...or...'.
2
Identify the three premises
The objection has three key premises: (1) God's knowledge determines necessity/impossibility, (2) otherwise ignorance would follow, (3) necessary/impossible things are not within power.
Pay attention to the reasoning about 'ability to refrain' and 'ability to act'.
3
Understand the problem
Ask yourself: Why would necessary/impossible events seem to exclude power? Because power implies the ability to both do and refrain; necessity removes one option.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Read the author's answer
Now read the answer: God knows that the event's occurrence by His power is impossible (i.e., He knows He will not do it). This kind of necessity is not from incapacity but from divine will.
The answer redefines 'necessity' as a consequence of God's own choice, not external constraint.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer preserves both divine power and divine knowledge without conflict. It shows that God's power is not limited by His knowledge; rather, His knowledge reflects His power.
This is a key point in Islamic theology regarding the compatibility of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن أثر الباري تعالى إما واجب الوقوع أو ممتنع الوقوع، لأنه إما يعلم في الأزل وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع ، وإلا لزم الجهل ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع بمقدور لزوال مكنة الترك في الأول والفعل في الثاني. بل كليهما في كليهما . والجواب : أنه يعلم ممتنع وقوعه بقدرته ومثل هذا الوجوب لا ينافي المقدورية بل يحققها .
The seventh [argument]: that the effect of God—exalted is He—is either necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence, because either He knows in eternity its occurrence, so it is necessary, or He knows its non-occurrence, so it is impossible; otherwise ignorance would follow. And nothing of the necessary and the impossible is within power, because the ability to refrain is removed in the first, and the ability to act in the second, rather both are removed in both. The answer: that He knows [that] its occurrence by His power is impossible, and such necessity does not contradict power-possession, but rather realizes it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection concerning divine power, followed by the author's answer. The objector argues: Everything that God produces (athar) must be either necessary or impossible in occurrence, because from eternity God either knows it will happen (making it necessary) or knows it will not happen (making it impossible). If neither, ignorance would follow. But neither the necessary nor the impossible can be the object of power, because what is necessary cannot be left (ability to refrain is gone) and what is impossible cannot be done (ability to act is gone). Therefore, God's effect is not within His power. The author answers: God knows that its occurrence by His power is impossible (i.e., He knows that if He were to bring it about, it would be impossible due to a contradiction, but He knows He will not bring it about). This kind of necessity (the necessity of impossibility through power) does not negate being empowered; rather, it confirms it, because power implies the ability to both do and refrain, and this impossibility is due to God's own decision not to do it, not due to incapacity.

Key Terms
أثر
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
علم أزلي
مقدورية
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows something will or will not happen, then no power is involved; the event is forced.
Correction: The author shows that God's knowledge of the impossibility of an act occurring through His power actually confirms His power, because it is based on His own volitional refusal, not lack of ability.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, does God's knowledge that an event is impossible to occur through His power conflict with His power over that event?
A) Yes, because knowledge of impossibility negates power.
B) No, because such knowledge actually realizes power-possession.
C) Yes, because power requires ability to both do and refrain.
D) No, because God does not know impossibilities.

Key Takeaway
Divine foreknowledge does not limit divine power; rather, it shows that God's power is so complete that He freely chooses what to create, and impossibility springs from His choice, not from external constraint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you trust that God's power is not limited by your perception of impossibility, or do you let your limited understanding constrain your reliance on Him? How will you shift your perspective today?
?
The lesson teaches that God's knowledge of what He will not do does not indicate weakness but confirms His sovereignty. In what area of your life have you doubted God's ability to act simply because He has not yet acted? Will you repent of that doubt and instead see His restraint as a sign of His perfect wisdom?
?
If God's power is so complete that even the absence of an action is a deliberate choice, how should this reshape your prayer life? Do you pray with the conviction that every outcome—whether granted or withheld—is a direct expression of His boundless power and love? What one prayer will you offer today with this deepened understanding?
