The Infinitude and Comprehensiveness of Divine Power
al-Sanusi proves God's power is infinite and all-encompassing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural basis for the infinitude and comprehensiveness of God's power, and why objections based on conditions and preventers are dismissed.

Core Idea
God's power has no limit—its scope is all possible things.

In this concluding section of his discussion on divine attributes, al-Sanusi establishes that God’s power is infinite in its duration and scope. He presents three senses of infinitude, a rational proof based on the divine essence and possibility, and then addresses an objection before preferring to rely on the Qur’an. This lesson will walk you through his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice that the passage begins with 'قال : خاتمة' and repeats 'قال : خاتمة'—this may indicate a new section.
2
Identify the three meanings of infinitude
The passage lists three meanings for 'power is infinite': no extended limit, no cessation, and no impossibility of attachment. Find each in the text.
The third meaning is the most significant for the argument.
3
Understand the rational proof
The proof hinges on two factors: the requirer (God's essence) and the rectifier (possibility). Notice why these are sufficient without further conditions.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objection is raised about conditions and preventers. Read the answer and note why the author considers it weak.
The author then prefers scriptural evidence over the rational answer.
5
Reflect on the scriptural appeal
The verse 'And God over everything is powerful' is cited as the best proof. Ask yourself why the author prioritizes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . بمعنى أن جواز تعلقها لا ينقطع، وشاملة للكل بمعنى أن تعلقها لا يقتصر على البعض، لأن المقتضى القادرية هو الذات. والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان ولا تمايز قبل الوجود يخصص البعض، والأولى التمسك بمثل : والله على كل شيء قدير . قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . إما بمعنى أنها ليست لها طبيعة امتدادية تنتهي إلى حد ونهاية أو بمعنى أنها لا يطرأ عليها العدم، فظاهر لا يحتاج إلى التعرض، وإما بمعنى أنها لا تصير بحيث يمتنع تعلقها فلأن ذلك عجز ونقص ولأن كثيراً من مخلوقاته أبدية كنعيم الجنان، وذلك بتعاقب جزئيات لا نهاية لها بحسب القوة والإمكان ، ولأن المقتضى للقادرية هو الذات، والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان ، ولا انقطاع لهما ، وبهذا استدلوا على شمول قدرة الله تعالى لكل موجود أنه ممكن بمعنى يصح تعلقها به ولما توجه عليه أنه لم لا يجوز اختصاص بعض الممكنات بشرط التعلق القدرة ، أو مانع عنه، ومجرد وجوده والمصحح لا يكفي بدون وجود الشرط وعدم المانع . أجيب بأنه لا تمايز للممكنات قبل الوجود ليختص البعض بشرائط التعلق وموانعه دون البعض، وهذا ضعيف على ما سبق فالأولى التمسك بالنصوص الدالة على شمول قدرته مثل : والله على كل شيء قدير
He said: Conclusion. The power of God—exalted is He—is infinite, meaning that the permissibility of its attachment does not cease, and comprehensive of all, meaning that its attachment is not restricted to some, because the requirer of being powerful is the Essence, and the rectifier of being empowered is possibility, and there is no distinction before existence to specify some. And the better [method] is to cling to such as: 'And God over everything is powerful' (al-Baqara 284). He said: Conclusion. The power of God—exalted is He—is infinite: either in the sense that it does not have an extended nature that ends at a limit or endpoint, or in the sense that non-existence does not befall it, which is apparent and does not need to be addressed; or in the sense that it does not become such that its attachment is impossible, because that would be incapacity and deficiency, and because many of His creatures are eternal like the bliss of Paradise, and that is through the succession of particulars that have no end according to potency and possibility, and because the requirer of being powerful is the Essence, and the rectifier of being empowered is possibility, and there is no interruption for them. And by this they argued for the comprehensiveness of God's power over every existent that is possible, meaning that it is correct for it to attach to it. Then an objection was raised against it: why is it not permissible that some possibles are specified by a condition for the attachment of power, or a preventing factor, and mere existence and rectifier are not sufficient without existence of the condition and absence of prevention? It was answered: there is no distinction among possibles before existence to specify some with conditions of attachment and preventers without others. And this is weak according to what preceded, so the better [method] is to cling to the textual proofs indicating the comprehensiveness of His power, such as: 'And God over everything is powerful' (al-Baqara 284).
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a concluding section (khātima) on the infinitude and comprehensiveness of divine power. It begins by stating that God's power is infinite in two ways: first, the permissibility of its attachment to objects never ceases; second, it is all-encompassing, not restricted to some things. The reason given is that the thing that necessitates being powerful (al-muqtaḍā li-l-qādiriyya) is the Divine Essence itself, and the factor that makes something an object of power (al-muṣaḥḥiḥ li-l-maqdūriyya) is its possibility (imkān). Before existence, possibles have no distinguishing features that would specify some as objects of power and not others. The author prefers to rely on scriptural evidence such as 'And God over everything is powerful' (Q 2:284). Then the text repeats the statement 'God's power is infinite' and analyzes three meanings: (1) it has no extended nature with a limit—obvious; (2) non-existence does not befall it—also obvious; (3) it does not become such that attachment is impossible, because that would be weakness, and because many of God's creatures are eternal (e.g., the bliss of Paradise) which involves an endless succession of particulars according to potentiality and possibility. The proof from requirer and rectifier is repeated: the Essence requires power, possibility rectifies being an object, and neither is interrupted. On this basis, theologians argued for the comprehensive scope of God's power over every possible existent. Then an objection is raised: why cannot some possibles be specified by a condition for attachment of power or by a preventing factor? Mere existence of the possibles and the rectifier (possibility) are not enough without the condition and absence of prevention. The answer: there is no distinction among possibles before existence that would cause some to be specified with conditions and preventers and not others. However, the author notes this answer is weak according to what preceded, so it is better to rely on scriptural texts like 'And God over everything is powerful'.

شاملة للكل
جواز التعلق

لا تمايز قبل الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power is limited because some possibles are not actualized, implying conditions or preventers restrict power.
Correction: The author argues that possibility alone is sufficient for God's power to attach, and there is no pre-existing distinction among possibles to impose conditions or preventers.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the rational answer to the objection about conditions and preventers to be weak?
A) Because it contradicts the scriptural evidence.
B) Because it relies on the lack of pre-creation distinction, which is not decisive.
C) Because it assumes that possibles have conditions before existence.
D) Because it affirms that God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
God's power is infinite and encompasses all possible things, with the strongest proof being the explicit Qur'anic verse 'And God over everything is powerful'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power truly encompasses all things, including every detail of your life, does your anxiety about the future or any current difficulty reflect a belief that His power is limited? What would it take for you to fully trust that He is 'over everything powerful' today?
?
The lesson teaches that God's power is not restricted by any condition or preventer. Is there a sin, a habit, or a relationship you have labeled as 'impossible' for God to change? How would your prayer life transform if you approached that impossibility with the conviction that God's power has no limit?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Did you truly believe that I am over everything powerful?' Where in your life have you acted as if your own power or human circumstances were the final reality? What specific step will you take today to surrender that area to His infinite power?

The Infinite Reach of Divine Power
Definition and rational proof of divine power's infinitude and comprehensiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's power being infinite and comprehensive, and the rational proof based on the Divine Essence and possibility.

Core Idea
God's power is limitless because its basis is the Essence and its object is whatever is possible.

This lesson establishes the foundational attributes of divine power: it is both infinite and all-encompassing. Sanusi begins by clarifying what 'infinite' means in this context—not merely that it never ends, but that its scope of attachment is unlimited. He then provides a concise rational proof rooted in the nature of God and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the author's own wording. Then read the translation to understand the core claims.
Notice the phrase 'جواز تعلقها لا ينقطع' indicates cessation is impossible.
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage defines 'infinite' as non-cessation of attachment, and 'comprehensive' as attachment not restricted to some. Recognize that these are two distinct aspects.
3
Understand the rational proof
The proof states that the basis for being powerful is the Essence itself, and the condition for being an object of power is possibility. Since both are continuous and no pre-existence differentiation, power covers all possibles.
Also note the author suggests scripture as a stronger foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . بمعنى أن جواز تعلقها لا ينقطع، وشاملة للكل بمعنى أن تعلقها لا يقتصر على البعض، لأن المقتضى القادرية هو الذات. والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان ولا تمايز قبل الوجود يخصص البعض، والأولى التمسك بمثل : والله على كل شيء قدير.

قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . إما بمعنى أنها ليست لها طبيعة امتدادية تنتهي إلى حد ونهاية أو بمعنى أنها لا يطرأ عليها العدم، فظاهر لا يحتاج إلى التعرض، وإما بمعنى أنها لا تصير بحيث يمتنع تعلقها فلأن ذلك عجز ونقص ولأن كثيراً من مخلوقاته أبدية كنعيم الجنان، وذلك بتعاقب جزئيات لا نهاية لها بحسب القوة والإمكان، ولأن المقتضى للقادرية هو الذات، والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان، ولا انقطاع لهما، وبهذا استدلوا على شمول قدرة الله تعالى لكل موجود أنه ممكن بمعنى يصح تعلقها به ولما توجه عليه أنه لم لا يجوز اختصاص بعض الممكنات بشرط التعلق القدرة، أو مانع عنه، ومجرد وجوده والمصحح لا يكفي بدون وجود الشرط وعدم المانع .

أجيب بأنه لا تمايز للممكنات قبل الوجود ليختص البعض بشرائط التعلق وموانعه دون البعض، وهذا ضعيف على ما سبق فالأولى التمسك بالنصوص الدالة على شمول قدرته مثل : والله على كل شيء قدير.
He said: Conclusion: The power of Allah the Exalted is infinite. Meaning that the possibility of its attachment is not cut off, and it is comprehensive of all, meaning that its attachment is not restricted to some, because the requisitum for being powerful is the Essence. And that which renders something capable of being apprehended by power is possibility, and there is no differentiation before existence that would specify some [over others]. And it is preferable to adhere to something like: 'And Allah is over all things competent' (Quran 2:284). He said: Conclusion: The power of Allah the Exalted is infinite. Either in the sense that it does not have an extended nature that reaches a limit and an end, or in the sense that non-existence does not occur to it, so it is apparent and needs no exposition; or in the sense that it does not become such that its attachment is impossible, because that would be incapacity and deficiency, and because many of His creatures are eternal like the bliss of Paradise, which occurs through a succession of particulars that have no end in terms of potentiality and possibility, and because the requisitum for being powerful is the Essence, and that which renders something capable of being apprehended by power is possibility, and there is no interruption for both of them. And by this they argued for the comprehensiveness of the power of Allah the Exalted over every existent, for it is possible in the sense that its attachment to it is valid. And when it was objected against it: Why is it not permissible that some possibles be specified by a condition of attachment of power, or a barrier against it, and merely its existence and the condition are not sufficient without the existence of the condition and the absence of the barrier? It was answered that there is no differentiation among the possibles before existence such that some would be specified by conditions of attachment and its barriers to the exclusion of others. But this is weak according to what preceded, so it is preferable to adhere to the texts that indicate the comprehensiveness of His power, such as: 'And Allah is over all things competent.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the infinitude and comprehensiveness of God's power. It begins with the matn (original text) stating that God's power is non-infinite, meaning its attachment to objects never ceases, and it is all-encompassing, not restricted to some objects. The reason given is that the underlying basis for being powerful (al-qudriyya) is the Divine Essence itself, and that which renders something a valid object of power (al-maqduriyya) is its possibility (al-imkan). There is no distinction among possibles prior to existence that would single out some for power and not others. The author then remarks that it is better to rely on scriptural evidence like the verse 'And Allah is over all things competent.' The commentary then expands: 'non-infinite' may mean either that God's power is not a linear entity that ends, or that it does not undergo annihilation—both are obvious. Alternatively, it means that God's power never reaches a state where attachment to an object becomes impossible, because that would be weakness. A proof is given: many of God's creatures are eternal, like the bliss of Paradise, which consists of an endless succession of particulars in potentiality and possibility. Furthermore, the requisitum for power is the Essence, and the condition for being an object of power is possibility—both are continuous. From this, theologians argued that God's power encompasses every possible existent. An objection is raised: why can't some possible objects be excluded due to a condition or obstacle? The answer is that there is no differentiation among possibles before existence to allow such specification. However, the author considers this answer weak and advises relying on scriptural texts as the stronger position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that 'infinite' means God's power is unlimited in quantity but might still exclude some possible objects.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'infinite' means the attachment never ceases, and 'comprehensive' means it includes all possible objects—nothing is excluded by its own nature.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the underlying basis (mūqtadā) for God's being powerful?
A) The possibility of objects
B) The Divine Essence (al-dhāt)
C) The absence of obstacles
D) The existence of creation

Key Takeaway
God's power is infinite and comprehensive, grounded in the Essence and possibility, and affirmed by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God's power is infinite because its basis is His own Essence, not your worthiness or circumstances. When you pray, do you truly believe His power is as available to you in your weakest moment as it was to the prophets? What 'impossible' situation in your life will you bring to Him today with that certainty?
?
The proof says no pre-existential differentiation excludes any possible thing from His power. What does that mean for the sin you think is too great for Him to forgive? Will you turn to Him now, not with empty words, but with the conviction that His power can transform even that?
?
The passage ends by directing us to the verse 'And Allah is over all things competent.' Is there any area of your life over which you have assumed God is incompetent—perhaps your finances, your relationships, or your inner struggles? Will you repent of that unspoken disbelief and submit that area to His power today?

Defending the Universality of Divine Power
Objection and answer regarding possible exclusion from divine power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that some possible objects could be excluded from divine power, and the response based on pre-existential uniformity and scriptural authority.

Core Idea
Before existence, all possibles are equal; only scripture can specify exceptions.

After establishing that God's power covers all possibles, a potential objection arises: what if some possibles require a special condition or face an obstacle? Sanusi presents the answer from the theologians and then weighs its strength, ultimately resting on the clear texts of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its context
Read the Arabic passage from 'ولما توجه عليه' to the end. Understand the objection: why can't some possibles be excluded due to conditions or obstacles?
The objection is based on the idea that mere possibility may not suffice for power attachment.
2
Analyze the theologians' answer
The answer states that before existence, all possibles are undifferentiated; hence no basis to specify conditions or obstacles for some but not others.
3
Understand the author's preference
Sanusi considers that answer weak and advises sticking to scriptural texts like the Quranic verse, which clearly state God's power over all things.
This shows a methodological preference for revelation over rational speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . بمعنى أن جواز تعلقها لا ينقطع، وشاملة للكل بمعنى أن تعلقها لا يقتصر على البعض، لأن المقتضى القادرية هو الذات. والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان ولا تمايز قبل الوجود يخصص البعض، والأولى التمسك بمثل : والله على كل شيء قدير.

قال : خاتمة قدرة الله تعالى غير متناهية . إما بمعنى أنها ليست لها طبيعة امتدادية تنتهي إلى حد ونهاية أو بمعنى أنها لا يطرأ عليها العدم، فظاهر لا يحتاج إلى التعرض، وإما بمعنى أنها لا تصير بحيث يمتنع تعلقها فلأن ذلك عجز ونقص ولأن كثيراً من مخلوقاته أبدية كنعيم الجنان، وذلك بتعاقب جزئيات لا نهاية لها بحسب القوة والإمكان، ولأن المقتضى للقادرية هو الذات، والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان، ولا انقطاع لهما، وبهذا استدلوا على شمول قدرة الله تعالى لكل موجود أنه ممكن بمعنى يصح تعلقها به ولما توجه عليه أنه لم لا يجوز اختصاص بعض الممكنات بشرط التعلق القدرة، أو مانع عنه، ومجرد وجوده والمصحح لا يكفي بدون وجود الشرط وعدم المانع .

أجيب بأنه لا تمايز للممكنات قبل الوجود ليختص البعض بشرائط التعلق وموانعه دون البعض، وهذا ضعيف على ما سبق فالأولى التمسك بالنصوص الدالة على شمول قدرته مثل : والله على كل شيء قدير.
He said: Conclusion: The power of Allah the Exalted is infinite. Meaning that the possibility of its attachment is not cut off, and it is comprehensive of all, meaning that its attachment is not restricted to some, because the requisitum for being powerful is the Essence. And that which renders something capable of being apprehended by power is possibility, and there is no differentiation before existence that would specify some [over others]. And it is preferable to adhere to something like: 'And Allah is over all things competent' (Quran 2:284). He said: Conclusion: The power of Allah the Exalted is infinite. Either in the sense that it does not have an extended nature that reaches a limit and an end, or in the sense that non-existence does not occur to it, so it is apparent and needs no exposition; or in the sense that it does not become such that its attachment is impossible, because that would be incapacity and deficiency, and because many of His creatures are eternal like the bliss of Paradise, which occurs through a succession of particulars that have no end in terms of potentiality and possibility, and because the requisitum for being powerful is the Essence, and that which renders something capable of being apprehended by power is possibility, and there is no interruption for both of them. And by this they argued for the comprehensiveness of the power of Allah the Exalted over every existent, for it is possible in the sense that its attachment to it is valid. And when it was objected against it: Why is it not permissible that some possibles be specified by a condition of attachment of power, or a barrier against it, and merely its existence and the condition are not sufficient without the existence of the condition and the absence of the barrier? It was answered that there is no differentiation among the possibles before existence such that some would be specified by conditions of attachment and its barriers to the exclusion of others. But this is weak according to what preceded, so it is preferable to adhere to the texts that indicate the comprehensiveness of His power, such as: 'And Allah is over all things competent.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the infinitude and comprehensiveness of God's power. It begins with the matn (original text) stating that God's power is non-infinite, meaning its attachment to objects never ceases, and it is all-encompassing, not restricted to some objects. The reason given is that the underlying basis for being powerful (al-qudriyya) is the Divine Essence itself, and that which renders something a valid object of power (al-maqduriyya) is its possibility (al-imkan). There is no distinction among possibles prior to existence that would single out some for power and not others. The author then remarks that it is better to rely on scriptural evidence like the verse 'And Allah is over all things competent.' The commentary then expands: 'non-infinite' may mean either that God's power is not a linear entity that ends, or that it does not undergo annihilation—both are obvious. Alternatively, it means that God's power never reaches a state where attachment to an object becomes impossible, because that would be weakness. A proof is given: many of God's creatures are eternal, like the bliss of Paradise, which consists of an endless succession of particulars in potentiality and possibility. Furthermore, the requisitum for power is the Essence, and the condition for being an object of power is possibility—both are continuous. From this, theologians argued that God's power encompasses every possible existent. An objection is raised: why can't some possible objects be excluded due to a condition or obstacle? The answer is that there is no differentiation among possibles before existence to allow such specification. However, the author considers this answer weak and advises relying on scriptural texts as the stronger position.

Key Terms
اختصاص
شرط
مانع
تمایز
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that rational proof alone can definitively establish the comprehensiveness of God's power without revelation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the rational answer is weak and that scripture provides a more secure foundation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the answer that there is no differentiation among possibles before existence considered weak?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran
B) Because it relies on a speculative principle that may be contested
C) Because it implies God's power is limited
D) Because it denies the possibility of conditions

Key Takeaway
While rational arguments can support God's power's universality, the strongest evidence comes from revelation.

?
Have you ever doubted God's ability to handle a specific situation in your life, thinking it was 'too difficult' for Him? What would change if you truly internalized the verse 'Allah is over all things competent' as the final word on that matter? Commit to reciting this verse five times today with reflection.

?
Sanusi rejects the weak rational answer and clings to the Quran. Is your faith built more on intellectual arguments or on the undeniable authority of revelation? If a well-reasoned argument ever seems to contradict a clear verse, which will you choose? Write down a personal pledge to prioritize the Quran over your own logic.

Ibn 'Abbad's Objection on God's Power over the Known Non-Occurring and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents Ibn Abbad's objection and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn 'Abbad's objection that God cannot have power over what He knows will not occur, and the Ash'ari response that such impossibility does not negate divine capability.

Core Idea
Knowing something won't happen does not limit God's power over it.

Ibn 'Abbad and his followers argued that if God knows something will never occur, then it is impossible for it to occur, and thus God cannot be said to have power over it. Taftazani presents this objection and a concise refutation: impossibility of actuality does not imply impossibility in itself. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style of Taftazani.
2
Identify the objection and its extension
The passage begins with Ibn 'Abbad's objection: God is not capable over what He knows will not occur because it is impossible. Then it mentions an extension from al-Muhassal: likewise for what He knows will occur, due to its necessity.
3
Understand the response
Taftazani's response is that such impossibility does not contradict capability (maqduriyya). Reflect on what this means: capability is about intrinsic possibility, not actual occurrence.
This is the key insight of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم ابن عباد وأتباعه القائلون بأنه ليس بقادر على ما علم أنه لا يقع لاستحالة وقوعه . قال في المحصل وكذا ما علم أنه يقع لوجوبه . والجواب : أن مثل هذه الاستحالة لا تنافي المقدورية
And among them is Ibn 'Abbad and his followers, who say that He is not able to do what He knows will not occur because its occurrence is impossible. The author of al-Muhassal said: And likewise what He knows will occur because of its necessity. The answer is: such impossibility does not contradict capability.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of Ibn 'Abbad and his followers: God cannot have power over what He knows will not occur, because its occurrence is impossible. They extend this to what He knows will occur, calling it necessary. Taftazani's response is succinct: such impossibility—whether of non-occurrence or occurrence—does not negate capability (maqduriyya). In other words, capability is about the inherent possibility of a thing, not its actual occurrence. God has power over all possibilities, even if they never actualize.

Key Terms
ابن عباد
استحالة
مقدورية
وجوب
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something will never happen, it is impossible for God to do it.
Correction: Impossibility of actualization does not mean the thing is impossible in itself. Divine capability pertains to intrinsic possibility, not actual outcome.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does the impossibility of something's occurrence entail for God's power over it?
A) It negates His power.
B) It does not negate His power.
C) It makes the thing impossible in itself.
D) It implies that God is incapable.

Key Takeaway
God's power encompasses all possibilities, regardless of whether they actually occur, because capability is about intrinsic possibility, not actual necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there an impossible situation in your life right now where you have stopped asking God because you assume His foreknowledge of its non-occurrence means He cannot act? What would change if you truly believed His power covers all possibilities, regardless of what He knows will happen?

The Five Interpretations of God's Comprehensive Power
Classification of five meanings of God's power comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'comprehensiveness of God's power' (شُمُول القُدْرَة) and which ones are agreed upon or disputed among theologians.

Core Idea
God's power is comprehensive, but its meaning varies from universal creation to exclusive agency.

Before we can appreciate the Ash'ari proof for God's exclusive causality, we must first clarify what 'comprehensiveness of power' even means. Scholars have proposed several interpretations, some universally accepted and others highly contested. This passage from al-Maqasid lays out those interpretations to set the stage for the correct position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first interpretation
Read the first part of the passage that describes the meaning all theists agree upon: everything is created by God directly or indirectly.
2
Identify the second interpretation
Notice the second meaning: that there is no agent besides God at all. Only a few theologians accept this, and the author calls it 'the truth'.
The author notes that the proofs for this (التوارد والتمانع) are weak.
3
Recognize the third and fourth interpretations
The third concerns God's ability over every possible, regardless of existence. The fourth is that all possibles are effects of God essentially or indirectly.
4
Locate the fifth interpretation
Find the final meaning: all existents occur directly by God's power and will with no other agent. This is the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما شمول قدرته
شمول قدرة الله تعالى
بمعنى أن الكل بإيجاده ابتداء أو بواسطة (۱) فلم يقع من القائلين بالصانع نزاع في ذلك . بل في تفاصيله وبمعنى أنه لا مؤثر سواه أصلاً فلم يذهب إليه إلا البعض من المتكلمين تمسكاً بظواهر النصوص، وهو الحق. وبدليل التوارد والتمانع، وفيهما ضعف .
قال: وأما شمول قدرته ما مرّ من الاختلاف كان في شمول قدرة الله تعالى بمعنى كونه قادراً على كل ممكن سواء تعلق به القدرة والإرادة فوجد أم لا؟ فلم يوجد أصلاً، أو وجد بقدرة مخلوق، وعلى هذا لا يتأتي اختلافات والفلاسفة ومن يجري مجراهم ممن لا يقول بكونه قادراً مختاراً، وقد يفسر شمول قدرته بأن كل ما يوجد من الممكنات فهو معلول له بالذات أو بالواسطة، وهذا مما لا نزاع فيه لأحد من القائلين بوحدة الواجب . وإنما الخلاف في كيفية الاستناد ووجود الوسائط وتفاصيلها، وأن كل ممكن (۲) أي إلى أي ممكن يستند حتى ينتهي إلى الواجب، وقد تفسر شمول قدرته بأن ما الذات والصفات من الموجودات واقع بقدرته وإرادته ابتداء سوی بحيث لا مؤثر سواه، وهذا مذهب أهل الحق من المتكلمين، وقليل ما هم، وتمسكوا
بوجوه .
الأول : النصوص الدالة إجمالاً على أنه خالق الكل لا خالق سواه (۳) وتفصيلاً على أنه خالق السموات والأرض ، والظلمات والنور، والموت والحياة، وغير ذلك من الجواهر والأعراض.
(۳) قال تعالى: ﴿ذلكم الله ربكم لا إله إلا هو خالق كل شيء فاعبدوه . الأنعام آية ١٠٣ . (٤) قال تعالى : الحمد لله الذي خلق السموات والأرض وجعل الظلمات والنور الأنعام آية رقم 1
(۱) في (ج) أو بوسط.
(۲) في ( أ ) بزيادة ( أي ) .
He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the comprehensiveness of God's power—in the sense that everything is by His bringing into existence directly or indirectly, there has been no dispute among those who affirm the Creator about that, but rather about its details. And in the sense that there is no agent besides Him at all, only some of the theologians have adopted it, holding to the apparent meanings of the texts, and it is the truth. And by the proof of mutual exclusion and opposition, and there is weakness in both. He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the difference that passed was about the comprehensiveness of God's power in the sense of His being able over every possible thing, whether power and will attach to it and it exists or not? So it did not exist at all, or it existed by a created power, and on this basis differences do not arise—and the philosophers and those who follow their way, who do not say that He is a powerful chooser. And the comprehensiveness of His power may be interpreted as: everything that exists among possibles is an effect of Him essentially or indirectly, and this is something about which there is no dispute among those who affirm the unity of the Necessary. Rather, the disagreement is about the manner of dependence and the existence of intermediaries and their details, and that every possible (2) i.e., to which possible does it depend until it terminates in the Necessary. And the comprehensiveness of His power may be interpreted as: that which is of existence—essence and attributes—occurs by His power and will directly, such that there is no agent besides Him. This is the school of the people of truth among the theologians, and few they are, and they adhered to proofs. The first: the texts that indicate generally that He is the Creator of all, there is no creator besides Him (3) and in detail that He is the Creator of the heavens and the earth, the darkness and the light, death and life, and other substances and accidents. (3) He said, exalted: 'That is Allah, your Lord; there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things, so worship Him.' (Surah al-An'am, verse 103). (4) He said, exalted: 'All praise is due to Allah, who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light' (Surah al-An'am, verse 1). (1) In (j): or by means. (2) In (a) with an addition: (i.e.).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of the different meanings of the comprehensiveness of God's power (شمول قدرة الله). The author first quotes the matn (قال:) and then explains. He lists several interpretations:
1) The first meaning: that everything is created by God directly or indirectly. This is agreed upon by all theists.
2) The second meaning: that there is no agent besides God at all. Only a few theologians hold this, relying on apparent texts; the author calls it the truth (وهو الحق), but notes the proofs (توارد وتمانع) have weakness.
3) The third meaning: that God is able over every possible regardless of whether it exists or not (by His power) or exists by created power. The philosophers who deny free will do not accept this.
4) The fourth meaning: that all existents are effects of God essentially or indirectly—again agreed upon by those affirming the Necessary. The disagreement is about the manner and intermediaries.
5) The fifth meaning: that all existents (essence and attributes) occur directly by God's power and will with no other agent. This is the school of the people of truth (أهل الحق) among the theologians, though few. They adhere to proofs, the first of which is textual evidence (النصوص) that declare God as the sole creator of everything, including heavens, earth, darkness, light, death, life, substances, and accidents. The author then cites verses as examples.

Key Terms
شمول القدرة
مؤثر
خالق
أهل الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'comprehensiveness of power' means only that God can do everything, i.e., omnipotence.
Correction: The passage shows it also includes the idea of causal exclusivity—whether other agents truly cause things or God is the only real agent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of the comprehensiveness of God's power is agreed upon by all who affirm a Creator?
A) Everything is created by God directly or indirectly.
B) No agent besides God at all.
C) God is able over every possible thing.
D) All possibles are effects of God essentially or indirectly.

Key Takeaway
The term 'comprehensiveness of power' has multiple meanings, but the Ash'ari position uniquely affirms that God is the only real agent directly creating all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you instinctively feel that other people, your circumstances, or your own efforts have ultimate control over the outcome? How would your response change if you truly believed that God is the only real agent (لا مؤثر سواه) in that moment? Pause and commit to one specific situation this week where you will consciously attribute all power to God alone.
?
You have just learned that many theologians throughout history have disagreed on how to understand God's comprehensive power, and that the author declares one interpretation as 'the truth.' Are you willing to re-examine your own assumptions about how God interacts with the world? Or do you find yourself resistant because it challenges your sense of autonomy? Write down one area of your life where you habitually rely on 'secondary causes' as if they were independent agents, and ask Allah to grant you the conviction that He alone is the true agent.
?
The passage notes that the Ash'ari position—that God directly creates all things without any secondary agents—is held by 'few' (قليل ما هم). If being part of the 'few' is the truth, how does that make you feel? Are you willing to stand with a minority view if it is the truth, even if it goes against widespread theological assumptions? Examine your heart: do you seek truth or popularity in your beliefs?

The Textual Proof for God's Exclusive Agency
Presentation of the textual proof for the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school uses revealed texts to prove that God is the sole creator of all things, including substances and accidents.

Core Idea
The Quran declares God as the Creator of everything, leaving no room for other creators.

In the previous lesson, we saw that the Ash'ari position on God's power is that no agent exists besides Him. But what evidence supports this? The author now presents the first proof: textual evidence from the Quran. This lesson examines that proof and how it establishes that God alone creates all essences and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the statement of the Ash'ari position: all existents occur directly by God's power and will with no other agent.
2
Examine the proof structure
The proof is divided into general (إجمالاً) and detailed (تفصيلاً) textual evidence.
3
Find the general evidence
Look for the verse that says God is the Creator of all things (خالق كل شيء).
This is a general statement.
4
Find the detailed evidence
Notice the other verse that specifies He created the heavens, earth, darkness, light, etc.
This covers substances and accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما شمول قدرته
شمول قدرة الله تعالى
بمعنى أن الكل بإيجاده ابتداء أو بواسطة (۱) فلم يقع من القائلين بالصانع نزاع في ذلك . بل في تفاصيله وبمعنى أنه لا مؤثر سواه أصلاً فلم يذهب إليه إلا البعض من المتكلمين تمسكاً بظواهر النصوص، وهو الحق. وبدليل التوارد والتمانع، وفيهما ضعف .
قال: وأما شمول قدرته ما مرّ من الاختلاف كان في شمول قدرة الله تعالى بمعنى كونه قادراً على كل ممكن سواء تعلق به القدرة والإرادة فوجد أم لا؟ فلم يوجد أصلاً، أو وجد بقدرة مخلوق، وعلى هذا لا يتأتي اختلافات والفلاسفة ومن يجري مجراهم ممن لا يقول بكونه قادراً مختاراً، وقد يفسر شمول قدرته بأن كل ما يوجد من الممكنات فهو معلول له بالذات أو بالواسطة، وهذا مما لا نزاع فيه لأحد من القائلين بوحدة الواجب . وإنما الخلاف في كيفية الاستناد ووجود الوسائط وتفاصيلها، وأن كل ممكن (۲) أي إلى أي ممكن يستند حتى ينتهي إلى الواجب، وقد تفسر شمول قدرته بأن ما الذات والصفات من الموجودات واقع بقدرته وإرادته ابتداء سوی بحيث لا مؤثر سواه، وهذا مذهب أهل الحق من المتكلمين، وقليل ما هم، وتمسكوا
بوجوه .
الأول : النصوص الدالة إجمالاً على أنه خالق الكل لا خالق سواه (۳) وتفصيلاً على أنه خالق السموات والأرض ، والظلمات والنور، والموت والحياة، وغير ذلك من الجواهر والأعراض.
(۳) قال تعالى: ﴿ذلكم الله ربكم لا إله إلا هو خالق كل شيء فاعبدوه . الأنعام آية ١٠٣ . (٤) قال تعالى : الحمد لله الذي خلق السموات والأرض وجعل الظلمات والنور الأنعام آية رقم 1
(۱) في (ج) أو بوسط.
(۲) في ( أ ) بزيادة ( أي ) .
He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the comprehensiveness of God's power—in the sense that everything is by His bringing into existence directly or indirectly, there has been no dispute among those who affirm the Creator about that, but rather about its details. And in the sense that there is no agent besides Him at all, only some of the theologians have adopted it, holding to the apparent meanings of the texts, and it is the truth. And by the proof of mutual exclusion and opposition, and there is weakness in both. He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the difference that passed was about the comprehensiveness of God's power in the sense of His being able over every possible thing, whether power and will attach to it and it exists or not? So it did not exist at all, or it existed by a created power, and on this basis differences do not arise—and the philosophers and those who follow their way, who do not say that He is a powerful chooser. And the comprehensiveness of His power may be interpreted as: everything that exists among possibles is an effect of Him essentially or indirectly, and this is something about which there is no dispute among those who affirm the unity of the Necessary. Rather, the disagreement is about the manner of dependence and the existence of intermediaries and their details, and that every possible (2) i.e., to which possible does it depend until it terminates in the Necessary. And the comprehensiveness of His power may be interpreted as: that which is of existence—essence and attributes—occurs by His power and will directly, such that there is no agent besides Him. This is the school of the people of truth among the theologians, and few they are, and they adhered to proofs. The first: the texts that indicate generally that He is the Creator of all, there is no creator besides Him (3) and in detail that He is the Creator of the heavens and the earth, the darkness and the light, death and life, and other substances and accidents. (3) He said, exalted: 'That is Allah, your Lord; there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things, so worship Him.' (Surah al-An'am, verse 103). (4) He said, exalted: 'All praise is due to Allah, who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light' (Surah al-An'am, verse 1). (1) In (j): or by means. (2) In (a) with an addition: (i.e.).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of the different meanings of the comprehensiveness of God's power (شمول قدرة الله). The author first quotes the matn (قال:) and then explains. He lists several interpretations:
1) The first meaning: that everything is created by God directly or indirectly. This is agreed upon by all theists.
2) The second meaning: that there is no agent besides God at all. Only a few theologians hold this, relying on apparent texts; the author calls it the truth (وهو الحق), but notes the proofs (توارد وتمانع) have weakness.
3) The third meaning: that God is able over every possible regardless of whether it exists or not (by His power) or exists by created power. The philosophers who deny free will do not accept this.
4) The fourth meaning: that all existents are effects of God essentially or indirectly—again agreed upon by those affirming the Necessary. The disagreement is about the manner and intermediaries.
5) The fifth meaning: that all existents (essence and attributes) occur directly by God's power and will with no other agent. This is the school of the people of truth (أهل الحق) among the theologians, though few. They adhere to proofs, the first of which is textual evidence (النصوص) that declare God as the sole creator of everything, including heavens, earth, darkness, light, death, life, substances, and accidents. The author then cites verses as examples.

Key Terms
النصوص
خالق
الجواهر
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the Quranic verses about creation do not exclude secondary causation; e.g., humans 'create' actions.
Correction: The verses explicitly say 'Creator of all things' and 'no creator besides Him', which Ash'aris interpret to mean all acts of creation are directly from God.

Checkpoint
Which verse is cited as general evidence that God is the sole creator?
A) 'All praise is due to Allah, who created the heavens and the earth'
B) 'That is Allah, your Lord; there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things'
C) 'He is the Originator of the heavens and the earth'
D) 'Blessed is He in whose hand is the dominion'

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly states God as the sole Creator of all, forming the primary textual proof for the Ash'ari doctrine of exclusive divine agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you truly feel that Allah alone created that success, or does your heart secretly attribute part of it to your own skill? What can you do today to train your soul to recognize His exclusive creation in every outcome?
?
If you truly believed that every single event—including your mistakes and hardships—is created directly by Allah with no secondary causes, would it change how you react to difficulty? Take a moment to identify one area of your life where you still act as if causes have independent power, and commit to reminding yourself of this verse when that situation arises.
?
The verse 'Creator of all things' leaves no exceptions. Is there any 'thing' in your life—a talent, a possession, a relationship—that you secretly consider 'your own creation'? How would your attitude toward it shift if you genuinely saw it as Allah's direct creation every moment?

Two Meanings of God's Comprehensive Power
Enumerates two meanings of God's power and their status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two interpretations of God's comprehensive power and the initial stance of Ahl al-Haqq.

Core Idea
All theists agree God creates, but only some say He is the sole real agent.

The author begins by clarifying that the phrase 'comprehensiveness of His power' can be understood in different ways. The first meaning—that everything is brought into existence by God directly or through intermediaries—is universally accepted among those who affirm a Creator. The second meaning—that there is no real agent besides God at all—is a stronger claim held only by some theologians, and the author declares it to be the truth, though he notes weaknesses in its standard proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's classification.
2
Identify the two meanings
The passage presents two distinct meanings of 'shumul qudratih'. Find where each begins and how the author presents them.
The first meaning is introduced with 'بمعنى أن الكل بإيجاده ابتداء أو بواسطة', the second with 'وبمعنى أنه لا مؤثر سواه أصلاً'.
3
Understand the consensus and the dispute
Note that the first meaning is undisputed; the only disagreement is in its details. The second meaning is adopted by only some theologians, and the author affirms it as the truth.
The author's phrase 'وهو الحق' signals his own position.
4
Examine the proofs mentioned
The author mentions two proofs for the second meaning: tawārud (conflict) and tamānuʿ (mutual opposition). He notes that they have weakness (ḍaʿf). Why might he still accept the position?
This shows that the truth of the meaning is not solely based on those proofs; other evidence exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما شمول قدرته شمول قدرة الله تعالى بمعنى أن الكل بإيجاده ابتداء أو بواسطة (۱) فلم يقع من القائلين بالصانع نزاع في ذلك . بل في تفاصيله وبمعنى أنه لا مؤثر سواه أصلاً فلم يذهب إليه إلا البعض من المتكلمين تمسكاً بظواهر النصوص، وهو الحق. وبدليل التوارد والتمانع، وفيهما ضعف . قال: وأما شمول قدرته ما مرّ من الاختلاف كان في شمول قدرة الله تعالى بمعنى كونه قادراً على كل ممكن سواء تعلق به القدرة والإرادة فوجد أم لا؟ فلم يوجد أصلاً، أو وجد بقدرة مخلوق، وعلى هذا لا يتأتى اختلافات والفلاسفة ومن يجري مجراهم ممن لا يقول بكونه قادراً مختاراً، وقد يفسر شمول قدرته بأن كل ما يوجد من الممكنات فهو معلول له بالذات أو بالواسطة، وهذا مما لا نزاع فيه لأحد من القائلين بوحدة الواجب . وإنما الخلاف في كيفية الاستناد ووجود الوسائط وتفاصيلها، وأن كل ممكن (۲) أي إلى أي ممكن يستند حتى ينتهي إلى الواجب، وقد تفسر شمول قدرته بأن ما الذات والصفات من الموجودات واقع بقدرته وإرادته ابتداء سوی بحيث لا مؤثر سواه، وهذا مذهب أهل الحق من المتكلمين، وقليل ما هم، وتمسكوا بوجوه .
He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the comprehensiveness of the power of Allah Almighty—in the sense that everything is by His bringing into existence either directly or through an intermediary, there has occurred no dispute among the affirmers of the Maker concerning that, but only in its details. And in the sense that there is no real agent besides Him at all, only some of the theologians have adopted it, clinging to the apparent meanings of the texts, and it is the truth, supported by the proofs of tawārud (conflict) and tamānuʿ (mutual opposition), though there is weakness in them. He said: As for the comprehensiveness of His power—the difference that passed was concerning the comprehensiveness of the power of Allah Almighty in the sense of His being powerful over every possible thing, whether power and will attach to it and it exists or not? It either does not exist at all, or exists by a created power. On this basis, differences do not arise for the philosophers and those who follow their way among those who do not say that He is a powerful free agent. And it may be interpreted that the comprehensiveness of His power means that everything that exists among possible things is caused by Him essentially or through intermediaries, and this is not disputed by anyone among those who affirm the unity of the Necessary Being. Rather, the disagreement is about the modality of dependency, the existence of intermediaries and their details, and that every possible (thing) i.e., to which possible thing it is attributed until it ends with the Necessary. And it may be interpreted that the comprehensiveness of His power means that what pertains to essence and attributes among existents occurs by His power and will directly, such that there is no real agent besides Him. This is the school of the People of Truth among the theologians, and they are few. And they adhered to proofs.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the different meanings of "shumul qudratih" (comprehensiveness of His power). The author begins with a meaning that the whole (everything) is brought into existence by Him directly or through intermediaries. He states that there is no dispute among theists that everything originates from God in this general sense; the disagreement is only in the details. Then he presents a second meaning: that there is no real agent besides Him at all (lā muʾaththir siwāhu aṣlan). This is held only by some of the theologians, clinging to the apparent meanings of the texts. The author asserts that this is the truth (al-ḥaqq), supported by the proofs of tawārud and tamānuʿ, though he notes that these proofs have weaknesses (wa fīhimā ḍaʿf). Then the author introduces a continuation: the difference that occurred about the comprehensiveness of God's power in the sense of His being powerful over every possible thing, whether the power and will attach to it and it exists or not. Some said it does not exist originally, or exists by a created power. On this basis, differences do not arise for the philosophers and those who deny that He is a free agent (qādir mukhtār). Another interpretation is that everything that exists among possibles is caused by Him essentially or through intermediaries. This is not disputed by anyone who affirms the unity of the Necessary Being (waḥdat al-wājib). The disagreement is only about the modality of dependency, the existence of intermediaries and their details. Finally, the author states that "shumul qudratih" can be interpreted as that all existents, including essences and attributes, occur by His power and will directly, such that there is no real agent besides Him. This is the madhhab of the Ahl al-Ḥaqq among the theologians, and they are few. They rely on three proofs (wujūh), which the author will present in subsequent lessons. This passage is a classification: it distinguishes between different senses of the term and clarifies which are agreed upon and which are disputed, culminating in the position of the Ashʿarī Ahl al-Ḥaqq.

Key Terms
شمول قدرته
مؤثر
توارد
تمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate among theologians is about whether God is powerful or not.
Correction: The passage shows that all theists agree God is powerful; the dispute is about whether other beings can be real agents (muʾaththir) alongside God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of God's comprehensive power is universally agreed upon among theists?
A) There is no real agent besides God at all.
B) Everything is brought into existence by God directly or through intermediaries.
C) Everything is caused by God essentially or through intermediaries.
D) God is powerful over every possible thing.

Key Takeaway
The two meanings diverge on whether secondary causes have real agency; only the second meaning removes all causal efficacy from creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty or loss, do you immediately think of seeking Allah's help alone, or do you instinctively rely on your own efforts or others? Which meaning of God's power does your reaction reflect?
?
Think of a recent success you achieved. Did you consider it as something you earned through your own ability, or did you see it as solely from Allah, with your efforts as mere means? How might adopting the second meaning change your gratitude and humility?
?
The author affirms that the second meaning (no real agent besides Allah) is the truth. But do you live as if that is true? Examine your attachments: is there a person, a skill, or a resource you trust in as if it has independent power? What would it take to transfer that trust entirely to Allah alone?

Philosophical Views and the Final Position of Ahl al-Haqq
Classifies further meanings and presents the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different interpretations of God's power in relation to philosophers and the definitive position of Ahl al-Haqq.

Core Idea
The Ahl al-Haqq affirm that only God's direct power and will bring about all existence.

This lesson continues the classification, addressing the stance of philosophers who deny that God is a free agent, and presenting two more interpretations: one about God's power over every possible thing, and another about causation through intermediaries. Finally, the passage culminates in the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq—that all existing things, including essences and attributes, are brought into existence directly by God's power and will, with no real agent besides Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the author transitions from the previous discussion to new meanings.
2
Identify the third meaning regarding possible things
The passage discusses an interpretation about God's being powerful over every possible thing, and how this relates to philosophers who do not believe God is a free agent.
Look for 'بمعنى كونه قادراً على كل ممكن'.
3
Understand the fourth meaning: causation through intermediaries
The next interpretation states that every possible thing is caused by God essentially or through intermediaries. This is agreed upon by all who affirm the unity of the Necessary Being.
The disagreement is only about the modality and details of intermediaries.
4
Learn the final position of Ahl al-Haqq
The last interpretation presented is that God's comprehensive power means all things, including essences and attributes, are directly by His power and will, with no other real agent. This is the school of Ahl al-Haqq among the theologians, who are few.
The author will present their proofs in subsequent lessons.

Source Text

?

?

?

Second Proof: Dalīl al-Tawārud (Conflict of Powers)
Explains the conflict of powers proof and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument of dalīl al-tawārud and its implications for divine and creaturely causality.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot produce one effect without logical contradiction.

This lesson explores the second proof, known as dalīl al-tawārud, which argues that if both God and a creature could produce the same act, then either preponderance without a cause or the impossible convergence of two independent causes would occur. We will examine the argument, its exception, and an objection that arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The argument presents two possibilities if both wills attach to one act: occurrence by one power or by both. Understand why each leads to a problem.
3
Understand the exception
The author mentions the motion of a body under two forces as a counterexample. Note why it does not apply here.
The key is lack of independence of each force.
4
Consider the objection
The passage ends with an objection: God's power is more complete, so the act occurs by Him alone. Think about how this objection challenges the proof.
The response to this objection is not in this passage.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this proof supports the Ash'arite position that God is the sole creator of all acts, including human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : دليل التوارد وهو أنه لو وقع شيء بقدرة الغير، وقد عرفت أنه مقدور الله تعالى أيضاً، فلو فرضنا تعلق الإرادتين به معاً فوقوعه إما بإحدى القدرتين، فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح وإما بهما فيلزم توارد العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد، لأن التقدير أن كلا منهما مستقل بالإيجاد، فلا يجوز أن تكون العلة هي المجموع، وهذا بخلاف حركة الجوهر الملتصق بكيفي جاذب ودافع، فإنه لا دليل على استقلال كل منهما بإيجاد تلك الحركة على الوجه المخصوص . نعم يرد عليه أن قدرة الله تعالى أكمل فيقع بها، وتضمحل قدرة العبد.
Second: the proof of conflict (tawārud), which is that if something were to occur by the power of another, and you have known that it is also within the power of Allah Almighty, then if we suppose the two wills are attached to it together, then its occurrence is either by one of the two powers, which necessitates preponderance without a cause, or by both, which necessitates the convergence of two independent causes upon one effect, because the assumption is that each of them is independent in bringing into existence, so it is not permissible for the cause to be the aggregate. This is unlike the motion of a body attached to two forces, attractive and repulsive, for there is no evidence for the independence of each in producing that motion in the specific manner. Yes, it can be objected that the power of Allah Almighty is more complete, so it occurs by it, and the power of the servant collapses.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, called dalīl al-tawārud (proof of conflict). The argument goes: if an event could be brought about by the power of a creature (al-ghayr) while it is also within the power of God, and we suppose both wills are directed to it, then either it occurs by one of the two powers, which would require a preponderance without a cause (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), or it occurs by both, which would require two independent causes producing one effect, which is impossible because each is supposed to be independent, so the aggregate cannot be the cause. The author gives an exception: the motion of a body subjected to two forces (attractive and repulsive) is not analogous because there is no evidence that each force independently produces the motion; rather, the combined effect is from both. Then the author mentions an objection: it can be said that God's power is more complete (akmal), so the act occurs by it and the creature's power is overridden (tadmaḥil). The lesson ends with this objection; the answer is not given here but may be addressed in the next proof.

Key Terms
دليل التوارد
ترجيح بلا مرجح
علة مستقلة
معلول واحد
قدرة أكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tawārud proof is the same as the tamānu' proof.
Correction: Tawārud deals with two wills directed to the same object, while tamānu' deals with two wills on opposites. They are distinct proofs.

Checkpoint
In the tawārud proof, what is the logical consequence if both powers produce the same effect?
A) Preponderance without a cause.
B) Two independent causes converge on one effect.
C) The effect does not occur.
D) God's power overcomes the creature's power.

Key Takeaway
The dalīl al-tawārud shows that attributing the same effect to two independent causes leads to a logical absurdity, preserving God's exclusive causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If no creature has independent power, then your successes and failures are not ultimately your own. Does your heart truly accept this, or do you still take pride in your abilities? What would change in your daily gratitude if you fully acknowledged that every outcome is directly from God alone?
?
The objection raised in the passage suggests that God's more complete power nullifies the creature's power. In your life, where are you still clinging to the illusion of your own independent power—perhaps in your career, relationships, or health? How would your reliance on God deepen if you treated every moment as if your own power were 'collapsed' before His?
?
This proof shows that attributing the same effect to two independent causes leads to absurdity. Yet we often act as if both God and we are co-creators of our actions. What specific thought pattern or habit can you change today to align your belief with the truth that God is the sole cause, and your role is only that of a servant receiving His gift?

Third Proof: The Argument of Mutual Opposition (Tamānu')
Presents the tamānu' argument with three logical branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the tamānu' argument and its three impossible consequences.

Core Idea
If a creature could create independently, it would force impossible contradictions.

The proof of mutual opposition (tamānu') is a cornerstone of Ashʿarī theology, demonstrating that only God can be the true creator. In this passage, the author presents a hypothetical scenario where a creature brings about an event while God wills the opposite. The resulting logical impossibilities show that such independent creation cannot occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three conditional scenarios presented.
The argument is structured as three 'if-then' statements.
2
Identify the three impossible outcomes
The argument lists three possibilities: both occur, neither occurs, or one occurs. Each leads to an impossibility. Write them down.
They are: conjunction of opposites, impotence of God, and preponderance without cause.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does this proof require us to deny that creatures can independently create? How does it uphold God's sole agency?
The proof relies on the impossibility of these outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : دليل التمانع ، وهو أنه لو وقع شيء بإيجاد الغير، وفرضنا تعلق قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته بضد ذلك الشيء في حال إيجاد الغير ذلك الشيء كحركة جسم وسكونه في زمان بعينه، فإن وقع الأمران جميعاً لزم اجتماع الضدين، وإن لم يقع شيء منهما لزم عجز الباري، وتخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة، وخلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون، وإن وقع أحدهما لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ، وفيه ما قد عرفت، لا يقال معنى كون قدرته أكمل أنها أشمل أي أكثر إيجاداً ولا أثر لهذا التفاوت في المقدور المخصوص بل نسبة القدرتين إليه على السواء. لأنا نقول : بل معناه أنها أقوى وأشد تأثيراً، فيترجح على قدرة العبد، ويظهر أثرها.
Third: the proof of mutual opposition (tamānuʿ), which is that if something were to occur by the bringing into existence of another, and we suppose that the power and will of Allah Almighty are attached to the opposite of that thing at the time of the other's bringing it into existence, like the motion of a body and its rest in a particular time, then if both occur, it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites; and if neither occurs, it necessitates impotence of the Creator, the failure of the effect to follow from the complete cause, and the body being devoid of motion and rest; and if one occurs, it necessitates preponderance without a cause—and you have already known what is in that. It cannot be said that the meaning of His power being more complete is that it is more inclusive, i.e., more productive, and this difference has no effect on the particular object of power; rather, the relation of the two powers to it is equal, for we say: rather, its meaning is that it is stronger and more intense in effectiveness, so it preponderates over the power of the servant, and its effect appears.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, dalīl al-tamānuʿ (proof of mutual opposition). The argument supposes that a creature brings about an event (e.g., a body's motion). If God's power and will are attached to the opposite of that event (e.g., the body's rest), then several impossible outcomes follow: if both occur, opposites coexist (impossible); if neither occurs, then God would be incapable (impossible) and the body would be without motion and rest (impossible); if one occurs, it would require preponderance without a cause (impossible). Thus, the original assumption that a creature can independently bring about an event is false. Then the author addresses an objection: one might say that the meaning of God's power being 'more complete' (akmal) is that it is more inclusive (ashmal) meaning more productive, but this difference does not affect a specific object of power—both powers have equal relation to it. The author refutes this: rather, 'more complete' means stronger and more intense in effectiveness, so it overcomes the creature's power, and its effect is manifest. This rebuttal upholds the Ashʿarī position that God alone is the real agent.

Key Terms
دليل التمانع
اجتماع الضدين
عجز الباري
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the tamānu' proof denies human free will entirely.
Correction: The proof only denies independent creation apart from God's will, not all creaturely causation.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the tamānu' proof regarding the creature's ability to create?
A) The creature can create if God does not will the opposite.
B) The creature cannot create independently because it leads to impossible outcomes.
C) The creature can create because God's power is more inclusive but not stronger.
D) The creature can create if its power is derived from God.

Key Takeaway
The tamānu' argument shows that independent creaturely causation would lead to logical absurdity, affirming that all real agency belongs to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on your own power as if it were independent, forgetting that every outcome is entirely dependent on God's will? What specific situation today can you consciously attribute solely to Him?
?
The tamānu' argument shows that if creatures had independent power, contradictions would arise. In your life, where do you experience the tension between your efforts and your trust in God's sovereignty? How can you actively surrender the illusion of your own autonomy?
?
Consider a moment when your plans were frustrated despite your best efforts. Did you recognize that frustration as a sign that God alone is the disposer of affairs? What practice can you adopt to remind yourself that no one can oppose His decree?

Refuting the Objection: God's Power is Stronger, Not Just More Inclusive
Objection and rebuttal regarding the meaning of God's complete power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the tamānu' argument and the Ashʿarī response that God's power is stronger, not merely more inclusive.

Core Idea
God's power is stronger in effectiveness, not just covering more objects.

After presenting the tamānu' proof, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps God's power being 'more complete' only means He creates more things, not that His power is more effective in a specific action. The author refutes this, clarifying that God's power is intrinsically more powerful and thus overrides any creature's attempt.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection starting with 'لا يقال'. Understand the claim that God's power being akmal means it is more inclusive (ashmal), not stronger.
The objection argues that the difference in power does not affect a specific object of power.
2
Read the rebuttal
Then read the author's response ('لأنا نقول'). Notice how he redefines 'akmal' as stronger and more intense in effect.
The rebuttal asserts that God's power is qualitatively stronger, not just quantitatively more.
3
Understand the implication
Realize that this rebuttal upholds the tamānu' argument: God's power always preponderates, so creatures cannot act independently.
Without this rebuttal, the tamānu' argument might be weakened.

Objection and Answer on Divine Power: Philosophers and Mu'tazila
Objection and answer clarifying the philosophers' and Mu'tazila's stance on divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers and Mu'tazila are counted as opponents regarding divine power, and how their affirmation differs in meaning from the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Affirming God's power in name is not the same as affirming it in reality.

The author anticipates an objection: if philosophers and Mu'tazila deny divine power, why list them as opponents? The answer reveals a crucial distinction between affirming God's power as a quality and affirming it in a sense that preserves true contingency. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how the philosophers' position reduces power and will to mere names.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...' This is a classic objection-response format.
The objector claims philosophers and Mu'tazila do not affirm power, so they shouldn't be counted.
2
Understand the key distinction: qudra vs. qadiriyya
The author clarifies that by 'power' he means 'qadiriyya' (being powerful). All parties affirm that God is powerful, but they differ on what that means. This semantic shift is crucial.
The Mu'tazila accept it; the philosophers accept it in a sense compatible with necessitation.
3
Examine the philosophical definition of 'qadir'
The passage defines a powerful agent as one from whom action may or may not issue. This possibility is power. Then it explains that action becomes necessary only when will is added. This is the core of the philosophical view.
But they reduce will to specific knowledge, undermining true contingency.
4
Note the consequences: eternality of the world and necessitarianism
Because the philosophers hold that God's attributes are not additional to His essence, they end up saying the world is eternal and God is 'mujib bi al-dhat' (necessary by essence). This contradicts true power and will.
The author's verdict: this is a claim of power and will in name only.
5
Grasp the author's verdict: nominal vs. real affirmation
The final sentence declares: 'The truth is that this is a claim of power and will in name, not in meaning.' This is the key takeaway—the philosophers' view fails to preserve genuine divine agency.
Thus, they are correctly counted as opponents despite verbal affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الفلاسفة والمعتزلة لا يقولون بالقدرة، فلا معنى لعدهم من المخالفين في شمولها . قلنا : المراد بالقدرة ها هنا القادرية، أي كونه قادراً، ولا خلاف للمعتزلة في ذلك، وكذا للفلاسفة، لكن بمعنى لا ينافي الإيجاب على ما قيل : إن القادر هو الذي أن يصدر عنه الفعل وأن لا يصدر، وهذه الصحة يصح هي القدرة، وإنما يترجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر بانضياف وجود الإرادة أو عدمها إلى القدرة، وعند اجتماعهما يجب حصول الفعل وإرادة الله تعالى علم خاص وعلمه وقدرته أزليان غير زائدين على الذات. فلهذا كان العالم قديماً. والصانع موجباً بالذات. والحق أن هذا قول بالقدرة والإرادة لفظاً لا معنى.
If it is said: The philosophers and the Mu'tazila do not affirm power (qudra), so there is no point in counting them among the opponents regarding its comprehensiveness. We say: What is meant by power here is qadiriyya (being powerful), i.e., that He is powerful. There is no disagreement from the Mu'tazila on that, nor from the philosophers, but in a sense that does not contradict necessitation (ijab), according to what has been said: The powerful (qadir) is he from whom the action may issue and may not issue, and this possibility is power. One side is only preferred over the other by the addition of the existence of will or its absence to power. When both are present, the occurrence of the action becomes necessary. And the will of God Most High is specific knowledge, and His knowledge and power are eternal and not additional to the essence. Hence the world was eternal, and the Maker is necessitating (mujib) by essence. The truth is that this is a claim of power and will in name, not in meaning.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that since the philosophers (falasifa) and the Mu'tazila do not affirm divine power (qudra) in the proper sense, they should not be counted among the opponents in a discussion about the comprehensiveness of divine power. The author responds by clarifying that what is meant by 'power' here is 'qadiriyya' (being powerful), i.e., that God is a powerful agent. The Mu'tazila undoubtedly affirm that God is powerful. The philosophers also affirm it, but in a sense that does not contradict their doctrine of divine necessitation (ijab). The author then explains the philosophical definition: a powerful agent (qadir) is one from whom both the performance and non-performance of an action can possibly issue. This possibility is what they call power. One of the two possibilities becomes preferred over the other only by the addition of will or its absence. When both power and will are present, the action necessarily occurs. The philosophers further reduce God's will to a specific knowledge, and they hold that God's knowledge and power are eternal and not additional to His essence. Consequently, they conclude that the world is eternal and the Maker is 'mujib bi al-dhat' (necessary by essence). The author's final verdict is that this is an affirmation of power and will in name only, not in true meaning (la ma'na).

Key Terms
القدرة
القادرية
الإيجاب
الإرادة
العلم
الذات
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers completely deny divine power.
Correction: The philosophers affirm divine power in a sense, but they interpret it in a way that is compatible with necessitarianism, making it a nominal affirmation that does not preserve genuine contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as described by the author, what is required for an action to necessarily occur from a powerful agent?
A) Power alone.
B) Will alone.
C) The combination of power and will.
D) The absence of any obstacle.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' and Mu'tazila's affirmation of divine power is undermined by their doctrines, making it a nominal rather than substantive belief, thus they are correctly counted as opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are there beliefs you hold 'in name only'—affirming them with your tongue while your heart and actions betray a different conviction? How does the philosophers' nominal affirmation of power mirror your own spiritual inconsistencies?
?
