Objection and Answer on the Meaning of Qudra
Objection and answer about Mu'tazila and philosophers' denial of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author addresses the objection that the Mu'tazila and philosophers deny God's power by distinguishing between qudra (power) and qadiriyya (being powerful), and why this distinction matters.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and philosophers do affirm God's power—just not in the same sense as the Ash'aris.

This passage tackles a common objection: if the Mu'tazila and philosophers deny qudra (power) in the usual Ash'ari sense, why count them as opponents? The answer hinges on a subtle distinction between qudra and qadiriyya. You'll see how the author clarifies terms and exposes the philosophical view that reduces qudra and irada to mere verbal affirmations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection and the author's initial response.
The objection claims the Mu'tazila and philosophers don't believe in qudra.
2
Understand the qudra/qadiriyya distinction
The author explains that what is meant by qudra is qadiriyya, i.e., being powerful. This is something the Mu'tazila and philosophers agree on, but in a sense that does not negate their concept of necessitation (ijab).
This distinction is key to the argument.
3
Analyze the philosophical view and conclusion
The author outlines the philosophical definition of qadir (powerful) as one from whom action may or may not issue. The actualization depends on will (irada). However, the philosophers hold that God's will is a special knowledge and His attributes are eternal and not additional to His essence, leading to the world's eternity and God as necessitating by essence. The author concludes this is just a verbal affirmation.
Consider why the author calls it 'verbal affirmation without meaning'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الفلاسفة والمعتزلة لا يقولون بالقدرة، فلا معنى لعدهم من المخالفين في شمولها . قلنا : المراد بالقدرة ها هنا القادرية (۲) ، أي كونه قادراً، ولا خلاف للمعتزلة في ذلك، وكذا للفلاسفة، لكن بمعنى لا ينافي الإيجاب على ما قيل : إن القادر هو الذي أن يصدر عنه الفعل وأن لا يصدر، وهذه الصحة يصح هي القدرة، وإنما يترجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر بانضياف وجود الإرادة أو عدمها إلى القدرة، وعند اجتماعهما يجب حصول الفعل وإرادة الله تعالى علم خاص وعلمه وقدرته أزليان غير زائدين على الذات. فلهذا كان العالم قديماً. والصانع موجباً بالذات. والحق أن هذا قول بالقدرة والإرادة لفظاً لا معنى.
If it is said: The philosophers and the Mu'tazila do not affirm qudra (power), so there is no meaning to counting them among the opponents in its inclusiveness. We say: What is meant by qudra here is qadiriyya (being powerful), i.e., His being powerful, and there is no disagreement from the Mu'tazila about that, nor from the philosophers, but in a sense that does not negate necessitation (ijab) as it is said: The powerful (al-qadir) is the one from whom the act can issue and from whom it can not issue, and this soundness — it is sound — is qudra (power). One of the two sides is only weighed against the other by the addition of the existence of irada (will) or its absence to qudra. And when both are present, the occurrence of the act is necessary. And the irada of Allah is a special knowledge, and His knowledge and power are eternal and not additional to the Essence. Hence the world is eternal, and the Maker is necessitating (mujib) by essence. The truth is that this is a verbal affirmation of qudra and irada without meaning.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer concerning the attribution of qudra (power) to God in relation to the Mu'tazila and philosophers. The objector argues that since the Mu'tazila and philosophers do not affirm qudra in the usual sense, there is no point in counting them as opponents in the scope of qudra. The author responds by clarifying that what is meant here by qudra is qadiriyya (being powerful), i.e., the attribute of being capable of action, and there is no disagreement with the Mu'tazila on this, nor with the philosophers, but in a sense that does not contradict their notion of necessitation. The author then explains the philosophical view: the powerful (al-qadir) is one from whom the act may or may not issue. The capacity to both do and not do is qudra itself. The actualization of one side over the other depends on the addition of irada (will) or its absence to qudra. When both qudra and irada exist together, the act necessarily occurs. The author notes that according to the philosophers, God's irada is a special knowledge, and His knowledge and power are eternal and not additional to the Essence, hence the world is eternal and God is necessitating (mujib) by essence. The author concludes that this is a verbal affirmation of qudra and irada without their true meaning.

القادرية
الإرادة
الموجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila and philosophers deny divine power entirely.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm God's power in a sense (qadiriyya) but interpret it in a way that leads to necessitation, not free choice.

Key Takeaway

How does today's lesson expose where you might be joining the philosophers in a 'verbal affirmation without meaning'?
?
The philosophers said God's will is just His knowledge — that He cannot will differently than He knows. Is there any area in your life where you treat God's will as if it were a rigid script, rather than a living, free choice? How would your dua change if you truly believed Allah selects freely?
?
This passage warns against affirming God's power with words while emptying it of real significance. Examine your own heart: in moments of doubt, do you instinctively revert to a mechanical, necessitarian view of the world? What would it mean for you to live out the Ash'ari conviction that Allah is truly powerful and free?

Second Proof: Divine Knowledge from Intentional Action
Sanusi presents the second proof that God is knowing because He acts with intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical connection between God's deliberate agency and His knowledge.

Core Idea
Intentional action proves that God knows what He intends.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful proof for God's attribute of knowledge. Sanusi argues that since God acts with intention and choice, He must know the object of His intention. We will unpack this simple yet profound inference, showing how it builds on the earlier proof of God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the brevity: two clauses form a complete argument.
The passage relies on a previously established premise about God's power.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'قادر', 'بالقصد والاختيار', and 'العلم بالمقصود'. Understand that each carries a specific meaning in this argument.
'بالقصد والاختيار' means acting with intention and free choice.
3
Follow the argument's logic
Trace the inference: God is powerful → He acts with intention → intention requires knowledge of the intended → therefore God knows. See how each step is necessary.
This is a deductive argument based on the nature of intentional action.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof is distinct from the first. It does not rely on the complexity of creation but on the very manner of God's acting.
The proof shows that knowledge is a necessary condition for intentional agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه قادر أي فاعل بالقصد والاختيار لما مر ، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم بالمقصود .
The second: that He is powerful, i.e., an agent with intention and choice, as has passed, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge of the intended object.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage states the second proof for God’s knowledge. It is a simple argument: God is powerful, meaning He acts with intention and choice (as previously established). Acting with intention requires that the agent know what He intends to do, because one cannot aim at something without knowing it. Therefore, since God acts deliberately, He must be knowing. This proof relies on the earlier proof of God’s power and choice.

Key Terms
قادر
بالقصد والاختيار
العلم بالمقصود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that intention requires knowledge of every minute detail of the intended object.
Correction: The proof only requires that the agent knows the general objective of the action, not all particulars. It is the intention itself that entails some knowledge, not omniscience.

Checkpoint
On what does the second proof for God's knowledge primarily rely?
A) The order of creation.
B) God's power and deliberate action.
C) The consensus of philosophers.
D) Revelation.

Key Takeaway
God's deliberate action logically entails that He knows what He does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If intentional action proves that God knows His purpose, what does your own intentional action reveal about your knowledge of your purpose in life? Are you truly intending what you claim to intend, or are you acting blindly?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you know what you intend by it? Are you satisfied with the level of knowledge you have about the meaning and purpose of your prayer, or are you content with mere habit?
?
God's knowledge is perfect and precedes His action. In contrast, how often do you act first and only later realize what you truly intended? How can you align your intentions with knowledge before you act, to be more mindful of God's presence?

The Second Proof: God's Power as Intentional Agency
Sanusi states the second proof: God is powerful as an agent by intention and choice, requiring knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the second proof for God's power defines Him as an agent by intention and choice, which necessarily implies knowledge of the intended purpose.

Core Idea
God's power is not blind force but intentional agency requiring knowledge.

Sanusi presents the second proof demonstrating God's attribute of power (qudra). He defines what it means for God to be powerful: not simply causing effects, but doing so with intention and choice. This definition immediately establishes a connection between power and knowledge, since intention presupposes knowledge of the goal. This lesson examines the concise statement of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the two key terms: 'qadir' (powerful) and 'fāʿil bi al-qasd wa al-ikhtiyār' (agent by intention and choice).
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the link to knowledge
The phrase 'lā yutāṣawwaru dhālika illā maʿa al-ʿilm bi al-maqṣūd' asserts that intentional agency is inconceivable without knowledge of the purpose. Reflect on why intention requires knowledge.
This will be important for later arguments against the design method.
3
Connect to previous proof
The phrase 'li-mā marra' refers back to the previously established attributes. Consider how this proof builds on earlier discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه قادر أي فاعل بالقصد والاختيار لما مر ، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم بالمقصود . فإن قيل : قد يصدر عن الحيوانات العجم بالقصد والاختيار أفعال محكمة، متقنة في ترتيب مساكنها، وتبرير معايشها كما النحل والعنكبوت وكثير من الوحوش والطيور على ما هو في الكتب مسطور، وفيما بين الناس مشهور مع أنها ليست من أولى العلم . قلنا: لو سلم أن موجد هذه الآثار هو هذه الحيوانات، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون فيها من العلم قدر ما تهتدي به إلى ذلك بأن يخلقها الله تعالى عالمة بذلك، أو يلهمها هذا العلم حين ذلك الفعل، ثم المحققون من المتكلمين على أن طريقة القدرة والاختيار أوكد، وأوثق من طريقة الإتقان والإحكام، لأن عليها سؤالاً صعباً، وهو أنه لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى موجداً يستند إليه تلك الأفعال المتقنة المحكمة، ويكون له العلم والقدرة، ودفعه بأن إيجاد مثل ذلك الموجود، وإيجاد العلم والقدرة فيه أيضاً فعل محكم، بل أحكم، فيكون فاعله عالماً لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه قادر مختار، إذ الإيجاب بالذات من غير قصد لا يدل على العلم، فيرجع طريق الإتقان إلى طريق القدرة مع أنه كاف في إثبات المطلوب، وقد يتمسك في كونه عالماً بالأدلة السمعية من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع .
The second: that He is powerful (qadir), i.e., an agent by intention and choice as previously mentioned, and that is not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended purpose. If it is said: Non-rational animals may produce by intention and choice precise actions, perfect in arranging their dwellings and managing their livelihoods, like bees, spiders, and many wild beasts and birds, as recorded in books and well-known among people, even though they are not possessors of knowledge. We say: Even if it is granted that the maker of these effects is these animals, then why is it not permissible that there should be in them some measure of knowledge by which they are guided to that, such that Allah creates them knowing that, or inspires them with this knowledge at the time of that action? Then the verifiers among the theologians (mutakallimun) hold that the method of power and choice is stronger and more reliable than the method of perfection and soundness (itqan wa ihkam), because upon it [the latter] there is a difficult question, namely: Why is it not permissible that the Creator (al-Bari) necessitates a being (mujib) to which those perfect, sound actions are attributed, and that being has knowledge and power? Its refutation is that the creation of such a being, and the creation of knowledge and power in it, is itself a sound action, nay more sound, so its agent being knowing cannot be completed except by demonstrating that He is powerful and choosing, because necessitation by essence without intention does not indicate knowledge. Thus the method of perfection (itqan) returns to the method of power, while it [the latter] is sufficient for establishing what is sought. And one may also rely for His being knowing on scriptural proofs from the Book, the Sunna, and consensus (ijma').
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument. First, it asserts the second attribute: God is qadir (powerful), meaning an agent by qasd (intention) and ikhtiyar (choice), which requires knowledge of the intended purpose. Then an objection is raised: non-rational animals (like bees, spiders) seem to produce precise, intentional acts without knowledge. The answer allows that they may have created knowledge or inspiration from God, so the objection does not deny divine power. Next, the author notes a methodological insight from the verifiers among the mutakallimun: the proof through power and choice is stronger and more reliable than the proof through itqan and ihkam (perfection and design). Why? Because the design method faces a difficult question: Why cannot God necessitate (yujib) a being (mujib) that would be the proximate agent of these perfect actions and possess knowledge and power? The refutation (daf') of this question is that creating such a being with knowledge and power is itself a perfect act—indeed more perfect—so to prove its creator is knowing requires first proving that creator is powerful and choosing (qadir mukhtar). Necessitation by essence (ijab bi al-dhat) without intention does not entail knowledge. Therefore, the design argument ultimately depends on the power-and-choice argument. That argument alone suffices to prove the desired conclusion (God's existence and attributes). Finally, one may also use scriptural proofs (sam'iyyat) from Quran, Sunna, and consensus to establish that God is knowing.

Key Terms
قادر
قصد
اختيار
علم بالمقصود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second proof establish about God's power?
A) It is sheer force without purpose.
B) It is agency by intention and choice, requiring knowledge.
C) It is limited to what He knows.
D) It is proven by design in nature.

Key Takeaway
God's power is intentional agency, which necessarily entails knowledge of the intended purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's power in your life, do you imagine a blind force or a deliberate, intentional will? How does that distinction affect your trust in His plan for you?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's power as intentional agency cannot exist without knowledge of the purpose. Does this change how you view the events in your life—are you willing to actively seek Allah's purpose in them, or do you often assume they are random?
?
If Allah's every act is intentional and purposeful, then nothing happens without meaning. Are you living as if you truly believe that, or do your actions sometimes show doubt? What one step will you take today to align your mindset with this reality?

Answering the Animal Behavior Objection
Presents an objection from animal behavior and provides a response preserving the link between intention and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that animals produce precise intentional actions without apparent knowledge, and how the answer resolves it without undermining divine power.

Core Idea
Animals' precise actions do not disprove divine power; they may have God-given knowledge.

A potential objection to the second proof arises from the animal kingdom. Bees, spiders, and others perform highly organized actions that seem to require intention and knowledge, yet they are not considered rational beings. The author presents this objection and offers a response: such animals may possess a measure of God-given knowledge or inspiration. This lesson unpacks the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part beginning 'Fa-in qīla'. Note the examples: bees, spiders, wild beasts, birds. Understand why this challenges the proof.
The objection claims that if intention requires knowledge, then animals must have knowledge, which is problematic if they are not 'aulī al-ʿilm'.
2
Analyze the answer
Read the answer starting 'Qulnā'. Note that even if we grant animals as agents, they could have been given knowledge by God or inspired. This preserves the link between intention and knowledge.
The answer does not deny that animals act intentionally; it provides a theistic explanation.
3
Evaluate the strength of the response
Consider whether this response fully counters the objection. The author seems to accept the premise for the sake of argument and then proposes a way that does not contradict divine power.

Source Text

The Superiority of the Power-and-Choice Argument over the Design Argument
Compares the power-and-choice method with the design method, arguing the former is foundational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun consider the argument from power and choice stronger than the design argument, and how design ultimately depends on power.

Core Idea
The design argument falls back on proving God's power and choice to avoid an infinite regress of necessitated causes.

After addressing the animal objection, Sanusi presents a methodological insight from the leading theologians (muḥaqqiqūn). They assert that the proof based on God's power and choice (qudra wa ikhtiyār) is more solid than the proof based on the perfection and design (itqān wa iḥkām) of creation. This is because the design argument faces a critical question: could not God have necessitated a being that produces the design? Answering this requires assuming that the ultimate agent is powerful and choosing. This lesson explores this subtle but important argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on methodology
Read from 'thumma al-muḥaqqiqūn' to the end. Focus on the claim that the power method is 'awkad wa awthaq' (stronger and more reliable).
This is a meta-argument about arguments.
2
Identify the difficult question
Find the question: 'why is it not permissible that the Creator necessitates a being (mujib) that has knowledge and power and to which the design is attributed?' Understand why this challenges the design argument.
The design argument cannot prove that the designer is knowing unless it first proves He is powerful and choosing.
3
Understand the refutation and reduction
The refutation shows that creating such a being is itself a perfect act, so its creator must be knowing. But this reasoning only works if the creator is already proven to be powerful and choosing. Thus, the design argument reduces to the power argument.
This shows the foundational nature of the power argument.
4
Note the scriptural backup
Finally, the author mentions that one may also use scriptural proofs (samʿiyyāt) for God's knowledge. This is a supplementary point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you more often see God's hand through the order of nature (design) or through your personal experience of His choices answering your prayers? Which one truly convinces you that He is a free agent who knows and cares for you?
?
If the design argument cannot stand without first proving that God is powerful and choosing, then what is the state of your faith? Do you assume God's knowledge from nature alone, or have you built a foundation on His revealed attributes? This lesson demands that you know why you believe what you believe.
?
Consider the possibility that you have been relying on an incomplete proof for God's knowledge. Are you willing to go deeper, to trace your convictions back to the fundamental attribute of God's free will? If not, what is holding you back from rigorous intellectual honesty in your faith?

Answering the Objection from Animal Actions
Objection and initial response about animal actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani counters the objection that animals perform well‑ordered actions without knowledge, by suggesting they may have divinely given knowledge or inspiration.

Core Idea
Animal order does not disprove divine knowledge; animals may act by inspired knowledge.

This lesson examines a common objection to the argument from design: if animals can produce intricate works without apparent knowledge, then order does not necessarily imply a knowing Creator. Taftazani provides a twofold response, beginning with the possibility that animals themselves receive knowledge from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic objection ("If it is said...") and its translation. Notice the examples: spider, wild animals, birds—all produce well‑ordered actions without being considered knowledgeable.
This objection targets the standard cosmological argument from order.
2
Understand the first response
Taftazani concedes the animals might be the originators, but asks: why not allow that God gives them a measure of knowledge or inspiration at the moment of action? This preserves the link between order and knowledge.
This response is a concession; the stronger reply comes next.
3
Reflect on the implication
Even if this reply is accepted, Taftazani is not basing the main proof on it. The real foundation is the way of power and choice. Use this lesson to see how the objection is handled without relying on the deeper argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد يصدر عن الحيوانات العجم بالقصد والاختيار أفعال محكمة، والعنكبوت وكثير من الوحوش والطيور على ما هو في الكتب مسطور ، وفيما بين الناس مشهور مع أنها ليست من أولى العلم . قلنا : لوسلّم أن موجد هذه الآثار هو هذه الحيوانات، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون فيها من العلم قدر ما تهتدي به إلى ذلك بأن يخلقها الله تعالى عالمة بذلك، أو يلهمها هذا العلم حين ذلك الفعل، ثم المحققون من المتكلمين على أن طريقة القدرة والاختيار أوكد، وأوثق من طريقة الإتقان والإحكام، لأن عليها سؤالاً صعباً، وهو أنه لم لا يجوز أن يوجب الباري تعالى موجداً يستند إليه تلك الأفعال المتقنة المحكمة، ويكون له العلم والقدرة، ودفعه بأن إيجاد مثل ذلك الموجود، وايجاد العلم والقدرة فيه أيضاً فعلاً محكماً، بل أحكم، فيكون فاعله عالماً لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه قادر مختار ، إذ الإيجاب بالذات من غير قصد لا يدل على العلم ، فيرجع طريق الإيقان إلى طريق القدرة مع أنه كاف في إثبات المطلوب، وقد يتمسك في كونه عالماً بالأدلة السمعية من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع .
If it is said: Non-speaking animals sometimes produce well-ordered actions with intention and choice, like the spider and many wild animals and birds, as is recorded in books and well-known among people, despite them not possessing knowledge. We say: Even if it is granted that the originator of these effects is these animals, why is it not possible that they have some amount of knowledge by which they are guided to that, by God creating them knowing that, or inspiring them with this knowledge at the time of that action? Then, the verifiers among the theologians hold that the way of power and choice is more firmly grounded and stronger than the way of perfection and precision, because there is a difficult question against it, namely: why is it not permissible that the Exalted Creator necessitates an originator to which those precise and well-ordered actions are attributed, and that he possesses knowledge and power? And repelling this by saying that the origination of such an existent and the origination of knowledge and power in it is also a well-ordered action, indeed more well-ordered, so its agent must be knowing, is not complete except by demonstrating that He is powerful and choosing, because necessary emanation without intention does not indicate knowledge. Thus the way of certainty returns to the way of power, and it is sufficient for proving what is sought. And one may also rely concerning His being knowing on scriptural evidences from the Book, the Sunna, and consensus.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (فإن قيل... قلنا) concerning the proof of God's knowledge from the order of the universe. The objector points out that animals, like spiders and birds, perform well-ordered and precise actions by intention and choice, yet they are not considered knowing. This seems to undermine the argument that order implies a knowing agent. The author (Taftazani) responds in several steps. First, he concedes the possibility that the animals themselves are the originators of these actions, but then suggests they may have a limited amount of knowledge from God or inspiration at the moment of action. Second, he appeals to the position of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) that the method of proving God's knowledge through His power and choice (qudra wa-ikhtiyār) is stronger than the method based on the perfection and precision (itqān wa-iḥkām) of actions. The latter method faces a difficult objection: one could claim that God necessitates an originator with knowledge and power to account for the ordered actions, but this response is incomplete unless it also proves that the agent is powerful and choosing, because necessary emanation (ījāb bi-dh-dhāt) without intention does not indicate knowledge. Thus, the sure path is to rely on the way of power and choice, which is sufficient. Finally, one may also use scriptural proofs (samʿiyyāt) from the Quran, Sunna, and consensus to establish God's knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
إتقان
إحكام
إلهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals perform skilled actions without any knowledge, proving that order does not require a knowing agent.
Correction: The passage suggests that animals may have limited knowledge from God or inspiration; thus their actions do not refute the principle that well‑ordered actions proceed from knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani first respond to the objection that animals perform well‑ordered actions without knowledge?
A) He denies that animals can perform such actions altogether.
B) He argues that animals do not act with intention or choice.
C) He suggests that animals may have been given knowledge by God or inspired at the time of action.
D) He says the argument from order is invalid regardless.

Key Takeaway
The objection from animal actions does not undermine the proof of divine knowledge because animals may act with divinely given knowledge or inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever dismiss the actions of animals as mere instinct, unaware that God may be inspiring them? How does this change how you view the creatures around you? Will you start looking for signs of divine wisdom in their behavior?
?
If even a spider acts with God's knowledge, what excuse do you have for ignoring the signs of God in your own life? What would it take for you to see God's hand in every ordered action?
?
The objector used animal actions to cast doubt on divine knowledge. In what areas of your life do you use apparent counterexamples to avoid acknowledging God's attributes? Are you willing to reconsider those exceptions today?

The Stronger Proof: Power, Choice, and Divine Knowledge
Methodological argument: power and choice as the surest proof of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifiers (muhaqqiqūn) consider the proof from God's power and choice to be more fundamental than the proof from the perfection of actions.

Core Idea
Order without intention implies nothing; only power and choice necessitate knowledge.

After addressing the animal objection, Taftazani presents the deeper methodological point. The most reliable way to prove God's knowledge is not through the mere order of created things, but through the fact that God acts with power and choice. This lesson unpacks that argument and explains why the 'way of perfection' (itqān wa-iḥkām) is insufficient on its own.

Guided Path
1
Understand the muhaqqiqūn's claim
The verifiers state that the method (tarīqa) of power and choice (qudra wa-ikhtiyār) is stronger and more firmly grounded than the method of perfection and precision (itqān wa-iḥkām). This is a key methodological shift.
Notice the comparative 'awkad wa-awthaq'.
2
Identify the problem with the perfection proof
The perfection proof faces a difficult question: one could claim that God simply 'necessitates' (yūjib) an originator with knowledge and power to produce the ordered actions. But this reply is incomplete unless it is shown that the originator is powerful and choosing.
The problem is that 'necessary emanation' (ījāb bi-dh-dhāt) without intention does not indicate knowledge.
3
See how the power/choice proof resolves it
By proving that God acts with power and choice (qādir mukhtār), we directly establish that He has knowledge, because choice requires awareness of alternatives. This method does not face the same objection.
So the 'way of certainty' returns to the way of power and choice, and it is sufficient.
4
Note the scriptural backup
Taftazani adds that one may also rely on scriptural evidences (sam‘iyyāt) from the Quran, Sunna, and consensus to prove God's knowledge. This shows the comprehensiveness of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Thus the way of certainty returns to the way of power, and it is sufficient for proving what is sought. Philosophers' Arguments for God's Knowledge
Al-Taftazani states two philosophical proofs for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two philosophical arguments used to prove that God is knowing: the immateriality argument and the principle entailment argument.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is proven by His immateriality and His role as the principle of all things.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents two concise proofs from the philosophers for God's knowledge. The first links knowledge to immateriality, and the second uses the idea that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of its effects. Let's examine each argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of the two proofs.
The passage is only two sentences, each containing a complete argument.
2
Analyze the first proof
Identify the premise: God is immaterial (mujarrad). Then the general principle: every immaterial is knowing. Conclude: God is knowing.
This is a categorical syllogism.
3
Analyze the second proof
Identify the premises: God knows His own essence; He is the principle (mabda') of all things. The principle: knowledge of the principle entails knowledge of what proceeds from it. Conclude: God knows all things.
This argument is based on causation and epistemic containment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الفلاسفة . أدلة الفلاسفة لأنه مجرد وكل مجرد عالم، ولأنه عالم بذاته وهو مبدأ للكل، والعلم بالمبدأ مستلزم للعلم بذي المبدأ .
He said: And according to the philosophers. The proofs of the philosophers: because He is immaterial (mujarrad) and every immaterial is knowing, and because He is knowing of His essence and He is the principle of all, and knowledge of the principle entails knowledge of that which proceeds from the principle.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage gives a concise summary of two arguments used by philosophers to prove that God is knowing. The first argument: God is immaterial (mujarrad), i.e., not a body or a bodily thing. Every immaterial being is inherently intellectual (ʿāqil) and thus knowing (ʿālim). The second argument: God knows His own essence, and since He is the principle (mabdaʾ) of all created things, knowledge of the principle entails knowledge of everything that originates from it. Hence God knows all things.

Key Terms

عالم
مبدأ
علم
فلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these are separate proofs for God's existence, but they specifically argue for God's knowledge, not His existence.
Correction: The arguments assume God's existence and are designed to demonstrate that He possesses knowledge, a necessary attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is God knowing?
A) Because He created everything
B) Because He is immaterial and every immaterial is knowing
C) Because He revealed scriptures
D) Because He is all-powerful

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's knowledge follows from His immaterial nature and from His being the principle of all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change if you truly believed His knowledge is as immediate as your own self-awareness? Pinpoint one habit you would stop if you fully internalized this.
?
The second proof says knowledge of the principle entails knowledge of its effects. Since God knows Himself, He knows everything about you—your struggles, your secrets. Can you accept that He knows you fully and still loves you? What area of your life are you most afraid to let Him see? Write a short prayer asking for the courage to be fully seen.

Detailed Exposition of the Immateriality-Intellect Argument
Al-Taftazani elaborates the philosophical proof that immateriality entails intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that immaterial beings necessarily engage in intellection, and how this argument grounds God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Immateriality implies intellection because an immaterial being is intrinsically intelligible.

Al-Taftazani presents the first of two philosophical proofs for God's knowledge. This argument draws on the concept of immateriality: if something is free from matter, it is automatically intelligible and thus must be actually intellected. The reasoning is subtle but foundational for understanding how theologians and philosophers conceive of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the dense philosophical vocabulary.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms mujarrad (immaterial), ʿāqil (intellectual), taʿaqqul (intellection), ʿuqūliyya (intelligibility), and muṣāḥaba (association). These are central.
3
Follow the logical argument
The argument proceeds: (1) God is immaterial. (2) Every immaterial is intellectual. (3) Reason: immateriality means free from matter and accidents, so no action needed to become intelligible. (4) If not intellected, it's due to the intellective power, not the immaterial itself. (5) Possibility of being intelligible implies possibility of association. (6) That possibility does not depend on the immaterial being in the intellect, because that would make possibility depend on posterior existence. Hence, actual intellection must occur.
This is a modal argument about possibility and actuality.
4
Understand the impossibility of dependency
The critical move: if the possibility of association depended on the immaterial object actually being in the intellect, then possibility would be conditioned upon actuality, which is impossible. This forces the conclusion that the immaterial must already be intellected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الفلاسفة . أورد من استدلالهم على علم الباري وجهان : الأول : أنه مجرد أي ليس بجسم ولا جسماني لما مرّ، وكل مجرد عاقل، أي عالم بالكليات لما وقعت الإشارة إليه في مباحث المجردات من أن التجرد يستلزم التعقل وبيانه أن التعقل يستلزم إمكان العقولية لأن المجرد بريء عن الشوائب المادية، واللواحق الغريبة وكل ما هو كذلك لا يحتاج إلى عمل يعمل به حتى يصير معقولاً. فإن لم يعقل، كان كذلك من جهة القوة العاقلة لا من جهته، وإمكان المعقولية يستلزم إمكان المصاحبة بينه وبين العاقل إياه، وهذا الإمكان لا يتوقف على حصول المجرد في جوهر العاقل، لأن حصوله فيه نفس المصاحبة، فتوقف إمكان المصاحبة على حصول المجرد فيه توقف إمكان الشيء على وجوده المتأخر عنه، وهو محال .
He said: And according to the philosophers. He adduced from their proofs for the knowledge of the Creator two aspects: The first: that He is immaterial (mujarrad), i.e., not a body nor a bodily thing, as previously mentioned, and every immaterial is intellectual (ʿāqil), i.e., knowing of universals, as was indicated in the investigations of immaterial things that immateriality entails intellection. Its explanation: that intellection entails the possibility of being intelligible (imkān al-ʿuqūliyya), because the immaterial is free from material impurities and extraneous accidents, and everything so characterized does not need an action performed upon it in order to become intelligible. If it is not intellected, that would be due to the intellective power, not due to itself. And the possibility of being intelligible entails the possibility of association (muṣāḥaba) between it and the intellect that intellection it. And this possibility does not depend on the occurrence of the immaterial in the substance of the intellect, because its occurrence in it is the association itself. Thus, making the possibility of association dependent on the occurrence of the immaterial in it would be making the possibility of a thing dependent on its posterior existence, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the philosophical argument that immateriality entails intellection. The author states that the philosophers have two proofs for God's knowledge; the first is elaborated here. The argument: God is immaterial (not a body or bodily), and every immaterial is intellectual (knowing universals). The reason is that immateriality implies being free from material impurities and extraneous attributes. Something so characterized does not require any external action to become intelligible; it is intrinsically intelligible. If such a being is not actually intellected, that must be due to the intellective power (the subject's deficiency), not due to the immaterial object itself. Furthermore, the possibility of being intelligible implies the possibility of association between the immaterial object and an intellecting subject. This possibility does not depend on the object actually being present in the intellect's substance, because presence in the intellect is the association itself. Therefore, to make the possibility of association depend on the object being in the intellect would be to make possibility depend on a later actuality, which is impossible. Hence, the immaterial being must be actually intellected.

Key Terms
مجرد
عاقل
تعقل
عقولية
مصاحبة
قوة عاقلة
مباحث المجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves God's existence.
Correction: The argument does not prove God's existence; it assumes God's immateriality and proves that He necessarily has knowledge (intellection).

Checkpoint
According to the detailed argument, why does an immaterial being not need an external action to become intelligible?
A) Because it is inherently free from material impurities and accidents
B) Because it is created by God
C) Because it is part of the intellect itself
D) Because it is a physical object

Key Takeaway
Immaterial beings are inherently intelligible and therefore necessarily engaged in intellection, forming the basis for the attribute of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is immaterial and therefore intrinsically known by God, why do you live as if your thoughts and actions are hidden from Him? How would your day change if you truly believed that every moment is exposed to an all-knowing Creator?
?
The argument shows that immaterial beings are necessarily intellectual. You possess an immaterial soul—are you actively fulfilling its intellectual nature by seeking knowledge of God and His attributes, or do you let it remain dormant? What one concrete step will you take today to exercise your soul's intellect?
?
The philosophers claim immateriality entails intellection, yet you may find your own mind distracted and forgetful. Does that disprove the argument, or does it reveal a deficiency in your own intellective power? What veils of preoccupation or sin cloud your intellect from grasping divine truths, and how will you remove them?

The Philosophers' First Proof for God's Knowledge from Immateriality
The author presents and critiques the philosophers' first argument for God's knowledge based on immateriality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God's immateriality necessitates His knowledge of universals, including the chain of premises and the author's critical evaluation.

Core Idea
Immateriality alone does not prove knowledge; the argument relies on debatable premises.

This passage presents the first argument used by Muslim philosophers to prove that God knows universals. Their reasoning connects immateriality to intellectuality through a series of logical steps. As you read, pay attention to how each premise builds on the previous one, and notice where the author hints at a weakness in the overall proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage first states that God is immaterial (mujarrad), then claims that every immaterial is intelligent (cāqil). Find these two claims in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning for the second claim
The author explains why immateriality necessitates intellection: the immaterial is free from material impurities, so it doesn't need an external act to become intelligible. Trace this reasoning step by step.
Look for the terms 'imkan al-ma'quliyya' (possibility of intellectuality) and 'musahaba' (association).
4
Examine the critique
The author concludes by noting the weakness of some premises. Identify which part of the argument he questions and why.
The critique suggests that if the association in existence counts as intellection, the proof could be simplified.
5
Reflect on the overall strength of the proof
Consider whether you find the argument convincing. What are the key assumptions that might be contested?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعند الفلاسفة . أورد من استدلالهم على علم الباري وجهان : الأول : أنه مجرد أي ليس بجسم ولا جسماني لما مرّ، وكل مجرد عاقل، أي عالم بالكليات لما وقعت الإشارة إليه في مباحث المجردات من أن التجرد يستلزم التعقل وبيانه أن التعقل يستلزم إمكان العقولية لأن المجرد بريء عن الشوائب المادية، واللواحق الغريبة وكل ما هو كذلك لا يحتاج إلى عمل يعمل به حتى يصير معقولاً. فإن لم يعقل، كان كذلك من جهة القوة العاقلة لا من جهته، وإمكان المعقولية يستلزم إمكان المصاحبة بينه وبين العاقل إياه، وهذا الإمكان لا يتوقف على حصول المجرد في جوهر العاقل، لأن حصوله فيه نفس المصاحبة، فتوقف إمكان المصاحبة على حصول المجرد فيه توقف إمكان الشيء على وجوده المتأخر عنه، وهو محال . إذ المجرد سواء وجد في العقل أو في الخارج يلزمه إمكان مصاحبة المعقول، ولا معنى للتعقل إلا المصاحبة ، فإذا كل مجرد يصح أن يعقل غيره، وكل ما يصح للمجرد يجب أن يكون بالفعل لبراءته عن أن يحدث فيه ما هو بالقوة، لأن ذلك شأن الماديات، ولا خفاء في ضعف بعض هذه المقدمات وفيه أنه لو صح أن مصاحبة المجرد للمعقولات في الوجود تعقل لها ، لكفى ذلك في إثبات المطلوب من غير احتياج إلى سائر المقدمات .
He said: And according to the philosophers. He presented from their inference for the knowledge of the Creator two aspects: The first: that He is immaterial, i.e., not a body nor corporeal as has passed, and every immaterial is intelligent, i.e., knowing universals, due to what was indicated in the investigations of immaterial things that immateriality necessitates intellection. Its explanation is that intellection necessitates the possibility of intellectuality because the immaterial is free from material impurities and extraneous accidents, and everything that is such does not need an act performed on it until it becomes intelligible. So if it does not know, it is like that from the aspect of the intellective power, not from itself. And the possibility of intellectuality necessitates the possibility of association between it and the intellective agent. And this possibility does not depend on the occurrence of the immaterial in the substance of the intellect, because its occurrence there is the association itself. So making the possibility of association dependent on the occurrence of the immaterial in it is making the possibility of a thing dependent on its existence posterior to it, which is impossible. For the immaterial, whether it exists in the intellect or externally, has the possibility of association with the intelligible, and there is no meaning of intellection except association. Therefore every immaterial can know other than itself, and everything that is possible for the immaterial must be actual, because of its freedom from having something potential occur in it, for that is the nature of material things. And there is no concealment of the weakness of some of these premises, and in it that if the association of the immaterial with intelligibles in existence counts as intellection for it, then that would suffice in proving the desired without needing the rest of the premises.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critique of the first argument used by the philosophers (falasifa) to prove God's knowledge. The author begins by attributing the argument to the philosophers. The first argument (al-awwal) states that God is immaterial (mujarrad), meaning not a body or corporeal, as established earlier. The key premise is: every immaterial is intelligent ('aaqil), i.e., knows universals. The author explains the reasoning in detail: immateriality (tajarrud) necessitates intellection (ta'aqqul) because the immaterial is free from material impurities and accidents. Such a being does not need an external act to become an intelligible; if it does not know, the deficiency must come from the intellective power, not from the immaterial itself. The possibility of being an intelligible (imkan al-ma'quliyya) implies the possibility of association (musahaba) between the immaterial and the intellective agent. This possibility does not depend on the immaterial existing in the intellect as a separate entity, because its existence there is precisely the association; to say otherwise would involve circularity. The author then argues that the immaterial, whether in the mind or externally, necessarily has the possibility of association with the intelligible, and intellection is nothing but that association. Therefore, every immaterial can know others, and what is possible for the immaterial must be actual, because it is free from potentiality, which is characteristic of material things. The author concludes by critiquing the argument: he notes the weakness of some premises and suggests that if the association of the immaterial with intelligibles in external existence counts as intellection, then that would prove the desired conclusion without needing the rest of the premises. Thus, the passage presents the philosophers' proof and then evaluates it.

Correction: The argument involves several steps, including the possibility of association and the necessity of actualization, each of which can be questioned.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first proof for God's knowledge is a logical deduction from immateriality, but its reliance on disputed premises makes it vulnerable to critique.

The Second Proof: Knowledge from Self-Knowledge
Argument from self-knowledge, objection, and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second argument for God's knowledge from His self-knowledge, the objection that not all concomitants are evident, and the answer that God's knowledge is complete.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, but only if it is complete knowledge.

The philosophers argue that because God knows His own essence, He must also know all things that proceed from it. This raises a key question: does knowing something automatically mean knowing everything that follows from it? An objection about non-evident concomitants and the distinction between partial and complete knowledge provides a powerful answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the self-knowledge argument
Read the passage carefully. The philosophers claim God knows His essence because His essence is present to itself. Then they extend this to knowledge of all possible things.
The argument relies on the principle that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect.
2
Identify the objection
The objection uses the example of a triangle: knowing the triangle's essence does not automatically give knowledge that its angles sum to two right angles. This challenges the claim that knowing the essence automatically yields knowledge of all concomitants.
Not all concomitants are evident (lazim bayyin).
3
Learn the answer: complete knowledge
The answer distinguishes between partial and complete knowledge. God's knowledge is complete (ilm tamm), meaning He knows each thing as it is in itself, including all its properties. Therefore, the objection does not apply.
Complete knowledge includes all logical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه عالم بذاته لأنه لا معنى لتعقل المجرد ذاته، سوى حضور ذاته عند ذاته، بمعنى عدم غيبته عنه لاستحالة حصول المثال لكونه اجتماعاً للمثلين، وهذا القدر وإن كان مبنياً على أصولهم، كان في إثبات كونه عالماً في الجملة. إلا أنهم حالوا إثبات علمه بما سواه. فقالوا : هو عالم بذاته الذي هو مبدأ الممكنات لما ذكرنا . والعلم بالمبدأ أعني العلة . عالم بذي المبدأ أعني المعلول لأن العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بلوازمه ، والعلية وهي التي لا تعقل بدون المعلول، بل المعلول نفسه، وما يتبعه من المعلولات كلها من لوازم الذات . واعترض بأن لازم الذات، وإن كان بلا وسط في الثبوت من لا يجب أن يكون لازماً بيناً يلزم من تعقل الذات تعقله لتساوي الزوايا الثلاث للقائمتين للمثلث، ولو ذلك لزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بجميع لوازمه القريبة والبعيدة لاستمراره وجب الاندفاع من لازم إلى لازم. وأجيب بأن الكلام في العلم التام أعني العلم بالشيء بماله في نفسه، ولا شك أن علم الباري بذلك كذلك.
The Second: that He is knowing of His essence, because there is no meaning for the intellection of the immaterial of its essence except the presence of its essence to itself, in the sense of its not being absent from it, due to the impossibility of obtaining a form because it would be a meeting of two likes. And this much, although it is based on their principles, was sufficient to prove that He is knowing in general. However, they attempted to prove His knowledge of what is other than Himself. So they said: He is knowing of His essence, which is the principle of possible things, as we mentioned. And knowledge of the principle, meaning the cause, is knowledge of the possessor of the principle, meaning the effect, because knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its concomitants. And causality, which is not intelligible without the effect, rather the effect itself and what follows it from all effects are from the concomitants of the essence. And it was objected that the concomitant of the essence, even if it is without an intermediary in existence, it is not necessary that it be an evident concomitant that follows from intellection of the essence, like the equality of the three angles to two right angles for the triangle. And if that were the case, then knowledge of a thing would necessitate knowledge of all its near and far concomitants, due to its continuity it would require passing from one concomitant to another. And it was answered that the discussion concerns complete knowledge, i.e., knowledge of a thing as it is in itself, and there is no doubt that the knowledge of the Creator is like that.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the philosophers for God's knowledge, followed by an objection and its answer. The second argument (al-thani) states that God knows His essence. The explanation is that for an immaterial being, self-knowledge is nothing but the presence of its essence to itself, meaning it is not absent from itself, because forming a mental image of itself would involve two identical forms, which is impossible. This, based on their principles, proves that He is knowing in general (fi al-jumla). The philosophers then attempt to prove His knowledge of things other than Himself. They argue: He knows His essence, which is the principle (mabda') of all possible things. Knowledge of the cause (al-illa) entails knowledge of the effect (al-ma'lul), because knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its necessary concomitants (lawazim). Causality ('illiyya) is not intelligible without the effect; the effect itself and all subsequent effects are among the concomitants of the essence. Thus, knowing the essence gives knowledge of everything that proceeds from it. An objection is then raised: a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence, even if it is co-existent without intermediary, is not necessarily an evident concomitant (lazim bayyin) that is known immediately upon intellection of the essence. For example, knowing the essence of a triangle does not automatically give knowledge that its angles equal two right angles; that requires additional reasoning. If every concomitant were immediately known, then knowledge of one thing would lead to knowledge of all its near and far concomitants, leading to an infinite regress. The answer is given: the discussion concerns complete knowledge (al-ilm al-tamm), which is knowledge of a thing as it is in itself (bi ma lahu fi nafsihi), including all its attributes and relations. There is no doubt that God's knowledge is complete in this sense, so the objection does not apply.

Key Terms
dhat
mabda'
illa
ma'lul
lazim
ilm tamm
mithal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any concomitant of the essence is automatically known when the essence is known, without qualification.
Correction: Only complete knowledge includes all concomitants; partial knowledge may not.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what type of knowledge does God have that makes the objection irrelevant?
A) Potential knowledge
B) Complete knowledge (al-ilm al-tamm)
C) Partial knowledge
D) Acquired knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of His essence is complete, so it necessarily includes knowledge of all effects that proceed from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's complete knowledge includes every detail of your life—your thoughts, actions, and even your secret desires—does that make you feel exposed or secure? How should this reality change the way you live today?
?
The philosophers distinguished partial knowledge from complete knowledge. In what areas of your life are you content with partial knowledge of God's attributes, when He has given you the means to seek complete understanding? What step will you take to move from partial to deeper knowledge?
?
Knowing that God's knowledge is complete, what are the implications for your prayers and supplications? Are you comfortable with the fact that He already knows every need you will ever have? How does that shape your trust in His response?

Proof of Divine Self-Knowledge and the Objection of Concomitants
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof that God knows His essence and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that God's knowledge of His essence entails knowledge of all things, and how the objection regarding necessary concomitants is answered by the concept of complete knowledge.

Core Idea
God's complete knowledge of His essence includes knowledge of all its necessary concomitants.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a second proof for divine self-knowledge and extends it to God's knowledge of creation. An objection challenges whether knowledge of the essence automatically yields knowledge of all its concomitants. The resolution introduces a key distinction: complete knowledge (al-ilm al-tamm) encompasses all aspects without regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow. Note the three main parts: proof, objection, and answer.
Pay attention to technical terms like 'lazim' (concomitant) and 'ilm tamm' (complete knowledge).
2
Understand the proof of self-knowledge
The proof states that for an immaterial being, intellection is simply the presence of the essence to itself. This establishes that God knows His own essence.
This is based on the principle that an immaterial being cannot have an image of itself, so knowledge is direct presence.
3
See how knowledge extends to other things
The text explains that because God knows His essence, which is the principle (cause) of all possible things, He thereby knows all effects as concomitants of His essence.
The key link: knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of its necessary concomitants, including effects.
4
Analyze the objection
The objection argues that not all necessary concomitants are manifest (bayyin) such that knowing the essence automatically yields knowledge of them. The example is the triangle's angles summing to 180 degrees.
If any concomitant were known automatically, then one would have to traverse an infinite chain of concomitants, leading to regress.
5
Grasp the answer: complete knowledge
The answer is that divine knowledge is complete (ilm tamm)—knowledge of a thing as it is in itself, which includes all its aspects and concomitants. Thus no regress occurs.
Complete knowledge is exhaustive, so it does not require a step-by-step progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه عالم بذاته لأنه لا معنى لتعقل المجرد ذاته، سوى حضور ذاته عند ذاته، بمعنى عدم غيبته عنه لاستحالة حصول المثال لكونه اجتماعاً للمثلين، وهذا القدر وإن كان مبنياً على أصولهم، كان في إثبات كونه عالماً في الجملة. إلا أنهم حالوا إثبات علمه بما سواه. فقالوا : هو عالم بذاته الذي هو مبدأ الممكنات لما ذكرنا . والعلم بالمبدأ أعني العلة . عالم بذي المبدأ أعني المعلول لأن العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بلوازمه ، والعلية وهي التي لا تعقل بدون المعلول، بل المعلول نفسه، وما يتبعه من المعلولات كلها من لوازم الذات . واعترض بأن لازم الذات، وإن كان بلا وسط في الثبوت من لا يجب أن يكون لازماً بيناً يلزم من تعقل الذات تعقله لتساوي الزوايا الثلاث للقائمتين للمثلث، ولو ذلك لزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بجميع لوازمه القريبة والبعيدة لاستمراره وجب الاندفاع من لازم إلى لازم. وأجيب بأن الكلام في العلم التام أعني العلم بالشيء بماله في نفسه، ولا شك أن علم الباري بذلك كذلك.
The second: that He is knowing by His essence, because there is no meaning for the intellection of an immaterial being of its essence except the presence of its essence to itself, meaning that it is not absent from itself, due to the impossibility of acquiring an image since that would be a conjunction of two similars. And this much, although based on their principles, suffices to prove that He is knowing in general. However, they sought to prove His knowledge of what is other than Him. So they said: He is knowing by His essence, which is the principle of possible things as we mentioned. And knowledge of the principle, meaning the cause, is knowledge of that which has the principle, meaning the effect, because knowledge of a thing necessitates knowledge of its concomitants. And causality is that which is not intelligible without the effect, rather the effect itself and all that follows from the effects are from the concomitants of the essence. And it was objected that a concomitant of the essence, even if it is without mediation in subsistence, it is not necessary that it be a manifest concomitant such that from intellection of the essence its intellection necessarily follows, like the equality of the three angles to two right angles for the triangle; and if that were the case, then knowledge of a thing would necessitate knowledge of all its proximate and remote concomitants, necessitating a continuous progression from one concomitant to another. And it was answered that the discussion concerns complete knowledge, meaning knowledge of a thing as it is in itself, and there is no doubt that the knowledge of the Creator is like that.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that the Necessary Being knows His own essence, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof states that for an immaterial being (mujarrad), 'intellection' (ta'aqqul) of its essence simply means the presence of its essence to itself, without any image, since an image would involve two similar things. This suffices to prove that He is knowing. Then the text explains that they sought to prove His knowledge of other things: because He knows His essence which is the principle (mabda') of possible things, and knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, since causality is not intelligible without the effect. Thus He knows all effects as concomitants of His essence. An objection is raised: a concomitant of the essence, even if it necessarily subsists with the essence, may not be a manifest (bayyin) concomitant such that knowing the essence automatically yields knowledge of it. The example is the equality of triangle angles to two right angles—knowing the triangle does not automatically give this knowledge. If it did, then one would have to know all concomitants, leading to an infinite regress. The answer is that the discussion concerns 'complete knowledge' (al-'ilm al-tamm), which is knowledge of a thing as it is in itself, and God's knowledge is of that kind—it includes all aspects, so no regress occurs.

Refuting the First Argument Against Divine Self-Knowledge
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the first argument denying God's knowledge of His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against God's knowledge of His essence and how it is refuted through the concept of conceptual distinctness (taghayur i'tibari).

Core Idea
Relations in God are conceptual, not real.

Al-Taftazani examines an objection that knowledge requires duality, which would compromise divine simplicity. The objection seems powerful, but the answer shows that knowledge of self involves only a conceptual distinction, not a real multiplicity. This lesson unpacks that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage noting the structure: an objection then an answer. Try to understand the objection's reasoning before looking at the answer.
The objection claims knowledge requires duality.
2
Understand the objection's two points
The objection has two parts: knowledge as relation leads to multiplicity in the essence, and also implies God is both receptive and active.
Receptivity and activity seem incompatible.
3
Examine the refutation's core concept
The answer introduces 'conceptual distinctness' (taghayur i'tibari) as a key idea. See how it defends divine simplicity while allowing relations.
Think of our self-knowledge: we know ourselves without being two.
4
Apply the analogy to God
Consider how the human self-knowledge analogy shows that a single entity can be both known and knower by different considerations.
What does this tell us about divine attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقيل لا يعلم ذاته . لأن العلم إضافة أو صفة ذات إضافة فلا بد من الاثنينية، ولا عبرة لإفضائه إلى كثرة في الذات وأيضاً يلزم كون الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً. وأجيب بأن تغاير الاعتبار كاف كما في علمنا بأنفسنا ولا استحالة في كثرة الإضافات. وفي القابلية مع الفاعلية.
He said: And it was said that He does not know His essence, because knowledge is a relation or an attribute possessing relation, so duality is necessary, and no consideration is given to its leading to multiplicity in the essence; also it necessitates that the One be both receptive and active. And it was answered that conceptual distinctness is sufficient, as in our knowledge of ourselves, and there is no impossibility in a multiplicity of relations, nor in receptivity together with activity.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage contains a brief objection to God's knowledge of His own essence, followed by a refutation. The objection argues that knowledge is either a relation (idafa) or an attribute that entails a relation, so it requires a duality (ithnayniyya) between knower and known. This would introduce multiplicity in the divine essence and also require God to be both receptive (qabil, receiving the form of knowledge) and active (fa'il, producing knowledge). The answer is that the distinction is merely conceptual (taghayur i'tibari), as in our own self-knowledge where the knower and known are the same entity but distinguished by consideration. Hence, a multiplicity of relations does not imply real multiplicity in God, and being both receptive and active is not impossible when the actions and receptions are relative to different aspects.

Correction: Relations can be merely conceptual (taghayur i'tibari) and do not entail real multiplicity.

C) By appealing to conceptual distinctness (taghayur i'tibari) as in self-knowledge
D) By denying that God can be both receptive and active

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of Himself involves only a conceptual distinction, preserving divine simplicity and unity.

The Second Argument Against Divine Self-Knowledge and Its Partial Answer
Al-Taftazani presents a two-part argument against God's self-knowledge and answers the first part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-aspect argument against God's self-knowledge and the response that conceptual distinctness suffices for the relational aspect.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge is attacked via duality and multiplicity, but defended by conceptual distinctness.

This passage presents a sophisticated challenge to the doctrine of God's knowledge of His own essence. The argument is divided into two main faces: the first targets the relational nature of knowledge, the second argues that knowledge is distinct from the essence, leading to an absurdity. Al-Taftazani then provides a response to the first face, relying on the notion of conceptual distinctness. Let's examine each part carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two-fold structure
The argument has two main faces (wajhan). The first face itself contains two sub-arguments. The second face is a separate point. Notice how the text labels them: 'أحدهما' and 'وثانيهما'.
The first face is about the nature of knowledge; the second about its distinctness.
2
Examine the first face's sub-arguments
First sub-argument: knowledge as relation or relational attribute necessitates duality and distinction between knower and known, impossible in the truly One. Second sub-argument: knowledge as form equaling the known; different known objects require different forms, leading to multiplicity in the essence.
The second sub-argument uses the idea that knowledge of one thing can be separate from another.
3
Study the second face
The second face argues that knowledge is distinct from the essence (based on earlier proofs), so it must be a possible accident caused by the essence. But then the essence would be both receptive (qabil) and active (fa'il), which is impossible.
This is a strong a priori argument against divine self-knowledge.
4
Analyze the response to the first face
Al-Taftazani answers the first face by conceding that if knowledge is a relational attribute, it does require distinctness, but this distinctness can be conceptual (i'tibari) rather than real, as in human self-knowledge.
The response is partial; the text cuts off before completing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إنه لا يعلم ذاته القائلون بأنه ليس بعالم أصلاً تمسكوا بوجهين : أحدهما : أنه لا يصح علمه بذاته ولا بغيره أما الأول فلأن العلم إضافة أو صفة ذات إضافة، وأياً ما كان يقتضي اثنينية وتغايراً بين العالم والمعلوم، فلا يعقل في الواحد الحقيقي. وأما الثاني : فلأنه يوجب كثرة في الذات الأحدى من كل وجه لأن العلم بإحدى المعلومين غير العلم بالآخر للقطع بجواز العلم بهذا، مع الذهول عن الآخر، ولأن العلم صورة مساوية للمعلوم، مرتسمة في العالم ، أو نفس الارتسام ولا خفاء في أن صور الأشياء المختلفة مختلفة، فيلزم بحسب كثرة المعلومات، كثرة الصور في الذات . وثانيهما : أن العلم مغاير للذات لما سبق من الأدلة، فيكون ممكناً معلولاً له ضرورة امتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته، وكمالاته إلى الغير، فيلزم كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلاً وهو محال . وأجيب عن الوجه الأول أولاً بعد تسليم لزوم التغاير على تقدير كون العلم صفة ذات إضافة، بأن تغاير الاعتبار كاف كما في علمنا بأنفسنا على ما سبق في بحث العلم، لا يقال التغاير الاعتباري، إنما هو بالمعلولية والمعلومية، وهو فرع حصول
It was said: He does not know His essence. Those who say that He is not knowing at all adhered to two aspects: The first: that His knowledge of His essence nor of other than Him is valid. As for the first (reason), because knowledge is a relation or an attribute possessing relation, and in either case it necessitates duality and distinction between the knower and the known, so it is not intelligible in the truly One. As for the second (reason), because it necessitates multiplicity in the unique essence from every aspect, because the knowledge of one of the two known things is other than the knowledge of the other, due to the certain possibility of knowing one while being heedless of the other, and because knowledge is a form equaling the known, impressed in the knower, or the very impression, and it is not hidden that the forms of different things are different, so according to the multiplicity of known things, multiplicity of forms in the essence follows. And the second (aspect): that knowledge is distinct from the essence due to the previous proofs, so it would be possible and caused by it, due to the impossibility of the Necessary having need of others for His attributes and perfections, so it necessitates that the thing be both receptive and active, which is impossible. And it was answered concerning the first aspect, after conceding the necessity of distinctness on the assumption that knowledge is a relational attribute, that conceptual distinctness is sufficient, as in our knowledge of ourselves as previously mentioned in the discussion of knowledge; it cannot be said that conceptual distinctness is only in terms of effect and being known, and that is subsequent to the occurrence
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage contains a more elaborate argument against God knowing His own essence, presented in two aspects (wajhan). The first aspect itself has two parts: first, that knowledge as a relation requires duality between knower and known, impossible in the truly One. Second, that it leads to multiplicity in the essence because different known objects require different knowledge forms, so multiple forms would inhere in the essence. The second aspect argues that knowledge is distinct from the essence (as earlier proofs showed), hence it would be a possible accident caused by the essence, but that would make the essence both receptive and active, which is impossible for the Necessary. Then the answer is given only for the first aspect: after conceding that on the assumption of knowledge being a relational attribute, conceptual distinctness (taghayur i'tibari) suffices, as in human self-knowledge. The text ends mid-sentence with 'وهو فرع حصول', indicating that the answer continues but is cut off in the OCR window. This answer mirrors the previous one but adds that conceptual distinctness is not just a matter of effect and being known, which depends on occurrence.

Misconception: One might think the argument is simply that knowledge requires an object, but the text shows it's about the relational or formal nature leading to duality/multiplicity.
Correction: The argument is more subtle: knowledge as relation implies duality; knowledge as form implies multiplicity. The response uses conceptual distinctness to avoid both.

Checkpoint

A) Because knowledge requires a subject and object
B) Because different known objects would require different forms, leading to multiple forms in the essence
C) Because knowledge is an accident
D) Because God cannot be both knower and known

Key Takeaway
Arguments against divine self-knowledge often hinge on the relational or formal nature of knowledge, but kalam scholars defend it by appealing to conceptual distinctness rather than real multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you willing to let go of every human mental model you have of God, even if it feels like losing your grip on Him? What specific assumption about God are you most attached to, and can you surrender it today?
?

Objection and Response on Divine Self-Knowledge
Objection and response on divine self-knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God cannot know His own essence because knowledge requires duality, and the response that conceptual distinction suffices without real multiplicity.

Core Idea
God's self-knowledge requires no real duality, only a conceptual distinction.

This passage presents a key objection from those who argue that divine self-knowledge is impossible, and the classical theological response. Understanding this exchange clarifies how Islamic theologians reconcile divine simplicity with God's knowledge of Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. The objection states that knowledge, being a relation or attribute requiring relation, necessitates duality between knower and known, which is impossible for the truly One.
Pay attention to the two separate arguments within the objection.
2
Identify the two arguments against divine self-knowledge
The objection presents two reasons: (1) knowledge requires a relation, implying a duality, (2) the One would have to be both a passive recipient and an active agent, which is problematic.
The second argument is introduced by 'and also' (wa-ayḍan).
3
Examine the response's two parts
The response counters with two points: (1) diversity of consideration (taghayyur al-iʿtibār) is sufficient to avoid real duality, as in human self-knowledge; (2) there is no impossibility in the multiplicity of relations or in being both recipient and agent in different respects.
The response uses the example of our knowledge of ourselves.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends divine simplicity. The key is that conceptual distinctions do not entail real divisions in God's essence.
This is a standard kalam move to reconcile attributes with tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة القائلين بأن الباري لا يعلم ذاته قال : وقيل لا يعلم ذاته . لأن العلم إضافة أو صفة ذات (۱) إضافة فلا بد من الاثنينية، ولا عبرة لإفضائه إلى كثرة في الذات وأيضاً يلزم كون الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً. وأجيب بأن تغاير الاعتبار كاف كما في علمنا بأنفسنا ولا استحالة في كثرة الإضافات. وفي القابلية مع الفاعلية) .
Evidence of those who say that the Eternal does not know His essence. He said: And it was said that He does not know His essence. Because knowledge is a relation or an attribute inherently requiring relation, so duality is necessary, and no consideration is given to its leading to multiplicity in the essence. And also it necessitates that the one be both recipient and agent. And it was answered that the diversity of consideration is sufficient, as in our knowledge of ourselves, and there is no impossibility in the multiplicity of relations, and in the recipient with the agent).
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading introducing the evidence of those who deny that God knows His own essence. It then presents an initial objection: knowledge, being a relation or an attribute that inherently requires a relation, necessitates a duality between knower and known, which is impossible for the truly One. It also claims that this would lead to multiplicity in the divine essence and would require the One to be both a passive recipient (qābil) and an active agent (fāʿil), which is deemed impossible. The answer (mujīb) is given in two parts: first, the diversity of consideration (taghayyur al-iʿtibār) is sufficient to avoid literal duality, as in human self-knowledge where the self knows itself without actual separation; second, there is no impossibility in the multiplicity of relations or in the same thing being considered both recipient and agent in different respects. The argument thus establishes that divine self-knowledge does not imply real division in the essence.

?
