The Proof from Duality and Multiplicity
First proof based on duality and multiplicity of forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used by those who deny that God knows anything, based on knowledge requiring duality and causing multiplicity.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires duality and multiplicity—so how could the One God know?

Those who deny divine knowledge present two rational proofs. First, knowledge is a relation that necessitates a distinction between knower and known, impossible in the truly One. Second, each known object would imprint a distinct form on the divine essence, causing multiplicity. Let's examine these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Examine the argument that knowledge, being a relation or attribute requiring relation, necessitates duality (ithnayniyya) and differentiation (taghayyur) between knower and known. This is inconceivable for the truly One (al-wāḥid al-ḥaqīqī).
Note that the argument assumes knowledge is either a relation (iḍāfa) or an attribute inherently requiring relation.
2
Read the second argument
Read the argument that knowledge multiplies forms in the essence: each known thing has a distinct form (ṣūra) impressed in the knower, so multiple known things imply multiple forms in the divine essence, contradicting absolute oneness.
The argument relies on the definition of knowledge as a form equal to the known (ṣūra musāwiya lil-maʿlūm).
3
Connect to the central challenge
Reflect on why these arguments pose a serious theological problem: they claim that divine simplicity is incompatible with the very concept of knowledge.
The upcoming lesson will present the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إنه لا يعلم ذاته القائلون بأنه ليس بعالم أصلاً تمسكوا بوجهين : أحدهما : أنه لا يصح علمه بذاته ولا بغيره أما الأول فلأن العلم إضافة أو صفة ذات إضافة، وأياً ما كان يقتضى اثنينية وقغايراً بين العالم والمعلوم، فلا يعقل في الواحد الحقيقي. وأما الثاني : فلأنه يوجب كثرة في الذات الأحدى من كل وجه لأن العلم بإحدى المعلومين غير العلم بالآخر للقطع بجواز العلم بهذا، مع الذهول عن الآخر، ولأن العلم صورة مساوية للمعلوم، مرتسمة في العالم ، أو نفس الارتسام ولا خفاء في أن صور الأشياء المختلفة مختلفة، فيلزم بحسب كثرة المعلومات، كثرة الصور فى الذات . وأجيب عن الوجه الأول أولاً بعد تسليم لزوم التغاير على تقدير كون العلم صفة ذات إضافة، بأن تغاير الاعتبار كاف (٢) كما في علمنا بأنفسنا على ما سبق في بحث العلم، لا يقال التغاير الاعتباري، إنما هو بالمعلولية والمعلومية، وهو فرع حصول العلم، فلو توقف حصول العلم على التغاير لزم الدور، وإنما يرد النقص بعلمنا بأنفسنا لو كانت النفس واحدة من كل وجه ، كالواجب وهو ممنوع . فيجوز كونها عالمة من وجه معلومة من وجه . لأنا نقول : إنما يلزم الدور لو كان توقف العلم على التغاير توقف سبق واحتياج، وهو ممنوع ، بل غايته أنه لا ينفك عن العلم، كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته . والمراد بالنقص، أن النفس تعلم ذات التي هي عالمة، لا أن يكون العالم شيئاً، والمعلوم شيئاً آخر. وثانياً: بأن علمه ليس إلا تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير ارتسام صورة في الذات فلا كثرة إلا في التعلقات ،والإضافات، وتحقيقه على ما ذكر بعض المتأخرين أن حصول الأشياء له حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور (۱) المعقولة لنا، حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للقابل وذلك بالإمكان ، ومع ذلك فلا يستدعي صوراً مغايرة لها، فإنك تعقل شيئاً بصورة يتصورها ويستحضرها فهي صادرة عنك بمشاركة ما من غيرك وهو الشيء الخارجي، ومع ذلك، فإنك لا تمل تلك الصورة بغيرها، بل كما تعقل ذلك الشيء بها، كذلك تعقلها أيضاً بنفسها من غير أن تتضاعف الصور فيك، وإذا كان حالك مع ما يصدر عنك بمشاركة غيرك هذه الحال فما ظنك بحال من يعقل ما يصدر عنه لذاته من غير مداخلة الغير فيه ، ثم ليس كذلك محلاً لتلك الصور شرطاً في التعقل بدليل أنك تعقل ذاتك بدون ذلك، بل المعتبر حضور الصورة لكل (۳) حالة كانت أو غير حالة والمعلولات الذاتية للعاقل الفاعل لذاته حاصلة له من غير حلول فيه، فهو عاقل إياها من غير أن تكون حالة فيه، على أن كثرة الصفات في الذات لا تمنع عندنا، بل عند الفلاسفة وأتباعهم .
It was said: He does not know His essence. Those who say that He is not knowing at all adhere to two aspects: One of them: that it is not valid that He knows His essence nor anything else. As for the first, because knowledge is a relation or an attribute inherently requiring relation, whichever it is, it necessitates duality and differentiation between the knower and the known, so it is not conceivable in the truly One. As for the second, because it necessitates multiplicity in the unique essence from every aspect, because knowledge of one known thing is different from knowledge of another, due to the certainty of the possibility of knowing this while being heedless of the other, and because knowledge is a form equal to the known, impressed in the knower, or the impression itself, and it is not hidden that forms of different things are different, so according to the multiplicity of known things, multiplicity of forms in the essence follows. And it was answered regarding the first aspect, first after conceding the necessity of differentiation on the assumption that knowledge is an attribute inherently requiring relation, that the diversity of consideration is sufficient, as in our knowledge of ourselves as previously in the discussion of knowledge, lest it be said that the conceptual diversity is only in terms of effect and being known, and it is a branch of the occurrence of knowledge, so if the occurrence of knowledge were to depend on diversity, circularity would follow, and the objection would only be applicable to our self-knowledge if the soul were one from every aspect like the Necessary, which is denied. So it is permissible that it is knower from one aspect and known from another. For we say: Circularity would only follow if the dependence of knowledge on diversity were a dependence of precedence and need, which is denied. Rather, its utmost is that it is not separate from knowledge, just as the effect is not separate from its cause. And what is meant by the objection is that the soul knows the essence that is knower, not that the knower is one thing and the known is another thing. And second: That His knowledge is nothing but an aspectual relation to the known without the impression of a form in the essence, so there is no multiplicity except in the relations and attributions. And its verification according to what some later scholars mentioned is that the occurrence of things for Him is the occurrence for the agent, and that is by necessity, and the occurrence of intelligible forms for us is the occurrence for the agent—no, occurrence for the agent and that by necessity, and the occurrence of intelligible forms for us is the occurrence for the recipient and that by possibility. Nevertheless, it does not require forms that are different from them, for you conceive a thing by a form that you imagine and present, and it is emanating from you with the participation of something other than you, which is the external thing. Nevertheless, you do not possess that form in exchange for another; rather, just as you conceive that thing by it, likewise you conceive it also by itself without the forms multiplying in you. And if your state with what emanates from you with the participation of another is this state, then what is your opinion of the state of one who conceives what emanates from him by his essence without the intervention of another? Moreover, being a locus for those forms is not a condition in intellection, evidenced by the fact that you conceive your essence without that. Rather, what is considered is the presence of the form in every state, whether it is a locus or not. And the essential effects of the rational agent by his essence are obtained by him without it being in-dwelling in him, so he is intellective of them without them being indwelling in him. Moreover, multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not impossible according to us, but rather according to the philosophers and their followers.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) of those who deny that God knows anything at all. The proof has two sub-parts: (i) knowledge inherently requires duality and differentiation between knower and known, inconceivable for the truly One; (ii) knowledge would multiply forms in the essence, since each known thing has a distinct form impressed in the knower. The answer (jawāb) begins by conceding the implication of differentiation but argues that conceptual diversity (taghayyur al-iʿtibār) suffices, as in human self-knowledge. It then refutes a potential objection that this conceptual diversity depends on the prior existence of knowledge, leading to circularity. The refutation clarifies that the dependence is not causal but concomitant: the diversity is inseparable from knowledge, like the inseparability of effect from cause. The second part of the answer denies that divine knowledge involves impression of forms; rather, it is a pure relation (taʿalluq) to the known. An elaborate analogy from the later scholars is given: when a human conceives an external object, the form emanates from the agent with participation from the object, yet there is no multiplication of forms. If this holds for humans, then even more so for God, whose intellection is purely self-sufficient without any external cause or in-dwelling. The passage concludes by noting that multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not impossible in our Ashʿarī school, but only according to the philosophers.

Key Terms
إضافة
اثنينية
تغاير
صورة
ارتسام

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is knowledge impossible for the truly One?
A) Because knowledge is an accident that cannot subsist in an eternal being
B) Because knowledge is a relation that requires duality between knower and known
C) Because the One cannot have multiple attributes
D) Because knowledge implies change in the knower

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that the very nature of knowledge—as a relation requiring differentiation and as an impression of forms—is incompatible with the absolute oneness of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that if God knew multiple distinct things, His essence would contain multiple distinct forms. Do you believe that God's knowledge is one simple act without multiplicity? Examine your own mental image of God—do you secretly imagine Him 'processing' information like we do? Write down one assumption you hold about divine knowledge that might be too human-like and commit to replacing it with what true tawḥīd demands.
?
The argument says knowledge of one object is different from knowledge of another, because you can know this while being heedless of that. But you claim God knows everything simultaneously. Does your mind rest in that paradox, or do you feel a tension? What would it mean for your worship if God’s knowledge were multiple or composite—would it make you trust Him less? Sit with that discomfort tonight and ask Allah to increase your certainty in His perfect oneness.
?
If knowledge necessarily requires duality (knower and known), then how can you even speak of God 'knowing' without reducing Him to a duality in your mind? Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God and His creation as two separate things that 'relate'? Repent of any mental categorization that places God in a box. From now on, when you say 'Allah knows', make it a declaration of His transcendent unity, not a comparison to human knowing.

The Refutation: Conceptual Diversity and Pure Relation
Refutation using conceptual diversity and pure relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part refutation of the proof from duality, including the concept of conceptual diversity and the nature of divine knowledge as pure relation.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is a pure relation, not an impression—so no multiplicity follows.

The refutation begins by conceding that knowledge involves a kind of differentiation, but argues it is merely conceptual, not real. It then rejects the notion that divine knowledge implies impressions, explaining it as a pure relation. An elaborate analogy from human intellection clarifies how multiplicity is avoided.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first answer: conceptual diversity
The refutation first concedes that if knowledge is a relation, it entails some differentiation. However, it argues that a conceptual diversity (taghayyur al-iʿtibār) is sufficient—just as in human self-knowledge where the soul is both knower and known without real duality.
The answer preempts an objection that this conceptual diversity would lead to circularity.
2
Follow the defense against circularity
Read how the author refutes the potential charge of circularity: the dependence of conceptual diversity on knowledge is not causal but concomitant, like the inseparability of effect from cause.
This nuance protects the first answer from logical collapse.
3
Grasp the second answer: pure relation without impression
The second answer denies that divine knowledge involves any impression of forms. Instead, it is a pure aspectual relation (taʿalluq) to the known. The supporting analogy from later scholars shows that human intellection does not necessarily multiply forms; even more so for God.
The passage ends by noting that multiplicity of attributes is not problematic for Ashʿarīs, only for philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إنه لا يعلم ذاته القائلون بأنه ليس بعالم أصلاً تمسكوا بوجهين : أحدهما : أنه لا يصح علمه بذاته ولا بغيره أما الأول فلأن العلم إضافة أو صفة ذات إضافة، وأياً ما كان يقتضى اثنينية وقغايراً بين العالم والمعلوم، فلا يعقل في الواحد الحقيقي. وأما الثاني : فلأنه يوجب كثرة في الذات الأحدى من كل وجه لأن العلم بإحدى المعلومين غير العلم بالآخر للقطع بجواز العلم بهذا، مع الذهول عن الآخر، ولأن العلم صورة مساوية للمعلوم، مرتسمة في العالم ، أو نفس الارتسام ولا خفاء في أن صور الأشياء المختلفة مختلفة، فيلزم بحسب كثرة المعلومات، كثرة الصور فى الذات . وأجيب عن الوجه الأول أولاً بعد تسليم لزوم التغاير على تقدير كون العلم صفة ذات إضافة، بأن تغاير الاعتبار كاف (٢) كما في علمنا بأنفسنا على ما سبق في بحث العلم، لا يقال التغاير الاعتباري، إنما هو بالمعلولية والمعلومية، وهو فرع حصول العلم، فلو توقف حصول العلم على التغاير لزم الدور، وإنما يرد النقص بعلمنا بأنفسنا لو كانت النفس واحدة من كل وجه ، كالواجب وهو ممنوع . فيجوز كونها عالمة من وجه معلومة من وجه . لأنا نقول : إنما يلزم الدور لو كان توقف العلم على التغاير توقف سبق واحتياج، وهو ممنوع ، بل غايته أنه لا ينفك عن العلم، كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته . والمراد بالنقص، أن النفس تعلم ذات التي هي عالمة، لا أن يكون العالم شيئاً، والمعلوم شيئاً آخر. وثانياً: بأن علمه ليس إلا تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير ارتسام صورة في الذات فلا كثرة إلا في التعلقات ،والإضافات، وتحقيقه على ما ذكر بعض المتأخرين أن حصول الأشياء له حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور (۱) المعقولة لنا، حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للقابل وذلك بالإمكان ، ومع ذلك فلا يستدعي صوراً مغايرة لها، فإنك تعقل شيئاً بصورة يتصورها ويستحضرها فهي صادرة عنك بمشاركة ما من غيرك وهو الشيء الخارجي، ومع ذلك، فإنك لا تمل تلك الصورة بغيرها، بل كما تعقل ذلك الشيء بها، كذلك تعقلها أيضاً بنفسها من غير أن تتضاعف الصور فيك، وإذا كان حالك مع ما يصدر عنك بمشاركة غيرك هذه الحال فما ظنك بحال من يعقل ما يصدر عنه لذاته من غير مداخلة الغير فيه ، ثم ليس كذلك محلاً لتلك الصور شرطاً في التعقل بدليل أنك تعقل ذاتك بدون ذلك، بل المعتبر حضور الصورة لكل (۳) حالة كانت أو غير حالة والمعلولات الذاتية للعاقل الفاعل لذاته حاصلة له من غير حلول فيه، فهو عاقل إياها من غير أن تكون حالة فيه، على أن كثرة الصفات في الذات لا تمنع عندنا، بل عند الفلاسفة وأتباعهم .
It was said: He does not know His essence. Those who say that He is not knowing at all adhere to two aspects: One of them: that it is not valid that He knows His essence nor anything else. As for the first, because knowledge is a relation or an attribute inherently requiring relation, whichever it is, it necessitates duality and differentiation between the knower and the known, so it is not conceivable in the truly One. As for the second, because it necessitates multiplicity in the unique essence from every aspect, because knowledge of one known thing is different from knowledge of another, due to the certainty of the possibility of knowing this while being heedless of the other, and because knowledge is a form equal to the known, impressed in the knower, or the impression itself, and it is not hidden that forms of different things are different, so according to the multiplicity of known things, multiplicity of forms in the essence follows. And it was answered regarding the first aspect, first after conceding the necessity of differentiation on the assumption that knowledge is an attribute inherently requiring relation, that the diversity of consideration is sufficient, as in our knowledge of ourselves as previously in the discussion of knowledge, lest it be said that the conceptual diversity is only in terms of effect and being known, and it is a branch of the occurrence of knowledge, so if the occurrence of knowledge were to depend on diversity, circularity would follow, and the objection would only be applicable to our self-knowledge if the soul were one from every aspect like the Necessary, which is denied. So it is permissible that it is knower from one aspect and known from another. For we say: Circularity would only follow if the dependence of knowledge on diversity were a dependence of precedence and need, which is denied. Rather, its utmost is that it is not separate from knowledge, just as the effect is not separate from its cause. And what is meant by the objection is that the soul knows the essence that is knower, not that the knower is one thing and the known is another thing. And second: That His knowledge is nothing but an aspectual relation to the known without the impression of a form in the essence, so there is no multiplicity except in the relations and attributions. And its verification according to what some later scholars mentioned is that the occurrence of things for Him is the occurrence for the agent, and that is by necessity, and the occurrence of intelligible forms for us is the occurrence for the agent—no, occurrence for the agent and that by necessity, and the occurrence of intelligible forms for us is the occurrence for the recipient and that by possibility. Nevertheless, it does not require forms that are different from them, for you conceive a thing by a form that you imagine and present, and it is emanating from you with the participation of something other than you, which is the external thing. Nevertheless, you do not possess that form in exchange for another; rather, just as you conceive that thing by it, likewise you conceive it also by itself without the forms multiplying in you. And if your state with what emanates from you with the participation of another is this state, then what is your opinion of the state of one who conceives what emanates from him by his essence without the intervention of another? Moreover, being a locus for those forms is not a condition in intellection, evidenced by the fact that you conceive your essence without that. Rather, what is considered is the presence of the form in every state, whether it is a locus or not. And the essential effects of the rational agent by his essence are obtained by him without it being in-dwelling in him, so he is intellective of them without them being indwelling in him. Moreover, multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not impossible according to us, but rather according to the philosophers and their followers.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) of those who deny that God knows anything at all. The proof has two sub-parts: (i) knowledge inherently requires duality and differentiation between knower and known, inconceivable for the truly One; (ii) knowledge would multiply forms in the essence, since each known thing has a distinct form impressed in the knower. The answer (jawāb) begins by conceding the implication of differentiation but argues that conceptual diversity (taghayyur al-iʿtibār) suffices, as in human self-knowledge. It then refutes a potential objection that this conceptual diversity depends on the prior existence of knowledge, leading to circularity. The refutation clarifies that the dependence is not causal but concomitant: the diversity is inseparable from knowledge, like the inseparability of effect from cause. The second part of the answer denies that divine knowledge involves impression of forms; rather, it is a pure relation (taʿalluq) to the known. An elaborate analogy from the later scholars is given: when a human conceives an external object, the form emanates from the agent with participation from the object, yet there is no multiplication of forms. If this holds for humans, then even more so for God, whose intellection is purely self-sufficient without any external cause or in-dwelling. The passage concludes by noting that multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not impossible in our Ashʿarī school, but only according to the philosophers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual diversity cannot be invoked without circularity because it depends on knowledge.
Correction: The diversity is concomitant, not causally prior; it is inseparable from knowledge like effect from cause.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation describe divine knowledge?
A) As an impression of forms in the essence
B) As a pure relation (taʿalluq) without impression
C) As identical to the essence
D) As an accidental attribute

Key Takeaway
The refutation establishes that divine knowledge is a pure relation, not an impressed form, and that conceptual diversity does not imply real multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What change must you make in your mental image of God's omniscience to align with this lesson, and how will you enact that change starting today?
?
The passage uses human self-knowledge to show conceptual diversity without real multiplicity. How does your own self-awareness—knowing yourself while being yourself—mirror God's knowledge? If you truly accept that God's knowledge is immediate and without barriers, what does that demand of your sincerity in private moments when no one else sees?
?
The refutation argues that multiplicity of attributes is not impossible in Ashʿarī theology. Does this challenge you to re-evaluate your understanding of tawḥīd? Are you willing to hold that God's attributes are distinct in meaning yet not multiple in essence, even if it feels uncomfortable? What in your life would change if you fully submitted to this nuanced unity?

Second Proof: Knowledge Distinct from Essence and Its Refutation
Second proof and its refutation by denying impossibility of dual role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that knowledge is distinct from the essence and the refutation that denies the impossibility of a single thing being both recipient and agent.

Core Idea
A thing can be both recipient and agent; that is not impossible.

This passage presents the second proof against God knowing His essence, arguing that if knowledge is distinct, it would be caused and make God both recipient and agent. The refutation simply denies that this combination is impossible. We will examine the logic and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument flow.
Notice the compact style; the passage contains both a proof and a refutation.
2
Identify the proof's premises
Break down the proof: knowledge distinct → possible and caused → essence must be both recipient and agent → this is claimed impossible.
3
Understand the refutation's key denial
The answer simply denies the impossibility of a single thing being both recipient and agent, which is the core rebuttal.
This refutation rejects the underlying assumption without further argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن العلم مغاير للذات لما سبق من الأدلة، فيكون ممكناً معلولاً له ضرورة امتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته، وكمالاته إلى الغير، فيلزم كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلاً وهو محال . وأجيب عن الثاني بمنع استحالة كون الشيء (٤) الواحد قابلاً أو فاعلاً.
And the second: that knowledge is other than the essence due to the preceding proofs, so it would be possible and caused by it, due to the impossibility of the Necessary in His attributes and perfections needing another, so it follows that the thing is both recipient and agent, which is impossible. And the second was answered by denying the impossibility of a single thing being recipient or agent.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This short passage presents the second proof (al-wajh al-thānī) that God does not know His essence. The argument is that previous proofs have established that knowledge is distinct from the essence, so it is possible and caused by the essence. Since the Necessary cannot depend on another for His attributes, this would entail that the essence is both recipient (qābil) of knowledge and agent (fāʿil) of knowledge, which the opponents claim is impossible. The answer (jawāb) is straightforward: it simply denies the impossibility of a single thing being both recipient and agent. This refutation is brief but effective, as it rejects the underlying premise that such a combination is inherently contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is absolutely impossible for one thing to be both recipient and agent.
Correction: The impossibility is denied; it is not necessarily impossible in all contexts, especially regarding divine attributes.

Checkpoint

B) By denying the impossibility of a single thing being both recipient and agent
C) By distinguishing between knowledge of essence and knowledge of others
D) By rejecting the premise that knowledge is distinct from the essence

Key Takeaway
The second proof against divine knowledge is refuted by denying that a single entity cannot be both recipient and agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that what seemed impossible (being both recipient and agent) is actually possible for God. Will you pray today for that impossibility to be removed?

Refuting the Circularity Objection in Divine Self-Knowledge
Refutation of the circularity objection regarding self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that divine self-knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, which would lead to circularity.

Core Idea
Divine self-knowledge does not require a real distinction—only inseparability like cause and effect.

The first objection against God's knowledge of Himself argues that knowledge requires a distinction between the knower and the known, leading to an infinite regress if God knows Himself. The author clarifies that the dependence is not one of temporal precedence but of logical inseparability, using the example of the soul's self-knowledge to illustrate that numerical unity does not preclude self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first objection
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Identify the objection and the author's initial response.
2
Understand the nature of dependence
Notice that the author distinguishes between dependence of precedence and need versus inseparability. This is key.
3
Analyze the soul analogy
The author uses the example of the soul knowing itself: it is one in essence but can be knowing from one aspect and known from another. This refutes the need for numerical distinction.
Think about how this applies to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم، فلو توقف حصول العلم على التغاير لزم الدور، وإنما يرد النقص بعلمنا بأنفسنا لو كانت النفس واحدة من كل وجه ، كالواجب وهو ممنوع . فيجوز كونها عالمة من وجه معلومة من وجه . لأنا نقول : إنما يلزم الدور لو كان توقف العلم على التغاير توقف سبق واحتياج، وهو ممنوع ، بل غايته أنه لا ينفك عن العلم، كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته . والمراد بالنقص، أن النفس تعلم ذاته التي هي عالمة، لا أن يكون العالم شيئاً، والمعلوم شيئاً آخر. وثانياً: بأن علمه ليس إلا تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير ارتسام صورة في الذات فلا كثرة إلا في التعلقات ،والإضافات، وتحقيقه على ما ذكر بعض المتأخرين أن حصول الأشياء له حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للقابل وذلك بالإمكان ، ومع ذلك فلا يستدعي صوراً مغايرة لها، فإنك تعقل شيئاً بصورة يتصورها ويستحضرها فهي صادرة عنك بمشاركة ما من غيرك وهو الشيء الخارجي، ومع ذلك، فإنك لا تمل تلك الصورة بغيرها، بل كما تعقل ذلك الشيء بها، كذلك تعقلها أيضاً بنفسها من غير أن تتضاعف الصور فيك، وإذا كان حالك مع ما يصدر عنك بمشاركة غيرك هذه الحال فما ظنك بحال من يعقل ما يصدر عنه لذاته من غير مداخلة الغير فيه ، ثم ليس كذلك محلاً لتلك الصور شرطاً في التعقل بدليل أنك تعقل ذاتك بدون ذلك، بل المعتبر حضور الصورة لكل حالة كانت أو غير حالة والمعلولات الذاتية للعاقل الفاعل لذاته حاصلة له من غير حلول فيه، فهو عاقل إياها من غير أن تكون حالة فيه، على أن كثرة الصفات في الذات لا تمنع عندنا، بل عند الفلاسفة وأتباعهم . وأجيب عن الثاني بمنع استحالة كون الشيء الواحد قابلاً أو فاعلاً.
Knowledge: if acquiring knowledge depended on distinction, then circularity would follow. The defect is only objected concerning our knowledge of ourselves if the soul were one in every respect, like the Necessary, which is denied. So it is possible for it to be knowing in one respect and known in another. For we say: circularity only follows if the dependence of knowledge on distinction were a dependence of precedence and need, which is denied; rather, its utmost is that it is inseparable from knowledge, just as the effect is inseparable from its cause. What is meant by 'defect' is that the soul knows its very essence which is knowing, not that the knower is one thing and the known another. Secondly: His knowledge is nothing but a relation to the known without the impression of a form in the essence, so there is no multiplicity except in relations and attributes. Its verification, as mentioned by some later scholars, is that the obtaining of things for Him is the obtaining of the agent by necessity, and the obtaining of intelligible forms for us is the obtaining of the recipient by possibility. Yet that does not require forms distinct from them, for you conceive a thing by a form that you imagine and bring to mind; it emanates from you with some participation from another (the external thing). Yet you do not possess that form without another; rather, just as you conceive that thing by it, so you also conceive the form itself without the forms multiplying in you. If your state with what emanates from you through the participation of another is this, then what do you think of the state of one who conceives what emanates from His own essence without any interference from another? Moreover, being a substrate for those forms is not a condition for intellection, as evidenced by your intellection of your own essence without that. Rather, what matters is the presence of the form for every state, whether it is subsisting or not. The essential effects of the self-active intellect are present to it without inherence in it; thus it is intellective of them without their being states in it, given that multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not prevented according to us, but only according to the philosophers and their followers. The second [objection] was answered by denying the impossibility of a single thing being both receptive and agent.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections concerning divine knowledge. The first objection claims that knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, leading to circularity if God knows Himself. The author denies that the dependence is one of precedence; rather, knowledge and its object are inseparable like cause and effect. The soul can know itself without numerical distinction. The second objection argues that knowledge requires a form impressed in the essence, implying multiplicity. The author responds that God's knowledge is a pure relation without impression, and that in human intellection, forms are not inherent but present as modes. The author concludes by noting that multiplicity of attributes is accepted in Sunni theology, unlike the philosophers.

Key Terms

دور
نفس
عالم
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine self-knowledge requires a real distinction between knower and known, leading to circularity.
Correction: The author shows that knowledge does not require temporal precedence, only inseparability, and that numerical oneness does not prevent self-knowledge, as seen in the soul.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be required for the circularity objection to be valid?
A) Knowledge requiring distinction as a matter of inseparability.
B) Knowledge requiring distinction as a matter of precedence and need.
C) The soul being composite.
D) God not knowing Himself.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because knowledge and its object are separable only in concept, not in existence, and numerical oneness does not preclude self-knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own soul, do you sense a division between the 'knower' and the 'known'? This lesson teaches that the soul knows itself without duality. How does that challenge your experience of inner fragmentation or self-deception? Will you today ask Allah to grant you a unified self-knowledge that leads to sincerity?
?
The objection claims that knowledge requires separation, but this is false for self-knowledge. Do you ever treat your relationship with Allah as if He needs a mediator to know you fully? Meditate on the fact that His knowledge of you is direct and inseparable from His essence. What one doubt about His intimate knowledge of you will you renounce right now?
?
The effect is inseparable from its cause; so is divine knowledge inseparable from His essence. If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge of your circumstances is immediate and without any need, how would your trust in His decree change today?

Refuting the Multiplicity Objection: Knowledge Without Impression of Forms
Refutation of the form-impression objection to divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends divine knowledge from the objection that it would require multiple forms to inhere in God's essence, implying multiplicity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is a pure relation without forms, and multiplicity of attributes is permissible in Sunni theology.

The second objection argues that knowledge requires the impression of a form in the knower, which would introduce multiplicity in God. The author counters by explaining that divine knowledge is a relation (تعلق) without forms, and even human intellection involves forms that are not inherent. He also notes that multiplicity of attributes is not problematic in Sunni thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key points: God's knowledge is pure relation; human intellection involves forms but not as inherent; multiplicity of attributes is allowed.
2
Understand the concept of 'talluq' (relation)
The author says divine knowledge is nothing but a relation (تعلق) to the known, without any form impressed in the essence. This avoids multiplicity.
Relate to the Sunni view of attributes as neither identical nor separate.
3
Examine the analogy from human intellection
The author uses an example: you conceive a thing via a form that you bring to mind, yet you also conceive that form itself without multiplication of forms. This shows forms need not be inherent.
Then he argues how much more so for God, who conceives directly.
4
Note the final point on multiplicity of attributes
The author concludes that multiplicity of attributes is not prevented in Sunni theology, only in philosophical systems.
This ties the argument to broader theological positions.

العلم، فلو توقف حصول العلم على التغاير لزم الدور، وإنما يرد النقص بعلمنا بأنفسنا لو كانت النفس واحدة من كل وجه ، كالواجب وهو ممنوع . فيجوز كونها عالمة من وجه معلومة من وجه . لأنا نقول : إنما يلزم الدور لو كان توقف العلم على التغاير توقف سبق واحتياج، وهو ممنوع ، بل غايته أنه لا ينفك عن العلم، كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته . والمراد بالنقص، أن النفس تعلم ذاته التي هي عالمة، لا أن يكون العالم شيئاً، والمعلوم شيئاً آخر. وثانياً: بأن علمه ليس إلا تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير ارتسام صورة في الذات فلا كثرة إلا في التعلقات ،والإضافات، وتحقيقه على ما ذكر بعض المتأخرين أن حصول الأشياء له حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للفاعل وذلك بالوجوب، وحصول الصور المعقولة لنا، حصول للقابل وذلك بالإمكان ، ومع ذلك فلا يستدعي صوراً مغايرة لها، فإنك تعقل شيئاً بصورة يتصورها ويستحضرها فهي صادرة عنك بمشاركة ما من غيرك وهو الشيء الخارجي، ومع ذلك، فإنك لا تمل تلك الصورة بغيرها، بل كما تعقل ذلك الشيء بها، كذلك تعقلها أيضاً بنفسها من غير أن تتضاعف الصور فيك، وإذا كان حالك مع ما يصدر عنك بمشاركة غيرك هذه الحال فما ظنك بحال من يعقل ما يصدر عنه لذاته من غير مداخلة الغير فيه ، ثم ليس كذلك محلاً لتلك الصور شرطاً في التعقل بدليل أنك تعقل ذاتك بدون ذلك، بل المعتبر حضور الصورة لكل حالة كانت أو غير حالة والمعلولات الذاتية للعاقل الفاعل لذاته حاصلة له من غير حلول فيه، فهو عاقل إياها من غير أن تكون حالة فيه، على أن كثرة الصفات في الذات لا تمنع عندنا، بل عند الفلاسفة وأتباعهم . وأجيب عن الثاني بمنع استحالة كون الشيء الواحد قابلاً أو فاعلاً.
Knowledge: if acquiring knowledge depended on distinction, then circularity would follow. The defect is only objected concerning our knowledge of ourselves if the soul were one in every respect, like the Necessary, which is denied. So it is possible for it to be knowing in one respect and known in another. For we say: circularity only follows if the dependence of knowledge on distinction were a dependence of precedence and need, which is denied; rather, its utmost is that it is inseparable from knowledge, just as the effect is inseparable from its cause. What is meant by 'defect' is that the soul knows its very essence which is knowing, not that the knower is one thing and the known another. Secondly: His knowledge is nothing but a relation to the known without the impression of a form in the essence, so there is no multiplicity except in relations and attributes. Its verification, as mentioned by some later scholars, is that the obtaining of things for Him is the obtaining of the agent by necessity, and the obtaining of intelligible forms for us is the obtaining of the recipient by possibility. Yet that does not require forms distinct from them, for you conceive a thing by a form that you imagine and bring to mind; it emanates from you with some participation from another (the external thing). Yet you do not possess that form without another; rather, just as you conceive that thing by it, so you also conceive the form itself without the forms multiplying in you. If your state with what emanates from you through the participation of another is this, then what do you think of the state of one who conceives what emanates from His own essence without any interference from another? Moreover, being a substrate for those forms is not a condition for intellection, as evidenced by your intellection of your own essence without that. Rather, what matters is the presence of the form for every state, whether it is subsisting or not. The essential effects of the self-active intellect are present to it without inherence in it; thus it is intellective of them without their being states in it, given that multiplicity of attributes in the essence is not prevented according to us, but only according to the philosophers and their followers. The second [objection] was answered by denying the impossibility of a single thing being both receptive and agent.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections concerning divine knowledge. The first objection claims that knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, leading to circularity if God knows Himself. The author denies that the dependence is one of precedence; rather, knowledge and its object are inseparable like cause and effect. The soul can know itself without numerical distinction. The second objection argues that knowledge requires a form impressed in the essence, implying multiplicity. The author responds that God's knowledge is a pure relation without impression, and that in human intellection, forms are not inherent but present as modes. The author concludes by noting that multiplicity of attributes is accepted in Sunni theology, unlike the philosophers.

Key Terms
تعلق
صورة
فاعل
قابل
إضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge necessarily requires a form to inhere in the knower, leading to multiplicity in God.
Correction: Knowledge can be a pure relation without inherence, and even human knowledge involves forms without inherence. Multiplicity of attributes is not problematic for Sunni theology.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that God's knowledge requires forms to inhere in His essence?
A) Forms are not needed because God's knowledge is a relation.
B) Forms are necessary but do not cause multiplicity.
C) The soul's knowledge of itself disproves any forms.
D) The philosophers agree with this view.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is a pure relation without forms, and multiplicity of attributes is not a problem in Sunni theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's knowledge of your actions is a pure relation without any impression or form, does that make you feel more or less accountable? How should this shape the way you think about being constantly known by God?
?
The passage argues that multiplicity of attributes is not a problem for God. Is there an attribute of God that you find yourself denying or downplaying because you feel it would 'complicate' His essence? How can you embrace the full depth of His attributes without reducing them to human limitations?

The Infinite Scope of Divine Knowledge
The author concludes that God's knowledge is infinite and encompasses all things, with proofs and refutation of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge must encompass all things—infinite, finite, existent, and non-existent—and how this is proven from scripture and reason.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not limited; His essence demands it.

In this concluding section on divine attributes, the author asserts the infinity and universality of God's knowledge. He provides both scriptural quotations and a rational argument that ties omniscience directly to the divine essence. He also addresses and dismisses three opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is a conclusion (خاتمة) and is densely packed with thesis, objections, and proofs.
2
Identify the thesis and the three objections
The passage starts with the thesis: God's knowledge is infinite and encompasses all things. Then it lists three dissenting opinions. Find each one and note what they deny.
The objections are: knowledge of knowledge, knowledge of the infinite, and knowledge of non-existent.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
The author cites several Quranic verses to support the claim. Read through them and see how they affirm God's comprehensive knowledge.
Verses like 2:282 and 6:73 are mentioned.
4
Understand the rational proof
The rational argument is that the divine essence is the ground for omniscience and relates equally to all objects. If knowledge were limited, a specifier (مخصص) would be needed, which contradicts God's necessity and self-sufficiency.
This is the core logical argument: no specification without a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة
قال : خاتمة .
علمه لا يتناهى ومحيط بما لا يتناهى كالأعداد والأشكال وبكل موجود ومعدوم وكلي جزئي لعمومات النصوص ولأن المقتضى للعالمية الذات وللمعلومية صحتها من غير مخصوص لتعاليه عن أن يفتقر في كماله وخالف بعضهم في العلم بالعلم لاقتضائه إلى صفات غير متناهية وبعضهم في العلم بما لا يتناهى لاستحالة وجوهها المحذور السابق وبعضهم في العلم بالمعدوم لأنه نفي محض لا تميز فيه والمعلوم متميز وضعف الكل ظاهر).

قال : خاتمة علم الله تعالى غير متناه بمعنى أنه لا ينقطع، ولا يصير بحيث لا يتعلق بالمعلوم ومحيط بما هو غير متناه كالأعداد والأشكال، ونعيم الجنان، وشامل لجميع الموجودات والمعدومات الممكنة والممتنعة وجميع الكليات والجزئيات إما سمعاً فبمثل قوله تعالى : والله بكل شيء عليم عالم الغيب والشهادة لا يعزب عنه مثقال ذرة في السموات ولا في الأرض يعلم خائنة الأعين، وما تخفي الصدور ) يعلم ما يسرون وما يعلنون إلى غير ذلك. وإما عقلاً فلأن المقتضى للعالمية هو الذات إما بواسطة المعين أعني العلم على ما هو رأي الصفاتية، أو بدونها على ما هو رأي النفاة وللمعلومية ،إمكانها ونسبة الذات إلى الكل على السوية . فلو اختصت عالميته بالبعض دون البعض لكان المخصص وهو محال لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته وكمالاته، لمنافاته الوجوب والغنى المطلق .
Conclusion. He said: Conclusion. His knowledge is infinite and encompasses what is infinite, like numbers and shapes, and every existent and non-existent, universal and particular, due to the generalities of the texts and because the ground for omniscience is the essence, and for knowability is its soundness without specification, due to His transcendence above needing perfection. Some disagreed about knowledge of knowledge, because it would entail infinite attributes; some about knowledge of the infinite, due to the impossibility of its aspects (the previous danger); some about knowledge of the non-existent, because it is a pure negation without differentiation, while the known is differentiated. And the weakness of all is apparent. He said: Conclusion: God's knowledge is infinite in the sense that it does not cease, nor does it become such that it does not relate to the known, and it encompasses what is infinite, like numbers, shapes, and the bliss of Paradise, and it includes all existents and non-existents, possible and impossible, and all universals and particulars. This is [proven] by transmission, such as His saying: 'And Allah of everything is knowing' (Qur'an 2:282), 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (Qur'an 6:73), 'Not absent from Him is the weight of a particle in the heavens or the earth' (Qur'an 34:3), 'He knows the treachery of the eyes and what the breasts conceal' (Qur'an 40:19), 'He knows what they conceal and what they declare' (Qur'an 16:22), and others. And by reason: because the ground for omniscience is the essence, either through an intermediary (i.e., knowledge, according to the attributes party) or without it (according to the negators), and for knowability is its possibility, and the relation of the essence to all is equal. So if His omniscience were specific to some to the exclusion of others, there would have to be a specifier, which is impossible due to the impossibility of the Necessary needing in His attributes and perfections, as it contradicts [His] necessity and absolute self-sufficiency.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (خاتمة) on divine knowledge. It first states the thesis: God's knowledge is infinite and encompasses infinite things as well as all existent and non-existent, universal and particular. The proof is both textual (مثل الآيات) and rational. The rational argument: the ground for omniscience is the divine essence itself, and the relation of the essence to all objects of knowledge is equal; thus specification to some would require a specifier (مخصص), which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The passage also mentions three dissenting opinions: (1) that knowledge of knowledge would lead to infinite attributes, (2) that knowledge of the infinite is impossible, and (3) that knowledge of the non-existent is impossible since it is pure negation. The author declares all these weak. The rest elaborates the thesis with scriptural evidence and reinforces the rational proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God cannot know the infinite, or cannot know non-existent things, or that knowledge of knowledge leads to infinite attributes.
Correction: The author argues that God's essence grounds omniscience equally for all objects; no specifier is needed. The objections are based on mistaken assumptions, and their weakness is apparent.

Checkpoint

C) Because God's knowledge is caused by external objects.
D) Because the philosophers proved it.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is unlimited and encompasses all things because His essence is the ground of omniscience and relates equally to all objects of knowledge.

God's Knowledge is Infinite and All-Encompassing
Sanusi enumerates proofs for the infinity and comprehensiveness of God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational proofs for the infinite and comprehensive nature of God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is unlimited, covering everything — existent and non-existent, universal and particular.

In this passage, Sanusi concludes his discussion on divine attributes by affirming that God's knowledge is infinite and encompasses all things. He provides both scriptural verses and a rational argument based on the necessity of the Essence. This lesson unpacks that argument and the scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines 'infinite' in terms of knowledge: it does not cease to relate to objects.
Pay attention to the list of what is included: numbers, shapes, bliss of paradise, etc.
2
Identify the Two Proofs
The passage presents two types of proof: scriptural (سمعاً) and rational (عقلاً). Find where each begins and note the verses cited.
Scriptural proofs are multiple Quranic verses. The rational proof is a single logical argument.
3
Understand the Rational Argument
The rational argument: The cause of omniscience is the Essence itself (either directly or through the attribute of knowledge). The relation of the Essence to all possible objects is equal. Therefore, restricting knowledge to some would require a particularizer (مخصص), which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
This argument shows that limitation would imply need, contradicting God's self-sufficiency.
4
Reflect on the Implication
Consider why the impossibility of a particularizer is central. It reinforces that God's knowledge is perfect and needs no external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة علم الله تعالى غير متناه بمعنى أنه لا ينقطع، ولا يصير بحيث لا يتعلق بالمعلوم ومحيط بما هو غير متناه كالأعداد والأشكال، ونعيم الجنان، وشامل لجميع الموجودات والمعدومات الممكنة والممتنعة وجميع الكليات والجزئيات إما سمعاً فبمثل قوله تعالى : والله بكل شيء عليم (۲) عالم الغيب والشهادة لا يعزب عنه مثقال ذرة في السموات ولا في الأرض (۳) يعلم خائنة الأعين، وما تخفي الصدور ) يعلم ما يسرون وما يعلنون (٥) إلى غير ذلك. وإما عقلاً فلأن المقتضى للعالمية هو الذات إما بواسطة المعين أعني العلم على ما هو رأي الصفاتية، أو بدونها على ما هو رأي النفاة وللمعلومية ،إمكانها ونسبة الذات إلى الكل على السوية . فلو اختصت عالميته بالبعض دون البعض لكان المخصص وهو محال لامتناع احتياج الواجب في صفاته وكمالاته، لمنافاته الوجوب والغنى (1) المطلق .
He said: Conclusion: The knowledge of God Most High is infinite, meaning it does not cease, nor does it become such that it does not relate to what is known, and it encompasses what is infinite such as numbers, shapes, and the bliss of paradise, and it includes all existents and non-existents, possible and impossible, and all universals and particulars. As for [proof] from scripture, it is by such as His saying Most High: 'And Allah is of all things Knowing' (2:282), 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed; not absent from Him is the weight of an atom in the heavens or the earth' (34:3), 'He knows the treachery of the eyes and what the breasts conceal' (40:19), 'He knows what they conceal and what they declare' (16:23; 2:77), and so forth. As for [proof] by reason, it is because that which necessitates omniscience is the Essence — either through an intermediary, namely knowledge, as is the view of the attributes-positers, or without it, as is the view of the negators — and what necessitates being an object of knowledge is its possibility, and the relation of the Essence to all is equal. So if His omniscience were restricted to some things rather than others, there would have to be a particularizer, which is impossible due to the impossibility of the Necessary [Existent] needing [something] in His attributes and perfections, because it contradicts absolute necessity and self-sufficiency.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a statement and proof of the infinite and comprehensive nature of God's knowledge. It begins by defining 'infinite' in terms of knowledge: it does not cease to relate to objects. Then it lists what is included: numbers, shapes, the bliss of paradise (infinite in extent), all existents and non-existents (possible and impossible), universals and particulars. Two types of proof are given: scriptural and rational. Scriptural proofs are several verses stating God's omniscience, like 'Allah is of all things Knowing'. The rational proof argues that the cause of omniscience is the Essence itself (either directly or via the attribute of knowledge), and that possibility is the condition for being known. The Essence's relation to all possible objects is equal, so restricting omniscience to some would require a particularizer (mukhassis) which is impossible for the Necessary Being, as it would imply neediness, contradicting absolute necessity and self-sufficiency.

Misconception: God's infinite knowledge means He only knows existent things.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's knowledge includes non-existents and impossible things as well.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of the Objection Against God's Knowledge of the Infinite
Sanusi refutes two arguments against God's knowledge of infinite things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections raised against God's knowledge of infinite things and the beginning of Sanusi's refutation.

Core Idea
Distinctness does not require finitude — infinite things can be distinct.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a second objection to God's comprehensive knowledge. Some argue that God cannot know infinite things because every known object must be distinct, and the infinite cannot be distinct. Sanusi presents two versions of this objection and begins his response by challenging the assumption that distinctness implies finitude. This lesson examines these arguments and the initial counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: two arguments followed by an answer.
2
Identify the two arguments
The objector gives two reasons why God cannot know infinite things: (1) every known thing must be distinct, but the infinite cannot be distinct; (2) it would entail infinite attributes. Find both.
The first argument uses 'أما أولاً' and the second 'وأما ثانياً'.
3
Examine the beginning of the answer
Sanusi responds to the first argument by denying that distinctness requires finitude. What does this imply?
The answer to the second argument is not included in this passage.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
If distinctness does not imply finitude, then infinite things can be distinct objects of knowledge. This opens the door for God's knowledge of the infinite.
This is a key move in the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : لا يجوز علمه بما لا يتناهى، أما أولاً : فلأن كل معلوم يجب كونه ممتازاً، وهو ظاهر، ولا شيء من غير المتناهي بممتاز، لأن المتميز عن الشيء منفصل عنه محدود بالضرورة.
وأما ثانياً : فلأنه يلزم صفات غير متناهية هي المعلوم لما عرفت من تعدد المعلوم بتعدد المعلومات .
والجواب : عن الأول إنا لا نسلم أن كل متميز عن غيره يجب أن يكون متناهياً،
And among them is one who says: His knowledge of what is infinite is not possible. First, because every object of knowledge must be distinct, and this is obvious, and nothing of the infinite is distinct, because that which is distinct from something is separate from it and limited necessarily. Second, because it would entail infinite attributes which are the [objects of] knowledge, because you have known that the object of knowledge is multiple according to the multiplicity of things known. The answer: as for the first, we do not concede that everything distinct from another must be finite.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a second objection against the comprehensiveness of God's knowledge, specifically against knowledge of infinite things. Two arguments are given: (1) Every object of knowledge must be distinct and separate, but the infinite cannot be distinct because distinctness implies limitation and finitude. (2) If God knows infinite objects, then there would be infinite attributes (the objects known), which is impossible. The answer to the first argument begins (the text cuts off before the answer to the second): 'We do not concede that everything distinct from another must be finite.' This suggests that distinctness does not necessarily require finitude — something can be distinct and yet not limited, as with infinite numbers or shapes. The answer to the second argument is not present in this window, but it is likely that the multiplicity is again conceptual, not real.

Key Terms
ممتاز
متميز
محدود
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against God knowing infinite things mentioned in this passage?
A) Infinite things cannot be known because they have no end.
B) Every object of knowledge must be distinct, and the infinite cannot be distinct because distinctness implies limitation.
C) Knowledge of the infinite would make God finite.
D) The infinite cannot be known because it cannot be encompassed.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of infinite things is possible because distinctness does not require finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God knows every detail of your life, even the things you consider infinite in number or complexity. Do you live as if you are fully known, or do you hide parts of yourself, thinking they are too vast or insignificant for Him to notice? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed that nothing in your life is beyond His knowledge?
?
The objection assumes that to be distinct, you must be limited. But you often define yourself by your limitations—your past, your failures, your capacity. What if God sees you as distinct yet without those limits? Can you let go of the identities built on your finite self and embrace that His knowledge of you is not bound by them?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses infinite things without causing multiplicity in His essence, then your finite mind cannot fully grasp Him. Does this truth humble you, or does it unsettle you? Will you accept that your understanding is limited and trust in His infinite knowledge, or will you insist on a God you can fully comprehend?

Answering the Objection on Divine Self-Knowledge
Refutes the claim that God cannot know His own knowledge due to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress against God's knowledge of His own knowledge is refuted through the concept of mental considerations.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, its relations are mental, not infinite external entities.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: if God knows His own knowledge, wouldn't that lead to an endless chain of cognitions? To answer this, he unpacks the nature of knowledge and its relations, showing that the alleged infinite regress is merely conceptual, not actual. This lesson will guide you through the objection, the reasoning behind it, and the precise response that saves the doctrine of all-encompassing divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its proof
Start by reading the arabic passage through once. Then focus on the objector's claim: God knowing His own knowledge leads to infinite actual knowns, which is impossible. Notice the three reasons they give for why this knowledge would be actual.
This sets the stage for the refutation.
2
Examine the counter-argument and refutation
The text then presents a potential counter-argument: 'knowledge is the essence' or 'knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge'. Read how the author refutes these, showing that knowledge is distinct from essence, and that knowledge of a thing differs from knowledge of knowledge.
This part clarifies that the relations are distinct.
3
Understand the main answer
The core answer is that knowledge is a single attribute with multiple relations that are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), not external entities. Grasp why this dissolves the infinite regress: mental considerations do not create actual multiplicity.
This is the key insight.
4
Reflect on the additional argument from Imam al-Razi
Imam al-Razi adds that the series of known things has no end but has a beginning, whereas the proof against infinite regress applies only to what has no beginning. Consider how this further blocks the objection.
This supplements the main answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمخالفون في شمول علمه منهم من قال يمتنع علمه بعلمه، وإلا لزم اتصافه بما لا يتناهى عدده من المعلوم وهو محال، لأن كل ما هو موجود بالفعل، فهو متناه على ما مرّ مراراً. وجه اللزوم : أنه لو كان جائزاً لكان حاصلا بالفعل، لأنه مقتضى ذاته، ولأن الخلو عن العلم الجائز عليه جهل ونقص، ولأنه لا يتصف بالحوادث، وينقل الكلام إلى العلم بهذا العلم وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. لا يقال علمه ذاته . ولو سلم فالعلم بالعلم نفس العلم . لأنا نقول : أما امتناع كون العلم نفس الذات فقد سبق. وأما امتناع كون العلم بالعلم نفس العلم، فلأن الصورة المساوية لأحد المتغايرين تغاير الصورة المساوية للمغاير الآخر، ولأن التعلق بهذا يغاير التعلق بذلك . والجواب : أن العلم صفة واحدة لها تعلقات هي اعتبارات عقلية لا موجودات عينية ليلزم المحال، ولا يلزم من كونه اعتباراً عقلياً أن لا تكون الذات عالماً، والشيء معلوماً في الواقع لما عرفت من أن انتفاء مبدأ المحمول لا يوجب انتفاء الحمل، على أن مغايرة العلم بالشيء للعلم بالعلم إنما هو بحسب الاعتبار، فلا يلزم كثرة الأعيان الخارجية، فضلاً عن لا تناهيها، وبهذا يندفع الاستدلال بهذا الإشكال على نفي علمه بذاته بل بشيء من المعلومات . وأجاب الإمام بأن هذه أمور غير متناهية لا آخر لها، والبرهان إنما قام على ما لا أول لها.
And the opponents concerning the comprehensiveness of His knowledge, among them are those who said: His knowledge of His own knowledge is impossible, otherwise He would be described with what is infinite in number of known things, and that is impossible, because everything that exists in actuality is finite, as has been repeated many times. The aspect of implication: that if it were possible, it would be actual, because it is a requirement of His essence, and because emptiness from possible knowledge is ignorance and deficiency, and because He is not described by accidents, and the discourse is transferred to knowledge of this knowledge, and so on to infinity. It cannot be said: His knowledge is His essence. And even if granted, then knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge. For we say: As for the impossibility of knowledge being the same as essence, it has preceded. And as for the impossibility of knowledge of knowledge being the same as knowledge, because the form corresponding to one of two distinct things differs from the form corresponding to the other distinct thing, and because the relation to this differs from the relation to that. The answer: that knowledge is a single attribute having relations that are mental considerations, not external entities, so that the impossible does not follow. And it does not follow from its being a mental consideration that the essence is not knowing, and the thing is not known in reality, because you have known that the negation of the basis of the predicate does not necessitate the negation of predication. Moreover, the difference between knowledge of a thing and knowledge of knowledge is only according to consideration, so multiplicity of external entities does not follow, let alone their infinity. And by this the argument based on this difficulty for negating His knowledge of His essence, even of anything among the known things, is repelled. And the Imam answered that these matters are infinite without end, but the proof is only established concerning that which has no beginning.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection that God cannot know His own knowledge because it would lead to an infinite regress of cognitions. The objectors argue that if God knows His own knowledge, then that knowledge is also known, leading to an infinite chain of known things, which is impossible since actual infinites are impossible. They also claim that such knowledge would be either possible (necessitating actual existence and deficiency if absent) or impossible. The author then rebuts a potential counterargument that God's knowledge is identical to His essence or that knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge. He argues that knowledge is a single attribute with multiple relations that are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), not external entities, so no actual multiplicity or infinity arises. The Imam (al-Razi) adds that these matters are infinite in the sense of having no end, but the proof against infinites applies only to what has no beginning, which does not affect this case. Thus the objection is repelled.

صفة واحدة
اعتبارات عقلية
تعلقات
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's knowledge has multiple relations, then there must be multiple actual attributes or infinite regress.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the relations are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), not separate external entities, so no multiplicity or infinity in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the relations (تعلقات) of God's knowledge?
A) They are actual external entities.
B) They are merely mental considerations.
C) They are infinite in number.
D) They are identical to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is a single attribute; its multiple relations are mental considerations, not external realities, so no infinite regress occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as limited like yours, with one thought after another? What would it mean for you to truly believe that His knowledge is a single, all-encompassing attribute? How would that change your moments of doubt?
?
The objector insisted that infinite regress disproves God's self-knowledge. Have you ever allowed your own limited reasoning to place a restriction on God's attributes? What would it take for you to surrender that pride and accept His perfection as described?
?
If God's knowledge includes every relation as a mere mental consideration, then nothing in your life is outside His awareness. How does that truth confront your secret sins or your hidden fears? Will you live today as if He sees everything, or will you hide again?

Answering the Objection That God Cannot Know Infinite Things
Refutes two arguments that God cannot know infinite things: distinctness and infinite attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections against God's knowledge of the infinite and the classical Ash'ari responses to them.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge of the infinite is defended by redefining distinctness and focusing on individuals.

The claim that God cannot know infinite things is a common philosophical challenge. This passage presents two arguments for this claim and their refutations. By studying this exchange, we see how Ash'ari theologians carefully distinguish between distinctness, finitude, and the nature of the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying objections and responses
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two objections introduced by 'أما أولاً' and 'أما ثانياً', and the answers beginning with 'والجواب'.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the argument.
2
Analyze the first objection and its refutation
The first objection says the infinite as a whole cannot be distinct. The response denies that distinctness implies finitude; separation simply means being other. Notice the key phrase 'إنا لا نسلم' (we do not grant).
Also note the Imam's response that each individual in the infinite is distinct.
3
Analyze the second objection and its refutation
The second objection claims that knowing infinite things would require infinite attributes. The response refers to previous arguments and clarifies that knowledge of the infinite is knowledge of its individuals, not a single distinct whole.
The alternative answer about distinctness only when there is an 'other' is also important.
4
Reflect on the key concepts
Consider how the concepts of distinctness (تميز), separation (انفصال), and individuals (أحاد) are used to resolve the problem. The whole is known through its parts.
This resolves the worry about the totality of all existents and non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : لا يجوز علمه بما لا يتناهى، أما أولاً : فلأن كل معلوم يجب كونه ممتازاً، وهو ظاهر، ولا شيء من غير المتناهي بممتاز، لأن المتميز عن الشيء منفصل عنه محدود بالضرورة. وأما ثانياً : فلأنه يلزم صفات غير متناهية هي المعلوم لما عرفت من تعدد المعلوم بتعدد المعلومات . والجواب : عن الأول إنا لا نسلم أن كل متميز عن غيره يجب أن يكون متناهياً، وأن انفصاله عن الغير يقتضي ذلك . كيف ولا معنى للانفصال عن الغير إلا مغايرته له . وعن الثاني : ما سبق وأجاب الإمام عن الأول بأن المتميز كل واحد منها وهو غير متناه . واعترض : بأنه إذا كان غير المتناهي معلوماً يجب أن يكون متميزاً، ولا يغيره تميز کل فرد. والجواب : أنه لا معنى للعلم بغير المتناهي إلا العلم بأحاده، وبهذا يندفع الإشكال على معلومية الكل، أي جميع الموجودات والمعدومات بأنه لا شيء بعد الجميع يعقل تميزه عنه. وقد يجاب أن تميز المعلوم إنما هو عند ملاحظة الغير، والشعور به فحيث لا غير لا يلزم التميز. ولم سلّم فيكفي التميز عن الغير الذي هو كل واحد من الأحاد.
And among them are those who said: His knowledge of the infinite is not permissible. First, because every known thing must be distinct, and that is obvious, and nothing of the infinite is distinct, because that which is distinct from a thing is separate from it and limited necessarily. Second, because it entails infinite attributes which are the known things, as you have known that the known thing is multiplied by the multiplicity of the knowns. The answer to the first: we do not grant that everything distinct from another must be finite, and that its separation from the other necessitates that. How so? For there is no meaning to separation from the other except its being other than it. And to the second: what has preceded. And the Imam answered the first by (saying) that each one of them is distinct, and it is infinite. And it was objected: that if the infinite is known, it must be distinct, and the distinctness of each individual does not change that. And the answer: that there is no meaning to knowledge of the infinite except knowledge of its individuals, and by this the difficulty concerning the knowability of the whole, i.e., all existents and non-existents, is repelled, because there is nothing after the whole that can be conceived as distinct from it. And it may be answered that the distinctness of the known is only when considering the other, and being aware of it; so where there is no other, distinctness is not required. And if it is not granted, then distinctness from the other which is each one of the individuals suffices.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the objection that God cannot know infinite things. The objectors give two arguments: First, every known must be distinct, but the infinite as a whole is not distinct from anything (since there is nothing outside it to distinguish it), so it cannot be known. Second, knowing infinite things would require infinite attributes (the knowns) in God, which is impossible. The response to the first argument denies that distinctness implies finitude; separation from another simply means being other than it, not necessarily being limited. The Imam responds that each individual in the infinite is distinct, and the infinite whole is simply the sum of its distinct individuals. To the objection that the whole still must be distinct, the answer is that knowledge of the infinite is knowledge of its individuals, and the whole is known through them. Alternatively, distinctness is only required when there is an other; if there is no other (as with the totality), no distinctness is needed. Thus the objections are repelled.

Key Terms
غير متناه
ممتاز
انفصال
أحاد
كل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that knowledge of an infinite whole requires the whole to be a single distinct entity separate from its parts.
Correction: Knowledge of the infinite is knowledge of its individuals; the whole is not a separate entity needing distinctness.

Checkpoint
How does the passage respond to the argument that every known must be distinct?
A) It admits the point but says God's knowledge is special.
B) It denies that distinctness requires finitude, as it simply means being other.
C) It argues that the infinite is not known at all.
D) It claims that knowledge does not require distinctness.

Key Takeaway
The infinite can be known by knowing its individuals, and distinctness does not imply finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim God knows everything, even the infinite. But do you ever find yourself thinking that God overlooks your small, repeated sins because they are too many to track? If His knowledge truly encompasses every individual moment of your life, how should that change your actions when no one is watching? What would you have to confess right now that you've been hiding, thinking it was lost in the infinite?
?
The objection says God cannot know infinite things because they would require infinite attributes. Do you sometimes limit God's knowledge by comparing it to human understanding, assuming He can't keep up with your endless worries or the complexity of your life? When you feel overwhelmed, do you trust that His knowledge is not strained by your infinite problems? If not, what concrete step can you take this week to lean on His unlimited knowledge instead of your limited perspective?
?
God knows you as one distinct individual among billions. Are you living as if you are just a number in a crowd, or as if you are personally known by the Creator of the universe? When you feel alone or insignificant, does the truth that God knows every detail about you—including your thoughts and intentions—bring you comfort or fear? How would you live differently if you truly believed that every second of your life is known and matters to Him?
