Answering the Objection About God's Knowledge of Non-Existents
Refutes the objection that God cannot know non-existent things via logical distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians refute the objection that God cannot know non-existent things by distinguishing between external and mental distinctness.

Core Idea
Non-existents can be known because distinctness in the mind suffices.

One of the classical objections to divine omniscience is that knowledge requires an object to be distinct, but non-existent things are not distinct. In this passage, Sanusi presents the objection and a precise logical refutation that saves the doctrine of God's knowledge of all things, both existent and non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part: 'And among them are those who said...' up to 'and nothing of the non-existent is distinct.' Understand the syllogism: knowledge requires distinctness, non-existents lack it, therefore God cannot know them.
Notice the logical structure: a syllogism with a minor and major premise.
2
Understand the refutation
Read the answer: 'The answer is: the minor premise is denied...' Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of 'distinct': external and mental. He shows that the objection fails under either interpretation.
This is a classic example of tackling equivocation.
3
Note the broader denial
The passage ends by mentioning other opponents who denied God's knowledge of His essence or of others. This shows the extreme consequences of rejecting the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : يمتنع علمه بالمعدوم، لأن كل معلوم متميز، ولا شيء من المعدوم بمتميز . والجواب منع الصغرى إن أريد المتميز بحسب الخارج. والكبرى إن أريد بحسب الذهن. ومن المخالفين من لم يجوز علمه بذاته، ومنهم من لم يجوز علمه بغيره تمسكاً بالشبهة المذكورة لنفي العلم مطلقاً.
And among them are those who said: His knowledge of the non-existent is impossible, because every known is distinct, and nothing of the non-existent is distinct. The answer is: the minor premise is denied if what is meant is distinctness in terms of external existence, and the major premise is denied if what is meant is distinctness in terms of the mind. And among the opponents are those who did not permit His knowledge of His essence, and among them are those who did not permit His knowledge of other than Him, clinging to the aforementioned doubt to negate knowledge absolutely.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the objection that God cannot know non-existent things. The objectors argue that every known thing must be distinct, but non-existent things are not distinct (since they have no external existence), so they cannot be known. The answer distinguishes between two types of distinctness: external (in the outside world) and mental (in the mind). If by 'distinct' they mean external distinctness, the premise that every known is externally distinct is false, because we know many things that are not externally present (like past events or mental concepts). If by 'distinct' they mean mental distinctness, then non-existent things can be distinct in the mind, so the major premise that no non-existent is distinct is false. Thus the objection fails. The passage concludes by noting that some opponents rejected God's knowledge of His essence, and others rejected His knowledge of anything else, clinging to the same doubt that was already refuted earlier in the chapter.

Key Terms
معدوم
متميز
خارج
ذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot know non-existent things because they have no external reality to be known.
Correction: Knowledge only requires mental distinctness, which non-existent things can have as objects of thought.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must a thing satisfy to be known?
A) It must be externally distinct.
B) It must be mentally distinct.
C) It must be both externally and mentally distinct.
D) It must be existent.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of non-existent things is possible because distinctness in the mind is sufficient for knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge of your future actions—things that do not yet exist—do you truly believe He knows them with complete certainty, or does your limited mind struggle to accept that? How can affirming the mental distinctness of non-existents strengthen your faith today?
?
The objection in this lesson tried to limit Allah's knowledge based on a human analogy. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously impose human limitations on Allah—perhaps in His mercy, His justice, or His knowledge of your hidden struggles? Will you consciously reject those limitations as you internalize this lesson?
?
The opponents here denied Allah's knowledge of His own essence because of a flawed argument. Is there any aspect of Allah's attributes that you find difficult to accept because it doesn't fit your mental framework? What concrete step will you take to submit your intellect to the truth revealed in this passage?

Objection about Distinguishing the Infinite and Its Answer
Author presents an objection about distinguishing the infinite and gives two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two ways that Islamic theologians answer the objection that God's knowledge of the infinite requires the infinite to be distinct from everything else.

Core Idea
Knowing the infinite means knowing its individuals, not a separate whole.

This passage addresses a tricky objection about God's knowledge of the infinite. If God knows everything, including infinite things, does He need to distinguish the infinite from others? The author presents two elegant answers that rely on understanding what 'knowledge of the infinite' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow.
Notice the objection starts with 'واعترض' and the answers with 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says: if the infinite is known, it must be distinct, but the distinction of each individual does not change the whole. What is the problem here?
3
Understand the first answer
The author says: knowledge of the infinite is nothing but knowledge of its individuals. Thus no separate distinction of the whole is needed.
This answer focuses on what 'knowledge of the infinite' means.
4
Understand the second answer
The second answer: distinction is only required when there is an 'other' to be distinct from. The infinite whole has no 'other', so no distinction is needed.
If one insists, distinction from each individual suffices.
5
Reflect on the solution
Consider how these answers defend God's knowledge without requiring the infinite to be a distinct entity separate from its parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن انفصاله عن الغير يقتضي ذلك. كيف ولا معنى للانفصال عن الغير إلا مغايرته له. وعن الثاني: ما سبق وأجاب الإمام عن الأول بأن المتميز كل واحد منها وهو غير متناه. واعترض: بأنه إذا كان غير المتناهي معلوماً يجب أن يكون متميزاً، ولا يغيره تميز كل فرد. والجواب: أنه لا معنى للعلم بغير المتناهي إلا العلم بأحاده، وبهذا يندفع الإشكال على معلومية الكل، أي جميع الموجودات والمعدومات بأنه لا شيء بعد الجميع يعقل تميزه عنه. وقد يجاب أن تميز المعلوم إنما هو عند ملاحظة الغير، والشعور به فحيث لا غير لا يلزم التميز. ولم سلّم فيكفي التميز عن الغير الذي هو كل واحد من الأحاد.
And that its separation from the other necessitates that. How? For there is no meaning to separation from the other except being different from it. And regarding the second: what preceded, and the Imam answered the first by saying that the distinct one is each one of them and it is infinite. And it was objected: that if the infinite is known, it must be distinct, and the distinction of each individual does not change it. The answer: that there is no meaning to knowledge of the infinite except knowledge of its individuals, and by this the problem is repelled regarding the knowability of the whole, i.e., all existents and non-existents, in that there is no thing after the whole by which its distinction from it could be conceived. And it may be answered that the distinction of the known is only when observing the other and being conscious of it; so where there is no other, distinction is not necessary. And if it is conceded, then distinction from the other which is each one of the individuals suffices.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning God's knowledge of the infinite. The argument begins with a premise: that separation from another implies distinction from it. The objector raises a concern: if the infinite is known, it must be distinct, but the distinction of each individual does not affect the whole. The author gives two answers. First, knowledge of the infinite is nothing but knowledge of its individuals; thus there is no need for a separate distinction of the whole beyond its parts. Second, distinction is only relevant when there is an 'other' to be distinct from; in the case of the infinite whole, there is no other, so no distinction is needed. If one insists on distinction, then distinguishing it from each individual is sufficient.

Key Terms
غير المتناهي (infinite)
تميز (distinction)
انفصال (separation)
أحاد (individuals)
جملة (whole)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing the infinite whole requires grasping it as a single distinct entity separate from its parts.
Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge of the infinite is nothing but knowledge of its individuals; the whole is not a separate object of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of knowledge of the infinite?
A) Knowledge of the infinite as a single whole distinct from its parts
B) Knowledge of its individuals only
C) Knowledge of both the whole and the individuals
D) Knowledge of the infinite as an abstract concept

Key Takeaway
The infinite is known through its individuals, and distinction only applies when there is an 'other' to distinguish from.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If knowledge of the infinite is simply knowledge of its individuals, then every single thing you know about creation is a part of knowing Allah's knowledge. Are you living as if every detail of your life is known by Allah and matters to Him? What one action will you change today to show you believe this?

Refuting the Objection on Divine Knowledge of Non-Existents
Author refutes the claim that God cannot know non-existent things because they are not distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam refutes the objection that God cannot know non-existent things by distinguishing between external and mental distinction.

Core Idea
Non-existents can be known because they are distinct in the mind, not necessarily in external reality.

Some theologians argued that God cannot know non-existent things because knowledge requires a distinct object, and non-existents lack external distinction. In this passage, the author presents a precise logical refutation by carefully defining what 'distinct' means. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance for divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection is stated, then answered by denying either the minor or major premise depending on interpretation.
The objection uses a syllogism: every known thing is distinct, no non-existent is distinct, therefore non-existents are not known.
2
Understand the two senses of 'distinct'
The author distinguishes between external distinction (in reality) and mental distinction (in the mind). Identify which premise is denied for each sense.
If 'distinct' means external, then not every known thing is externally distinct (e.g., mental concepts). If 'distinct' means mental, then some non-existents are mentally distinct.
3
See how the refutation works
By clarifying the term 'mumayyaz' (distinct), the author shows the argument is invalid: it commits a fallacy of equivocation. The opponent cannot prove God's ignorance of non-existents.
This is a classic example of logical analysis in kalam.
4
Reflect on broader implications
The passage hints that some opponents went further to deny God's knowledge of His essence or others. Consider how this refutation protects the doctrine of omniscience.
The key move is that knowledge can have mental objects without external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال: يمتنع علمه بالمعدوم، لأن كل معلوم متميز، ولا شيء من المعدوم بمتميز. والجواب منع الصغرى إن أريد المتميز بحسب الخارج. والكبرى إن أريد بحسب الذهن. ومن المخالفين من لم يجوز علمه بذاته، ومنهم من لم يجوز علمه بغيره تمسكاً بالشبهة المذكورة لنفي العلم مطلقاً.
And among them is he who said: His knowledge of the non-existent is impossible, because every known thing is distinct, and no non-existent thing is distinct. The answer is to deny the minor premise if what is meant is distinction externally, and the major premise if what is meant is distinction mentally. And among the opponents are those who did not permit His knowledge of His essence, and among them are those who did not permit His knowledge of other than Him, clinging to the aforementioned doubt to negate knowledge absolutely.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection: some argue that God cannot know non-existent things because knowledge requires the object to be distinct, but non-existents are not distinct. The author responds by distinguishing between two senses of 'distinction': external (biḥasab al-khārij) and mental (biḥasab al-dhihn). If the objector means external distinction, then the minor premise (that every known thing is externally distinct) is false, because we can know non-existents mentally. If they mean mental distinction, then the major premise (that no non-existent is mentally distinct) is false, because non-existents can be mentally distinguished. Thus the argument fails. The passage concludes by mentioning that some opponents go further to deny God's knowledge of His own essence or of others, based on similar doubts, but these are not elaborated here.

Key Terms
معدوم (non-existent)
متميز (distinct)
خارج (external)
ذهن (mind)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'distinct' can only refer to external reality, so non-existents cannot be known.
Correction: The author shows that 'distinct' also applies mentally; knowledge can be of mentally distinct objects without external existence.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that non-existents cannot be known because they are not distinct?
A) He denies that non-existents can be known at all
B) He distinguishes between external and mental distinction
C) He claims that non-existents are actually distinct externally
D) He says that knowledge does not require distinction

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge of non-existents is possible because mental distinction suffices for knowledge, refuting the objection that non-existents are not distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage shows that some extremists denied God's knowledge entirely due to this objection. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if taken to an extreme, could lead you to deny a fundamental attribute of God? How can you guard against such a slide?

Relational Change Does Not Affect Divine Essence
Author presents and refutes the philosophers' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that divine knowledge of particulars implies change in God, and the refutations that some particulars are unchanging and that relational change does not affect the essence.

Core Idea
Change in relation does not change the essence.

The philosophers argued that if God knows particulars like events in time, His knowledge would have to change as events unfold—an impossibility for the Eternal. The author counters by first noting that some particulars, like God's own essence, are unchanging. More importantly, he explains that a change in relation (iḍāfa)—such as God being prior to a created thing versus coexisting with it—does not imply any change in God Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the opening lines where the philosophers claim God cannot know particulars because it would necessitate change, with the example of Zayd entering.
Notice the phrase 'ينقلب جهلاً أو يزول إلى علم آخر'—turning into ignorance or passing away to another knowledge.
2
Identify the first refutation
Find the line that says some particulars are unchanging, like God's essence. This undercuts the claim that knowing particulars always implies change.
3
Understand the principle of relational change
Read the section that states 'تغير الإضافة لا يوجب تغير المضاف إليه'. The example of the Eternal existing before, with, and after a temporal thing illustrates that relations change without the subject changing.
This is a key logical principle in kalam.
4
Apply to knowledge
See how the author applies this to knowledge: whether knowledge is a relation or an attribute with a relation, neither the knowledge nor the essence changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعاء الفلاسفة أن الله تعالى لا يعلم الجزئيات على وجه الجزئية لاستلزامه التغير في القديم كما إذا علم أن زيداً سيدخل ثم دخل فإنه ينقلب جهلاً أو يزول إلى علم آخر. ورد بأن من الجزئيات ما لا يتغير كذات الله تعالى. وإن تغير الإضافة لا يوجب تغير المضاف إليه كالقديم يوجد قبل الحادث ثم معه ثم بعده فمن جعل العلم إضافة لم يلزمه تغير الذات ومن جعله صفة ذات إضافة لم يلزمه تغيره فضلاً عن الذات. وإلى هذا يشير ما قيل: إن علم الباري بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس علمه بأنه وجد فإن من استمر إلى الغد على أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً فهو بهذا العلم بعينه يعلم في الغد أنه دخل والعلم لا يتغير بتغير المعلوم كما لا يتكثر بمنزلة مرآة تنكشف بها الصور، وإنسان ينتقل الجالس عن يمينه إلى يساره.
The claim of the philosophers that God Almighty does not know particulars under the aspect of particularity, because it necessitates change in the Eternal, as when He knows that Zayd will enter and then he enters, then it turns into ignorance or passes away to another knowledge. And it is refuted by saying that among particulars are those that do not change, like the essence of God Almighty. And that change of relation does not necessitate change in the thing related; like the Eternal exists before the originated, then with it, then after it. So whoever makes knowledge a relation, change of essence does not follow, and whoever makes it an attribute having a relation, its change does not follow, let alone the essence. And to this refers what was said: that God's knowledge that a thing will exist is the same as His knowledge that it existed; for whoever continues until tomorrow that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, then by this very knowledge he knows tomorrow that he entered; and knowledge does not change with the change of the known, just as it does not multiply, like a mirror by which images are revealed, and a person sitting to his right moves to his left.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophers' argument that God cannot know particulars because such knowledge would entail change in God (e.g., knowing that Zayd will enter, then knowing that he has entered). The author gives several responses. First, some particulars are unchanging, such as God's essence. Second, change in relation (iḍāfa) does not imply change in the subject: for example, the Eternal exists before a temporal thing, with it, and after it – the relation changes but God does not. If knowledge is a relation, then no change in essence; if it is an attribute with a relation, still no change in the attribute or essence. Third, knowledge of future existence is identical to knowledge of past existence: one who knows that Zayd will enter tomorrow, on the next day knows by that same knowledge that he entered. Knowledge does not change with the change of the known, nor does it multiply; it is like a mirror that reveals images despite the images changing.

Key Terms
جزئيات (particulars)
تغير (change)
قديم (Eternal)
إضافة (relation)
علم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot know changing particulars because His knowledge would have to change.
Correction: Change in the known object only changes the relation, not God's knowledge or essence. Some particulars are unchanging, and relational change does not affect the subject.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does a change in relation (iḍāfa) cause?
A) A change in the essence of the related subject
B) No change in the essence of the related subject
C) A change in the attribute of knowledge only
D) A change in the known object

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that divine knowledge of particulars leads to change is refuted by the principle that relational change does not affect the eternal essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your circumstances shift — a loss, a gain, a sudden turn — do you unconsciously imagine that God's knowledge of you has changed? This lesson says relational change does not affect the Knower. Can you let that truth settle the turbulence in your heart right now?
?
You know that God knows your past, present, and future. But do you live as though He knows your *today* differently than He knew your *yesterday*? If relational change does not touch His essence, what does that demand from your trust in Him this very moment?
?
The philosophers worried that God’s knowledge of changing events would make Him change. But the author says no — the relation changes, not the essence. Now ask yourself: when you witness change in your life, do you attribute instability to God's will? How can you anchor your soul in His unchanging knowledge today, even as everything around you shifts?

The Unity of Divine Knowledge: Mirror Analogy
Author argues for the identity of divine knowledge of future and past and uses analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge of a thing before it exists is identical to His knowledge after it exists, as illustrated by the mirror analogy and the example of a person knowing Zayd's future entry.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future and past is one and the same.

In this passage, the author deepens his refutation by arguing that divine knowledge does not change even from the standpoint of the object known. He explains that if someone knows today that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, that same knowledge tomorrow is knowledge that Zayd has entered. Knowledge remains identical despite the change in the known event. The mirror analogy makes this vividly clear: a mirror does not change when different images are reflected in it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument of identity
Read the sentence: 'إن علم الباري بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس علمه بأنه وجد'. This is the core claim: knowledge of future existence is the same as knowledge of past existence.
2
Examine the human analogy
Consider the example: someone who knows today that Zayd will enter tomorrow, by that same knowledge knows tomorrow that he entered. This illustrates how knowledge does not change.
The phrase 'بهذا العلم بعينه' emphasizes the sameness.
3
Understand the general principle
The author states: 'العلم لا يتغير بتغير المعلوم كما لا يتكثر' — knowledge does not change with the change of the known, nor does it multiply.
This is the logical conclusion drawn from the analogy.
4
Analyze the mirror analogy
The mirror remains unchanged while revealing different images. This shows that a knower can have unchanging knowledge of changing objects.
The additional example of the person moving from right to left reinforces the point.

Abu al-Husayn's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Divine Knowledge
Abu al-Husayn presents two contradictions in the Mu'tazila view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's two arguments against the Mu'tazila view that God's knowledge of future events is identical to His knowledge of them after they occur.

Core Idea
Future knowledge and present knowledge have different conditions.

The Mu'tazila argued that God's knowledge of future particulars is the same as His knowledge of them after they happen. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri refutes this by showing that such a view leads to contradictions. In this passage, he presents two clear objections that expose the logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Focus on the structure: it opens with a general statement then presents two arguments.
The passage uses first… second… (أولاً… وثانياً…) to organize the refutation.
2
Identify the two arguments
Notice that Abu al-Husayn gives two distinct reasons. The first is an example involving a person in a dark room. The second is a logical point about conditions being contradictory.
3
Understand the first argument
Think about the person who expects Zayd to enter tomorrow but is in a dark room and cannot see. Does he know that Zayd actually entered? No. This shows that future expectation is not the same as present knowledge.
The argument relies on the idea that if knowledge were identical, the expectation would suffice for knowledge of the event's occurrence.
4
Understand the second argument
Knowledge that something exists requires that it exists; knowledge that it will exist requires that it does not exist yet. These conditions are contradictory, so the two knowledges cannot be the same.
Otherwise, God would simultaneously know something as existing and not existing, which would be ignorance.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
This refutation defends the distinction between God's eternal knowledge and temporal events. It shows that divine knowledge must accommodate different temporal modalities.
This ties into broader discussions of God's knowledge of particulars and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولظهور أن هذا لا يصح على القول بكون العلم تعلقاً بأن العالم والمعلوم رد أبو الحسين على ما قال به من المعتزلة: أولاً: بأن من استمر على أن زيداً يدخل البلد غداً وجلس في بيت مظلم بحيث لم يعلم دخول الغد لم يكن عالماً بأنه دخل. وثانياً: بأن متعلقهما مختلفان وشرطهما متنافيان إذ العلم بأنه وجد مشروط بوجوده وبأنه سيوجد مشروط بعدمه وإلا لكان جهلاً.
And because it is apparent that this is not valid on the view that knowledge is a relation between the knower and the known, Abu al-Husayn refuted what the Mu'tazila said: First, that whoever continues that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and sits in a dark room such that he does not know the entering of tomorrow, he is not knowledgeable that he entered. Second, that their objects are different and their conditions are contradictory, for knowledge that it existed is conditioned by its existence, and knowledge that it will exist is conditioned by its non-existence, otherwise it would be ignorance.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Husayn's refutation of the Mu'tazila position on God's knowledge of particulars. The Mu'tazila likely argued that God's knowledge of future events is identical to His knowledge of them after they occur. Abu al-Husayn gives two objections. First, a person who expects Zayd to enter tomorrow but is in a dark room and cannot see, does not know that Zayd actually entered – thus future knowledge is not the same as present knowledge. Second, the objects of knowledge (mutā'alliqān) are different: knowledge of existence is conditioned by actual existence, while knowledge of future existence is conditioned by non-existence. If these conditions are not distinguished, it would lead to ignorance. Thus the view that knowledge of future and present are identical is invalid.

Key Terms
تعلق (relation)
معلوم (known)
شرط (condition)
متعلق (object)
جهل (ignorance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing a future event is the same as knowing it after it occurs.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn shows they have different conditions: knowledge of future requires non-existence, knowledge of past requires existence.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument Abu al-Husayn uses against the Mu'tazila?
A) The objects of knowledge are the same but conditions differ
B) The objects and conditions of knowledge are different and contradictory
C) Knowledge of the future is impossible without revelation
D) God cannot know temporal things at all

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn demonstrates that knowledge of a future event and knowledge of its occurrence have contradictory conditions, thus cannot be identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anticipate something happening in your life, do you treat your knowledge of that future event as if it were already certain? How might the lesson's distinction between conditions of future and present knowledge challenge your assumption? Examine your heart: are you truly certain, or are you conflating hope with knowledge? Commit to distinguishing between what you know and what you expect.
?
Consider a time you claimed to know something would happen but it did not. Were you genuinely certain, or did you confuse a strong feeling with knowledge? How does Abu al-Husayn's argument about contradictory conditions reveal a flaw in your reasoning? Take a moment to repent for any unwarranted certainty about the unseen, and resolve to speak only with knowledge validated by proper conditions.
?
Have you ever assumed that your knowledge of a past event is the same type as your knowledge of a future one? Reflect on how the contradictory conditions shown in this lesson might apply to your own judgments about God's knowledge. Do you unknowingly project your own limited knowledge onto Him? Ask Allah to grant you humility in acknowledging His unique knowledge, which transcends created conditions.

The Philosophers' Claim and the Rebuttal from Immutability of Relations
Presents the philosophers' argument and a rebuttal based on immutability of relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God cannot know particulars and the first rebuttal based on the immutability of relations.

Core Idea
Change in relation does not necessitate change in the Essence.

The philosophers argue that God's knowledge of changing particulars would require change in God, which is impossible. But how do we respond? This lesson presents the classical rebuttal: relational change does not affect the essence. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the philosophers' claim and the rebuttal.
Pay attention to the key terms: علم, جزئيات, تغير, إضافة.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Notice how they argue that knowledge of particular changing things would lead to change in God. Find the example with Zayd entering.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Focus on the idea that change in relation does not imply change in the related entity. Consider the example of the Eternal existing before, with, and after temporal events.
This is the core idea of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the mirror analogy
Read the analogy of the mirror that reveals changing images without changing itself. Consider how it illustrates that the knower remains unchanged while the known changes.
Also note the potential limitation mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعاء الفلاسفة أن الله تعالى لا يعلم الجزئيات قال والفلاسفة في العلم بالجزئيات. على وجه الجزئية لاستلزامه التغير في القديم كما إذا علم أن زيداً سيدخل ثم دخل فإنه ينقلب جهلاً أو يزول إلى علم آخر، ورد بأن من الجزئيات ما لا يتغير كذات الله تعالى . وإن تغير الإضافة لا يوجب تغير المضاف إليه كالقديم يوجد قبل الحادث ثم معه ثم بعده فمن جعل العلم إضافة لم يلزمه تغير الذات ومن جعله صفة ذات إضافة لم يلزمه تغيره فضلاً عن الذات، وإلى هذا يشير ما قيل : إن علم الباري بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس علمه بأنه وجد فإن من استمر إلى الغد على أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً فهو بهذا العلم بعينه يعلم في الغد أنه دخل والعلم لا يتغير بتغير المعلوم كما لا يتكثر بمنزلة مرآة تنكشف بها الصور، وإنسان ينتقل الجالس عن يمينه إلى يساره. ولظهوراً [؟] أن هذا لا يصح على القول) بكون العلم تعلقاً بأن العالم والمعلوم
The claim of the philosophers that God Most High does not know particulars. He said: And the philosophers are in [discussion] about knowledge of particulars in terms of particularity, because it necessitates change in the Eternal, as when He knows that Zayd will enter, then he entered, then it turns into ignorance or changes to another knowledge. And it was replied that among particulars there is what does not change, like the essence of God Most High. And that change of relation does not necessitate change of the related entity, like the Eternal exists before the temporal event, then with it, then after it. So whoever makes knowledge a relation is not bound to change of the essence, and whoever makes it an attribute with relation is not bound to its change, let alone the essence. And to this points what was said: that the knowledge of the Creator that a thing will exist is the very knowledge that it has existed. For whoever persists until tomorrow that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, then with this very knowledge he knows tomorrow that he entered. And knowledge does not change with the change of the known, just as it does not multiply, like a mirror by which images are revealed, and a person sitting to his right moves to his left. And it is apparent that this is not valid on the theory that knowledge is a relation in the world and the known.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that God cannot know particulars in their particularity because such knowledge would involve change in the Eternal—if He knows Zayd will enter, then Zayd enters, knowledge either turns into ignorance or changes to another knowledge. The rebuttal refutes this by noting that some particulars, like God's own essence, are unchanging. More importantly, change in relation does not imply change in the related subject. For example, the Eternal exists before, with, and after temporal events without change. Thus if knowledge is a relation (idafa), change in the relation does not necessitate change in the essence. If knowledge is an attribute involving relation, it does not necessitate change in the attribute, let alone the essence. The passage then supports this with the analogy of a mirror: a mirror reveals changing images without itself changing. Similarly, God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of its occurrence—the knowledge does not change even if the known object changes. The final line notes a possible objection: this analogy may not hold if knowledge is considered a relational tie to both the knowing subject and the object.

Key Terms
علم
جزئيات
تغير
إضافة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Abu al-Husayn's Three Objections on Divine Knowledge
Abu al-Husayn presents three detailed objections to the Mu'tazilite view on God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's three arguments that distinguish knowledge of future events from knowledge of present/past events, and why they challenge the Mu'tazilite view of identical divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Future and present knowledge have contradictory conditions – they cannot be identical.

Abu al-Husayn, a prominent Mutakallim, offers three incisive objections to the Mu'tazilite claim that God's knowledge of future and present events is the same. Each objection highlights a logical or experiential distinction between knowing that something will happen and knowing that it has happened. The passage presents these objections concisely, so careful reading helps grasp the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of Abu al-Husayn's three objections. Notice the clear enumeration: 'First', 'Second', 'Third'.
The objections are short and dense, so read them slowly.
2
Analyze each objection
For each objection, identify the key scenario or logical point. The first uses a dark house example, the second contrasts conditions of existence vs. non-existence, the third shows potential separation of the two types of knowledge.
3
Synthesize the overall argument
Consider how the three objections together refute the Mu'tazilite position. They demonstrate that knowledge of future and present cannot be identical because their truth conditions differ and they can occur independently.
This connects to broader debates on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رد أبو الحسين على ما قال به من المعتزلة :أولاً : بأن من استمر على أن زيداً يدخل البلد غداً وجلس في بيت مظلم بحيث لم يعلم دخول الغد لم يكن عالماً بأنه دخل . وثانياً: بأن متعلقهما مختلفان وشرطهما متنافيان إذ العلم بأنه وجد مشروط بوجوده وبأنه سيوجد مشروط بعدمه وإلا لكان جهلاً. وثالثاً : بأنهما قد يفترقان كما إذا علم أن زيداً سيقدم وعند قدومه لم يعلم أنه قدم وبالعكس .
Abu al-Husayn responded to what was said by the Mu'tazila: First, that whoever persists that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and sits in a dark house such that he does not know the arrival of tomorrow is not knowing that he entered. Second, that their two relata differ and their conditions are mutually exclusive, for knowledge that it has existed is conditioned by its existence, and knowledge that it will exist is conditioned by its non-existence; otherwise it would be ignorance. Third, that the two may separate, as when one knows that Zayd will arrive, and at his arrival he does not know that he has arrived, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage contains Abu al-Husayn's three objections against the Mu'tazilite position that God's knowledge of future and present events is identical. The first objection: if someone persists that Zayd will enter tomorrow but is in a dark house and does not actually know the moment of entry, he cannot be said to know that Zayd has entered. The second objection: the objects of knowledge (future existence vs. past existence) are different, and their conditions are contradictory—knowledge of existence requires existence, while knowledge of future existence requires non-existence. Treating them as identical would be ignorance. The third objection: the two types of knowledge can separate; one might know that Zayd will arrive but not know when he actually arrives, or know that he arrived without prior knowledge. This disproves the claimed identity.

أبو الحسين
معتزلة
مشروط

Correction: Abu al-Husayn shows they have different conditions (existence vs. non-existence) and can occur independently, so they cannot be identical.

Checkpoint

B) Because the conditions for each are mutually exclusive (existence vs. non-existence).
C) Because God's knowledge is unchanging.
D) Because the Mu'tazila reject God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn's objections demonstrate that knowledge of future events and knowledge of past/present events are logically distinct because their truth conditions differ and they can occur independently.

Are you willing to trust His decree even when your own foresight fails you? What is one decision today where you will consciously submit to His knowledge over your own?

The Philosophers' Doctrine on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Explains the philosophers' doctrine that God knows particulars universally, unaffected by time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God knows particulars in a universal, timeless manner, avoiding change in His knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of changing things does not change because He knows them timelessly.

The philosophers grappled with how an unchanging God can know changing particulars. Their solution distinguishes between knowing things as changing particulars (impossible) and knowing them in a universal, timeless way (possible). This passage lays out that distinction and defends it with an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the author begins with a statement, then provides a second point marked by 'ثانياً'.
Pay attention to the distinction between knowing as particulars and knowing universally.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two key claims: first, that God cannot know particulars as particulars; second, that He knows them in a universal way not tied to time. Find the phrase that introduces the second claim.
Look for the word 'ثانياً'.
3
Understand the example
The moon and eclipse example illustrates how God's knowledge of a temporal event remains constant before, during, and after the event. Reflect on how this shows timeless knowledge.
The example shows that God knows the eclipse at all times without change.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The passage concludes that attaching knowledge to a temporal thing does not make knowledge temporal. Consider why this is important for defending God's immutability.
This conclusion resolves the dilemma about change in God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والفلاسفة في العلم بالجزئيات المشهور من مذهبهم . أنه يمتنع علمه بالجزئيات على وجه كونها جزئيات . أي من حيث كونها جزئيات يلحقها التغير، لأن تغير المعلوم يستلزم تغير العلم، وهو على الله تعالى محال في ذاته وصفاته. وأما من حيث انها غير متعلقة بزمان فتعلقها يعقل بوجه كلي لا يلحقه التغير، فالله تعالى يعلم جميع الحوادث الجزئية، وأزمنتها الواقعة هي فيها، لا من حيث أن بعضها واقع الان وبعضها في الزمان الماضي، وبعضها في الزمان المستقبل . ليلزم تغيره بحسب تغير الماضي والحال والمستقبل بلا علماً . ثانياً : أبد الدهر غير داخل تحت الأزمنة. مثلاً يعلم أن القمر يتحرك كل يوم كذا درجة والشمس كذا درجة. فيعلم أنه يحصل لهما مقابلة في يوم كذا، وينخسف القمر في أول الحمل مثلاً ، وهذا العلم ثابت له، حال المقليلة وقبلها وبعدها، ليس في علمه كان ، وكائن، ويكون ، بل هي حاضرة عنده في أوقاتها أزلاً وأبداً، وإنما التعلق بالأزمنة في علومنا والحاصل أن علق العلم بالشيء الزماني المتغير لا يلزم أن يكون زمانياً ليلزم تغيره .
He said: And the philosophers, the well-known from their doctrine concerning knowledge of particulars: that His knowledge of particulars in terms of their being particulars is impossible, i.e. insofar as they are particulars subject to change, because change of the known necessitates change of knowledge, and that is impossible for God Most High in His essence and attributes. But insofar as they are not related to time, their relation is conceived in a universal manner not affected by change. So God Most High knows all temporal events and their times that occur in them, not insofar as some are occurring now and some in past time and some in future time, lest His change follow the change of past, present, and future without knowledge. Secondly: the eternity of time is not included under times. For example, He knows that the moon moves each day such-and-such degrees and the sun such-and-such degrees. So He knows that a conjunction occurs between them on such-and-such a day, and the moon eclipses at the beginning of Aries for example. And this knowledge is constant for Him, at the time of conjunction, before it, and after it. In His knowledge there is no 'was', 'is', and 'will be', rather they are present with Him in their times eternally and forever. And the relation to times is only in our sciences. The conclusion is that the attachment of knowledge to a temporal changing thing does not necessitate that it be temporal, so that change would follow.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' well-known position on God's knowledge of particulars. They hold that God cannot know particulars precisely as particulars (i.e., as changing individuals) because that would entail change in His knowledge, which is impossible. However, He can know them in a universal way, not tied to specific times, so His knowledge remains unchanging. God knows all events and their times without the limitations of past, present, or future. The passage then adds a second point: 'the eternity of time' (abad al-dahr) is not subject to temporal divisions. Using the example of the moon's movement and an eclipse, it explains that God's knowledge of such events is constant before, during, and after their occurrence. God's knowledge has no temporal tenses ('was', 'is', 'will be'); all events are present to Him eternally. Finally, it concludes that attaching knowledge to a temporal changing thing does not require that knowledge itself be temporal and thereby subject to change.

أزلاً وأبداً

Correction: The passage argues that God knows changeable things in a timeless, universal way, so His knowledge remains unchanged.

Al-Imam's Argument on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Al-Imam enumerates two objections against God's knowledge of changing and composite particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Imam argues that God cannot know changing or composite particulars based on divine immutability and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot involve change or dependence.

Al-Imam presents a crisp argument against the philosophers' claim that God knows all particulars. He identifies two types of particulars that would force unacceptable consequences: change in God's knowledge (if the particular changes) or neediness in God (if the particular is composite). This lesson unpacks these two objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the argument is complete in thought.
2
Identify the two types of particulars
Notice that al-Imam mentions two cases: changing (mutaghayyir) and composite (mutashakkil). Highlight these terms.
3
Understand the consequences
For each case, identify why it leads to impossibility: change in God's knowledge for the first, neediness for the second.
Consider how these consequences contradict God's attributes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Though the sentence ends abruptly, the argument implies that God's knowledge cannot encompass such particulars. Consider how this challenges the philosophers' position.
This argument is part of a larger debate on divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام: إن اللائق بأصولهم ، أن الجزء إن كان متغيراً، أو متشكلاً يمتنع أن يتعلق به علم الواجب لما يلزم في الأول من تغير العلم وفي الثاني من الافتقار إلى
And al-Imam said: Indeed, consistent with their principles, if the particular is changing or composite, it is impossible that knowledge of the Necessary Being attach to it, because of what is necessitated in the first case of change in knowledge, and in the second case of neediness for
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a statement from al-Imam (likely Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). He argues that according to the philosophers' own principles, if a particular (juz') is either changing (mutaghayyir) or composite (mutashakkil), it cannot be an object of God's knowledge. In the case of a changing particular, knowledge would change, which is impossible for God. In the case of a composite particular, God's knowledge would depend on its parts, implying neediness (iftiqar). The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: God's immutability and self-sufficiency preclude knowledge of certain types of particulars.

A) It would cause change in God's knowledge.
B) It would imply neediness (iftiqar) in God.
C) Composite particulars do not exist.
D) God has no knowledge of anything composite.

Key Takeaway
Al-Imam argues that God's knowledge cannot attach to changing or composite particulars because it would entail change or neediness in God.

The Philosophers on God's Knowledge of Particulars
Author explains the philosophers' view that God cannot know changing particulars directly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position that God knows all particular events in a universal, timeless way, without change in His knowledge.

Core Idea
God knows everything, but not through temporal change.

The philosophers propose a nuanced view: God cannot know changing particulars in their particularity, but He knows them in a universal way. This passage explains their reasoning and provides an example to clarify how God's knowledge remains constant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the general principle to the example.
Pay attention to the distinction between knowing particulars as particulars and knowing them universally.
2
Understand the main argument
The philosophers argue that God cannot know changing particulars because that would entail change in His knowledge. However, He can know them from a universal, timeless perspective.
The key is that God's knowledge is not subject to temporal categories.
3
Analyze the example
The example of the moon and sun illustrates how God's knowledge of events like eclipses is constant, not changing with time.
Notice that God's knowledge has no 'was', 'is', or 'will be'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that knowing a temporal thing does not make God's knowledge temporal. This preserves divine transcendence.
This is a common philosophical solution to the problem of God's knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والفلاسفة في العلم بالجزئيات المشهور من مذهبهم أنه يمتنع علمه بالجزئيات على وجه كونها جزئيات أي من حيث كونها جزئيات يلحقها التغير، لأن تغير المعلوم يستلزم تغير العلم، وهو على الله تعالى محال في ذاته وصفاته. وأما من حيث انها غير متعلقة بزمان فتعلقها يعقل بوجه كلي لا يلحقه التغير، فالله تعالى يعلم جميع الحوادث الجزئية، وأزمنتها الواقعة هي فيها، لا من حيث أن بعضها واقع الان وبعضها في الزمان الماضي، وبعضها في الزمان المستقبل ليلزم تغيره بحسب تغير الماضي والحال والمستقبل بلا [؟] علماً. ثانياً : أبد الدهر غير داخل تحت الأزمنة. مثلاً يعلم أن القمر يتحرك كل يوم كذا درجة والشمس كذا درجة. فيعلم أنه يحصل لهما مقابلة في يوم كذا، وينخسف القمر في أول الحمل مثلاً، وهذا العلم ثابت له، حال المقابلة وقبلها وبعدها، ليس في علمه كان، وكائن، ويكون، بل هي حاضرة عنده في أوقاتها أزلاً وأبداً، وإنما التعلق بالأزمنة في علومنا والحاصل أن علق العلم بالشيء الزماني المتغير لا يلزم أن يكون زمانياً ليلزم تغيره.
And the philosophers said concerning knowledge of particulars: the well-known from their doctrine is that it is impossible for His knowledge of particulars in the aspect of their being particulars, i.e., insofar as they are particulars that are subject to change, because change in the known necessitates change in knowledge, and that is impossible for God Almighty in His essence and attributes. But insofar as they are not related to time, their relation can be conceived in a universal manner that is not subject to change. So God Almighty knows all particular events and their times in which they occur, not insofar as some are now, some in the past, and some in the future, so that His knowledge would change according to the change of past, present, and future without [؟] knowledge. Second: the eternal duration is not included under times. For example, He knows that the moon moves each day such-and-such degrees and the sun such-and-such degrees. So He knows that there will be an opposition between them on such-and-such day, and that the moon will be eclipsed at the beginning of Aries, for instance. And this knowledge is constant for Him, at the time of opposition, before it, and after it. In His knowledge there is not 'was', 'is', and 'will be'; rather they are present with Him at their times eternally. Rather, the relation to times is only in our knowledges. The upshot: that the attachment of knowledge to a temporal changing thing does not necessitate that it be temporal so that change would be necessitated.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' well-known doctrine concerning God's knowledge of particulars. They argue that God cannot know particulars insofar as they are changing particulars, because change in the known would necessitate change in knowledge, which is impossible for God. However, they allow that God knows them from a universal perspective not tied to temporal change. For instance, God knows all particular events and their times without the distinction of past, present, future that would imply change. Then the author adds a second point: eternal duration is not under time. He gives the example of God's eternal knowledge of the moon's motion and the eclipse, which is constant and not temporal. The conclusion: knowing a temporal thing does not make God's knowledge temporal.

The Imam's Classification of Particulars: Changing, Shaped, and Divine Knowledge
Imam classifies particulars by change and shape to determine divine knowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam classifies particulars based on change and shape, and why this classification determines the possibility of divine knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge extends only to unchanging, shapeless particulars.

This passage from the Imam presents a key philosophical principle: divine knowledge cannot grasp particulars that are changing or shaped, as that would entail change in God or reliance on bodily instruments. The Imam illustrates this with examples from the cosmos, then clarifies that God does know unchanging particulars like His own essence and the intellects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once, noting the logical flow from classification to conclusion.
2
Understand the two conditions
Identify the two prohibitive conditions (changing and shaped) and why each makes knowledge impossible.
Changing things would cause change in God's knowledge; shaped things would require a bodily instrument.
3
Examine the examples
See how celestial bodies, accidents/forms, and generated bodies fit the classification.
Celestial bodies are shaped but not changing; forms/accidents are changing; generated bodies are both.
4
Recognize the exception
Note the category of unchanging, shapeless particulars (God's essence and intellects) that God necessarily knows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام: إن اللائق بأصولهم أن الجزء إن كان متغيراً أو متشكلاً يمتنع أن يتعلق به علم الواجب لما يلزم في الأول من تغير العلم وفي الثاني من الافتقار إلى الآلة الجسمانية، وذلك كالأجرام الفلكية فإنها متشكلة وإن لم تكن متغيرة في ذواتها وكالصور والأعراض فإنها متغيرة وكالأجرام الكائنة الفاسدة فإنها متغيرة ومتشكلة، وأما ما ليس بمتغير ولا متشكل كذات الواجب وذوات المجردات فلا يستحيل بل يجب العلم به على ما يقرره الحكماء من أنه عالم بذاته الذي هو مبدأ العقل الأول بالذات ولا شك أن كلا منهما جزئي
And the Imam said: What is befitting to their principles is that if the particular is changing or shaped, it is impossible for knowledge of the Necessary Being to be attached to it, because of what is entailed in the first case of change in knowledge, and in the second case of need for a bodily instrument. That is like the celestial bodies, for they are shaped though not changing in their essences; and like forms and accidents, for they are changing; and like the generated and corruptible bodies, for they are changing and shaped. But as for what is neither changing nor shaped, like the essence of the Necessary Being and the essences of the intellects, it is not impossible, rather knowledge of it is necessary, according to what the philosophers establish, that He is knower of His essence which is the origin of the first intellect by essence, and there is no doubt that each of them is particular.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage contains the Imam's classification of particulars based on change and shape. He states that according to the philosophers' principles, if a particular is changing (متغير) or shaped (متشكل), it is impossible for God's knowledge to pertain to it. The reason: for changing things, knowledge would change; for shaped things, knowledge would require a bodily instrument (since shaping involves extension). Examples: celestial bodies are shaped but not changing in essence; forms and accidents are changing; generated bodies are both. But things that are neither changing nor shaped, like God's essence and the essences of intellects (the abstract intellects), can be known by God. Indeed, philosophers affirm God's knowledge of His own essence, which is the origin of the first intellect, and each of these is particular.

Key Terms
متغير
متشكل
آلة جسمانية
أجرام فلكية
مجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Imam agrees with philosophers that God cannot know any particulars.
Correction: The Imam distinguishes: God cannot know changing or shaped particulars, but He necessarily knows unchanging, shapeless particulars like His essence and the intellects.

Checkpoint
According to the Imam, which of the following can God know?
A) Celestial bodies
B) Forms and accidents
C) Generated bodies
D) The essences of intellects

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge encompasses unchanging, shapeless particulars but not changing or shaped ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge does not extend to your changing circumstances, does that make you feel abandoned or free? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on God's detailed awareness of your daily struggles?
?
The Imam says God cannot know shaped particulars. Are you comfortable with a God whose knowledge is not bound by physical limitations, or do you find yourself wishing for a God who sees your physical form and suffering? How does this affect your intimacy with Him?
?
Given that God necessarily knows His own essence and the essences of intellects, do you strive to connect with these unchanging, spiritual realities rather than being consumed by the changing and shaped world? What would change if you truly believed this?

The Philosophers' Argument and the Answer on Divine Knowledge
Philosophers' core argument using Zayd's example and the answer distinguishing unchanging particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' core argument against God's knowledge of changing particulars and the theological response that defends divine omniscience without implying change in God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of changing particulars must be understood without implying change in His essence.

The philosophers argue that if God knows a future event like Zayd's entry, then when it happens, His knowledge must either become false (ignorance) or change—both impossible for the Eternal. This lesson unpacks that argument and its answer, showing how the theologians defend God's knowledge of all particulars without compromising His immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage twice: first to get the overall argument, then to follow the logical flow. Pay attention to the dilemma posed about Zayd's entry.
The passage contains both the argument and a potential objection, so read to the end.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The philosophers claim that if God knew Zayd would enter, then after entry either God's knowledge remains (becoming false/ignorance) or changes (becoming true but impossible for the Eternal). Identify these two options.
The two horns are explicitly stated: 'فإن نفى' and 'وإن زال'.
3
Examine the objection and the answer
The author raises a possible objection: that absence of matching belief is also ignorance. Then the answer is given: some particulars (like God's essence and attributes) are unchanging, so the proof does not apply. Notice how the answer limits the scope.
The answer distinguishes between changing and unchanging particulars.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters in kalām. How does it defend God's omniscience while preserving His immutability? The passage also hints at a universal-aspect interpretation.
This connects to the learning objective about defending divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمدة في احتجاج الفلاسفة أنه لو علم أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً فإذا دخل زيد الدار في الغد، فإن نفى العلم بحاله بمعنى أنه يعلم أن زيداً يدخل غداً فهو جهل لكونه غير مطابق للواقع، وإن زال وحصل العلم بأنه دخل لزم التغير للعلم الأول من الوجود إلى العدم والثاني من العدم إلى الوجود وهذا على القديم محال. لا يقال كما أن الاعتقاد الغير مطابق جهل فكذا الخلو عن الاعتقاد المطابق بما هو واقع. لأنا نقول لو سلّم فإذا لم يعلمه على وجه كلي. والجواب: إن من الجزئيات ما لا يتغير كذات الباري تعالى وصفاته الحقيقية عند من يثبتها وكذوات العقول فلا يتناولها الدليل، وتخصيص الحكم بالبعض على ما يشير إليه كلام الإمام إنما يصح في القواعد الشرعية دون العقلية، ولما أمكن البعض التقصي عن هذا بأنه يجوز أن يكون المدعي العام هو أنه لا يعلم شيئاً من المتغيرات، أو أن يبين الامتناع في الجزئيات المتغيرة بهذا الدليل وفي غير المتغيرة بدليل آخر، وأن يقصدوا إبطال كلام الخصم وهو أنه عالم بجميع الجزئيات على وجه الجزئية.
And the core of the philosophers' argument is that if He knew that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, then when Zayd entered the house the next day, if the knowledge of his state is negated, meaning that He knows that Zayd will enter tomorrow, then it is ignorance because it does not correspond to reality; and if it ceases and knowledge that he entered occurs, then change in the first knowledge is necessitated, from existence to non-existence, and the second from non-existence to existence, and this is impossible for the Eternal. It should not be said: just as belief that does not correspond is ignorance, so also the absence of corresponding belief regarding what is actual. For we say: even if granted, then if He does not know it from a universal aspect. And the answer: that among particulars there are those that do not change, like the essence of God Almighty and His real attributes according to those who affirm them, and like the essences of intellects, so the proof does not apply to them. And specification of the ruling to some, as indicated by the Imam's words, is only valid in legal principles, not rational ones. And perhaps some may examine this by saying it is possible that the general claim is that He does not know any of the changing things, or that they may demonstrate the impossibility in changing particulars by this proof, and in non-changing ones by another proof, and that they intend to refute the opponent's statement that He knows all particulars as particulars.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the main argument (العمدة) of the philosophers and the answer. The argument: if God knew Zayd would enter tomorrow, then when Zayd actually enters, either God's knowledge remains (and becomes false, hence ignorance) or it changes (and becomes true, but change in eternal knowledge is impossible). The author then raises a possible objection: maybe it's like absence of matching belief is ignorance. But the answer: even if that objection is accepted, the real answer is that some particulars, like God's essence and attributes and intellects, are unchanging, so the proof does not apply. The philosophers might claim they only deny knowledge of changing particulars, and the opponent's claim that God knows all particulars as particulars is refuted.

Key Terms

جهل
تغير
قديم
وجه كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God cannot know any particulars at all.
Correction: The argument only applies to changing particulars; there are unchanging particulars (e.g., God's essence) that God knows without change. The theologians also argue that God knows changing particulars from a universal aspect.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' core argument concerning God's knowledge of Zayd's entrance tomorrow?

B) God's knowledge would either be false or change, both impossible
C) God's knowledge is eternal and thus cannot know temporal events
D) God knows all events but does not care

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of particulars does not entail change because some particulars are inherently unchanging, and His knowledge of changing ones is from a universal aspect.

You learned that some particulars (like God's essence) are unchanging. What does this teach you about where your ultimate security and stability should be sought—in the changing world or in the unchanging God? Unchanging Particulars Are Knowable
Al-Taftazani distinguishes unchanging particulars as knowable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some particulars, such as the essence of God and the immaterial intellects, are unchanging and therefore their knowledge by God is not problematic for the philosophers' argument.

Core Idea
Not all particulars change; some are static and fully knowable.

The philosophers' argument against divine knowledge of particulars only targets changing particulars. But some particulars, like God's own essence and the intellects, are unchanging. Al-Taftazani sets this as a crucial starting point before presenting the main argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the idea that some particulars are unchanging.
2
Identify the unchanging particulars
Note the two examples given: the essence of the Necessary Being and the essences of the immaterial intellects.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter? It shows the philosophers' argument does not apply to these particulars.
This is the foundation for the answer later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ليس بمتغير ولا متشكل كذات الواجب وذوات المجردات فلا يستحيل بل يجب العلم به على ما يقرره الحكماء من أنه عالم بذاته الذي هو مبدأ العقل الأول بالذات ولا شك أن كلا منهما جزئي والعمدة في احتجاج الفلاسفة أنه لو علم أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً فإذا دخل زيد الدار في الغد فإن نفى العلم بحاله بمعنى أنه يعلم أن زيداً يدخل غداً فهو جهل لكونه غير مطابق للواقع وإن زال وحصل العلم بأنه دخل لزم التغير للعلم الأول من الوجود إلى العدم والثاني من العدم إلى الوجود وهذا على القديم محال لا يقال كما أن الاعتقاد الغير مطابق جهل فكذا الخلو عن الاعتقاد المطابق بما هو واقع لأنا نقول لوسلّم فإذا لم يعلمه على وجه كلي والجواب إن من الجزئيات ما لا يتغير كذات الباري تعالى وصفاته الحقيقية عند من يثبتها وكذوات العقول فلا يتناولها الدليل وتخصيص الحكم بالبعض على ما يشير إليه كلام الإمام إنما يصح في القواعد الشرعية دون العقلية ولما أمكن البعض التقصي عن هذا بأنه يجوز أن يكون المدعي العام هو أنه لا يعلم شيئاً من المتغيرات أو أن يبين الامتناع في الجزئيات المتغيرة بهذا الدليل وفي غير المتغيرة بدليل آخر وأن يقصدوا إبطال كلام الخصم وهو أنه عالم بجميع الجزئيات على وجه الجزئية
And as for that which is not changing nor shaped, like the essence of the Necessary Being and the essences of the immaterial intellects, it is not impossible, rather knowledge of it is necessary according to what the philosophers determine: that He is knowing by His essence which is the principle of the First Intellect essentially, and there is no doubt that each of them is particular. And the main point in the philosophers' argument is that if He knew that Zayd would enter the house tomorrow, then when Zayd enters the house tomorrow, if He negates the knowledge of his state, meaning that He knows that Zayd will enter tomorrow, that is ignorance because it does not correspond to reality; and if it ceases and knowledge that he has entered occurs, then change would be necessary for the first knowledge from existence to non-existence and for the second from non-existence to existence, and that is impossible for the Eternal. It cannot be said: just as the belief that does not correspond is ignorance, similarly the absence of the corresponding belief to what is actual [is ignorance]. For we say: granted, but if He does not know it in a universal manner. Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument against divine knowledge of particulars, followed by a response. It begins by noting that some particulars, such as the essence of God and the essences of immaterial intellects, are unchanging, so knowledge of them is not problematic. It then states the philosophers' core argument: if God knew that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, then when Zayd enters, either God's knowledge remains (becoming ignorance) or it changes (to knowledge of entry), both impossible for God. A potential objection is raised: absence of corresponding belief is also ignorance. The response is that this would hold only if God does not know the event universally. The answer given is that some particulars are unchanging (e.g., God's essence and attributes, intellects), so the argument does not apply to them. Furthermore, specifying the ruling to some particulars is only valid in religious principles, not rational ones. The passage then discusses possible escapes from the objection, such as the philosophers' claim that God does not know changing particulars at all.

The Philosophers' Core Argument
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers' core argument on divine knowledge of changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that divine knowledge of a future particular event would entail change in God, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If God knew a changeable event, His knowledge would either become false or change—both impossible.

Al-Taftazani now presents the main argument used by philosophers to deny that God knows particulars in their particularity. The argument hinges on the impossibility of change in divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about Zayd

2
Understand the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities: either God's knowledge becomes ignorance or it changes. Both are impossible for God.

Why is change impossible for God? Because God is eternal and perfect; any change implies imperfection.

Answering the Philosophers: Some Particulars Are Unchanging
Al-Taftazani answers the philosophers' argument and discusses its limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer to the philosophers' argument: it only applies to changing particulars, and the claim that God knows all particulars in detail is not undermined.

Core Idea
The argument fails because it does not cover unchanging particulars, and the claim can be restricted.

After presenting the philosophers' argument, Al-Taftazani gives the answer. He also addresses a potential objection about the absence of corresponding belief, and discusses the validity of specifying the ruling to some particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage starting with 'لا يقال' to see the objection and the immediate response.
2
Understand the main answer
The answer is that some particulars (like God's essence) are unchanging, so the argument does not apply to them.
3
Examine the specification and escape
Al-Taftazani notes that specifying the ruling to some particulars is only valid in religious principles, and discusses a possible escape from the argument.

And the main point in the philosophers' argument is that if He knew that Zayd would enter the house tomorrow, then when Zayd enters the house tomorrow, if He negates the knowledge of his state, meaning that He knows that Zayd will enter tomorrow, that is ignorance because it does not correspond to reality; and if it ceases and knowledge that he has entered occurs, then change would be necessary for the first knowledge from existence to non-existence and for the second from non-existence to existence, and that is impossible for the Eternal. It cannot be said: just as the belief that does not correspond is ignorance, similarly the absence of the corresponding belief to what is actual [is ignorance]. For we say: granted, but if He does not know it in a universal manner. And the answer: Among particulars there are those that do not change, like the essence of God Most High and His real attributes according to those who affirm them, and like the essences of the intellects, so the argument does not apply to them. And specifying the ruling to some, as indicated by the speech of the Imam, is only valid in religious principles, not rational ones. And when it was possible for someone to escape from this by [saying] that the general claim may be that He does not know any of the changing things, or that the impossibility is shown for changing particulars by this argument and for non-changing ones by another argument, and that they intend to refute the opponent's statement that He knows all particulars in a particular manner.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument against divine knowledge of particulars, followed by a response. It begins by noting that some particulars, such as the essence of God and the essences of immaterial intellects, are unchanging, so knowledge of them is not problematic. It then states the philosophers' core argument: if God knew that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, then when Zayd enters, either God's knowledge remains (becoming ignorance) or it changes (to knowledge of entry), both impossible for God. A potential objection is raised: absence of corresponding belief is also ignorance. The response is that this would hold only if God does not know the event universally. The answer given is that some particulars are unchanging (e.g., God's essence and attributes, intellects), so the argument does not apply to them. Furthermore, specifying the ruling to some particulars is only valid in religious principles, not rational ones. The passage then discusses possible escapes from the objection, such as the philosophers' claim that God does not know changing particulars at all.

متغير

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer to the philosophers' argument only works within religious principles, not rational ones.
Correction: The specification of the ruling to some particulars is only valid in religious principles, but the main answer (unchanging particulars) is rational.

Divine Knowledge as Relation: The Mainstream Answer
Al-Taftazani presents the mainstream answer that divine knowledge is a relation or relational attribute, so change in the known object does not entail change in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority of theologians refute the argument that God's knowledge of changing particulars would imply change in God, by defining knowledge as a relation or relational attribute.

Core Idea
Change in the known object changes only the relation, not the Knower.

The philosophers argued that if God knows changing particulars, His knowledge must change, implying imperfection. The majority of theologians responded by analyzing the nature of knowledge. They argued that knowledge is either a relation or a quality that inherently involves a relation. A change in the relation does not affect the essence of the knower. This lesson presents their reasoning and the example of God's relation to temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical flow: denial of implication, basis, example, two cases.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'relation' (إضافة), 'relational attribute' (صفة ذات إضافة), and 'implication' (ملازمة). Understand how they function in the argument.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example of God being described as 'before', 'with', and 'after' a temporal event without change in His essence. How does this illustrate the principle?
The relation changes, but the related subject (God) remains unchanged.
4
Examine the two assumptions
The passage considers two possibilities: knowledge as pure relation, and knowledge as a quality with a relation. For each, trace the conclusion about change in knowledge and essence.
In the first case, only knowledge changes; in the second, neither knowledge nor essence changes.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Summarize how the argument successfully denies the philosophers' implication and preserves divine immutability.
This answer became the mainstream position in kalam.

Source Text

اقتصر الجمهور في الجواب على منع الملازمة مستنداً بأن العلم إما إضافة أو صفة ذات إضافة وتغير الإضافة لا يوجب تغير المضاف كالقديم يتصف بأنه قبل الحادث إذا لم يوجد الحادث ومعه إذا وجد وبعده إذا فني من غير تغير في ذات القديم فعلى تقدير كون العلم إضافة لا يلزم من تغير المعلوم إلا تغير العلم دون الذات وعلى تقدير كونه صفة ذات إضافة لا يلزم تغير العلم فضلاً عن الذات
The majority restricted themselves in the answer to denying the implication, based on the fact that knowledge is either a relation or a quality with a relation, and change of the relation does not necessitate change of the related term, like the Eternal is described as being before the temporal event when the event does not exist, and with it when it exists, and after it when it perishes, without any change in the essence of the Eternal. Thus, on the assumption that knowledge is a relation, from the change of the known object nothing follows except change of the knowledge, not the essence; and on the assumption that it is a quality with a relation, change of the knowledge does not follow, let alone the essence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the answer of the majority of theologians to the philosophers' argument. They deny the implication that change in the known object necessitates change in the knower. Their basis is that knowledge is either a pure relation or a quality that inherently involves a relation. A change in the relation does not cause a change in the substance that has the relation. The example given is the Eternal God: He is described as being before a temporal event when it does not exist, with it when it exists, and after it when it perishes, yet His essence undergoes no change. Therefore, if knowledge is a mere relation, then a change in the known object only entails a change in the relation (knowledge), not in the divine essence. If knowledge is a quality that has a relation, then not even the knowledge itself changes, let alone the essence. Thus the philosophers' argument is refuted.
