Correction: The passage shows that if knowledge is a relation, only the relation changes, not the knower's essence. The example of God's relation to temporal events demonstrates that relations can change without affecting the subject.

Key Takeaway
The mainstream response to the problem of divine knowledge of particulars is that knowledge is a relation, so change in the known changes the relation, not the essence.

The Identity of God's Knowledge of Future and Past
The text presents the answer that God's future knowledge is identical to His knowledge of occurrence, and Abu al-Husayn's first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's knowledge of future events is identical to His knowledge of past events, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's first objection to this view.

Core Idea
Future and past are both present to God's knowledge.

Some theologians argued that God's knowledge that something will happen is the same as His knowledge that it has happened, since a continuous knowledge of a future event becomes knowledge of its occurrence. This view, however, faces objections, as Abu al-Husayn al-Basri points out with a thought experiment involving a person in a dark room.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. This will help you grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the technical terms like 'al-ʿālamiyya' and 'al-ṣifātiyya'.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the claim that God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of its past occurrence. Note the reasoning about continuity of knowledge.
3
Examine the analogies
The passage uses analogies of a mirror and a person changing relative position. Understand how these illustrate that knowledge does not change with the object known.
Compare with the view that knowledge is a relation.
4
Analyze Abu al-Husayn's first objection
Focus on the thought experiment: a person knows Zayd will enter, but remains in the dark and does not gain knowledge of entry. Why does this refute the identity claim?
The objection shows that the two knowledges are distinct.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this discussion relates to divine attributes and the debate between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب كثير من المعتزلة وأهل السنة : بأن علم الله تعالى بأن الشيء سيحدث هو نفس علمه بأنه حدث للقطع بأن من علم أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً واستمر على هذا العلم إلى مضي الغد علم بهذا العلم أنه دخل الدار من غير افتقار إلى علم مستأنف فعلى هذا لا تغير في العالمية التي يثبتها المعتزلة والعلم الذي أثبته الصفاتية وهذا بخلاف علم المخلوق فإنه لا يستمر ومرجع هذا الجواب إلى ما سبق من كون العلم أو العالمية غير الاضافة إذ لا شبهة في تغير الإضافة بتغير المضاف إليه ولهذا أوضحوا هذا المعنى المدعى بأن العلم لو تغير بتغير المعلوم لتكثر بتكثره ضرورة فيلزم كثرة الصفات بل لا تناهيها بحسب لا تناهي المعلومات وبأن العلم صفة تنجلي بها المعلومات بمنزلة مرآة تنكشف بها الصور فلا يتغير بتغير المعلوم كما لا تتغير المرآة بتغير الصور وبأنه صفة يعرض لها إضافات وتعلقات بمنزلة إنسان جلس زيد عن يساره ثم قام يجلس عن يمينه فإنه يصير متيامناً لزيد بعد ما كان متياسراً له من غير تغير فيه أصلاً فظاهر أن هذا لا يتم على القول بكون العلم تعلقاً بين العالم والمعلوم على ما يراه جمهور المعتزلة فلهذا رده أبو الحسين البصري بوجوه : أحدها: بأن من علم أن زيداً يدخل البلد غداً وجلس مستمراً على هذا عالماً الاعتقاد إلى الغد في بيت مظلم بحيث لم يعلم دخول الغد فإنه لا يصير بدخول زيد ولو كان العلم بأنه سيدخل نفس العلم بأنه دخل لوجب أن يحصل هذا العلم في هذه الصورة فإذا لم يحصل لم يكن بل الحق أن العلم بأنه دخل
And many of the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna answered: that God's knowledge that a thing will happen is identical to His knowledge that it has happened, because it is certain that whoever knows that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow and continues upon this knowledge until the passing of tomorrow, knows by that same knowledge that he has entered the house, without needing a new knowledge. So on this basis, there is no change in the 'worldliness' (al-ʿālamiyya) that the Mu'tazila affirm, nor in the knowledge that the Attributes-affirmers (al-ṣifātiyya) affirm. This is unlike the knowledge of the creature, for it does not continue. And the return of this answer is to what preceded: that knowledge or worldliness is not a relation, since there is no doubt about the change of relation with the change of the related term. For this reason, they clarified this claimed meaning by [saying]: if knowledge changed with the change of the known, it would necessarily multiply with its multiplication, thus requiring multiplicity of attributes, indeed their infinity according to the infinity of known things; and that knowledge is an attribute by which known things are disclosed, like a mirror by which images are revealed, so it does not change with the change of the known, just as the mirror does not change with the change of images; and that it is an attribute to which relations and attachments occur, like a man beside whom Zayd sat on his left, then he stood and sat on his right, so he becomes right-sided to Zayd after he was left-sided to him, without any change in himself. It is apparent that this does not hold on the view that knowledge is a relation between the knower and the known according to what the majority of Mu'tazila hold. For this reason, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri refuted it with several points: The first: that whoever knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, and remains continuously upon this knowledge and belief until the next day in a dark room such that he does not know the arrival of the next day, then he does not become [knowing] by Zayd's entry; and if the knowledge that he will enter were the same as the knowledge that he has entered, it would be necessary that this knowledge be obtained in this case; and since it is not obtained, it is not [the case]; rather the truth is that knowledge that he has entered [is different].
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a second answer given by many Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, followed by a refutation by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. The answer claims that God's knowledge that something will happen is identical to His knowledge that it has happened. The reasoning is that if someone knows that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow and continues holding that knowledge until tomorrow passes, he then knows that Zayd has entered by that same knowledge, without requiring a new act of knowledge. Therefore, there is no change in the attribute of knowledge (or worldliness, as the Mu'tazila call it). The answer further argues that if knowledge changed with the object, it would multiply infinitely, which is impossible. Analogies are given: knowledge is like a mirror that does not change when images change, or like a person who becomes right-sided to Zayd after being left-sided without any change in himself. However, the author notes that this reasoning does not hold if knowledge is considered a relation between knower and known, as the majority of Mu'tazila hold. Therefore, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri refuted it with several points. The first point is a thought experiment: if someone knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow and remains in that belief in a dark room without knowing when tomorrow arrives, he does not automatically know that Zayd has entered when Zayd actually enters. If the knowledge of the future event were identical to knowledge of its occurrence, then this person would have that knowledge, but he does not. Hence the two knowledges are distinct. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that Abu al-Husayn offers further points.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of a future event and knowledge of its occurrence are identical.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn shows they are distinct because a person can have the former without the latter, as in the dark room scenario.

Checkpoint

God's knowledge is unchanging and encompasses all times, but the argument that future and past knowledge are identical faces logical objections from human experience.

The Majority's Response: God's Knowledge Does Not Change
The majority responds to an objection about change in God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority of theologians defend the immutability of God's knowledge by denying the implication that change in known objects necessitates change in God's essence.

Core Idea
Change in the known changes only the relation, not the knower.

The objection that God's knowledge must change as objects change seems intuitive, but the majority of theologians (al-Jumhūr) respond by analyzing the nature of knowledge itself. They argue that knowledge is either a pure relation or a quality that bears a relation, and relations can change without affecting the subject. This passage introduces that argument, using the example of the Eternal (al-Qadīm) being described 'before,' 'with,' and 'after' an event without any change in His essence. Pay close attention to how they block the logical implication (manʿ al-mulāzama) that would force change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Try to read the Arabic passage without looking at the translation. Notice the key terms: الجمهور, منع الملازمة, إضافة, صفة ذات إضافة, القديم.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the majority's main strategy
The majority does not prove that knowledge is unchanging; they deny the implication (منع الملازمة) that change in the known forces change in the knower. Find where they state this.
This is the core of their defense.
3
Understand the example of the Eternal
They give an example: the Eternal is described as 'before the event,' 'with the event,' and 'after the event' without any change in His essence. How does this support their denial?
Relational descriptions change, but the essence does not.
4
Analyze the two assumptions about knowledge
They consider two possibilities: knowledge is a relation (إضافة) or a quality that possesses a relation (صفة ذات إضافة). For each, trace what changes and what does not.
Notice the different conclusions for each assumption.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Restate in your own words why the objection fails according to the majority. What is the key insight?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقتصر الجمهور في الجواب على منع الملازمة مستنداً بأن العلم إما إضافة، أو صفة ذات إضافة وتغير الإضافة لا يوجب تغير المضاف كالقديم يتصف بأنه قبل الحادث، إذا لم يوجد الحادث، ومعه إذا وجد وبعده إذا فني من غير تغير في ذات القديم. فعلى تقدير كون العلم إضافة لا يلزم من تغير المعلوم إلا تغير العلم دون الذات، وعلى تقدير كونه صفة ذات إضافة لا يلزم تغير العلم فضلاً عن الذات.
The majority restricted the answer to denying the implication, based on the fact that knowledge is either a relation or a quality possessing a relation, and change in the relation does not necessitate change in the related thing, just as the Eternal is described as before the event when the event does not exist, and with it when it exists, and after it when it perishes, without change in the essence of the Eternal. So on the assumption that knowledge is a relation, change in the known entails only change in the knowledge, not in the essence; and on the assumption that it is a quality possessing a relation, change in knowledge is not entailed, let alone change in the essence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the majority (جمهور) in response to an objection that God's knowledge must change with changing objects. The majority's response is a denial of the implication (منع الملازمة) that change in the known necessitates change in God's essence. They base this on the analysis of knowledge as either a pure relation (إضافة) or a quality that has a relation (صفة ذات إضافة). They argue that a relation can change without the subject changing, using the example of the Eternal (القديم) being described as 'before the event' when the event is absent, 'with it' when it is present, and 'after it' when it perishes, all without change in the essence. So if knowledge is a relation, a change in the known only changes the knowledge relation, not God's essence. If knowledge is a quality with a relation, even the knowledge itself need not change, let alone the essence. Thus, the objection fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any change in the known objects must force a change in God's knowledge or essence.
Correction: The majority shows that change in the known only affects the relation (or a relational quality), not the essence of the knower, using the analogy of the Eternal's relational descriptions.

Key Takeaway

God's Knowledge of Future and Past Is the Same
Many Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna argue God's knowledge of future is same as past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how many Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna argue that God's knowledge of future events is identical to His knowledge of past events, and the implications of this for divine immutability.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not change; knowing the future is the same as knowing the past.

This lesson presents a key argument from both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna theologians defending the immutability of divine knowledge. They claim that God's knowledge of a future event is exactly the same as His knowledge of it after it occurs, using analogies from human experience. The passage also addresses the limitation of this argument if knowledge is considered a mere relation, as most Mu'tazila hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the main claim and the supporting arguments.
2
Identify the main argument
Notice that the answer asserts identity between God's knowledge of future and past events, supported by the example of Zayd entering the house.
3
Examine the three clarifications
The passage provides three reasons why knowledge does not change: multiplicity of attributes, mirror analogy, and the person-moving example.
4
Understand the limitation
The argument works only if knowledge is not a mere relation; otherwise, the analogies fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب كثير من المعتزلة وأهل السنة : بأن علم الله تعالى بأن الشيء سيحدث هو نفس علمه بأنه حدث للقطع بأن من علم أن زيداً يدخل الدار غداً، واستمر على هذا العلم إلى مضي الغد علم بهذا العلم أنه دخل الدار من غير افتقار إلى علم مستأنف فعلى هذا لا تغير في العالمية التي يثبتها المعتزلة، والعلم الذي أثبته الصفاتية، وهذا بخلاف علم المخلوق، فإنه لا يستمر ومرجع هذا الجواب إلى ما سبق من كون العلم أو العالمية غير الاضافة إذ لا شبهة في تغير الإضافة بتغير المضاف إليه . ولهذا أوضحوا هذا المعنى المدعى بأن العلم لو تغير بتغير المعلوم لتكثر بتكثره ضرورة. فيلزم كثرة الصفات بل لا تناهيها بحسب لا تناهي المعلومات. وبأن العلم صفة تنجلي بها المعلومات بمنزلة مرآة تنكشف بها الصور، فلا يتغير بتغير المعلوم، كما لا تتغير المرآة بتغير الصور، وبأنه صفة يعرض لها إضافات وتعلقات بمنزلة إنسان جلس زيد عن يساره، ثم قام يجلس عن يمينه فإنه يصير متيامناً لزيد بعد ما كان متياسراً له من غير تغير فيه أصلاً، فظاهر أن هذا لا يتم على القول، بكون العلم تعلقاً بين العالم والمعلوم على ما يراه جمهور المعتزلة.
And many of the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna answered: That Allah's knowledge that a thing will happen is the same as His knowledge that it happened, conclusively because whoever knows that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, and continues upon this knowledge until the passing of tomorrow, knows with this same knowledge that he entered the house without needing a new knowledge. Thus, there is no change in the 'worldiness' (العالمية) that the Mu'tazila affirm, nor in the knowledge that the Attributes-affirmers (الصفاتية) affirm. This is different from created knowledge, for it does not persist. The reference of this answer is to what preceded, that knowledge or worldiness is not a relation, since there is no doubt about the change of relation with the change of its relatum. For this they clarified this claimed meaning: that if knowledge changed with the change of the known, it would necessarily multiply with its multiplicity. Then multiplicity of attributes would follow, indeed their infiniteness according to the infinity of known things. And because knowledge is a quality by which known things are unveiled, like a mirror by which images are revealed, so it does not change with the change of the known, just as the mirror does not change with the change of images. And because it is a quality that has relations and attachments, as in the example of a person who sat to Zayd's left, then stood and sat to his right: he becomes to the right of Zayd after having been to his left, without any change in himself at all. So it is apparent that this does not hold on the view that knowledge is a relation between the knower and the known, as the majority of Mu'tazila hold.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an answer from many Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna. They argue that God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of it after it occurs, using the analogy of human knowledge: if someone knows that Zayd will enter the house tomorrow, and continues that knowledge until tomorrow, then upon Zayd's entry he knows with the same knowledge that he entered. This shows no change in the attribute of knowledge (or العالمية). They then provide three clarifications: (1) If knowledge changed with the known, it would multiply infinitely with infinite knowns, leading to infinite attributes. (2) Knowledge is like a mirror that reveals images without changing. (3) Knowledge has relations that can change without changing the knowledge itself, like a person moving from left to right of Zayd without self-change. However, these analogies do not hold if knowledge is considered a mere relation, as most Mu'tazila believe. Thus, the argument presupposes that knowledge is not a relation.

Key Terms
كثير من المعتزلة
أهل السنة
العالمية
الصفاتية
علم المخلوق
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows something in the future and then later knows it as past, His knowledge must change.
Correction: The argument shows that God's knowledge of the event is the same before and after; it does not change.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the relationship between God's knowledge that something will happen and His knowledge that it happened?
A) They are different and change
B) They are the same knowledge
C) One is a relation, the other is a quality
D) They are both relations

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of an event is identical before and after it occurs, demonstrating divine immutability, but this argument assumes knowledge is not a mere relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that God's knowledge of your future sins is identical to His knowledge of them after they happen affect your view of His mercy and justice? Will you change your behavior knowing that your actions are known to Him with perfect clarity before you even commit them? Commit to one specific change in your life after reflecting on this.
?
If God's knowledge does not change, then He knows your deepest secrets and your most private thoughts as clearly as He knows your public deeds. How does this reality confront your hidden sins? Are you willing to bring them into the light of His unchanging knowledge and seek His forgiveness today?
?
The mirror analogy suggests that knowledge reveals without changing. When you look into the mirror of God's knowledge, what do you see about your own spiritual state? Are you content with what is revealed, or does it provoke you to repent right now? Write down one thing you will do this week to align your life with the truth that God sees all.

Abu al-Husayn's First Objection: Knowledge of Future vs. Past
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri objects that knowledge of future and past are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's argument that knowledge of a future event is not identical to knowledge of the same event after it occurs, using the example of a person in a dark room.

Core Idea
Knowledge of future and past are distinct; their identity would logically require impossible knowledge.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri raises a powerful objection against the claim that God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of it after it occurs. He constructs a simple thought experiment involving a person who knows Zayd will enter tomorrow but remains in a dark room unaware of when tomorrow arrives. The failure of knowledge to arise upon the actual event shows the two knowledges are not the same.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the argument's structure come through before analysis.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'if ... then ...'.
2
Understand the scenario
Visualize the person who knows Zayd will enter tomorrow but is secluded in a dark room, unaware of when tomorrow arrives. When Zayd actually enters, does the person know that Zayd has entered? No.
3
Analyze the reasoning
If the knowledge of future entry were identical to knowledge of past entry, then the person should know of the entry immediately upon its occurrence. Since he does not, the two knowledges cannot be identical.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Abu al-Husayn states the true relationship: knowledge of past entry is 'generated' (متولد) from knowledge of future entry plus knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow. Thus they are distinct.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument challenges the view that God's knowledge of future events is identical to His knowledge of them after they occur, since God does not lack temporal awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلهذا رده أبو الحسين البصري بوجوه : أحدها: بأن من علم أن زيداً يدخل البلد غداً، وجلس مستمراً على هذا عالماً الاعتقاد إلى الغد في بيت مظلم، بحيث لم يعلم دخول الغد، فإنه لا يصير بدخول زيد، ولو كان العلم بأنه سيدخل نفس العلم بأنه دخل، لوجب أن يحصل هذا العلم في هذه الصورة، فإذا لم يحصل لم يكن بل الحق أن العلم بأنه دخل علم متولد من العلم بأنه سيدخل غداً، ومن العلم بوجود الغد.
For this, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri refuted it with several points: First, that if someone knows that Zayd will enter the town tomorrow, and remains continuously upon this as a knower of belief until tomorrow in a dark room, such that he does not know the arrival of tomorrow, then upon Zayd's entry he does not become (knowing) by Zayd's entry. And if the knowledge that he will enter were the same as the knowledge that he entered, then this knowledge would have to be obtained in this scenario. Since it is not obtained, it is not (the case). Rather, the truth is that the knowledge that he entered is a knowledge generated from the knowledge that he will enter tomorrow and from the knowledge of the existence of tomorrow.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri against the claim that God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of it after it occurs. Abu al-Husayn uses a counterexample: Suppose a person knows that Zayd will enter the town tomorrow, but remains in a dark room unaware of the arrival of tomorrow. When Zayd actually enters, the person does not know that Zayd has entered. If the knowledge of the future event were identical to the knowledge of the past event, then upon Zayd's entry the person should have that knowledge. Since he does not, the two knowledges are not identical. Rather, the knowledge of the past event is generated from the knowledge of the future event plus the knowledge of the arrival of tomorrow. Thus, they are distinct.

Key Terms
أبو الحسين البصري
وجوه
علم متولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of a person in a dark room disproves the identity of past and future knowledge.
Correction: The example actually supports the point that identity would lead to a contradiction, so the identity claim is false.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu al-Husayn argue that the knowledge of future entry and past entry are not identical?
A) Because they have different objects
B) Because a person in a dark room does not know the arrival of tomorrow
C) Because knowledge is a relation
D) Because God's knowledge is eternal

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn's example shows that knowledge of the future and knowledge of the past are distinct, as the latter depends on additional temporal awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew that a specific trial would come your way, would you prepare differently? This lesson shows that knowing the future is not the same as experiencing it. What are you preparing for spiritually today, and how are you ensuring you are aware of the present moment so you can recognize God's help when it arrives?
?
The person in the dark room lacked awareness of the passage of time. Are there aspects of your faith where you are 'in the dark' about God's timing? What is one area where you need to trust that His knowledge is complete, even though your understanding is incomplete?
?
Knowledge of the past is generated from knowledge of the future plus time. When you look back at a past sin, do you feel that your regret is based only on what you knew then, or do you acknowledge that God's unchanging knowledge of your future repentance already encompassed that moment? How does this lesson affect your view of God's mercy?

The Second Objection: Objects and Conditions Differ
Abu al-Husayn presents the second objection: objects differ due to opposite conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against Abu al-Husayn: that the objects of knowledge differ because they are conditioned on opposite states.

Core Idea
Opposite conditions on objects make the two knowledges different in themselves.

In the second objection, Abu al-Husayn argues that the objects of the two knowledges—future entry and past entry—are bound by opposite conditions: non-entry and actual entry. Since the relations to such opposing conditions are different, the knowledges themselves must be distinct. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for God's immutable knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection and its translation. Notice how the conditions 'non-entry' and 'actual entry' are contrasted.
2
Identify the two objects and their conditions
The first object is 'he will enter' with condition of non-entry; the second is 'he entered' with condition of actual entry. Recognize how these conditions are opposites.
3
Understand why relations differ
The relation to one opposite condition (e.g., non-entry) is different from the relation to the other (actual entry). Therefore the knowledges that involve these relations are different.
This is a key step in the objection.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: if the knowledges are different, does that imply change in God? The author will address that later; here we just see the argument for difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن متعلق العلم الأول هو أنه سيدخل وشرطه عدم الدخول ومتعلق العلم الثاني أنه دخل وشرطه تحقق الدخول، ولا خفاء في أن الإضافة إلى أحد المتخلفين أو الصورة المطابقة له تغاير الإضافة إلى الآخر أو الصورة المطابقة له وكذا المشروط بأحد المنافيين تغاير المشروط بالآخر. وثالثها : أن كلاً من العلمين قد يحصل بدون الآخر، كما إذا علم أن زيداً سيقدم البتة، لكن عند قدومه لم يعلم أنه قدم من غير سابقة علم له بأنه سيقدم. والحق أن العلمين متغايران وأن التغير في الإضافة أو العالمية لا يقدح في قدم الذات، ومن المعتزلة من سلم تغاير العلمين ومنع تغيرهما ، وقال : تعلق عالمية الباري بعدم السبت تعلقان مختلفان أزليان لا يتغيران أصلاً، فإنه في يوم الجمعة يعلم دخوله في السبت وفي يوم السبت يعلم عدم دخوله في يوم الجمعة، غاية الأمر أنه يمكن التعبير عن العدم في الحال، والوجود في الاستقبال سيوجد ، وبعد الوجود لا يمكن وهذا تفاوت وصفي لا يقدح في الحقائق، وكذا عالميته بعدم العالم في الأزل لا يتغير بوجود العالم فيما لا يزال .
And the second: That the object of the first knowledge is that he will enter, and its condition is non-entry, while the object of the second knowledge is that he entered, and its condition is the actualization of entry. It is not hidden that the relation to one of the two opposites or the form corresponding to it differs from the relation to the other or the form corresponding to it. Likewise, that which is conditioned by one of the two contradictory things differs from that which is conditioned by the other. And the third: That each of the two knowledges may occur without the other, as when someone knows that Zayd will surely arrive, but upon his arrival he does not know that he arrived, without prior knowledge that he would arrive. And the truth is that the two knowledges are different, and that change in the relation or worldiness does not impair the eternity of the essence. And among the Mu'tazila are those who conceded the difference of the two knowledges but denied their change, saying: The attachment of the worldiness of the Creator to the non-existence of Saturday are two different eternal attachments that do not change at all. For on Friday He knows his entry on Saturday, and on Saturday He knows his non-entry on Friday. At most, it is possible to express the non-existence in the present, and the existence in the future will exist, and after existence it is not possible. And this is a descriptive difference that does not impair the realities. Similarly, His worldiness regarding the non-existence of the world in eternity does not change with the existence of the world in the future.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage continues Abu al-Husayn's refutation with two more points. The second objection: The objects of the two knowledges differ—first is 'will enter' conditioned on non-entry, second is 'entered' conditioned on actual entry. The relation to opposite conditions ensures the knowledges are different. The third objection: The two knowledges can occur independently, as someone may know that Zayd will arrive but not know when he arrives if he lacks additional information. So the knowledges are distinct. However, change is only in the relation or the attribute of worldiness, not in God's eternal essence. Then the text notes that some Mu'tazila conceded the difference between the two knowledges but denied any change in God, proposing instead that God has two eternal relations regarding the same event—one relative to Friday (entry on Saturday) and one relative to Saturday (non-entry on Friday)—both eternal and unchanging. This is a descriptive difference that does not affect the unchanging reality of God's attribute.

Key Terms
متعلق العلم
شرط
تغاير
المتخلفين
المنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's knowledge of a future event and His knowledge of the same event after it occurs are identical in every respect.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn argues that the objects are different (one is 'will enter', the other 'entered') and each is conditioned on opposite states, so the knowledges are distinct. This does not, however, imply change in God.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the object of the first knowledge according to the second objection?
A) Actual entry
B) Non-entry
C) Both entry and non-entry
D) No condition

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that knowledge of future and past events differ because their objects are conditioned on opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of your past sins is not a barrier to His knowledge of your future repentance? Knowing that the conditions are opposite, does that make you feel judged or hopeful?
?
When you consider that God knows your future entry into Paradise and also knows your past failures, does that create a sense of urgency to change? How does the difference in conditions affect your reliance on Him?
?
If God's knowledge of your future obedience is conditioned on your current struggle, does that motivate you to act now? Or do you use His foreknowledge as an excuse for delay?

Third Objection and Mu'tazili Concession
Abu al-Husayn gives a third objection; author states truth; Mu'tazila propose two eternal attachments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection (knowledge can occur independently) and the Mu'tazili concession that God has two eternal attachments that do not change.

Core Idea
Knowledges can be independent, yet change is only in relation, not in God's essence.

The third objection argues that each knowledge can occur without the other, showing they are distinct. The author then states the truth: the knowledges differ, but change is only in the relation or attribute (worldliness), not in God's eternal essence. Finally, some Mu'tazila offer a concession: God has two eternal attachments regarding the same event, which never change. This lesson explores these points and their reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic for the third objection and the author's response. Notice the example of knowing Zayd will arrive but not knowing his arrival.
2
Understand the independence argument
The objection says each knowledge can exist without the other. This proves they are distinct, because if they were identical they would always occur together.
3
Examine the author's truth claim
The author agrees the knowledges differ but insists change is only in the relation or worldliness, not in the eternal essence.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazili concession
Some Mu'tazila say God has two different eternal attachments (e.g., regarding Saturday) that never change. They use the example of Friday and Saturday to show that the attachments are eternally distinct.
5
Reflect on the descriptive difference
The concession concludes that the difference is merely descriptive, not a real change in God's essence. This preserves divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Objection on Assent Knowledge and the Response
An objection about God's knowledge of future contingents and the initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge of future contingents would be ignorance if their objects are absent, and the beginning of the response that denies this.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge is not ignorance; it is knowledge of future existence.

This passage presents a philosophical objection against divine knowledge of future contingent propositions. The objector argues that if the object of a proposition does not yet exist, then God's 'worldiness' attaching to that proposition would be ignorance. The response begins by denying this premise, asserting that God's knowledge pertains to the future existence of the proposition. However, the full argument is cut off, and we examine only the opening exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection (فإن قيل) followed by an answer (أجيب).
The passage is incomplete; the full answer may require later context.
2
Identify the objection
Understand the objector's reasoning: God's worldiness is attached to a proposition like 'Zayd will enter on Saturday'. If the object of the proposition (the actual entry) does not yet exist, then the attachment would be ignorance because there is no independent proposition (نسبة استقلالية) to correspond to.
3
Identify the answer's denial
The answer begins with 'بالمنع' (denial). It claims that the attachment in the state of non-existence is that 'it will exist' (سيوجد), and the proposition remains as it is. This counters the idea that absence of the object makes the knowledge ignorance.
The answer is cut off, so we only have the initial rebuttal.
4
Reflect on key terms
Focus on 'العلم التصديقي' (assent knowledge, knowledge of propositions) and 'النسبة الاستقلالية' (the independent proposition, the state of affairs itself). Understanding these terms is essential to grasp the objection and response.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of an answer concerning 'assent knowledge' (العلم التصديقي), which is knowledge of propositions or judgments. If the object of this proposition (the independent state of affairs) is absent, then the attachment would be ignorance because the proposition is false. The answer denies this, stating that the attachment of worldiness in the state of non-existence is that it will exist, and the proposition remains as it is. However, the text cuts off mid-sentence, so the full answer is not provided in this window.

Three Arguments for the Distinction of Two Knowledges
The author enumerates three proofs for the distinction of two divine knowledges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments proving that God's knowledge of future events and His knowledge of their occurrence are distinct, and why this distinction does not imply change in His eternal essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of tomorrow and knowledge of today are truly distinct, yet His essence remains eternal.

This passage presents three rational arguments for a subtle but crucial distinction in divine knowledge: the knowledge that something will happen is different from the knowledge that it has happened. Without this distinction, we might mistakenly think God learns new things over time. The author also addresses a Mu'tazilite objection to preserve divine immutability. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation. Get a sense of the three arguments and the concluding discussion.
Notice the parenthetical numbers (۱), (۲) which mark the arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments
Locate each argument: the first (labeled 'third') about generation from two prior knowledges, the second about differing objects and conditions, and the third about the independence of one knowledge from the other.
The first argument is introduced with 'ثالث' but it is the first of three in this passage.
3
Understand the author's conclusion and the Mu'tazilite view
Read the section after 'والحق أن العلمين متغايران' where the author affirms distinction and explains that change is only in relation, not essence. Then see how some Mu'tazila concede distinction but deny change, using the Saturday example.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this matters: if God's knowledge changed from 'will happen' to 'has happened', does that imply a change in God? The passage argues no, only the relation changes. Reflect on how this preserves divine simplicity.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that change in knowledge means change in essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم الغد. (۱) ثالث متولد من العلم بأنه سيدخل غداً، ومن العلم بوجود وثانيها : أن متعلق العلم الأول هو أنه سيدخل وشرطه عدم الدخول ومتعلق العلم الثاني أنه دخل وشرطه تحقق الدخول، ولا خفاء في أن الإضافة إلى أحد المتخلفين أو الصورة المطابقة له تغاير الإضافة إلى الآخر أو الصورة المطابقة له وكذا المشروط بأحد المنافيين تغاير المشروط بالآخر. وثالثها : أن كلاً من العلمين قد يحصل بدون الآخر، كما إذا علم أن زيداً سيقدم البتة، لكن عند قدومه لم يعلم أنه قدم من غير سابقة علم له بأنه سيقدم. والحق أن العلمين متغايران وأن التغير في الإضافة أو العالمية لا يقدح في قدم الذات، ومن المعتزلة من سلم تغاير العلمين ومنع تغيرهما، وقال : تعلق عالمية الباري بعدم السبت تعلقان مختلفان أزليان لا يتغيران أصلاً، فإنه في يوم الجمعة يعلم دخوله في السبت وفي يوم السبت يعلم عدم دخوله في يوم الجمعة، غاية الأمر أنه يمكن التعبير عن العدم في الحال، والوجود في الاستقبال سيوجد ، وبعد الوجود لا يمكن وهذا تفاوت وصفي لا يقدح في الحقائق، وكذا عالميته بعدم العالم في الأزل لا يتغير بوجود العالم فيما لا يزال .
Knowledge of tomorrow. (1) Third, generated from the knowledge that he will enter tomorrow, and from the knowledge of existence. And its second: that the object of the first knowledge is that he will enter and its condition is non-entry, and the object of the second knowledge is that he entered and its condition is the realization of entry, and it is not hidden that the relation to one of the two differing things or the form matching it is different from the relation to the other or the form matching it, and likewise that which is conditioned by one of the two opposites is different from that conditioned by the other. And its third: that each of the two knowledges may obtain without the other, as when one knows that Zayd will definitely arrive, but upon his arrival does not know that he arrived without prior knowledge that he would arrive. And the truth is that the two knowledges are distinct and that the change in the relation or attribution does not impair the eternity of the essence. Among the Mu'tazila, some conceded the distinctness of the two knowledges and denied their change, and said: the attachment of the divine attribution regarding the non-existence of Saturday is two different eternal attachments that do not change at all. For on Friday He knows his entry into Saturday, and on Saturday He knows his non-entry into Friday. The only thing is that it is possible to express non-existence in the present, and existence in the future will exist, and after existence it is not possible, and this is a descriptive difference that does not impair the realities. Likewise, His attribution regarding the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity does not change with the existence of the world in what is to come.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments for the distinction between two types of divine knowledge: the knowledge that something will occur (e.g., Zayd's entry tomorrow) and the knowledge that it has occurred. The author first enumerates three arguments. The first argument (labeled 'third' due to textual ordering) states that the knowledge is generated from two prior knowledges. The second argument notes that the two knowledges have different objects and conditions: one is about a future event conditioned on non-occurrence, the other about a past event conditioned on actual occurrence. The relation to each differs. The third argument shows that one knowledge can exist without the other: one may know that Zayd will definitely arrive but not know at the moment of arrival that he has arrived, lacking prior knowledge. The author then affirms that the two knowledges are truly distinct and that change occurs only in the relation or attribution, not in the eternal essence of God. He reports a Mu'tazilite position that concedes the distinctness but denies any change, explaining with the example of Saturday: God eternally knows the entry into Saturday on Friday and the non-entry on Saturday, with only a descriptive difference. The same applies to the world's non-existence in pre-eternity and its subsequent existence.

Key Terms
علم الغد
تغاير العلمين
العالمية
قدم الذات
متولد
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That change in God's knowledge implies change in God's essence.
Correction: The text explains that the change occurs only in the relation (إضافة) or attribution (العالمية), while God's eternal essence remains unchanged. The Mu'tazilite position even denies any change in the knowledges themselves, attributing the difference to descriptive aspects.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the arguments given for the distinction of the two knowledges?
A) The two knowledges have different objects and conditions.
B) The relation to one differing thing differs from the relation to the other.
C) One knowledge can exist without the other.
D) The two knowledges are identical in essence and differ only in description.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of future events and His knowledge of their occurrence are distinct knowledges, yet this distinction involves only a change in relation, not in His eternal essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God’s knowledge of your past sins is distinct from His knowledge of your future obedience. Are you living today as if His knowledge of your future is a fixed excuse for your current failures? Write a sincere plan to stop using God’s foreknowledge as a cover for procrastination in repentance.
?
The passage shows change only in relations, not in God’s essence. Reflect: Is your concept of God’s immutability used to avoid feeling accountable for your changing relationship with Him? How can you today reorient your heart to actively pursue a changed relationship with the unchanging Lord?
?
If God’s knowledge of your tomorrow does not guarantee your tomorrow's obedience, then your today matters absolutely. What specific act of obedience will you perform in the next hour to demonstrate that you believe your future is not fixed but open before God’s distinct knowledge?

Refutation of the Objection Regarding Ignorance
The author answers an objection about divine knowledge of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that divine knowledge of non-existent future events would be ignorance.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge of future non-existence is not ignorance because it knows the temporal state as it is.

The objection claims that if God knows something will not happen, that would be ignorance when it does not occur. The author has a precise answer: knowledge attaches to the relation of non-existence at a specific time, not to existence. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection concerns 'assertive knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-taṣdīqī) and argues that God's knowledge of a future event that does not occur would be ignorance due to the non-existence of its object.
Note the phrase 'independent relation' (al-nisba al-istiqlāliyya).
2
Identify the author's denial
The author answers by 'denial' (al-manʿ): the attachment of knowledge in the state of non-existence is that the event will exist, not that it exists now.
This is the key move: the object of knowledge is the relation, not the actualized event.
3
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between attachment in the state of non-existence (it will exist) and attachment in the state of existence (it exists now). Ignorance would require the latter in the former state.
True ignorance is to attach existence-knowledge to a non-existent state.
4
Follow the example of the world
The example: God eternally knows the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity, its existence in the future, and its annihilation later—without any change.
This shows that multiple temporal attachments coexist in God's eternal knowledge without contradiction.
5
Appreciate the refutation of the Imam's objection
The Imam's objection (If God knows the world exists now, then either His prior knowledge remains causing ignorance or ceases causing change) is repelled: God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity does not remain in the present; rather, the present state is known differently.
The principle: what is proven eternal cannot cease, but the knowledge of temporal states is eternal in its scope, not its content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الكلام في العلم التصديقي. ولا خفاء في أن تعلق عالميته بهذه النسبة ، وهو أنه يحصل له الدخول يوم السبت وللعالم الوجود فيما لا يزال لو بقي يوم السبت، وفيما لا يزال لكان جهلاً لانتفاء متعلقه الذي هو النسبة الاستقلالية. أجيب : بالمنع فإن ذلك التعلق حال عدمه بأنه سيوجد وهذه النسبة بحالها، وإنما الجهل هو أن يحصل التعلق حال وجوده بأنه سيوجد وهو غير التعلق الثاني والحاصل أن التعلق بالعدم في حال معينة، والوجود في حالة أخرى باق أزلاً وأبداً لا ينقلب جهلاً أصلاً. فقد علم الباري في الأزل عدم العالم في الأزل، ووجوده فيما لا يزال، وفناه بعد ذلك ويوم القيامة أيضاً بعلمه كذلك من غير تغير أصلاً، وهذا الكلام يدفع اعتراض الإمام ، بأن الباري تعالى إذا أوجد العالم ، وعلم أنه موجود في الحال، فإما أن يبقى علمه في الأزل بأنه معدوم في الحال فيلزم الجهل والجمع بين الاعتقادين المتنافيين وإما أن يزول فيلزم زوال القديم، وقد تقرر أن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه .
If it is said: The discussion is about assertive knowledge (al-ʿilm al-taṣdīqī). It is not hidden that the attachment of His attribution to this relation, which is that he will have entry on Saturday and the world will have existence in what is to come, if Saturday remains and in what is to come, would be ignorance due to the non-existence of its object, which is the independent relation. The answer is: By denial, for that attachment in the state of its non-existence is that it will exist, and this relation remains as it is. Ignorance is only that the attachment occurs in the state of its existence as that it will exist, which is other than the second attachment. The upshot is that the attachment to non-existence in a specific state and to existence in another state remains eternally and forever and does not turn into ignorance at all. For God in pre-eternity knew the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity, its existence in what is to come, and its annihilation after that, and on the Day of Resurrection also, with His knowledge likewise without any change. And this speech repels the objection of the Imām that if God, exalted, brings the world into existence and knows that it exists in the present, then either His knowledge in pre-eternity that it is non-existent in the present remains, necessitating ignorance and the combination of two contradictory beliefs, or it ceases, necessitating the cessation of the eternal, and it is established that what is proven eternal is impossible to cease.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that God's knowledge of a future event (like entry on Saturday) when that event does not occur would be ignorance, because the object of knowledge (the relation) is non-existent. The author answers by denying the premise: the attachment of knowledge in the state of non-existence is that the event will exist, not that it exists now. True ignorance would be to attach knowledge of existence when the event has already occurred but is known as future. The author clarifies that attachment to non-existence at one time and existence at another is eternal and does not become ignorance. He gives the example of God's knowledge of the world: God eternally knows its non-existence in pre-eternity, its existence in the future, and its annihilation later, without any change. This refutes the Imām's objection that if God knows the world as existing now, then either His prior knowledge of its non-existence remains (causing ignorance) or it ceases (causing change in the eternal). The established principle is that what is proven eternal cannot cease.

Key Terms
علم تصديقي
نسبة استقلالية
جهل
اعتراض الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge of something as future implies ignorance when it occurs.
Correction: God's knowledge is of the temporal state of the event: He knows it as non-existent when it is non-existent, and as existent when it is existent. This is not ignorance but accurate knowledge of the relation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is God's knowledge of the world's non-existence in pre-eternity not ignorance when the world later exists?
A) Because God's knowledge changes to match new realities.
B) Because the attachment to non-existence in one state and existence in another is eternal and does not become ignorance.
C) Because God does not know particulars until they occur.
D) Because the object of knowledge is the essence, not the relation.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of future non-existence is not ignorance because it knows the temporal state of non-existence as it is, and that knowledge remains eternal and unchanging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your past sins that God knew before you committed them, do you ever feel that His knowledge of your evil actions somehow diminishes His perfection? How does this lesson's insistence that God's knowledge of non-existence and future states is not ignorance reshape your understanding of His holiness?
?
You claim to believe that God knows everything, including the times you will fail Him. But when you are about to sin, do you truly act as if He already knows that moment as a present reality to Him? What would change if you let the truth that God's knowledge of that future moment is eternal and unchanging sink into your heart right before you choose?
?
The lesson refutes the idea that God's knowledge of non-existence is ignorance. In your prayer life, do you sometimes treat God's silence as if He doesn't know what you need? How can you train yourself to trust that His knowledge of your current lack is a real, eternal knowledge that moves Him to act—if you will only seek Him?

The View that Divine Knowledge of Particulars Changes
The author reports the view that God's knowledge of particulars changes after their occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that God's knowledge of particulars changes after their occurrence, as held by Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge covers essences; knowledge of particular events arises in time.

The passage reports a controversial view within Islamic theology: that God's knowledge of changing particulars is not eternal but occurs only after those particulars exist. This view was advocated by early sectarian figures. Understanding this position helps clarify the orthodox rejection of change in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the distinction between knowledge of essences (ḥaqāʾiq) and knowledge of particular occurrences (ashkhāṣ wa aḥwāl).
The passage begins with a parenthetical (qāla) indicating a quotation.
2
Identify the two domains of knowledge
The passage states that God eternally knows realities and essences, but knows particular persons and states only after they occur. This is the core of the doctrine.
3
Recognize the proponents
Note the names: Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn (likely al-Basri). They held this view without denying the eternity of God's essence.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
The passage clarifies that this change in knowledge does not imply a change in God's essence, only in the objects of knowledge.
This is crucial to avoid misrepresenting the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) والتزم تغير علمه بالجزئيات المتغيرة كما ذهب إليه هشام من أنه عالم في الأزل بالحقائق والماهيات، وإنما يعلم الأشخاص والأحوال بعد حدوثها) . والتزم يعني ذهب أبو الحسين إلى أن علم الباري بالجزئيات يتغير بتغيرها، ويحدث بعد وقوعها، ولا يقدح ذلك في قدم الذات كما هو مذهب جهم بن صفوان ، وهشام بن الحكم من القدماء، وهو في أنه في الأزل إنما يعلم الماهيات والحقائق وأما التصديقات أعني الأحكام بأن هذا قد وجد، وذلك قد عدم، فإنما يحدث فيما لا يزال، وكذا تصور الجزئيات الحادثة. وبالجملة فذاته توجب العلم
(He said) and he committed to the change of His knowledge of changing particulars, as Hisham held that He is knowing in pre-eternity of realities and essences, and only knows persons and states after their occurrence. And 'committed' means Abu al-Husayn held that God's knowledge of particulars changes with their change and occurs after their occurrence, and that does not impair the eternity of the essence, as is the doctrine of Jahm ibn Ṣafwān and Hisham ibn al-Ḥakam among the ancients. And it is that in pre-eternity He only knows essences and realities, but the assertions (taṣdīqāt), meaning the judgments that this has existed and that has non-existed, only occur in what is to come, and likewise the conception of occurring particulars. In short, His essence necessitates knowledge...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the theological view that God's knowledge of particulars is not eternal but occurs after their existence. The author reports that Hisham (likely Hisham ibn al-Ḥakam) held that God in pre-eternity knows only universals (realities and essences) and knows particular persons and states only after they occur. The phrase 'committed' refers to Abu al-Husayn (al-Baṣrī?) who held a similar view, stating that divine knowledge of changing particulars changes with them and comes into being after their occurrence, without impairing the eternity of God's essence—a doctrine also attributed to Jahm ibn Ṣafwān and Hisham ibn al-Ḥakam. The distinguishing feature is that God's eternal knowledge is of essences and realities (al-māhiyāt wa al-ḥaqāʾiq), while assertive judgments (taṣdīqāt) about existence and non-existence occur only in time (fīmā lā yazāl). Similarly, the conception of particular events arises only after they happen. The passage concludes that God's essence necessitates knowledge, but the text breaks off.

Key Terms

الماهيات
الحقائق
التصديقات
الأشخاص والأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this view implies a change in God's essence.
Correction: The proponents maintain that only God's knowledge changes, not His essence, which remains eternal.

Checkpoint

B) Only essences and realities, not particular occurrences.
C) Only the future, not the past.
D) Nothing at all in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine teaches that God's eternal knowledge encompasses universal essences, while knowledge of particular events occurs after their existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Objection on God's Knowledge of Temporal Objects
Refutes the claim that God's knowledge must change with temporal existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classical response refutes the claim that God's knowledge must change with temporal events.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one eternal knowledge with multiple relational aspects.

This passage addresses a key objection about divine knowledge: if God knows temporal events, does His knowledge change? The author clarifies that God's eternal knowledge encompasses all times without alteration, preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the definition of ignorance at the start.
2
Understand the definition of ignorance
The passage defines ignorance as a relation (talluq) that occurs at a specific time. Recognize that this definition sets the stage for the objection and response.
3
Grasp the objection (the dilemma)
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: either God's eternal knowledge remains (leading to ignorance) or it ceases (leading to change in the eternal). Understand why this is problematic.
This objection is attributed to Imam al-Razi.
4
See the response
The response argues that God's knowledge is one eternal knowledge that relates to different states without change. The relational aspect differs, but the essence remains the same.
This preserves divine immutability.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why this response is crucial: it maintains that God is not subject to change and His knowledge is perfect and timeless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهل هو أن يحصل التعلق حال وجوده بأنه سيوجد وهو غير التعلق الثاني والحاصل أن التعلق بالعدم في حال معينة، والوجود في حالة أخرى باق أزلاً وأبداً لا ينقلب جهلاً أصلاً. فقد علم الباري في الأزل عدم العالم في الأزل، ووجوده فيما لا يزال، وفناه بعد ذلك ويوم القيامة أيضاً بعلمه كذلك من غير تغير أصلاً، وهذا الكلام يدفع اعتراض الإمام، بأن الباري تعالى إذا أوجد العالم، وعلم أنه موجود في الحال، فإما أن يبقى علمه في الأزل بأنه معدوم في الحال فيلزم الجهل والجمع بين الاعتقادين المتنافيين وإما أن يزول فيلزم زوال القديم، وقد تقرر أن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه.
Ignorance is that the relation (talluq) occurs at the time of its existence that it will exist, and it is other than the second relation. The conclusion is that the relation to non-existence in a specific state, and to existence in another state, remains eternally and forever, and does not turn into ignorance at all. For the Creator knew from eternity the non-existence of the world in eternity, and its existence in the future, and its annihilation after that and on the Day of Resurrection as well, with His knowledge similarly, without any change at all. And this speech repels the objection of the Imam that if the Exalted Creator brings the world into existence, and knows that it exists presently, then either His knowledge in eternity that it is non-existent presently remains, necessitating ignorance and the combination of two contradictory beliefs, or it ceases, necessitating the cessation of the eternal, and it has been established that whatever is proven eternal is impossible to cease.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection about God's knowledge of temporal objects. It begins by defining ignorance as a relation (talluq) that holds when something exists, but the relation is different from another relation. The key point is that God's knowledge relates to non-existence at one time and existence at another, yet this does not imply change in His essence—the knowledge itself remains one eternal knowledge with multiple relational aspects. The objection (from Imam al-Razi?) states: when God creates the world, He knows it exists now; either His eternal knowledge that it was non-existent remains (leading to ignorance and contradiction) or it ceases (implying change in the eternal). The response argues that the knowledge relation is timeless and does not transform; it is the same knowledge that encompasses both states without alteration, so no ignorance or change follows.

Key Terms
تعلق
علم الباري
جهل
قديم
اعتراض الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge must change if its objects change, confusing relational aspects with essence.
Correction: God's knowledge is one eternal knowledge that relates to different temporal states without alteration in its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God's knowledge not become ignorance when He knows the world exists after knowing it would be non-existent?
A) Because His knowledge changes with the objects.
B) Because the knowledge relation is singular and eternal, encompassing both states without alteration.
C) Because ignorance is impossible for God.
D) Because the world does not actually come into existence.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one unchanging eternal knowledge that relates to different temporal states without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knew every sin you would commit before you were born, yet He still created you? How does that reality shape your response to your own failures and your trust in His mercy?
?
If God's knowledge is unchanging and your repentance does not surprise Him, does that make you more or less motivated to sincerely turn back to Him? Explain why.
?
The objection in this lesson arises from limiting God to human attributes. Where in your life do you subtly impose human limitations on God's knowledge, power, or will, and how will you actively correct that this week?

The Doctrine of Changing Knowledge of Particulars
Presents the doctrine and its justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doctrine that God's knowledge of particulars changes, its proponents, and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars is conditioned on their existence, not eternal.

Some theologians argued that God's knowledge of changing particulars must itself change. This lesson introduces that view, attributed to Hisham, Abu al-Husayn, and Jahm, and explains how they saw it consistent with God's eternal essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponents
Read the passage and find the names of those who held this view (Hisham, Abu al-Husayn, Jahm).
They are mentioned in the first few lines.
2
Distinguish two kinds of knowledge
Note the distinction between knowledge of essences (māhiyāt) and knowledge of individuals and affirmations (tasdiqāt). The former is eternal, the latter arises with existence.
3
Understand the key claim
Grasp the core idea: God's essence necessitates knowledge only on condition of existence. Therefore, knowledge of a thing occurs only when it exists and perishes with it.
This does not imply change in the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتزم تغير علمه بالجزئيات المتغيرة كما ذهب إليه هشام من أنه عالم في الأزل بالحقائق والماهيات، وإنما يعلم الأشخاص والأحوال بعد حدوثها. والتزم يعني ذهب أبو الحسين إلى أن علم الباري بالجزئيات يتغير بتغيرها، ويحدث بعد وقوعها، ولا يقدح ذلك في قدم الذات كما هو مذهب جهم بن صفوان، وهشام بن الحكم من القدماء، وهو في أنه في الأزل إنما يعلم الماهيات والحقائق وأما التصديقات أعني الأحكام بأن هذا قد وجد، وذلك قد عدم، فإنما يحدث فيما لا يزال، وكذا تصور الجزئيات الحادثة. وبالجملة فذاته توجب العلم بالشيء بشرط وجوده فلا يحصل قبل وجوده، ولا يبقى بعد فنائه، ولا امتناع في اتصاف الذات بعلوم حادثة هي تعلقات وإضافات، ولا في حدوثها مع كونها مستندة إلى القديم بطريق الإيجاب دون الاختيار لكونه مشروطة بشروط حادثة. وأما اعتراض الإمام بأن كل صفة تعرض للواجب، فذات الواجب إما أن يكفي في ثبوتها أو انتفائها، فيلزم دوام ثبوتها أو انتفائها بدوام الذات من غير تغير، وإما أن لا تكفي فيتوقف ثبوتها أو انتفاؤها على أمر منفصل، وبالذات لا تنفك عن ثبوت تلك الصفة أو انتفائها الموقوف على ذلك الأمر. فيلزم توقف الذات عليه، لأن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقف على ذلك الشيء؛ فيلزم إمكان الواجب، لأن الموقوف على الممكن أولى بأن يكون ممكناً، ففي غاية الضعف لأن ما لا ينفك عن الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون متوقفاً عليه، كما في وجود زيد مع وجود عمر و أو عدمه إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى، وقد يستدل على علمه بالجزئيات، بأن الخلو عنه جهل ، ونقص، وبأن كل أحد من المطيع والعاصي يلجأ إليه في كشف الملمات، ودفع البليات، ولولا أنه مما تشهد به فطرة جميع العقلاء لما كان كذلك، وبأن الجزئيات مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء وتوسطاً. وقد اتفق الحكماء على أنه عالم بذاته، وأن العلم بالعلة، يوجب العلم بالمعلول.
And he committed to the change of His knowledge of changing particulars, as Hisham went to that he knows in eternity the realities and essences, and he only knows individuals and states after their occurrence. 'Committed' means Abu al-Husayn went to that the knowledge of the Creator of particulars changes with their change, and occurs after their occurrence, and that does not impair the pre-eternity of the essence, as is the doctrine of Jahm ibn Safwan and Hisham ibn al-Hakam among the predecessors: that in eternity He only knows the essences and realities; as for the affirmations, i.e., the judgments that this has existed and that has ceased, they occur in the future, and likewise the conceptualization of occurring particulars. In summary, His essence necessitates knowledge of a thing on condition of its existence, so it does not occur before its existence, nor remain after its annihilation, and there is no impossibility in the essence being described with occurring sciences that are relations and attributes, nor in their occurrence while they are dependent on the Eternal by way of necessitation without choice, because they are conditioned by occurring conditions. As for the objection of the Imam that every attribute that occurs to the Necessary Being, either the essence of the Necessary is sufficient for its affirmation or negation, then the permanence of its affirmation or negation follows from the permanence of the essence without change; or it is not sufficient, then its affirmation or negation depends on a separate matter, and the essence is never free from the affirmation or negation of that attribute which is dependent on that matter. Then the dependence of the essence on it follows, because that which is dependent on what is dependent on something is dependent on that something; thus the possibility of the Necessary follows, because what is dependent on the possible is more deserving of being possible. But this is extremely weak, because that which is never free from something does not necessarily depend on it, as in the existence of Zayd with the existence of Amr or his non-existence and innumerable other examples. And it has been argued for His knowledge of particulars by the fact that being devoid of it is ignorance and deficiency, and that every individual, whether obedient or disobedient, resorts to Him in uncovering misfortunes and repelling calamities, and were it not that this is witnessed by the natural disposition of all rational beings, it would not be so. And by the fact that particulars are dependent on God, may He be exalted, initially and through intermediaries. And the philosophers have agreed that He is knowledgeable of His essence, and that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the position that God's knowledge of particulars changes over time. It begins by attributing this view to Hisham and Abu al-Husayn, who claimed God knows essences (māhiyāt) in eternity but only knows individuals and states after they occur. This position is also associated with Jahm ibn Safwan. The argument is that God's essence necessitates knowledge only on condition of existence, so such knowledge arises only when the object exists. They see no impossibility in God acquiring new relational knowledges (talluqāt) dependent on temporal conditions without implying change in the essence. The author then presents an objection (from Imam al-Razi?) that if an attribute (such as knowledge) occurs to the Necessary Being, either the essence alone suffices for its existence, in which case it would be eternal, or it depends on an external condition, and since the essence is never without that attribute when the condition is met, the essence becomes dependent on that condition, implying contingency (imkān) of the Necessary. The author refutes this objection as extremely weak, because 'never being free from something' does not imply dependence; for example, Zayd's existence is not dependent on Amr's existence or non-existence even though they coincide. Finally, the author provides independent proofs for God's knowledge of particulars: (1) lack of it is ignorance and deficiency, (2) universal human instinct to pray to God in distress shows this knowledge, (3) particulars are caused by God directly or indirectly, and (4) the philosophers' principle that knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of the effect.

Key Terms
ماهيات
تصديقات
تغير
تعلق
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does God acquire knowledge of individual particulars?
A) In eternity
B) At the time of their occurrence
C) After their annihilation
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Some theologians maintained that God's knowledge of particulars is not eternal but arises when the objects exist, without affecting His eternal essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God’s knowledge of your every action arises only at the moment you do it, would that make you feel more accountable or less? Would you pray differently?
?
The passage describes God’s occurring knowledges as ‘relational attributes’ that do not change His essence. Does this idea bring you comfort or discomfort? What does your reaction reveal about how you imagine God?
?
If God knows your intentions only when you actually form them, how does that affect your view of divine justice? Does it make you more careful about what you intend, or does it reduce your sense of being known?

Refutation and Proofs of God's Knowledge of Particulars
Refutes an objection and provides positive proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that changing knowledge implies contingency of God, the refutation of that objection, and two independent proofs for God's knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars does not change; rather, arguments confirm His eternal knowledge of all things.

This lesson presents the critical objection that if God's knowledge of particulars changes, then His essence becomes contingent. The author refutes this objection as weak, and then provides positive arguments: the universal human instinct to pray to God in distress, and the principle that knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection attributed to Imam al-Razi that if God's knowledge depends on external conditions, the Necessary Being becomes possible.
The objection uses the concept of 'dependent on the possible'.
2
Understand the refutation
Note the author's response: 'never being free from something does not imply dependence', using the example of Zayd and Amr.
This refutation is described as 'extremely weak' by the author.
3
Examine the first proof
Read the proof from fitra (natural disposition): all people, whether obedient or disobedient, instinctively turn to God in distress.
This shows that people believe God knows their particular situations.
4
Examine the second proof
Read the proof from causality: particulars are caused by God, and knowledge of the cause yields knowledge of the effect, as agreed by philosophers.

Misconception & Correction
