Refuting the Objection against Originated Attributes
Refutes an objection about originated attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that originated attributes would necessitate possibility of the Necessary Being is invalid.

Core Idea
Inseparability does not imply dependence: a key logical distinction.

This passage addresses a common objection that originated attributes in God would lead to His dependence on created things, implying His possibility. The author refutes this by clarifying the logical relationship between inseparability and dependence, using a simple example.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection as presented by the Imam. Note the two branches: either the essence suffices or it does not.
2
Identify the refutation
The author argues that inseparability does not entail dependence. Find the key sentence and the example of Zayd and 'Amr.
3
Reflect on the logical distinction
Consider how this distinction resolves the objection. Why is it valid to say that something can be inseparable without being dependent?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالشيء بشرط وجوده فلا يحصل قبل وجوده، ولا يبقى بعد فنائه، ولا امتناع في اتصاف الذات بعلوم حادثة هي تعلقات وإضافات، ولا في حدوثها مع كونها مستندة إلى القديم بطريق الإيجاب دون الاختيار لكونه مشروطة بشروط حادثة. وأما اعتراض الإمام بأن كل صفة تعرض للواجب، فذات الواجب إما أن يكفي في ثبوتها أو انتفائها، فيلزم دوام ثبوتها أو انتفائها بدوام الذات من غير تغير، وإما أن لا تكفي فيتوقف ثبوتها أو انتفاؤها على أمر منفصل، وبالذات لا تنفك عن ثبوت تلك الصفة أو انتفائها الموقوف على ذلك الأمر. فيلزم توقف الذات عليه، لأن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقف على ذلك الشيء؛ فيلزم إمكان الواجب، لأن الموقوف على الممكن أولى بأن يكون ممكناً، ففي غاية الضعف لأن ما لا ينفك عن الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون متوقفاً عليه، كما في وجود زيد مع وجود عمر و أو عدمه إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى، وقد يستدل على علمه بالجزئيات، بأن الخلو عنه جهل ، ونقص، وبأن كل أحد من المطيع والعاصي يلجأ إليه في كشف الملمات، ودفع البليات، ولولا أنه مما تشهد به فطرة جميع العقلاء لما كان كذلك، وبأن الجزئيات مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء وتوسطاً. وقد اتفق الحكماء على أنه عالم بذاته، وأن العلم بالعلة، يوجب العلم بالمعلول.
By the thing with the condition of its existence, so it does not occur before its existence, and it does not remain after its annihilation, and there is no impossibility in the essence being described by originated knowledges that are relations and additions, nor in their occurrence while they are dependent on the Eternal by way of necessitation without choice because they are conditioned by originated conditions. As for the objection of the Imam that every attribute that occurs to the Necessary, the essence of the Necessary either suffices for its affirmation or negation, so the permanence of its affirmation or negation is necessitated by the permanence of the essence without change, or it does not suffice, so its affirmation or negation depends on a separate matter, and the essence is inseparable from the affirmation or negation of that attribute that is dependent on that matter. Thus the dependence of the essence on it is necessitated, because what is dependent on what is dependent on a thing is dependent on that thing; thus the possibility of the Necessary is necessitated, because what is dependent on the possible is more deserving of being possible. This is extremely weak, because what is inseparable from a thing is not necessarily dependent on it, as in the existence of Zayd with the existence of 'Amr or its absence and so on ad infinitum. And one may adduce proof for His knowledge of particulars: that being devoid of it is ignorance and deficiency; and that every person, obedient and disobedient, takes refuge in Him in uncovering calamities and repelling misfortunes, and were it not that this is witnessed by the innate disposition of all rational beings, it would not be so; and that particulars are dependent on God, may He be exalted, initially and intermediately. And the philosophers have agreed that He is knowing by His essence, and that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the possibility of originated attributes in the divine essence and then provides proofs for God's knowledge of particulars. The author first explains that there is no impossibility in the essence being described with originated knowledges that are relations and additions, because they are conditioned by originated conditions. Then he presents the objection of the Imam (likely al-Razi or al-Ghazali) which argues that if the essence does not suffice for the attribute, then the essence would depend on something else, leading to the possibility of the Necessary, which is absurd. The author refutes this by pointing out that inseparability does not entail dependence, giving the example of Zayd's existence with 'Amr's existence or absence. Then he provides three proofs for God's knowledge of particulars: 1) ignorance of them would be a deficiency, 2) universal human instinct to turn to God in difficulties, and 3) particulars are caused by God directly or indirectly. Finally, he cites the consensus of philosophers that God knows Himself and that knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect.

Key Terms
الواجب
الممكن
الموقوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot have originated attributes because that would imply change in His essence.
Correction: The attributes are relations and additions that do not affect the essence; they are conditioned by external conditions and do not change the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the objection that inseparability implies dependence?
A) The existence of Zayd and 'Amr
B) The existence of the world and God
C) The relationship of fire and heat
D) The attribute of knowledge in humans

Key Takeaway
The objection that originated attributes necessitate possibility is invalid because inseparability does not entail dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's knowledge of your actions does not depend on those actions, does that make you feel more accountable or less? How does the distinction between inseparability and dependence shape your answer?
?
If your worship is merely a conditioned relation to an unchanging divine attribute, does that diminish its value or make it purely about your own benefit? What does this lesson demand of your sincerity?
?
The example of Zayd and 'Amr shows coexistence without dependence. In your struggles, can you identify areas where you assume that because something is always present with your trials, it must be the cause? How will you reorient your reliance on Allah based on this lesson?

Three Proofs for God's Knowledge of Particulars
Provides three proofs for God's knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs for God's knowledge of particulars: the argument from perfection, universal human instinct, and causal dependency.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of particulars is necessary for His perfection and is witnessed by innate human nature.

After refuting an objection, the author presents positive proofs that God knows particulars. He gives three arguments: ignorance would be a deficiency, all people naturally turn to God in distress, and all particulars are caused by God. Additionally, he cites the philosophers' consensus that knowledge of the cause gives knowledge of the effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs
Read carefully and list the three reasons given. Also note the additional consensus argument.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof: being devoid of knowledge of particulars is ignorance and deficiency. Why is this a valid argument for God's perfection?
Ignorance is a defect, and God is free from defects.
3
Reflect on the second proof
The second proof: all people, obedient and disobedient, take refuge in God during calamities. This shows an innate recognition of God's power, which would not exist if false.
This is based on fitrah (innate disposition).
4
Consider the third proof
The third proof: all particulars are dependent on God directly or indirectly. Therefore He must know them as their cause.
This is a causal argument.

Consensus and Differences on Divine Will
Discusses consensus on God being willing and the differing views on His will's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal consensus that God is willing and the differing theological interpretations of the nature of His will as held by Ash'aris, Jubbā'is, and Karrāmīs.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal and essential, yet scholars disagree on its exact nature.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses the divine attribute of will (irāda). While all theologians agree that God is willing, they differ on whether this will is an eternal attribute subsisting in God, an attribute without a locus, or a created attribute. The author presents the Ash'ari position as the correct one and explains the rational argument that underpins it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the repeated heading 'المبحث الرابع' indicating a structured investigation.
2
Identify the three positions
Locate the views of the Ash'aris, Jubbā'is, and Karrāmīs on divine will. Note how each school defines the attribute.
The Ash'ari position is described first and in more detail.
3
Understand the Ash'ari argument
Follow the reasoning: specification of possible outcomes requires a special attribute distinct from knowledge and power. This attribute is eternal and subsists in God.
The argument avoids infinite regress and maintains compatibility with choice.
4
Reflect on the consensus
Note that despite differences, all groups affirm that God is willing. The consensus is based on scripture and the concept of agent by choice.
This shows the foundational nature of the attribute of will in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع في أنه تعالى مريد في أنه مريد، اتفقوا على ذلك ودل عليه كونه فاعلاً بالاختيار، فعندنا بصفة قديمة قائمة بذاته على قياس سائر الصفات للقطع بأن تخصيص أحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع يكون لصفة خاصة نجدها من أنفسنا ليست هي العلم والقدرة ونحوهما، وتعلقها لذاتها فلا يلزم تسلسل الإرادات ووجوب المراد بها لا ينافي الاختيار وقدمها لا يوجب قدمه، ولا ينافي حدوث تعلقها) . المبحث الرابع : اتفق المتكلمون والحكماء وجميع الفرق على إطلاق القول بأنه مريد، وشاع ذلك في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الأنبياء عليهم السلام (۱) ، ودل عليه ما ثبت من كونه تعالى فاعلاً بالاختيار، لأنه معناه القصد والإرادة مع ملاحظة ما للطرف الآخر، فكأن المختار ينظر إلى الطرفين ويميل إلى أحدهما ، والمريد ينظر إلى الطرف الذي يريده لكن كثرة الخلاف في معنى إرادته فعندنا صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات قائمة به على ما هو شأن سائر الصفات الحقيقية. وعند الجبائية (۲) صفة زائدة قائمة لا محل وعند الكرامية صفة حادثة
The fourth investigation: concerning that He, exalted is He, is willing. Regarding His being willing, they have agreed upon that, and His being an agent by choice indicates it. For us, He has an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, analogous to the other attributes, because it is certain that the specification of one of the two sides of the possible to occur is due to a special attribute that we find in ourselves that is not knowledge, power, and the like. And its relation is by its essence, so an infinite regress of wills is not necessitated, and the necessitation of the object of will by it does not contradict choice, and its pre-eternity does not necessitate His pre-eternity, nor does it contradict the origination of its relation. The fourth investigation: The theologians, philosophers, and all sects have agreed on the unrestricted statement that He is willing, and that is widespread in the speech of God, exalted is He, and the speech of the prophets, peace be upon them. And what has been established of His being an agent by choice indicates it, because its meaning is intention and will with consideration of the other side, as if the chooser looks at the two sides and inclines to one of them, and the willing one looks at the side that He wills. However, there is much disagreement concerning the meaning of His will. For us, it is an eternal attribute additional to the essence subsisting in Him, as is the case with the rest of the real attributes. According to the Jubbā'īs, it is an additional attribute subsisting without a locus. According to the Karrāmīs, it is an originated attribute.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the topic of God's will (irāda) and presents the consensus on it along with differing schools' views. It is a classification of the positions. The author begins by stating the fourth investigation (al-mabḥath al-rābiʿ), which examines God being willing. He notes universal agreement on the attribution of will to God, supported by His being an agent by choice (fāʿil bil-ikhtiyār). For the Ashʿarīs (implied by 'us'), will is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence, analogous to other real attributes. The argument is that the specification of one of two possible outcomes requires a special attribute distinct from knowledge and power. This attribute relates to its objects by its essence, avoiding infinite regress of wills. The necessitation of the object of will by this attribute does not negate choice, and the attribute's eternity does not imply the eternity of its objects, nor does it contradict the temporal origination of its relational aspect. The author then repeats the heading 'The fourth investigation' and elaborates that theologians, philosophers, and all sects agree on the statement that God is willing, citing scriptural evidence. He explains that being an agent by choice implies intention and will, with the chooser considering alternatives. However, he notes disagreement on the nature of this will: the Ashʿarīs hold it to be an eternal additional attribute subsisting in God; the Jubbā'īs (a Muʿtazilī school) consider it an additional attribute subsisting without a locus; and the Karrāmīs say it is an originated attribute.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المريد
الفاعل بالاختيار
الصفة القديمة
الجبائية
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is temporal like human will.
Correction: The author clarifies that God's will is eternal while its relation to created things may be temporal; the attribute itself is pre-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the Ashʿarīs as presented in this passage, God's will is:
A) An eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence.
B) An originated attribute.
C) An attribute subsisting without a locus.
D) Identical to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
While all theologians agree that God is willing, they differ on whether His will is an eternal attribute (Ash'ari), an attribute without a locus (Jubbā'i), or an originated attribute (Karrāmī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is willing, but have you ever truly felt His will in your daily choices? When you face a decision, do you seek His guidance as a willing Lord, or do you rely solely on your own reasoning? What would change if you acted today as if God's will is actively choosing for you?
?
The passage says God's will is eternal and does not change, yet you may have experienced times when you thought God's will for you changed. Does this passage challenge your understanding of God's constancy? How will you reconcile His unchanging will with your changing circumstances?
?
"The specification of one side of a possible to occur is due to a special attribute." Think of a specific past event in your life that seemed random. Can you now see it as a result of God's will? How does acknowledging that God's will is a real, eternal attribute change your trust in His plan for your future?

The Consensus That God Is Willing and the Proof from Agency by Choice
Introduction to the fourth investigation on God's will, stating consensus and proof from free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal consensus that God is described as willing (مريد), the proof from His agency by choice, and the distinction between 'chooser' and 'willer' that sets up the debate on the nature of divine will.

Core Idea
All thinkers agree God is willing; the proof lies in His free agency, but the meaning of His will remains debated.

This lesson opens the fourth investigation on God's will. The author begins by stating a remarkable consensus: theologians, philosophers, and all sects affirm that God is willing. The proof is drawn from the fact that God is an agent by choice, which implies intention and will. However, as the passage notes, this agreement is only on the word; the real debate is on what God's will actually means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the consensus
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Observe that the author explicitly states that all groups—theologians and philosophers—agree that God is described as 'willing' (مريد). This consensus is found in the Qur'an and Sunnah.
The consensus is on the attribution, not the meaning.
2
Identify the proof from agency by choice
The passage states that the proof for God being willing is that He is an agent by choice (فاعل بالاختيار). Since choosing implies intention and will, God must have will.
This proof connects divine will to divine action.
3
Understand the distinction and the remaining debate
The author distinguishes between 'chooser' (مختار) and 'willer' (مريد): the chooser considers two sides and inclines to one, while the willer focuses on the preferred side. Despite consensus on the term, there is abundant disagreement on the meaning of God's will.
This sets up the next lessons which explore different theological and philosophical views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع : اتفق المتكلمون والحكماء وجميع الفرق على إطلاق القول بأنه مريد، وشاع ذلك في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الأنبياء عليهم السلام (۱) ، ودل عليه ما ثبت من كونه تعالى فاعلاً بالاختيار، لأنه معناه القصد والإرادة مع ملاحظة ما للطرف الآخر، فكأن المختار ينظر إلى الطرفين ويميل إلى أحدهما ، والمريد ينظر إلى الطرف الذي يريده لكن كثرة الخلاف في معنى إرادته
The Fourth Investigation: That He, Exalted, is Willing. The theologians, the philosophers, and all sects agreed on affirming that He is willing, and that is widespread in the speech of God Almighty and the speech of the prophets, peace be upon them. And it is indicated by what has been established that He, Exalted, is an agent by choice, because it means intention and will while taking into account the other side. So it is as if the one who chooses looks at the two sides and inclines to one of them, and the one who wills looks at the side that he wills. But the disagreement is abundant regarding the meaning of His will.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth investigation on whether God is willing (مريد). The author states that all theologians and philosophers agree that God is described as willing, a concept found in the Qur'an and prophetic traditions. The proof for this is that God is an agent by choice (فاعل بالاختيار). Acting by choice entails intention and will, because the agent considers alternatives and chooses one. The author distinguishes between 'chooser' (مختار) and 'willer' (مريد): the chooser looks at both sides and leans to one, while the willer looks specifically at what he wills. Despite this consensus, there is great disagreement on the precise meaning of God's will, which sets up the discussion of various views in the next passage.

Key Terms
مريد
فاعل بالاختيار
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sects agree on the meaning of God's will as well.
Correction: The passage only states consensus on affirming that God is willing, not on the precise meaning of His will. The author explicitly notes that there is abundant disagreement (كثرة الخلاف) on the meaning of His will.

Checkpoint
What is the proof given that God is willing (مريد)?
A) The fact that God is the creator of all things.
B) The fact that God is an agent by choice.
C) The fact that God is powerful.
D) The fact that God is knowing.

Key Takeaway
The universal affirmation that God is willing is rooted in His agency by choice, but the precise nature of divine will remains a subject of deep debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is willing. But does your daily life reflect a trust in His will, or do you often act as if your will is independent? What specific decision today can you consciously submit to His will?
?
The author says a chooser looks at two sides and inclines. When you face choices, do you genuinely consider how your decision aligns with what God wills, or do you prioritize your own preference? What is one area where you need to align your will with His?
?
The passage notes disagreement on the meaning of God's will. Have you ever settled for a superficial understanding of this attribute? What would it mean for your worship to truly ponder that God's will is perfect and unlike any human will?

The Many Views on God's Will
Enumerates different theological positions on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diversity of theological views on the nature of God's will and the Ash'ari position within that spectrum.

Core Idea
God's will is understood differently by every major theological school.

In classical Islamic theology, the nature of God's will (إرادة) was a matter of intense debate. Different schools proposed radically different definitions: some saw it as an eternal attribute, others as a created one, and still others as identical to knowledge or essence. This lesson presents the key positions, setting the stage for the Ash'ari proof that will follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation to understand each distinct view.
Notice that each school is identified by a key term or phrase.
2
Identify each school and its definition
Go through the list one by one. For each school, note how they define God's will. Compare the definitions to see how they differ.
There are eight distinct views listed.
3
Focus on the Ash'ari view
The Ash'ari view is presented first (عندنا). Understand that it considers will an eternal attribute additional to the essence. Compare this to the other views.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Ask yourself: why are there so many views? What does this tell us about the complexity of divine attributes? This diversity sets the stage for the need for rigorous theological reasoning.
The passage shows that this is a central issue in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعندنا صفة قديمة زائدة على الذات قائمة به على ما هو شأن سائر الصفات الحقيقية. وعند الجبائية (۲) صفة زائدة قائمة لا محل وعند الكرامية صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار (۱) نفس الذات، وعند النجار (۲) صفة سلبية هي الفاعل ليس بمكره ولا ساء، وعند الفلاسفة العلم بالنظام الأكمل، وعند الكلبي إرادته لفعله تعالى العلم به ولفعل غيره الأمر به، وعند المحققين من المعتزلة هي العلم بما في الفعل من المصلحة
According to us, it is an eternal attribute additional to the essence, subsisting in Him, in the manner of all other real attributes. According to the Jubba'ites, it is an additional attribute subsisting without a locus. According to the Karramites, it is a created attribute subsisting in the essence. According to Dirar, it is the essence itself. According to an-Najjar, it is a negative attribute: that the agent is not compelled and not evil. According to the philosophers, it is knowledge of the most perfect system. According to al-Kalbi, His will for His own act is knowledge of it, and for the act of another it is the command to it. According to the verifiers among the Mu'tazilites, it is knowledge of the benefit in the act.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage lists the different theological views on the nature of God's will (إرادة). The author enumerates them as follows: (1) According to the Ash'aris (عندنا), it is an eternal attribute additional to the essence, subsisting in Him like other real attributes. (2) According to the Jubba'ites (the followers of Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i and his son), it is an additional attribute but subsisting without a locus (لا محل). (3) According to the Karramites, it is a created attribute (صفة حادثة) subsisting in the essence. (4) According to Dirar ibn 'Amr, it is the essence itself (نفس الذات). (5) According to an-Najjar, it is a negative attribute: that the agent is not compelled and not evil. (6) According to the philosophers, it is knowledge of the most perfect system (النظام الأكمل). (7) According to al-Kalbi, God's will for His own act is knowledge of it, and for the act of another it is the command to it. (8) According to the verifiers among the Mu'tazilites, it is knowledge of the benefit in the act. This enumeration shows the wide range of opinions, setting the stage for the Ash'ari proof that will follow.

Key Terms
صفة قديمة
صفة زائدة
صفة حادثة
نفس الذات
صفة سلبية
العلم بالنظام الأكمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari view is the only one; this list shows many other positions.
Correction: The Ash'ari view is one among many. The diversity shows that the nature of God's will was a hotly debated issue in Islamic theology.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, what is the nature of God's will?
A) It is the essence itself.
B) It is a created attribute.
C) It is an eternal attribute additional to the essence.
D) It is knowledge of benefit.

Key Takeaway
The nature of God's will was defined in many ways by different schools, with the Ash'aris holding that it is an eternal, real attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading these eight different definitions, which one most closely matches what you actually believe about God's will? Are you willing to accept that your view might be just one among many theological positions?
?
If God's will is defined as 'knowledge of benefit' (Mu'tazilite view), how would that change the way you seek His guidance in your daily decisions? Would you look for wisdom or for signs? Be honest: does your current practice reflect any of these definitions?
?
Consider the Ash'ari view that God's will is an eternal, real attribute. If that is true, then God's will is as much a part of His nature as His mercy. How should this affect your trust in His plan, especially when life seems chaotic? What would it mean for your heart to truly believe that His will is not separate from Him but eternal and perfect?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Ash'ari Proof for Will as a Distinct Attribute
Ash'ari proof that will is a distinct attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that God's will is a distinct eternal attribute, and how it is distinguished from power and knowledge.

Core Idea
Specification of opposites requires a specifier – that specifier is God's will, distinct from His essence.

The Ash'ari school argues that the fact that God creates some things and not others, at specific times, cannot be due to His essence alone, because the essence is equally related to all possibilities. A distinct attribute of will is required to explain this specification. This lesson presents the core proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with the problem of specification.
2
Understand the problem of specification
If God's essence were the only factor, all opposites would be created equally. But they are not. So there must be a specifier.
3
Identify the attribute of will
The specifier cannot be external (God would be dependent). It must be an internal attribute called will.
Will is defined as the attribute that specifies one possibility over another.
4
Distinguish will from power and knowledge
Power relates equally to all possibilities; will tips the balance. Knowledge is universal; will is particular.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The passage affirms that will is distinct, clear to the intellect, and additional to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك أصحابنا بأن تخصيص بعض الأضداد بالوقوع دون البعض وفي بعض الأوقات دون البعض مع استواء نسبة الذات إلى الكل، لا بد أن يكون لصفة شأنها التخصيص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، وامتناع احتياج الواجب في فاعليته إلى أمر منفصل وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالإرادة، وهو معنى واضح عند العقل، مغاير للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات شأنه التخصيص والترجيح لأحد طرفي المقدور من الفعل والترك على الآخر، وينبه على مغايرتها للقدرة أن نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء بخلافها وللعلم أن مطلق العلم نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، والعلم بما فيه من المصلحة. أو بأنه سيوجد في وقت كذا سابق على الإرادة والعلم بوقوعه تابع للوقوع المتأخر عنها وفيه نظر. إذ قد لا يسلم الخصم سبق العلم بأنه يوجده في وقت كذا على (۳) إرادته ذلك، ولا تأخر علمه بوقوعه حالاً عن إرادته الوقوع حالاً. وما يقال أن العلم تابع للوقوع ، فمعناه أنه يعلم الشيء كما يقع ، وأن المعلوم هو الأصل في التطابق لأنه مثال وصورة له، لا بمعنى تأخره عنه في الخارج البتة. والحق أن مغايرة الحالة التي نسميها بالإرادة للعلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات ضرورية، ثم لقد تبين قدمها، وزيادتها على الذات
Our companions adhere to the fact that the specification of some opposites to occur rather than others, and in some times rather than others, while the relation of the essence to all is equal, must be due to an attribute whose function is specification, because specification without a specifier is impossible, and the impossibility of the Necessary needing in His agency a separate matter. And that attribute is called 'will'. It is a meaning clear to the intellect, distinct from knowledge, power, and other attributes. Its function is specification and weighting of one of the two sides of the possible—act or omission—over the other. It indicates its distinctness from power: that the relation of power to both sides is equal, unlike it; and from knowledge: that absolute knowledge's relation to all is equal, and knowledge of the benefit in it. Or that it will exist at a certain time is prior to the will, and knowledge of its occurrence follows the occurrence that is later than the will. But there is a problem: the opponent may not concede the priority of knowledge that He will bring it into existence at a certain time over His willing that, nor the posteriority of His knowledge of its occurrence at present to His willing its occurrence at present. And what is said that knowledge follows occurrence means that He knows the thing as it occurs, and that the known is the origin in correspondence because it is an example and image of it, not meaning its posteriority in external existence at all. The truth is that the distinctness of the state we call 'will' from knowledge, power, and other attributes is necessary. Then its eternity and its being additional to the essence are established.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof that God's will is a distinct eternal attribute. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The specification of some opposites (e.g., creating the world at one time rather than another, or creating one thing rather than its opposite) cannot be due to the essence alone, because the essence's relation to both opposites is equal. A specifier is needed. (2) That specifier cannot be an external cause, because the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything external in His agency. Therefore, it must be an attribute inherent in Him, which is called 'will'. (3) This attribute is distinct from power because power is equally related to both opposites, while will specifies one. It is distinct from knowledge because knowledge is equally related to all things, while will involves a particular inclination. The author then addresses an objection: some might claim that will is nothing but knowledge of the benefit or knowledge of the time of occurrence. The author argues that knowledge of the benefit is not the same as will, because will involves a specific inclination. Regarding the timing, if someone says God knows He will bring something into existence at time t, and that knowledge is prior to will, the opponent may not concede that. The proper understanding of 'knowledge follows occurrence' is that knowledge corresponds to the known, not that it is temporally posterior. The conclusion is that the distinctness of will from other attributes is self-evident, and its eternity and addition to the essence are proven.

Key Terms
تخصيص
مخصص
إرادة
صفة
قدرة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will is identical to knowledge of what is beneficial or knowledge of the time of occurrence.
Correction: The passage distinguishes will from knowledge: knowledge is universal, while will specifies one possibility. The function of will is not to know but to incline.

Checkpoint
Why must the specifier of opposites be an attribute within God rather than an external cause?
A) Because external causes would make God dependent.
B) Because God's essence is not powerful enough.
C) Because the attribute is created.
D) Because the attribute is knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Will is a distinct eternal attribute that specifies creation, distinct from God's power and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that every detail of creation is specified by God's will, not by chance. Do you truly live as if your successes and failures are specifically willed by God, or do you secretly attribute them to luck or your own effort? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized that nothing happens except by His will?
?
The proof shows that God's will is distinct from His knowledge and power. Does your relationship with God reflect a trust in His will as a personal, specifying attribute, or do you only think of Him as all-knowing and all-powerful? How might your dua (supplication) change if you believed that God's will is actively specifying good for you even when you cannot see it?
?
The Ash'aris argue that the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything external. If God's will is eternal and self-sufficient, what does that say about your own need for external validation or reasons to do good? Are you willing to obey God simply because He wills it, even when you do not see the benefit?
